### [1] [CLIR-Bench: Benchmarking Multimodal Question Answering over Irregular Clinical Time Series](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09880)
*Frank Nie,Ethan B. Liu,Yuan Zhu,Loe Yan,Wei Fan,Jindong Han*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces CLIR-Bench, a benchmark to evaluate how well models perform question answering over irregular, sparse clinical time series, showing current models struggle with such data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical time series used in ICUs are crucial for monitoring and decision support but are typically sparse, irregularly sampled, and asynchronous. Existing QA benchmarks focus on regularly sampled signals or static clinical text, so they do not test whether models can accurately use and justify answers based on irregular temporal observations. There is a need for a benchmark that specifically stresses this capability.

Method: The authors construct CLIR-Bench from de-identified ICU records using a structured four-stage pipeline. They define 6,600 QA instances spanning 11 clinical variables and organize them into four capability dimensions and 11 tasks. Each QA pair is tied to explicit temporal evidence segments and task-specific answer derivation rules so that both answer correctness and evidence grounding can be evaluated. They then run experiments with existing generalist models on this benchmark.

Result: CLIR-Bench is released as a public dataset with 6,600 QA examples over irregular clinical time series, each annotated with evidence and rules. Experimental results show that current generalist models have difficulty retrieving and reasoning over the sparse, irregular clinical evidence in these questions, resulting in relatively poor performance.

Conclusion: CLIR-Bench fills an important gap in evaluation resources by specifically targeting QA over irregular clinical time series with explicit evidence grounding. The poor performance of existing models on this benchmark indicates that stronger methods for reasoning over irregular time series are needed, and CLIR-Bench can serve as a testbed for developing and benchmarking such methods.

Abstract: Clinical time series are central to patient monitoring, risk assessment, and clinical decision support. However, they are often sparse, irregularly sampled, and asynchronous, making it difficult for models to identify the temporal evidence required for clinical Question Answering (QA). Existing benchmarks primarily focus on regularly sampled time-series QA or medical QA over static data, and therefore rarely assess whether models can faithfully ground their answers in irregular temporal observations. To fill this gap, we introduce CLIR-Bench, a benchmark for irregular clinical time series QA constructed from de-identified ICU records through a principled four-stage pipeline. CLIR-Bench contains 6,600 QA instances spanning 11 clinical variables, organized into four capability dimensions and 11 tasks. Each question is linked to explicit temporal evidence and task-specific answer derivation rules, enabling evaluation of both answer accuracy and evidence use. Experiments show that existing generalist models struggle to retrieve and reason over sparse clinical evidence, highlighting the need for stronger irregular time-series reasoning methods. Our code and data are available at https://huggingface.co/datasets/winall/CLIR-Bench.

</details>


### [2] [Index SLM Technical Report](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09885)
*Lusheng Zhang,Shien He,Tianxing Yan,Mengran Yu,Ziang Cui,Kai Zhao,Xiaojing Liu,Tianjiao Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Index-1.9B, a family of 1.9B-parameter small language models that, through tailored pre-training and alignment strategies, achieve performance comparable to much larger open models and are fully open-sourced.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To build highly capable yet computationally efficient small language models that can compete with significantly larger open models, especially for Chinese/English use cases, and to systematically study how factors like data quality, learning-rate schedules, model depth, and instruction data during pre-training affect performance.

Method: They construct four 1.9B-parameter variants: a base pre-trained model, a "pure" model with instruction-like data removed, a chat model aligned via supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization, and a character model that adds retrieval-augmented generation for few-shot role-playing. Pre-training uses a Warmup-Stable-Decay learning-rate schedule that increases the proportion of curated high-quality data during the decay stage, and a Norm-Head output layer to stabilize training with high learning rates. They run controlled experiments varying model depth, learning-rate magnitude and schedule, data quality over the decay phase, and inclusion of instruction-like data in pre-training, and evaluate on standard benchmarks spanning exams, reasoning, math, and code.

Result: Index-1.9B-Base achieves an average score of 64.92 across multiple benchmarks, matching or outperforming several open models that are multiple times larger. Controlled studies reveal how learning-rate schedules, data quality, and instruction data in pre-training influence performance, and they observe an unexpected performance jump mid-way through the constant-learning-rate phase.

Conclusion: Careful design of training recipes—including the Warmup-Stable-Decay schedule with increasing data curation in the decay phase, Norm-Head stabilization, and targeted alignment—can make a 1.9B-parameter model competitive with much larger open LMs. Their systematic ablations provide insight into how training dynamics and data composition shape performance, though some phenomena, such as a mid-training performance surge, remain unexplained. All models and evaluation code are released to support further research.

Abstract: We present Index-1.9B, a series of open small language models developed at Bilibili. The series comprises four models: Index-1.9B-Base, a foundation model with 1.9 billion non-embedding parameters pre-trained on 2.8 trillion predominantly Chinese and English tokens; Index-1.9B-Pure, a control variant trained with an identical recipe but with all instruction-like data strictly filtered from the corpus; Index-1.9B-Chat, aligned from the base model with supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization; and Index-1.9B-Character, which augments the chat model with retrieval-augmented generation for few-shot role-playing customization. Pre-training employs a Warmup-Stable-Decay learning-rate schedule in which the concentration of curated data is raised substantially during the decay phase, together with a Norm-Head output layer that stabilizes training under large learning rates. On a suite of standard benchmarks covering examination, reasoning, mathematics, and code, Index-1.9B-Base attains an average score of 64.92, competitive with or exceeding open models of several times its size. We further report controlled studies on model depth, learning-rate magnitude and scheduling, the interaction between learning-rate decay and data quality, and the effect of including instruction data during pre-training, and we document an unexplained surge in benchmark performance midway through the constant-learning-rate phase. All models, together with evaluation code, are released at https://github.com/bilibili/Index-1.9B.

</details>


### [3] [RouteRec: Strict Evaluation of Recommender-Agent Selection and Aggregation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09908)
*Kaiji Zhou,Vladimir Kalmykov,Yue Feng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces RouteRec, a framework for choosing among heterogeneous recommender agents (including an LLM reranker) under cost constraints, and shows that item-level aggregation of agents’ scores is more effective than request-level hard selection of a single agent.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern recommender systems can use multiple heterogeneous agents—such as collaborative filtering, sequential models, content-based retrieval, and LLM-based reranking—but no single agent is consistently best for all user–item requests. Practitioners must decide which agent(s) to use per request under computational and monetary cost constraints, especially when LLM rerankers are expensive. Existing “routing” approaches often select a single agent per request, and it is unclear how much benefit is available from smarter routing versus aggregating signals across agents. The paper aims to quantify the potential gains from cross-agent information and to understand which decision granularity (request-level vs item-level) is more effective under realistic, leakage-free evaluation protocols.

Method: The authors propose RouteRec, a framework that compares two main approaches for leveraging multiple recommender agents: (1) request-level hard selection (routing), where a meta-policy picks one agent’s full ranked list for each request; and (2) item-level learned aggregation, where a model combines signals (e.g., scores or ranks) from several agents per candidate item. They consider four traditional recommender agents and one LLM-based reranker, and evaluate on the MovieLens-1M dataset. A “full quality oracle” that always picks the best agent per request provides an upper bound to quantify cross-agent headroom. The evaluation uses a leakage-free 5-fold out-of-fold protocol to avoid information leakage from training to test. They study different configurations: purely cheap agents, selective LLM escalation, and gated all-agent aggregation, measuring metrics such as Hit Rate@10 (HR@10) and NDCG, and tracking the proportion of LLM calls to account for cost.

Result: The oracle that can pick the best agent per request achieves a high HR@10 of 0.584 on MovieLens-1M, indicating substantial potential gains from cross-agent differences. However, under the strict leakage-free out-of-fold protocol, request-level hard selection performs poorly: it underperforms a BM25 baseline in HR@10 (0.223 vs 0.254), and adding selective LLM escalation does not fix this gap. In contrast, item-level learned aggregation produces stronger results. A variant that aggregates only cheap agents matches BM25’s HR while slightly improving NDCG (0.123 vs 0.114). A gated variant that aggregates across all agents, including the LLM reranker with selective usage, achieves HR@10 = 0.295 while invoking the LLM on 70.2% of requests (or items), thus outperforming BM25 and hard selection while controlling LLM cost.

Conclusion: The study concludes that, in this sparse, fixed-candidate recommender setting, routing at the level of choosing a single agent’s entire list per request is too coarse-grained to effectively exploit heterogeneous agents, even though large cross-agent potential exists. Hard selection fails to surpass a strong BM25 baseline, and selectively escalating to an LLM reranker does not resolve the issue. Item-level learned aggregation, however, successfully leverages complementary signals from multiple agents and can outperform BM25 under reasonable LLM usage rates. The key takeaway is that future work on multi-agent recommendation under cost constraints should focus on finer-grained, item-level aggregation or scoring actions rather than request-level agent routing alone.

Abstract: Recommender systems increasingly face a choice among heterogeneous agents -- collaborative filters, sequential models, content-based retrievers, and LLM-based rerankers -- yet no single agent is uniformly best. We study this choice as task-aware agent ranking under cost constraints using RouteRec, a framework that compares request-level hard selection with item-level learned aggregation over four traditional recommender agents and one LLM reranker agent. On MovieLens-1M, the full quality oracle has substantial headroom (HR@10 = 0.584), confirming that useful cross-agent signal exists. Under a leakage-free 5-fold out-of-fold protocol, however, hard selection remains below BM25 (0.223 vs. 0.254), and selective LLM escalation does not improve it. The same protocol yields a different outcome for learned aggregation: its cheap-only variant matches BM25 in HR and has a higher NDCG point estimate (0.123 vs. 0.114), while gated all-agent aggregation reaches HR@10 = 0.295 with 70.2\% LLM calls. The resulting lesson is not that routing is solved, but that request-level selection of one complete agent list is too coarse for this sparse fixed-candidate setting; item-level aggregation is the more promising action space.

</details>


### [4] [Global Merger-Arbitrage Forecasting with Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09921)
*Hinal Jajal,Michal Mucha,Charles Sweat,Chris Pulman,Charlie Flanagan,Peter Anderson*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper builds an LLM-based forecasting system that predicts outcomes of announced M&A deals (close, higher bid, or termination) using long-context reasoning and hindsight-guided finetuning, outperforming markets, XGBoost, and baseline LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Merger arbitrage is a high-stakes domain where investors must forecast outcomes of announced M&A deals, but existing LLM forecasting research focuses on broad, mixed-topic benchmarks with short contexts like news snippets. These do not reflect realistic financial workflows that require reading and reasoning over hundreds of pages of technical documents. There is a need to understand whether and how LLMs can be effectively adapted to such specialized, long-context, high-stakes decision settings, and whether they can outperform traditional quantitative models and market-implied probabilities.

Method: The authors design a language-model-based forecasting system tailored to merger arbitrage. They use expert-guided context engineering to construct long-context inputs from extensive technical documents for each deal. They then finetune a language model using hindsight-guided reasoning traces derived from historical M&A deals, so the model learns structured reasoning patterns that lead to correct outcome predictions. For each announced deal, the system outputs a probability distribution over three mutually exclusive outcomes: (1) closing at the announced terms, (2) a higher bid emerging, or (3) deal termination. Performance is evaluated on an out-of-sample dataset of more than 400 large deals across 42 countries and compared against market-implied probabilities, XGBoost models, and frontier LLM baselines. Ablation studies isolate the contribution of finetuning, context engineering, and hindsight-based supervision.

Result: On the out-of-sample set of 400+ large deals in 42 countries, the finetuned LLM system achieves the lowest class-balanced Brier score among all evaluated methods, reaching 0.151. This represents a 24% improvement relative to calibrated market-implied probabilities, a 19% improvement relative to an XGBoost model, and a 25–42% improvement over frontier LLM baselines without the specialized training and context engineering. The ablations indicate that both expert-designed long-context construction and hindsight-guided finetuning on reasoning traces are critical to achieving these gains.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that LLM-based forecasting can be made effective in specialized, high-stakes financial tasks that require long-context analysis, such as predicting M&A outcomes for merger arbitrage. Carefully engineered context and hindsight-based supervision on historical reasoning traces substantially improve calibration and accuracy, enabling the system to outperform markets, standard machine-learning models, and general-purpose LLMs. This suggests a promising path for deploying tailored LLM forecasters in other complex, domain-specific decision workflows that hinge on detailed document analysis and structured probabilistic predictions.

Abstract: We present a language-model forecasting system for merger arbitrage, a specialized high-stakes financial setting in which the task is to predict the outcome of announced M\&A deals. Unlike prior work on judgmental forecasting with LLMs, which has focused on broad mixed-topic benchmarks and short context such as news snippets, we study a setting that requires long-context reasoning over hundreds of pages of technical documents. Our system combines expert-guided context engineering with finetuning on hindsight-guided reasoning traces derived from historical deals. Given an announced deal, it outputs a probability distribution over three mutually exclusive outcomes: closing at announced terms, a higher bid, or deal termination. On an out-of-sample set of more than 400 large deals spanning 42 countries, our finetuned system achieves the best performance of any method we evaluate, reducing class-balanced Brier score to 0.151. This is 24\% below calibrated market-implied probabilities, 19\% below XGBoost, and 25-42\% below frontier language models. These results, together with ablation studies, show that LLM-based forecasting can succeed in specialized, long-context financial workflows, with hindsight-based supervision and expert-designed context playing a critical role.

</details>


### [5] [Faithful by Design: Evaluating and Improving LLM-Generated Clinical Trial Summaries for Multi-Stakeholder Audiences](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09932)
*Robert Williams*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They build and apply a benchmark to test how factually faithful LLM-written clinical trial summaries are for different audiences, and show that adding knowledge-graph-based retrieval makes summaries slightly more faithful.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are being used to summarize clinical trial data for doctors, patients, and payers, but hallucinations and unfaithful statements can cause serious harm in medical decision-making. There is a need for a systematic, audience-aware way to measure and improve the factual faithfulness of these summaries.

Method: The authors construct a benchmark from 200 stratified clinical trials from the Aggregate Analysis of ClinicalTrials.gov database. They design audience-specific prompts for three stakeholder groups and define a six-dimension faithfulness annotation schema. They then generate 1,800 summaries using three LLMs (GPT-4o, Claude Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 2.5 Flash) and score them with a cross-encoder NLI model. Finally, they develop a knowledge-graph-augmented retrieval system and compare its faithfulness scores against the baseline models.

Result: Across models, the main faithfulness problem is Unsupported Claims, with a low mean score (1.55/3). Using the NLI-based scoring, the knowledge-graph-augmented retrieval system yields statistically significant but modest gains in faithfulness metrics: entailment increases by 0.0125 and overall faithfulness by 0.0130 (p < 0.0001). The type of improvement differs by model: GPT-4o mainly reduces contradictions, while Claude Sonnet 4.6 and Gemini 2.5 Flash mainly increase entailment.

Conclusion: The paper shows that existing LLMs often add unsupported information when summarizing clinical trials, underscoring safety risks in medical communication. Their benchmark and annotation schema provide a structured way to evaluate faithfulness for different audiences, and knowledge-graph-augmented retrieval can improve factual grounding, though gains are currently small and model-specific.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used to summarize clinical trial results for healthcare providers, patients, and payers, but their tendency to hallucinate poses significant risks in this high-stakes context. This study introduces a benchmark evaluation framework for measuring the faithfulness of LLM-generated clinical trial summaries across three stakeholder audiences. The framework consists of 200 stratified trials drawn from the Aggregate Analysis of ClinicalTrials.gov database, evaluated using audience-specific prompt templates and a six-dimension faithfulness annotation schema. Baseline measurements were established for GPT-4o, Claude Sonnet 4.6, and Gemini 2.5 Flash across 1,800 generated summaries scored using a cross-encoder natural language inference (NLI) model. Unsupported Claims was identified as the dominant failure mode across all three models, with a mean annotation score of 1.55 out of three. A knowledge-graph-augmented retrieval system was developed and evaluated against the baseline, producing statistically significant improvements in NLI-based faithfulness scores (entailment +0.0125, faithfulness +0.0130, p < 0.0001). Improvement pathways were model-dependent, with GPT-4o improving primarily through contradiction reduction while Claude Sonnet 4.6 and Gemini 2.5 Flash improved through increased entailment.

</details>


### [6] [Workload-Driven Optimization for On-Device Real-Time Subtitle Translation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09957)
*Tsz-To Wong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: On-device subtitle translation model for English-to-Traditional-Chinese in Taiwan, optimized by shrinking the vocabulary to speed up decoding under strict latency and privacy constraints, while maintaining or surpassing Google Translate quality on short subtitles.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing language-model serving optimizations often target long-context or high-throughput scenarios, which do not match on-device subtitle translation needs characterized by short, real-time inputs, single-sample inference, strict latency, and privacy constraints. Additionally, quantization shifts inference cost, making vocabulary projection relatively more expensive and motivating architectural changes that reduce this bottleneck while preserving translation quality for a Taiwan-specific subtitle domain.

Method: Start from the LMT-60-0.6B model and profile its decode-time computation after GGUF quantization, observing that the vocabulary projection layer becomes a dominant cost. To reduce this cost, replace the 151k-token vocabulary with a smaller, 64k-token tokenizer tailored to subtitle data. Migrate the model’s embedding space to this new tokenizer, then adapt the model using an embedding calibration phase followed by full supervised fine-tuning on subtitle translation data. Evaluate translation quality via pairwise GPT-4o judgments against Google Translate and measure latency on Apple M2 Metal for different vocabulary sizes.

Result: The adapted model, LocalSubs, reaches a 59.2% tie-excluded win rate over Google Translate on a fixed 500-example subset of the OpenSubtitles2024 test set, as judged by GPT-4o. Its relative advantage is greatest on short subtitle cues and diminishes as cue length grows. Preliminary performance profiling on Apple M2 Metal with a 64k-vocabulary model shows a 1.63× speedup compared with a 151k-vocabulary baseline, though the benchmark setup is not yet fully finalized.

Conclusion: Reducing vocabulary size and adapting embeddings for a subtitle-specific tokenizer can meaningfully accelerate on-device English-to-Traditional-Chinese subtitle translation under quantized inference, without sacrificing and even slightly surpassing mainstream cloud translation quality on short cues. Vocabulary projection is an important optimization target in this regime, and carefully designed tokenizer and embedding adaptation pipelines are a promising direction for low-latency, privacy-preserving, on-device translation systems tailored to local domains like Taiwanese subtitles.

Abstract: This report studies on-device English-to-Traditional-Chinese subtitle translation for Taiwan under short inputs, short outputs, batch-size-one inference, low latency, and privacy constraints. These conditions limit the value of optimizations designed for long-context or high-throughput language-model serving.
  Starting from LMT-60-0.6B, preliminary profiling suggests that vocabulary projection becomes a more important decode-time cost after GGUF quantization reduces the relative cost of Transformer blocks. We replace the original 151k-token vocabulary with a 64k-token subtitle-domain tokenizer, migrate the embedding space, and adapt the model through embedding calibration followed by full supervised fine-tuning.
  On a fixed 500-example subset of the OpenSubtitles2024 test set, the LocalSubs achieves a 59.2% tie-excluded win rate against Google Translate under GPT-4o pairwise judging. Performance is strongest on short cues and declines as cue length increases. Preliminary Apple M2 Metal measurements on a 64k-vocabulary model show a 1.63$\times$ speedup over a 151k-vocabulary profiling baseline. The raw benchmark configuration is incomplete, so the latency result is treated as preliminary.

</details>


### [7] [Silent Failures in Quantized LLM Reasoning: A Taxonomy-Based Analysis of Hollow Convergence and Failure Mode Shifts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09999)
*Renuka Oladri,Mohan Vamsi Varadaraju Priya,Jerry Wu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Paper studies how post-training quantization affects LLM reasoning patterns despite similar accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Quantized LLMs are widely deployed for efficiency, but evaluation focuses on accuracy, potentially missing qualitative changes in reasoning behavior caused by low-precision formats.

Method: Construct a six-category failure taxonomy for chain-of-thought reasoning, validate it with human annotators, and apply it to 30,000 CoT outputs from five instruction-tuned LLMs (3B–14B) evaluated at three numeric precisions (FP32, FP16, NF4) on four reasoning benchmarks, then analyze how failure types and Hollow Convergence frequency shift with model size, precision, and dataset; additionally test whether Hollow Convergence can be detected from surface text features via classifiers.

Result: Accuracy across FP32, FP16, and NF4 changes minimally (≤3.1 percentage points), but the distribution of reasoning failures shifts noticeably under NF4, especially for smaller models and for certain benchmarks (LogiQA, ARC-Challenge); Hollow Convergence decreases sharply for small models under NF4 but stays stable for ≥12B models, GSM8K shows no such effect, and for LLaMA 3.2-3B the dominant wrong-answer failure mode under NF4 becomes Shortcut Collapse (44%→78%) while Confidence Snowballing nearly disappears; automatic classifiers can only weakly detect Hollow Convergence from text (best F1=0.53).

Conclusion: Quantization, particularly NF4 on smaller models and challenging benchmarks, can qualitatively distort reasoning processes without significantly harming task accuracy, introducing subtle failure modes like Hollow Convergence and shifts toward shortcut-based errors that standard accuracy-centric evaluations and simple text-based detectors fail to capture, so deployment and evaluation of quantized LLMs should account for reasoning-pattern shifts, not just top-line performance.

Abstract: We show that post-training quantization can silently alter how large language models reason even when task accuracy is preserved. Using a six-category failure taxonomy validated by two independent human annotators (Cohen's $κ$ = 0.906), we classify 30,000 chain-of-thought outputs from five instruction-tuned LLMs (3B--14B parameters) across three quantization precisions (FP32, FP16, NF4) and four reasoning benchmarks. We find that while accuracy is robust across precisions (maximum 3.1 pp drop), Hollow Convergence (correct answers reached through incomplete or unverifiable reasoning) shows a significant size-dependent shift under NF4, dropping sharply for the two smallest models tested but remaining invariant for models at 12B parameters and above. This effect is also benchmark-specific: GSM8K is categorically immune while LogiQA and ARC-Challenge show the largest shifts. Furthermore, under NF4, Shortcut Collapse rises from 44% to 78% of wrong-answer failures in LLaMA 3.2-3B while Confidence Snowballing collapses from 15.8% to near zero, a qualitative shift invisible to accuracy metrics. Finally, we show Hollow Convergence cannot be reliably detected from surface-level text features (best F1 = 0.53), establishing it as a deployment-relevant failure mode that standard evaluation pipelines cannot catch.

</details>


### [8] [Robust, Scalable Detection of Text Containment in Large Web-Crawled Corpora](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10020)
*Lars Henry Berge Olsen,Pierre Lison,Martin Jullum,Mark Anderson*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: FindMyText is an open-source Python tool that efficiently checks whether a given text appears partially or fully in large text corpora, with improved detection of near-verbatim copying.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing text similarity and document fingerprinting methods struggle to robustly detect near-verbatim reuse, especially in large web-scale corpora. There is a practical need to verify whether specific (potentially copyrighted) texts are contained within massive datasets used for tasks like model training, auditing, or plagiarism detection.

Method: The authors build on document fingerprinting techniques and introduce a novel mechanism that explicitly identifies and links sequences (chains) of matching fingerprints between a query text and a corpus. This chain-based representation allows the system to distinguish true near-verbatim reuse from incidental or sparse similarities. The implementation uses a distributed, disk-based indexing framework to support scalable search over large, web-crawled datasets.

Result: On a newly proposed benchmark for text containment, FindMyText is empirically compared with alternative approaches across three datasets: ArXiv papers, Wikipedia, and generic web content. The tool achieves superior performance on these benchmarks, indicating more accurate detection of text containment and near-verbatim copies than competing methods.

Conclusion: FindMyText provides a scalable, open-source solution for text containment detection, with a fingerprint-chain mechanism that improves reliability over traditional similarity-based approaches. Its strong empirical performance and ability to handle large web-scale corpora make it particularly useful for applications like copyright verification and dataset auditing.

Abstract: We present FindMyText, an open-source Python package designed to efficiently assess whether a given text appears, in part or in full, within a text corpus. The tool builds on prior techniques for document fingerprinting, but extends them with a novel mechanism to explicitly capture sequences of matching fingerprints. By identifying such chains, the tool can more reliably detect near-verbatim copies of a given text rather than mere textual similarities. This makes FindMyText particularly suited for verifying the presence of copyrighted material in a corpus. Leveraging a distributed, disk-based indexing framework, the system scales to large web-crawled datasets. Using a new benchmark for evaluating text containment methods, we show that FindMyText outperforms alternative approaches across three datasets (ArXiv papers, Wikipedia, and generic web content).

</details>


### [9] [Efficiently Adapting Spoken Language Models for the Singaporean Context](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10092)
*Ng Jia Sheng Jason*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They adapt an open-source spoken language model to a sensitive, multilingual Singaporean security context, using LoRA, a surrogate text-QA dataset, and a multi-task reweighting scheme, plus a new large multilingual QA dataset; the resulting 5B model rivals or beats much larger SLMs while largely retaining original QA skills.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Spoken language models can jointly handle speech understanding and reasoning, but adapting them to domain-specific, sensitive settings is hard when the original pretraining data is unavailable and the deployment requires multilingual, spoken interactions. There is a need for a tailored SLM for Singapore’s Home Team (public safety and security agencies), handling several speech tasks and four official languages, without losing the model’s original capabilities.

Method: They take an open-source SLM and adapt it with: (1) LoRA parameter-efficient fine-tuning; (2) a surrogate text-based QA dataset designed to mitigate catastrophic forgetting of the model’s original speech QA skills; and (3) a multi-task training objective that extends the CoBa reweighting scheme to speech tasks. They also construct HTD-multilingual-QA, a large multilingual QA dataset (over 500k samples) with both text and spoken forms, and use it in training/evaluation across five speech-related tasks in Singapore’s four official languages.

Result: The adapted model, HT-Moonstone (5B parameters), matches or outperforms spoken language models up to seven times larger on most evaluated tasks. It achieves the best accent and gender recognition performance among all tested models and suffers less than a 2% decrease in its original speech question-answering ability, indicating limited catastrophic forgetting.

Conclusion: Carefully designed, parameter-efficient adaptation with multi-task reweighting and a targeted multilingual QA dataset can effectively specialize an open-source spoken language model for a sensitive, multilingual security context. This yields a relatively small SLM that competes with or surpasses much larger models across diverse speech tasks while mostly preserving its original QA capabilities.

Abstract: Spoken language models (SLMs) unify speech perception and reasoning, but adapting them to sensitive domains is underexplored, especially when the original training data is inaccessible and the use case demands multilingual, spoken-query interaction. We adapt an open-source SLM to the Singaporean Home Team context across five speech tasks in Singapore's four official languages, combining LoRA fine-tuning, a surrogate text-QA dataset that guards against catastrophic forgetting, and a multi-task objective that adapts the CoBa reweighting scheme to speech. We also build HTD-multilingual-QA, a 504,853 sample multilingual QA dataset in text and spoken form. The resulting HT-Moonstone (5B) matches or outperforms SLMs up to 7x its size on most tasks, attains the best accent and gender recognition among all models evaluated, and loses under 2\% of its original speech QA ability.

</details>


### [10] [Cost of Reasoning in non-English Languages: A Case Study on Japanese](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10114)
*Yuu Jinnai*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether a language model can be trained to perform explicit chain-of-thought style reasoning in Japanese, and finds that language-controlled reasoning is feasible but does not surpass strong English-reasoning models and does not automatically improve performance on Japanese cultural tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most strong reasoning language models perform best when reasoning in English, because most reasoning-focused data is in English. However, for interpretability, safety, and usability, it is desirable that models reason in the user’s preferred language, such as Japanese. The authors are motivated to understand whether we can systematically control the language of the model’s reasoning traces without sacrificing reasoning quality, and whether reasoning in a local language also boosts performance on culturally specific tasks.

Method: The authors start from Qwen-3-8B and use continual pretraining to obtain Qwen-3-Swallow-8B, a Japanese-focused LLM. They then use GRPO (a reinforcement-style reasoning optimization method) to further train a variant so that its chain-of-thought reasoning is in Japanese. They evaluate this Japanese-reasoning model on multiple benchmarks spanning coding, math, science, and separate Japanese cultural benchmarks, and compare performance against strong English-reasoning baseline models.

Result: The Japanese-reasoning version of Qwen-3-Swallow-8B successfully produces reasoning traces in Japanese, demonstrating that the language of reasoning can be controlled via training with GRPO. On coding, math, and science benchmarks, its performance generally reaches, but does not exceed, strong English-reasoning baselines. On Japanese cultural benchmarks, its performance is actually worse than baseline models, indicating that Japanese-language reasoning does not automatically translate to better performance on culturally specific tasks.

Conclusion: Reasoning-language control—training a model so that its chain-of-thought is in a specific target language like Japanese—is feasible using continual pretraining and GRPO. However, simply reasoning in Japanese does not yield clear performance gains over English-reasoning models on standard reasoning benchmarks, and can even underperform on Japanese cultural tasks. Additional targeted data or methods are likely needed if the goal is to improve culturally grounded performance, not just change the language of the reasoning trace.

Abstract: Reasoning Language Models (RLMs) achieve their strongest performance when they reason in English, the language for which reasoning-oriented training data is most abundant. However, reasoning trace is a clue for model interpretability and safety, and useful in practice for both the model users and for model developers. Thus, it is desirable to be able to develop a model that reasons in a language of the user's choice, while still maintaining strong reasoning performance. To this end, we study the feasibility of training a model that reasons in Japanese. We develop a Japanese-reasoning variant of Qwen-3-Swallow-8B, which is a Japanese LLM continually pretrained from Qwen-3-8B, with GRPO and evaluate it across coding, math, and science benchmarks. The study shows that reasoning-language control is feasible by training a Japanese continually pretrained model with GRPO. However, its performance is at best on par with strong English-reasoning baselines on several benchmarks. We also evaluate the trained model on Japanese cultural benchmarks and observe that the model's performance is worse than the baseline models, suggesting that the reasoning in Japanese does not immediately improve performance on culturally relevant tasks for free.

</details>


### [11] [Instruction Set and Language for Hypergraphs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10194)
*Mario Pascual-Gonzalez,Ezequiel Lopez-Rubio*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces IsalHG, a compact string-based representation for finite connected hypergraphs with bounded hyperedge arity, and uses it to define a canonical form aimed at deciding hypergraph isomorphism natively, then benchmarks this approach against existing Levi-graph-based tools.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hypergraph isomorphism is usually solved by reducing the hypergraph to a Levi incidence graph and then applying graph isomorphism algorithms. This indirect approach may be inefficient or conceptually unsatisfying, and there is no widely used native hypergraph representation that supports a canonical form and direct isomorphism testing. The authors want a compact, machine-friendly way to represent any bounded-arity hypergraph as a string so that canonical forms and isomorphism checks can be done natively at the hypergraph level, and they wish to compare such a native approach experimentally with the established Levi reduction methods.

Method: They define a small virtual machine model whose state consists of a sparse hypergraph, a circular doubly-linked list (CDLL) of node references, and k traversal pointers, where k is an upper bound on hyperedge arity. Over this machine they define a finite instruction alphabet Σ_HG. Each instruction moves a pointer along the CDLL or inserts a hyperedge, optionally adding new nodes. This yields a closed encoding scheme: every string over Σ_HG decodes to some valid hypergraph. They then design a greedy HypergraphToString (h2s) encoding algorithm that, given any connected hypergraph, emits one such instruction string. A refined backtracking variant, initialized at nodes with lexicographically maximal structural tuples, searches over encodings to produce a canonical string w*, which is conjectured to be a complete isomorphism invariant. For evaluation, they check the round-trip property s2h(h2s(H)) ≅ H on random uniform hypergraphs and known combinatorial designs, and they benchmark their canonical-string algorithm against nauty, Traces, and bliss run on the 2-coloured Levi graph across a grid of hypergraph size (n) and edge count (c), with multiple random seeds per grid cell.

Result: They successfully show that every string over the instruction alphabet decodes to a valid hypergraph, and empirically verify that s2h(h2s(H)) is isomorphic to the original H on 150 random connected uniform hypergraphs and several named combinatorial designs. In a benchmark of 600 hypergraph-isomorphism instances, their canonical-string procedure and the three Levi-graph-based baselines (nauty, Traces, bliss) agree on all isomorphism/non-isomorphism decisions, providing empirical support for the conjectured completeness of the canonical string invariant. However, their method is substantially slower in wall-clock time: across the tested (n, c) grid, the Levi-based algorithms are between 311× and 117,672× faster on geometric mean, dominating performance in every tested cell.

Conclusion: The paper contributes a new representation framework, IsalHG, that encodes bounded-arity connected hypergraphs as strings over a compact instruction alphabet using a simple virtual-machine model. Building on this encoding, they define a canonical string whose equality is conjectured to fully characterize hypergraph isomorphism, and they provide the first systematic benchmark that contrasts a native hypergraph isomorphism method with traditional Levi-graph reductions. While the proposed canonical algorithm is orders of magnitude slower than mature Levi-based tools in current form, it is conceptually clean, empirically consistent with existing methods on tested instances, and establishes a foundation for further work on native hypergraph isomorphism, optimization of the encoding, and potential proof or refinement of the completeness conjecture.

Abstract: We present IsalHG, a method for representing the structure of any finite, connected hypergraph of bounded hyperedge arity as a string over a compact instruction alphabet $Σ_{\mathrm{HG}}$. The encoding is executed by a small virtual machine comprising a sparse hypergraph, a circular doubly-linked list (CDLL) of node references, and $k$ traversal pointers, where $k$ bounds the hyperedge arity. Instructions either move a pointer through the CDLL or insert a hyperedge, optionally together with new nodes, into the hypergraph. Every string over $Σ_{\mathrm{HG}}$ decodes to a valid hypergraph; the alphabet is closed. A greedy \emph{HypergraphToString} (h2s) algorithm encodes any connected hypergraph into a string; a backtracking variant seeded at nodes of lexicographically maximal structural tuple produces a \emph{canonical string} $w^{*}$, which we conjecture to be a complete isomorphism invariant. Canonical-string equality then decides hypergraph isomorphism natively, without the standard reduction to the Levi incidence graph followed by a graph-isomorphism engine. We verify the round-trip property $s2h(h2s(H)) \cong H$ on 150 connected random uniform hypergraphs and on named combinatorial designs, and we benchmark the canonical algorithm against the three practically available exact baselines -- nauty, Traces, and bliss operating on the 2-coloured Levi graph -- across a $(n, c)$ grid with ten seeds per cell. All four methods agree on every one of 600 isomorphism verdicts, consistent with the completeness conjecture. On wall-clock time the Levi baselines dominate every tested cell by three to five orders of magnitude (geometric-mean ratio $311\times$ to $117{,}672\times$), which we report as measured. We contribute the representation framework, a conjecture of canonical completeness, and the first native-versus-Levi benchmark for hypergraph isomorphism.

### [12] [Equal Accuracy, Unequal Evidence: Search APIs as Decision Surfaces for Tool-Using Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10198)
*Sriram Selvam,Anneswa Ghosh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper reconceptualizes commercial search APIs as decision surfaces for agents using progressive disclosure, evaluating how different providers (Brave, Tavily, Firecrawl) affect an LLM agent’s retrieval behavior and evidence use rather than just answer accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most evaluations of search APIs for LLM agents focus on answer accuracy and recall, ignoring how ranked snippets and URLs shape an agent’s decisions about when to answer, reformulate, or retrieve full pages. With progressive disclosure architectures, token costs make this decision process critical, so there is a need to study search providers as sources of decision surfaces and evidence economies, not just correctness.

Method: The authors fix a single GPT-5.4 agent with two tools (search_web and fetch_page) and a fixed interaction policy, then vary only the search provider: Brave, Tavily, and Firecrawl. Using 100 hard questions from SEALQA-HARD, they log every content element visible to the agent (URLs, titles, snippets, and fetched page content). A separate Kimi-K2.6 oracle labels 6,869 per-URL instances for whether they semantically match the audited correct answer. They then analyze metrics including answer accuracy, distribution of gold answers across snippets and ranks, exploration behavior, and a new surface contradiction-to-gold URL ratio.

Result: All three providers achieve similar overall correctness (25, 25, and 26 correct answers out of 100), but they differ in how and where supporting evidence appears and how they steer the agent’s behavior. Brave tends to surface snippets that already contain gold answers, Tavily concentrates gold-supporting URLs in the top rank, and Firecrawl correlates with broader exploration by the agent under the same policy. The newly proposed contradiction-to-gold URL ratio differs substantially across providers, from 0.92 up to 2.59, indicating variation in how much contradictory versus supportive evidence an agent encounters on the surface.

Conclusion: Search providers should be evaluated as decision surfaces that shape agent retrieval strategies, token spending, and susceptibility to contradictory evidence, not solely based on answer recall. Provider choice is therefore a joint retrieval-budget and policy design decision: different APIs induce different evidence economies and exploration patterns even when headline accuracy is similar. Metrics like semantic-match evidence distribution and contradiction-to-gold ratios offer more nuanced ways to compare and select search APIs for agent systems.

Abstract: Search APIs are the fundamental retrieval layer for many agents and are often their most frequently used tool. Traditional search APIs provide URLs, titles, and snippets that preview website contents. Because full-page retrieval is token-intensive, agent retrieval architectures increasingly use progressive disclosure: the agent first sees snippets and then chooses whether to fetch full pages. In such systems, search API performance is often evaluated primarily by answer accuracy. We argue that a commercial search API is better understood as a decision surface: the ranked snippets, URLs, and metadata that determine whether an agent answers immediately, searches again, or spends tokens opening pages. We test this claim with one frozen GPT-5.4 agent, two tools (search_web and fetch_page), and 100 questions from SEALQA-HARD, varying only the search provider (Brave, Tavily, Firecrawl). A Kimi-K2.6 oracle labels every content element visible to the agent (URL, title, snippet, and fetched page, when fetched), producing 6,869 valid per-URL judgments. We use an audited correct-answer label, semantic match, which preserves exact matches while accepting harmless formatting and naming variants. Under this measure, the providers remain close (25, 25, 26 / 100), but their evidence economies differ sharply: Brave offers gold-answer-rich snippets, Tavily concentrates gold-supporting URLs at rank 1, and Firecrawl is associated with broader exploration under this fixed agent policy. We also introduce a surface contradiction-to-gold URL ratio, which varies from 0.92 to 2.59. Provider choice is therefore a retrieval-budget and policy decision, not merely a recall decision.

### [13] [Consensus vs. Dissent: Dynamic LLM Modeling of Subjective Preferences in Group Recommenders](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10235)
*Cedric Waterschoot,Nava Tintarev,Francesco Barile*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Group recommender systems must aggregate diverse user preferences, and prior work shows that different preference distributions within a group (e.g., majority/minority splits, coalitions) call for different aggregation strategies to be perceived as fair and satisfactory. Existing systems struggle to capture nuanced human judgments of fairness, satisfaction, and consensus in real time. The authors are motivated to see whether LLMs can internalize these human value trade-offs and act as judgment engines that help pick the most appropriate aggregation strategy for a given group configuration.

Method: The authors fine-tune LLMs (e.g., Llama and OLMo) on human survey data that capture judgments of fairness, satisfaction, and consensus for different group recommendation outcomes and aggregation strategies. They construct a reasoning dataset distilled from DeepSeek-V3.1 plus human ground-truth assessments to train "Judgmental Llama" and "Judgmental OLMo" to simulate group assessments. In a recommendation pipeline, they first generate multiple candidate recommendations using various social-choice-based aggregation strategies, then query the fine-tuned LLMs to predict human-like evaluations of each candidate and select the one with the highest predicted fairness/satisfaction/consensus. They then run a user study (n=284) to compare this pipeline to alternatives and analyze the interaction between group configuration and the LLM-based method.

Result: The LLM-based judgment models successfully differentiate between recommendation candidates produced by different aggregation strategies and can choose those that align better with human evaluations. In the user study with 284 participants, recommendations chosen by the LLM-augmented pipeline receive the highest ratings for satisfaction and group consensus compared to baselines. Moreover, alignment between LLM judgments and human perceptions of fairness, satisfaction, and consensus is strongest when the system explicitly accounts for interaction effects between the LLM-based method and the group configuration (such as presence of minorities or coalitions).

Conclusion: LLMs can be fine-tuned to act as real-time judgmental models within group recommender systems, effectively capturing nuanced human perceptions of fairness, satisfaction, and consensus. Dynamically adapting preference aggregation strategies based on group configuration and LLM-predicted human judgments leads to higher satisfaction and consensus than static strategies. The study reinforces the importance of sensitivity to within-group preference distributions and highlights LLMs as a powerful tool for implementing adaptive, human-aligned aggregation in group recommendation settings.

Abstract: Previous work in group recommender systems has demonstrated a sensitivity to the distribution of preferences within a group. Specifically, the selection of the preference aggregation strategy benefits from considering such group configurations. In this paper, we study whether LLMs are able to mimic this sensitivity and to select the ideal aggregation strategy (and corresponding recommendation) according to nuanced human perceptions of fairness, satisfaction, and consensus.
  We do this by fine-tuning Large Language Models (LLMs) on human survey data to serve as real-time judgmental models within the recommendation pipeline. Using a reasoning dataset distilled from DeepSeek-V3.1 and human ground truth assessments, we develop Judgmental Llama and Judgmental OLMo to simulate group assessments. Our pipeline successfully generates multiple recommendation candidates based on social choice-based aggregation strategies and dynamically selects the one that maximizes these predicted human-like evaluations. We further validate these suggestions in a user study (n=284) and find that our methodology achieved the highest scores for satisfaction and group consensus. Furthermore, we find that LLM judgments are most aligned with human perceptions of fairness, satisfaction and consensus when we also consider interaction effects between our LLM-based method and group configuration (e.g., minority or coalition). These findings give further support for dynamically adapting aggregation strategies to specific within-group preference distributions, and highlight the advantage of using LLMs for an adaptation that is aligned with subjective human judgments.

### [14] [PTEI: Integrating Personality Traits to Enhance Emotional Intelligence in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10245)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors introduce PTEI, a personality-aware EI framework. From each emotional scenario, they automatically infer personality profiles using MBTI and OCEAN trait representations. These predicted traits are then injected as contextual knowledge into specially designed prompts that guide LLMs’ emotional inference. Additionally, they build a contrastive-learning-based retrieval module that indexes and retrieves scenarios that are both emotionally and personally similar to the target case, further enriching the context. The framework is evaluated across multiple LLMs and combined with Chain-of-Thought prompting to test synergistic effects.

Result: Across standard Emotional Intelligence benchmarks, PTEI improves Emotional Understanding performance for several LLMs, with GPT-family models showing the largest gains. When PTEI is combined with Chain-of-Thought reasoning, accuracy increases by an additional 4 percentage points beyond PTEI alone, indicating complementary benefits of personality grounding and step-by-step reasoning.

Conclusion: Integrating explicit personality trait modeling (MBTI and OCEAN) and personality-aware retrieval into LLM prompts leads to more accurate emotional inference and better Emotional Understanding scores. The results suggest that enriching LLMs with structured social-psychological knowledge, particularly about stable individual differences, is a promising direction for building AI systems with more advanced, human-like emotional intelligence.

Abstract: Despite advances in Emotional Intelligence (EI), Large Language Models (LLMs) still significantly underperform humans in complex emotional reasoning. This gap originates partly from the limited incorporation of individual differences, particularly personality traits, which are fundamental to human emotional inference. To address this, we propose PTEI, a novel framework for integrating Personality Traits into Emotional Intelligence tasks using LLMs. In PTEI, MBTI and OCEAN personality traits are first extracted directly from the given emotional scenarios and then utilized as contextual knowledge within personality-aware prompts, guiding LLMs to accurately infer emotions and their underlying causes. To ensure optimal contextual grounding, we employ Contrastive Learning to construct an optimized retrieval system that surfaces emotionally and personally aligned scenarios, enhancing reasoning quality. Extensive experiments on established EI benchmarks show that PTEI enhances the Emotional Understanding (EU) capabilities of various LLMs, with the strongest improvement observed in GPT models. Combining PTEI with Chain-of-Thought (CoT) reasoning yields an additional 4 percent increase in accuracy. These findings underscore PTEI's contribution toward advancing AI systems with more sophisticated social and psychological grounding.

### [15] [One mechanism for many mental spaces: a shared router over a value slot in language models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10248)
*Oliver Steele,Jiangtao Wen,Yuxing Han*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates how transformer language models represent different discourse contexts (like belief, fiction, counterfactual, time) and finds a unified, mechanistic “router + slot” structure that generalizes across these context types.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In natural language, we effortlessly talk about multiple distinct mental or discourse spaces—what is real, believed, remembered, depicted, or hypothetical—where the same entity can have different properties (a flower is actually red but painted purple). Formal semantics typically treats these with different logics (modal, temporal, doxastic, depictive) and separate mechanisms, while Fauconnier’s mental-space theory proposes a single, unified space-building operation. With large transformer language models now performing sophisticated reasoning over such contexts, the authors want to know which of these theoretical perspectives best matches the internal mechanisms of real models: do they separate context types, or do they implement a shared “mental space” mechanism, and how is it realized mechanistically?

Method: The authors carry out mechanistic interpretability analyses on multiple transformer language model families. They search for and characterize a shared representational pattern they call a “router/slot” format, where a value slot stores attributed content and a router (space index) selects which mental space is accessed. They use Distributed Alignment Search (DAS) to train low-dimensional subspaces that can control a particular space type (counterfactual, belief, fictional, temporal) by intervening on activations. They then test cross-generalization: a subspace trained to control one space type is evaluated on its ability to control others. They further analyze the learned router’s structure (low rank, additive composition with entity identity, localization to specific late-layer attention heads). Finally, they run causal interventions: (1) training a subspace on rule-derived conclusions to flip what the model infers while holding surface reports fixed, and (2) composing multiple space-builders in the model to see if this yields a new router over a shared content slot.

Result: Across three transformer model families, the authors find that a single router/slot mechanism underlies multiple discourse context types. A reusable value slot represents the attributed content about entities, and a causally manipulable router vector—or low-rank subspace—functions as a space index that determines which mental space is read from or written to. Subspaces trained with DAS to control one space type (e.g., counterfactual) also substantially control others (belief, fictional, temporal) well above chance, indicating cross-type generality. Belief, which formal semantics often treats as a special, distinct case, is not cleanly separated mechanistically from the other space types. The router has low intrinsic rank, composes additively with entity identity representations, and exerts its effect primarily through a few late-layer attention heads. Causal tests show that intervening in this subspace can flip the model’s inferences without changing what it explicitly reports in text, and that composing multiple space-builders leads to the emergence of a new, distinct router operating over the same content slot.

Conclusion: Transformer language models implement something close to Fauconnier’s unified mental-space operation rather than sharply distinct mechanisms for each formal semantic context type. The internal structure is well-characterized by a router/slot format: a shared content slot for attributed properties, and a low-rank, additively compositional router that indexes different discourse spaces and is realized in a small set of late-layer heads. This single mechanism generalizes across counterfactual, belief, fictional, and temporal contexts, with belief not being a mechanistic outlier despite its theoretical prominence in philosophy, psychology, and linguistics. Moreover, this router mechanism is causally responsible for certain forms of inference and can be composed to generate new space indices. The paper thus provides mechanistic evidence for a cross-type, unified implementation of mental spaces in language models, and sets the stage for a companion paper that explores belief representations in more depth given their special theoretical status.

Abstract: Language builds discourse contexts other than the actual: a painting, a belief, a memory, a hypothetical. Each is a mental space in which the same entity can take a different value, as when a flower is red in reality but purple in a portrait. Formal semantics keeps these contexts apart because their logics differ (modal, temporal, doxastic, depictive); Fauconnier's mental-space theory treats them as one space-building operation. We ask which of these a transformer language model implements, and find a mechanistic version of Fauconnier's unification. The model uses one router/slot format across the inventory: a reusable value slot stores attributed content, and a causally manipulable router (the space index) selects which space is read. A subspace trained with Distributed Alignment Search to control one space type, counterfactual, belief, fictional, or temporal, also controls the others, well above a random floor, on three model families; belief, which formal semantics marks as a distinct case, is not specially separated. The router is low-rank, composes additively with entity identity, and acts through a few late-layer heads. Two further results show the mechanism drives inference and composes: a subspace trained on a rule-derived conclusion flips what the model infers while dissociating from what it reports, and composing space-builders mints a fresh router over the shared slot. This paper establishes the cross-type generality. A companion paper develops belief in depth, because of its special status in philosophy, psychology, and linguistics (epistemology, theory of mind, and propositional attitude reports).

</details>

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Low-resource languages like Warlpiri lack sufficient transcribed speech to train robust ASR systems, so models must rely on transfer from high-resource languages. However, it is unclear which source languages are most beneficial and how to systematically select them. The paper is motivated by the need for a principled way to leverage language similarity—both acoustic and linguistic—to maximize transfer performance for extremely low-resource ASR.

Method: The authors propose a ranking framework that scores potential high-resource source languages based on (1) acoustic similarity derived from pre-trained speech models, and (2) linguistic similarity derived from typological information, phoneme inventories, and grammatical/syntactic features. They apply this framework to select candidate source languages for transfer to Warlpiri and then conduct ASR experiments using Whisper. They compare monolingual and multilingual fine-tuning baselines with setups where Whisper is fine-tuned using data from top-ranked similar languages. They also perform correlation analysis to relate different similarity metrics to downstream ASR performance in zero-shot and fine-tuning scenarios.

Specifically, using Assamese and Hindi as source languages leads to large reductions in both word error rate (WER) and character error rate (CER) compared to the baselines. Correlation analysis reveals that acoustic similarity is most strongly associated with improvements when fine-tuning is performed, while phoneme-inventory and broader typological similarity are more predictive of zero-shot transfer effectiveness.

Conclusion: Language similarity can be exploited systematically to improve cross-lingual ASR transfer in extremely low-resource settings. Acoustically and typologically similar high-resource languages are particularly effective sources for transfer to Warlpiri, outperforming standard monolingual and multilingual approaches. Acoustic similarity is the best predictor for fine-tuning gains, whereas phoneme inventory and typological similarity better explain zero-shot performance. The framework provides a principled way to select source languages for low-resource ASR, and the findings highlight the distinct roles of different similarity types in transfer learning.

Abstract: This paper investigates how language similarity can improve cross-lingual transfer for automatic speech recognition (ASR) in extremely low-resource settings. Warlpiri, an Australian Aboriginal language, has very limited transcribed speech data, making transfer learning essential. We propose a framework combining acoustic similarity from pre-trained speech models with linguistic similarity based on typology, phoneme inventories, grammatical, and syntactic features to rank high-resource source languages and evaluate their effectiveness for ASR transfer to Warlpiri. Experiments with Whisper show that acoustically and typologically similar languages outperform monolingual and multilingual baselines. Assamese and Hindi achieve substantial reductions in word and character error rates. Correlation analysis further indicates that acoustic similarity is the strongest predictor of fine-tuning performance, while phoneme inventory and typological similarity better explain zero-shot transfer.

### [17] [Language Re-generation: An investigation into information locality effects on reconstruction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10268)
*Amirhossein Mohammadi,Laurence E. Frank,Albert Gatt,Robert A. Bagheri*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how information locality affects both human language processing and language model learning by testing if GPT-2 can reconstruct natural English from "impossible" languages with disrupted locality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the inductive biases of language models—specifically their bias toward information locality—by going beyond traditional learnability tests of impossible languages and examining whether and how models can recover natural language structure from perturbed input.

Method: The authors fine-tune pre-trained GPT-2 models on versions of English that have been transformed into "impossible" languages via three types of locality-disrupting perturbations. They then ask the models to reconstruct the original natural English and evaluate recovery using metrics for structural accuracy (dependency Triple F1), surface accuracy (Exact Match), fluency, and dependency length, while also analyzing how sentence length and perturbation severity affect performance.

Result: Recovered sentences show shorter dependency lengths than the original text, indicating a strong locality preference in the model’s internal representations. Recovery becomes more difficult as perturbations disrupt locality more strongly. Structural recovery can diverge from surface-form recovery, and fluent outputs can still be structurally unfaithful, especially under global word shuffling. Longer sentences help recovery when local structure remains but cause model performance to collapse under global shuffling. Recovery difficulty patterns align with prior learnability difficulty results across perturbation types.

Conclusion: Information locality acts as a key shared constraint for both the learnability of artificial "impossible" languages and the ability of GPT-2 to reconstruct natural language from them. GPT-2 exhibits an architectural bias toward shorter dependency lengths and locality that goes beyond what can be inferred from learnability experiments alone, as evidenced by systematic patterns in reconstruction behavior under different perturbations and sentence lengths.

Abstract: Information locality, the tendency for syntactically related words to appear close together, shapes both human language processing and language model learning. While prior work has examined whether language models can acquire impossible languages, it remains unclear whether they can recover natural language from such input and what this reveals about their inductive biases. We address this by complementing learnability-based approaches with a reconstruction framework: fine-tuning GPT-2 models pre-trained on impossible languages to reconstruct natural English from three perturbation types. Our findings show that the recovered structures exhibit shorter dependency lengths than the original text, mirroring the locality preference observed in unconstrained language model generation and providing a quantitative signature of an architectural bias that learnability experiments alone do not reveal. Recovery difficulty increases with the degree of locality disruption. Structural recovery (dependency Triple F1) dissociates from surface recovery (Exact Match), while fluency dissociates from faithful reconstruction under global shuffling. Sentence length further modulates performance: longer sentences facilitate recovery when local structure is preserved but lead to complete collapse under global shuffling. Finally, recovery difficulty tracks learnability difficulty across perturbation types, suggesting that information locality is the shared constraint governing both.

### [18] [PolyInterview: An LLM-based Platform for Immersive Mock Interview Practice with Comprehensive Multimodal Assessment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10310)
*Zhiyuan Wen,Jiannong Cao,Zijian Wang,Chen Chen,Xiaoyun Liu,Jianing Yin,Zhuo Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: PolyInterview is an LLM-based, multimodal mock interview platform that creates tailored, spoken interview simulations with a digital human and provides structured, evidence-backed feedback on content and behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Job seekers need realistic, adaptive interview practice, but real interviews are rare, expert coaching is expensive, and self-practice lacks interactive dialogue and rigorous assessment. Existing tools are fragmented, often offering only static questions, limited modalities, or generic feedback without clear behavioral evidence. The paper aims to fill this gap with an integrated, scalable system that supports personalized, realistic practice and detailed performance evaluation.

Method: The authors build PolyInterview, an LLM-powered system that: (1) ingests job descriptions and CVs to generate role- and candidate-specific interview question plans; (2) conducts multi-turn spoken interviews using a lip-synced digital human interviewer capable of answer-aware follow-up questions; (3) captures multimodal signals (verbal content, voice, and non-verbal behavior) and passes them to four parallel evaluators; (4) extracts 13 behavior-level features, aggregates them into 10 assessment aspects and two competency tracks, and structures feedback using KSA (Knowledge, Skills, Abilities) and STAR (Situation, Task, Action, Result) frameworks; and (5) generates reports linking scores to concrete behavioral evidence and improvement suggestions. They analyze platform usage logs and run an expert evaluation of question quality and feedback usefulness.

Result: PolyInterview accumulated 101 accounts, 1,564 interview sessions, 7,665 generated questions, and 1,422 five-stage question sets. In 93.7% of sessions, generated questions aligned more closely with the target job description than with cross-role descriptions, indicating effective job-specific tailoring. Ten expert evaluators judged the system’s question plans to be strong and its feedback actionable, suggesting that PolyInterview can produce high-quality practice scenarios and useful guidance.

Conclusion: An integrated LLM-based, multimodal mock interview system can deliver realistic practice and structured, evidence-backed feedback at scale. By tailoring questions to job descriptions and CVs and assessing content, vocal, and non-verbal behaviors within established frameworks (KSA, STAR), PolyInterview addresses key limitations of existing tools. Early usage statistics and expert evaluations indicate that the approach is feasible, produces well-aligned questions, and offers practically valuable feedback, warranting further deployment and study.

Abstract: Preparing for job interviews is important for securing desired positions, yet realistic practice remains difficult to access: real interviews are infrequent, expert mock coaching is costly, and self-practice offers neither adaptive dialogue nor structured assessment. Existing systems typically address only parts of this need through fixed question sequences, limited communication channels, or feedback with little supporting evidence. We present PolyInterview, an LLM-based platform for immersive mock interview practice with comprehensive multimodal assessment. PolyInterview uses the target job description and CV to generate questions tailored to the role and candidate, conducts multi-turn spoken interviews with a lip-synced digital human interviewer that asks answer-aware follow-up questions, and evaluates response content, vocal delivery, and non-verbal behavior. Four parallel evaluators produce 13 behavior-level features that are aggregated into 10 assessment aspects and two competency tracks. Guided by the KSA and STAR frameworks, the report links each score to behavioral evidence and actionable recommendations. PolyInterview is publicly accessible. Its current all-account snapshot contains 101 accounts, 1,564 interview sessions, 7,665 generated questions, and 1,422 five-stage question sets. Generated questions are more closely aligned with their matched job description than with cross-role job descriptions in 93.7% of sessions. An evaluation by ten experts found strong question plans and actionable feedback.

### [20] [Neutralizing Structural Inequality in the Nigerian FinTech Sector](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10317)
*Muhammad Abdullahi Said*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a hierarchical human-AI triage framework for POS fraud detection that substantially improves fraud recall and reduces regional bias compared to a fully automated system.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Algorithmic fraud detection in financial services often uses proxy variables that unintentionally encode structural inequalities, particularly harming users in infrastructure-poor or rural regions. In the Nigerian FinTech POS context, poor network quality and other infrastructure issues generate aleatoric noise (e.g., timeouts) that algorithms can misinterpret as fraud. This leads to discrimination laundering, where systemic disadvantages are treated as individual risk signals, excluding already-marginalized users from financial services. The paper is motivated by the need to both enhance fraud detection performance and provide substantive equality of opportunity across regions.

Method: The authors design a three-tier hierarchical human-AI triage system for POS fraud detection. First, they train and calibrate an ensemble model that serves as the primary automated filter. This model outputs not just scores but calibrated uncertainty estimates. Based on these outputs, a routing policy determines which transactions are handled fully automatically and which are escalated. Transactions with high epistemic uncertainty, such as those from new (cold start) accounts, are routed to specialist human analysts, while the highest-stakes or most critical cases are escalated further to a senior supervisor. To manage limited human capacity, the framework introduces a dynamic shadow price that adaptively rations human attention, deciding how many and which cases to escalate given resource constraints. Additionally, the system incorporates a random audit mechanism: a subset of automatically processed cases is periodically reviewed by humans to maintain and monitor human expertise and prevent skill atrophy.

Result: Empirical evaluation on Nigerian FinTech POS data shows that the hierarchical human-AI system significantly outperforms a fully autonomous baseline. It yields a 1.88% complementarity gap, indicating measurable performance gains from human-AI collaboration beyond either component alone. The system achieves a 24.79 percentage point increase in fraud recall compared to the autonomous baseline, meaning it captures substantially more fraudulent transactions. Importantly, it reduces the regional performance gap—from 19.43 to 2.88 percentage points—demonstrating that the method mitigates structural bias against users in less-developed or infrastructure-poor regions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that hierarchical human-AI collaboration is an effective mechanism to reconcile accuracy, fairness, and resource constraints in financial fraud detection. By explicitly modeling uncertainty, triaging cases to different human expertise levels, and managing human effort via dynamic pricing and random audits, the system both improves fraud detection and substantially reduces regional disparities. This framework operationalizes a "We Are All Equal" worldview by offering substantive equality of opportunity: rural and infrastructure-disadvantaged users are less likely to be unfairly flagged or excluded due to environmental brute luck, while the overall system becomes more robust, fair, and performant.

Abstract: Algorithmic decision systems in financial services often rely on data proxies that inadvertently encode structural inequalities. This paper introduces a hierarchical human-AI triage model for Point of Sale fraud detection in the Nigerian FinTech sector. Adopting a We Are All Equal worldview, we address the challenge of discrimination laundering, wherein the system misinterprets infrastructure related aleatoric noise such as rural network timeouts as fraudulent intent. We implement a three-tier routing policy utilizing a calibrated ensemble model as a primary filter. The policy routes transactions characterized by epistemic uncertainty such as cold start new accounts to specialist analysts while reserving high stakes cases for a senior supervisor. To manage finite human capacity, we utilize a dynamic shadow price to ration human attention and implement a random audit mechanism to prevent human skill atrophy. Our experimental results demonstrate a statistically significant 1.88\% complementarity gap and a 24.79\% percentage point gain in fraud recall over an autonomous baseline. Crucially, the model reduces the regional performance gap from 19.43 to 2.88 percentage points, neutralizing structural bias. Hierarchical collaboration provides a robust mechanism for substantive equality of opportunity, ensuring that rural accounts are not excluded from the digital economy due to environmental brute luck.
