### [21] [CAFE: A Compound-AI Factorial Evaluation Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10380)
*Fabian Lukassen,Christoph Weisser,Thomas Kneib,Alexander Silbersdorff*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CAFE is an open-source platform that uses factorial experimental design and mixed-effects models to systematically evaluate which components of a compound AI system (like retrievers, models, and prompts) most affect answer quality, along with cost/latency trade-offs and judge reliability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Compound AI systems (e.g., retrieval-augmented QA pipelines) contain many interchangeable components, but practitioners usually do not know which elements (retriever, LLM, prompt, etc.) actually drive answer quality, or whether observed differences between configurations are statistically significant. Existing tools largely focus either on searching for a strong configuration or scoring outputs one-by-one, without explaining the contribution and interactions of individual components. There is a need for a principled, experiment-design-based framework that can attribute performance variance to specific factors, quantify interactions, and relate quality to cost and latency.

Method: The authors propose CAFE, which treats each swappable pipeline component as a factor in a factorial experimental design. Users define components (e.g., retrievers, models, prompts) as factors and CAFE automatically constructs and runs all corresponding configurations. Output answers are evaluated on a shared rubric using a configurable LLM judge, optionally combined with human raters. CAFE then applies mixed-effects statistical models to the collected ratings to decompose answer-quality variance into contributions from components and their interactions. It computes effect sizes, significance levels, identifies the best configuration, analyzes cost and latency trade-offs, and measures agreement between LLM judges and human raters.

Result: On a retrieval-augmented QA pipeline evaluated over the HotpotQA dataset, CAFE successfully recovers known (“planted”) factor effects, demonstrating its ability to correctly identify the influence of specific components. It also remains calibrated under a permutation-based null hypothesis test, suggesting its statistical conclusions are well-founded. The system is implemented and released as both a Python package and a web application, making it accessible for practitioners.

Conclusion: CAFE provides a principled, design-of-experiments-based framework for evaluating compound AI systems. Beyond just finding good configurations, it explains which components and interactions drive answer quality and whether observed differences are statistically significant, while also exposing cost, latency, and judge-human reliability metrics. This makes it a practical tool for systematically optimizing and understanding complex AI pipelines in settings such as retrieval-augmented QA.

Abstract: We introduce CAFE (Compound-AI Factorial Evaluation), an open-source platform that brings design of experiments to the evaluation of compound AI systems (CAIS). Such systems expose many interchangeable choices - e.g. which retriever, model, or prompt - and practitioners rarely know which of them most affects answer quality. With CAFE, a practitioner registers each swappable component of a pipeline as a factor to build a factorial design over the chosen factors, run the resulting configurations, and score the answers on a shared rubric using a configurable LLM judge together with human raters. From these ratings it attributes answer-quality variance to the components and their interactions with mixed-effects models and reports effect sizes, significance, the best configuration, cost and latency trade-offs, and judge-human reliability. Whereas existing tools mostly either search for a good configuration or score outputs in isolation, CAFE also explains which component drives quality and whether an observed difference is significant. We validate CAFE on a retrieval-augmented question-answering (QA) pipeline over the HotpotQA benchmark dataset, where it recovers planted factor effects and stays calibrated under a permutation null. CAFE is released as a Python package and as a Web application.

</details>


### [22] [Structured Thoughts For Improved Reasoning And Context Pruning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10386)
*Zain Sarwar,Supriyo Chakraborty,Berkcan Kapusuzoglu,Chia-Hsuan Lee,Anirban Das,Stephen Rawls,Kartik Balasubramaniam,Sambit Sahu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Introduces a structured reasoning format (<try>/<outcome>) for LLMs that improves reasoning performance and enables aggressive context pruning with limited accuracy loss.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long chains-of-thought from LLMs are verbose and memory-inefficient, making it hard to keep full reasoning traces in context while scaling to complex tasks. The authors seek a way to retain the benefits of explicit reasoning while compressing and structuring it for better efficiency and performance.

Method: Define a structured reasoning format with alternating <try> (scratch/exploration) and <outcome> (concise conclusion) blocks. Build a dataset by segmenting existing CoT traces into <try> steps and using an LLM to summarize each into an <outcome>. Fine-tune foundation models on this structured data so they learn to reason in this format. Additionally, implement a context-pruning scheme that discards <try> blocks after their corresponding <outcome> has been produced, keeping only distilled conclusions in context.

Result: Fine-tuned models trained with structured thoughts outperform standard supervised fine-tuning by up to 8.08% on reasoning benchmarks. The proof-of-concept pruning strategy achieves around 85% reduction in memory/context usage at the cost of an 8.67% performance drop on mathematical reasoning tasks.

Conclusion: Structuring LLM reasoning into explicit <try>/<outcome> steps both improves reasoning quality and enables substantial context compression by pruning scratch work. This suggests that explicit structural constraints on chain-of-thought can simultaneously enhance performance and efficiency, offering a promising direction for scalable, memory-efficient reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) excel at generating long chains of thought, but long reasoning traces are often verbose and memory-inefficient. In this work, we introduce Structured Thoughts, a framework that organizes reasoning into alternating <try> and <outcome> blocks: <try> captures exploratory scratch work, while <outcome> contains the distilled conclusion of that step. We construct a dataset of structured thoughts by segmenting reasoning traces into <try> blocks and prompting an LLM to summarize each step into its corresponding <outcome>. Fine-tuning pretrained foundation models on this reformatted data produces models that adopt the structured reasoning style, leading to performance gains of up to 8.08\% on reasoning benchmarks compared to standard SFT. The explicit structure also enables context pruning: after each <try>/<outcome> pair, the <try> can be pruned, allowing the model to retain conclusions without keeping the full scratch work in the context. A proof-of-concept pruning implementation achieves an average of 85\% memory / context savings with an 8.67\% performance drop across mathematical tasks.

The results demonstrated the effectiveness of our method.

### [24] [Enjoy Your Talk: A Human-Centered Benchmark for Multi-Turn Dialogue with Decoupled User Simulation, Target Modeling, and Judging](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10428)
*Jinglan Gong,Jiefan Lu,Hewei Guo,Kehan Li,Zhiyuan Han,Jihang Jiang,Wenwen Tong,Lewei Lu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EYT-Bench is a human-centered benchmark to evaluate LLMs as multi-turn conversational partners, focusing on intent tracking, persona use, and goal completion rather than single-turn metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing single-turn benchmarks fail to capture crucial aspects of multi-turn dialogue, such as persona consistency, evolving intent, emotional dynamics, and the ability to complete user goals over time. There is also concern that benchmarks relying on LLM-synthesized personas introduce bias and do not reflect realistic, human-grounded behavior.

Method: The authors design EYT-Bench with a three-party decoupled setup: (1) a persona-grounded user simulator that uses personas sampled from public, human-curated corpora (Nemotron-Personas-USA and PersonaMem-v2), (2) a target LLM that separates intent perception from response generation, and (3) an independent third-party LLM judge, with an option to ensemble multiple judges. They introduce two trajectory-level evaluation metrics: embedding-based intent drift over the conversation and final-intent completion rate (FICR), inspired by tau-bench. They then run a large-scale evaluation over 17 target models and 200 dialogues per model.

Result: Across 17 models, they find: (i) closed- and open-source LLMs are very similar on subjective metrics like empathy, persona consistency, and anthropomorphism (differences ≤ 0.3) but can differ up to 9x on objective intent tracking; (ii) enabling explicit reasoning (“thinking on”) substantially improves objective tracking for long-context personas (+0.47–0.50 in latent-intent accuracy for Gemma-4) with little change in subjective ratings; (iii) persona representation format strongly affects performance trajectories, with FICR nearly saturating (>0.95) on Nemotron-USA but varying widely (0.53–0.88) on PersonaMem-v2; and (iv) a warm-up effect (early turns improving later performance) occurs for 16 of 17 models, with GPT-5.5 as the lone counterexample, and model rankings remain stable across judge hyperparameter alpha in [0.05, 0.15]. A cross-judge ablation using deepseek-v4-pro shows that model rankings and final-intent satisfaction are consistent across different judges.

Conclusion: EYT-Bench offers a more realistic, human-centered way to evaluate LLMs as conversational partners by emphasizing multi-turn intent tracking, persona fidelity, and goal completion. The benchmark reveals that models that appear similar on traditional, subjective dialogue metrics can diverge substantially on objective, trajectory-level capabilities, and that reasoning modes, persona formats, and warm-up behavior significantly shape multi-turn performance. The use of human-grounded personas and robust LLM judging supports more reliable comparison of conversational models.

Abstract: Evaluating large language models (LLMs) as multi-turn conversational partners requires probing capabilities that single-turn benchmarks miss: persona consistency, evolving intent tracking, emotional dynamics, and goal completion. We introduce EYT-Bench, a human-centered benchmark built around a three-party decoupled design: a persona-grounded user simulator, a target model that separates intent perception from response generation, and an independent third-party LLM judge with optional multi-judge ensembling. Personas are sampled from public human-curated corpora, Nemotron-Personas-USA and PersonaMem-v2, rather than synthesized, reducing LLM-induced persona bias. EYT-Bench also introduces two trajectory-level metrics: embedding-based intent drift and final-intent completion rate (FICR), inspired by tau-bench. In a 17-target x 200-dialogue evaluation, EYT-Bench reveals four findings: (i) state-of-the-art closed- and open-source models are statistically close on subjective dimensions (empathy / persona / anthropomorphism vary within <= 0.3), but differ by up to 9x on objective intent tracking; (ii) reasoning ("thinking on") sharply improves objective tracking on long-context personas (+0.47-0.50 latent-intent accuracy on Gemma-4) while leaving subjective scores nearly unchanged; (iii) persona format dominates trajectory spread, with FICR saturating above 0.95 on Nemotron-USA but spreading from 0.53 to 0.88 on PersonaMem-v2; and (iv) the warm-up effect is robust on 16/17 models (one outlier, GPT-5.5, reverses the effect), with stable rankings across alpha in [0.05, 0.15]. A cross-judge ablation using deepseek-v4-pro confirms that target rankings and final-intent satisfaction are preserved across judges.

### [25] [Hallucination Detection in Large Language Models Using Diversion Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10476)
*Basel Abdeen,S M Tahmid Siddiqui,Meah Tahmeed Ahmed,Anoop Singhal,Latifur Khan,Punya Parag Modi,Ehab Al-Shaer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes diversion decoding, a decoding-time technique that challenges an LLM’s own answers to derive cheap and effective uncertainty signals for hallucination detection, outperforming prior methods at lower computational cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs can generate fluent but factually incorrect content (hallucinations), which harms reliability and trust. Existing hallucination detection and uncertainty estimation methods are often probabilistic, require many sampled outputs (ensembles, MC sampling), or other heavy computations, making them expensive and hard to deploy in real-time. There is a need for a lightweight, effective way to estimate an LLM’s uncertainty and detect hallucinations during generation.

Method: The authors introduce diversion decoding, a decoding strategy that, while the LLM is generating an answer, deliberately perturbs or challenges the ongoing generation to elicit alternative candidate continuations. From these decoding-time interventions, they derive features that reflect how resistant the model is to changing its answer (e.g., how easily it diverges to different continuations, stability of tokens, variation in alternatives). They then use these features as inputs to a separate machine-learning model that learns a heuristic mapping from these behavioral signals to an uncertainty score for each response.

Result: Experiments show that diversion decoding provides stronger correlation with hallucinations than existing uncertainty or confidence estimation baselines. It achieves better hallucination detection accuracy while using significantly fewer forward passes or less sampling, resulting in much lower computational overhead compared to probabilistic and ensemble-based methods.

Conclusion: Diversion decoding is an efficient and robust approach for LLM uncertainty estimation and hallucination detection. By integrating active challenges into the decoding process and learning from resulting behavioral features, it yields a practical heuristic that outperforms more computationally intensive methods, making it suitable for real-world deployment where both reliability and efficiency are critical.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have emerged as a powerful tool for retrieving knowledge through seamless, human-like interactions. Despite their advanced text generation capabilities, LLMs exhibit hallucination tendencies, where they generate factually incorrect statements and fabricate knowledge, undermining their reliability and trustworthiness. Multiple studies have explored methods to evaluate LLM uncertainty and detect hallucinations. However, existing approaches are often probabilistic and computationally expensive, limiting their practical applicability. In this paper, we introduce diversion decoding, a novel method for developing an LLM uncertainty heuristic by actively challenging model-generated responses during the decoding phase. Through diversion decoding, we extract features that capture the LLM's resistance to produce alternative answers and utilize these features to train a machine-learning model to develop a heuristic measure of the LLM's uncertainty. Our experimental results demonstrate that diversion decoding outperforms existing methods with significantly lower computational complexity, making it an efficient and robust solution for evaluating hallucination detection.

### [26] [When Reasoning Hurts Legal Drafting: The Verbalization Bottleneck in Patent Claim Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10480)
*Lekang Jiang,Wenjun Sun,Stephan Goetz*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies whether Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting helps patent claim generation and finds that a tailored, implicit CoT strategy improves claim quality more than explicit CoT.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Patent claim drafting is difficult because it demands technical precision, strict formal structure, and careful logical relationships between elements. Although CoT prompting is popular for boosting LLM reasoning, prior work suggests it may hurt performance on highly structured, pattern-sensitive tasks. The authors want to know if CoT helps or harms in the specific, high‑stakes context of patent claim generation.

Method: The authors design a task-specific Chain-of-Thought prompting framework adapted to patent claim drafting. They compare three setups: no CoT, explicit CoT (where reasoning steps are verbalized), and implicit CoT (where reasoning is encouraged but not output). They evaluate generated claims using automatic metrics and expert human judgment, and then conduct systematic analyses to understand how different prompting styles affect information flow and error patterns in the generated claims.

Result: Reasoning-enhanced prompting improves patent claim quality overall compared to not using CoT. However, implicit CoT consistently outperforms explicit CoT across automatic metrics and expert evaluations. Analyses show that explicit CoT often acts as an information bottleneck and can degrade final claims through abstraction of important details, disruption of learned drafting patterns, and amplified cascading errors from earlier reasoning steps.

Conclusion: Task-tailored CoT helps patent claim generation, but keeping reasoning implicit is more effective than verbalizing it. Explicit CoT can unintentionally restrict information and introduce errors, making it less suitable for highly structured legal drafting. The paper suggests that for legal and similarly structured tasks, CoT should be designed to leverage internal reasoning without forcing the model to expose its intermediate steps, and it offers broader insights for how CoT should be applied in such domains.

Abstract: Patent claim drafting is a challenging legal drafting task that requires technical expertise, precise linguistic control, strict adherence to formal conventions, and the preservation of complex logical relationships among claim elements. While Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting has been widely used to improve the reasoning capabilities of large language models (LLMs), recent evidence suggests that its benefits may be limited, or even negative, in highly structured or pattern-sensitive tasks. Therefore, this paper investigates whether CoT prompting benefits patent claim generation. We propose a task-specific CoT method for patent claim generation and evaluate its effectiveness through both automatic metrics and human expert assessment. Our results show that reasoning-enhanced prompting can improve claim quality. Moreover, we demonstrate a counter-intuitive but important empirical finding: implicit CoT, where reasoning is kept internal rather than explicitly verbalized, consistently outperforms explicit CoT. Through systematic analysis, we show that explicit CoT can introduce an unnecessary information bottleneck for claim generation. Verbalized reasoning may compromise the quality of final outputs through three specific mechanisms: abstraction of critical details, disruption of internalized generation patterns, and cascading error propagation. Our findings provide new insights into legal tasks and CoT applications.

### [27] [Articulate Intuition or Genuine Analysis? Benchmarking Epistemic Reliability in LLM-as-a-Judge Peer Reviews](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10511)
*Nuo Chen,Qian Wang,Qingyun Zou,Bingsheng He*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Kahneman4Review, a benchmark and rubric based on dual-process theory to evaluate peer-review texts, showing that current decision tiers and LLM “analytical” judgments are weakly aligned with true epistemic quality, and proposing design principles for more trustworthy LLM judges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is growing use of LLMs as “judges” of text quality, including for peer review. However, it is unclear whether what LLMs label as analytical or high-quality actually matches what human committees value epistemically in peer review. This gap has both practical implications (for automating review and meta-review) and philosophical implications about what counts as good reasoning. The authors want a principled, theory-grounded way to evaluate the reliability and trustworthiness of LLM-based review judges, separating superficial form (e.g., being long, structured, or fluent) from deeper epistemic function (e.g., finding real faults and providing evidence-based reasoning).

Method: They translate Kahneman’s dual-process theory (System 1 vs. System 2 thinking) into a structured, multi-dimensional rubric for peer-review quality. Using this rubric, they build Kahneman4Review, a dataset of 3,563 peer reviews annotated along nine textual dimensions grounded in epistemic and cognitive theory, plus eight bias diagnostics and a continuous reasoning-quality score. They then run several empirical analyses: (1) correlate rubric-based epistemic-quality scores with existing decision tiers to test alignment; (2) compare scores of public, showcase-style agentic reviews (e.g., LLM or curated reviews) to pooled human reviews, controlling for confounds such as length and venue; (3) analyze how ICLR review diagnostics change from 2022 to 2023, a period overlapping with widespread LLM availability; and (4) run a matched function-probe pilot, crafting textual probes that differ in whether they provide genuine fault-finding vs. merely fluent surface commentary, to test whether the rubric can distinguish epistemic function from stylistic polish.

Result: They find that: (1) the decision tier (e.g., accept/reject categories) does not show detectable alignment with their text-grounded proxy for epistemic quality, suggesting current decision labels are a poor training signal for LLM judges. (2) Public, agentic reviews receive higher raw rubric scores than pooled human reviews, but this advantage is largely explained by confounders like review length and venue, and the samples are not matched on the same underlying papers, so headline comparisons are misleading. (3) Diagnostic properties of ICLR review texts shift between 2022 and 2023 in ways temporally aligned with the broader availability of LLMs, but they cannot attribute a clear causal mechanism. (4) The rubric successfully differentiates function-designed probes that exhibit genuine critical reasoning from those that only display surface fluency, supporting its ability to separate epistemic function from textual form.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that robust benchmarks for LLM judges of peer review must explicitly disentangle analytical form (structure, fluency, length) from epistemic function (truth-tracking, fault-finding, evidence-based reasoning). Existing labels like decision tier or naive “high quality” tags are not reliable proxies for epistemic quality and may mislead LLM training. Kahneman4Review offers a theory-grounded, multi-dimensional rubric and dataset that better captures epistemic aspects of review quality and can serve as a more trustworthy reliability benchmark. They also provide concrete design recommendations for future LLM-judge benchmarks and tools, and release an interactive demo for exploration.

Abstract: When an LLM judge calls a peer review analytical and a human committee calls another review high quality, are they tracking the same thing? We argue they are not, and that the difference matters philosophically. We operationalise Kahneman's dual-process theory into a structured rubric for peer review and release Kahneman4Review, a benchmark of 3,563 rated reviews scored along nine theoretically motivated textual dimensions, eight bias diagnostics, and a continuous reasoning-quality score. Three findings bear on trustworthiness: decision tier is not detectably aligned with the rubric's text-grounded epistemic-quality proxy; public-showcase agentic reviews receive higher raw scores than pooled human reviews, but length and venue explain most of the gap and the samples are not paper-paired; and ICLR review-text diagnostics shift at the 2022--2023 transition, temporally coincident with widespread LLM availability but without identifying its cause. A matched function-probe pilot further shows that the rubric distinguishes textual probes designed to contrast genuine fault-finding with surface fluency. We argue that a trustworthy reliability benchmark for LLM judges must separate analytical form from epistemic function, and propose concrete design choices toward that goal. An interactive demo is available at https://huggingface.co/spaces/nuojohnchen/Kahneman4Review.

</details>


### [28] [UNIBROWSE: A Data-to-Agent Framework for Multimodal BrowseComp](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10557)
*Xiyu Wei,Qingwei Zong,Zhuocheng Yu,Sujian Li*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: UNIBROWSE is a unified data pipeline and 35B-scale agent for multimodal web browsing that supports all three key information-flow patterns (text-only, image-to-text, text-to-image), leading to state-of-the-art results on BrowseComp benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal browsing agents struggle with realistic web tasks that require combined perception, tool use, and long-horizon reasoning over dynamic web pages. Real-world browsing involves three information-flow patterns (text-only, image-to-text, text-to-image), but current data construction methods only cover text-only and image-to-text, neglecting text-to-image. This gap limits the generality, robustness, and real-world applicability of browsing agents.

Method: The authors propose UNIBROWSE, a unified data pipeline that: (1) systematically generates training data for all three information-flow patterns (text-only, image-to-text, text-to-image); (2) augments curated knowledge graphs with live web retrieval to better reflect up-to-date and realistic web content; and (3) introduces a new exploration-degree metric to quantify and filter out low-signal trajectories, improving the efficiency of reinforcement learning. Using this pipeline, they construct high-quality cold-start tool-use trajectories and exploration-heavy QA pairs, then train a 35B-parameter agent via supervised fine-tuning followed by exploration-aware RL.

Result: The UNIBROWSE pipeline yields a trained 35B agent that performs strongly on multimodal BrowseComp benchmarks. It achieves an average accuracy of 54.4 across five diverse benchmarks, representing a 10.5-point improvement over its base model Qwen3.5-35B-A3B. It also outperforms several competitive closed-source agent systems, including GPT-5 (42.9), Gemini-2.5 Pro (44.8), and Gemini-2.5 Flash (41.3).

Conclusion: By unifying training data generation across all three real-world information-flow patterns and incorporating exploration-aware filtering and RL, UNIBROWSE significantly advances the robustness and effectiveness of multimodal browsing agents. The resulting model sets a new state of the art on BrowseComp tasks and demonstrates that comprehensive pattern coverage plus exploration-focused training are key to strong web-browsing performance.

Abstract: Multimodal BrowseComp tasks require agents to combine perception, tool use, and long-horizon reasoning over dynamic web content, challenging their ability to handle compositional structure, open-world uncertainty, and multimodal integration across extended interactions. Crucially, real-world multimodal browsing involves three distinct information-flow patterns: text-only, image-to-text, and text-to-image, yet existing data construction methods cover only the text-only and image-to-text patterns, leaving text-to-image largely unaddressed and limiting agent generality and robustness. We introduce UNIBROWSE, a unified data pipeline that for the first time simultaneously generates training data covering all three patterns, augments curated knowledge graphs with live web retrieval for improved fidelity, and introduces a novel metric of exploration degree to filter low-signal instances for efficient reinforcement learning. Through this pipeline, we produce high-quality cold-start tool-use trajectories and exploration-rich QA pairs, and train a 35B-scale agent via supervised fine-tuning and exploration-aware RL.The resulting UNIBROWSE agent achieves state-of-the-art performance on multimodal BrowseComp benchmarks, attaining an average accuracy of 54.4 across five diverse benchmarks -- an improvement of 10.5 points over its base model Qwen3.5-35B-A3B -- and surpassing serveral closed-source agent workflows such as GPT-5 (42.9), Gemini-2.5 Pro (44.8), and Gemini-2.5 Flash (41.3).

### [29] [Demographic Prompting at Scale: When More Attributes Hurt LLM--Human Agreement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10590)
*Mahammed Kamruzzaman,Shrabon Kumar Das,Gene Louis Kim*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how including annotator demographic attributes in prompts affects how well LLM predictions align with human annotations, finding that only a small, well-chosen subset of attributes helps and full demographic detail can hurt.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly aligned to human judgments, many datasets include annotator demographic metadata and practitioners sometimes try to steer models toward particular "perspectives" using demographic prompts. However, it is unclear when and how such demographic prompting actually improves alignment with human annotations, whether more demographic detail is always better, and what model-internal mechanisms underlie these effects. This paper aims to clarify the conditions under which demographic prompting is beneficial or harmful for alignment.

Method: The authors run controlled experiments across five annotation-style tasks using five open-source LLMs. For each task, they prompt the model with different combinations of annotator demographic attributes (e.g., individual attributes and full multi-attribute bundles) and measure how closely model outputs match human annotations. They then analyze how alignment changes with the number and type of attributes, characterize attribute signal properties such as learnability and directional coherence, and apply neuron probing to identify specialized activations associated with demographic prompting and relate them to alignment outcomes.

Result: They find: (1) Alignment between LLM outputs and human annotations is highest when prompts include one to three high-signal demographic attributes; adding all available attributes typically reduces alignment, indicating an over-specification threshold. (2) The mere strength of demographic effects on human annotations does not identify which attributes will improve alignment; both how learnable an attribute’s signal is and how consistent its directional effect is across examples must be considered. (3) Neuron probing shows that specialized activations linked to demographic cues correlate with alignment improvements only when the underlying annotation signals are coherent; large activation volume by itself does not guarantee that the model can be successfully steered via those attributes.

Conclusion: Demographic prompting should be treated as a nuanced, context-dependent tool rather than a universally beneficial alignment technique. Its effectiveness depends on choosing a small set of high-quality, coherent attributes suited to the task and model, and more demographic detail can be counterproductive. Additionally, the presence of strong internal activations for demographic cues does not automatically translate into reliable steerability, underscoring the need for careful evaluation of both attribute signal properties and model behavior when using demographics in prompts.

Abstract: We investigate how annotator demographic attributes, supplied as prompt cues, shape the alignment between large language model (LLM) predictions and human annotations across five tasks. Using five open-source LLMs, we systematically vary the number and composition of demographic components in the prompt, spanning every combination from single-attribute through full-attribute configurations. Our experiments reveal three principal findings. First, alignment consistently peaks with one to three high-signal attributes and degrades under the full attribute set, establishing a clear over-specification threshold. Second, the overall magnitude of demographic influence on human annotations does not predict which attributes improve LLM alignment; instead, both the learnability and the directional coherence of each attribute's annotation signal need to be considered jointly. Third, neuron probing reveals that specialized activation correlates with alignment gains only under coherent annotation signals, and that activation volume alone does not imply steerability. Together, these results demonstrate that demographic prompting is not a monolithic intervention: its utility is highly context-dependent, shaped by attribute signal quality, task characteristics, and model architecture.

### [30] [Non-binary bottom-up constituency parsing without arity actions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10591)
*Jungyeul Park,Eunkyul Leah Jo,Zihao Huang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new non-binary bottom-up constituency parser that eliminates arity-specific reduction actions by deriving arity from stack delimiters, keeping accuracy competitive while using a much smaller action set.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing non-binary bottom-up constituency parsers typically require arity-specific reduce actions (e.g., Reduce-X#k), where each action jointly encodes the constituent label and the number of children. This leads to a large and sparse action inventory, complicates learning, and may not be theoretically necessary. The authors aim to show that arity does not need to be an explicit transition primitive and to design a parser that can still directly construct original non-binary trees without arity-specific actions.

Method: They design a delimiter-guided bottom-up transition system. Constituent labels are introduced via separate actions, while special delimiter markers on the stack indicate boundaries for reductions. In well-formed configurations, the combination of an active delimiter and a label marker uniquely determines the arity of the reduction, so arity becomes an implicit, derived property of the parser state. This factorization removes label–arity combinations from the action space. They implement this parser within a standard neural transition-based parsing framework and compare it against an arity-specific bottom-up baseline on PTB and CTB.

Result: On both PTB and CTB, the proposed delimiter-guided parser achieves parsing performance that is competitive with the arity-specific bottom-up baseline when using the same implementation framework. At the same time, it substantially reduces the size of the action inventory. Error and behavior analyses show that the parser’s predicted arity distribution closely matches that of the gold treebanks and that it continues to correctly predict high-arity constituents instead of degenerating into low-arity structures.

Conclusion: Arity does not need to be an explicit transition primitive in non-binary bottom-up constituency parsing. By deriving arity from delimiter-bounded stack configurations and separating label introduction from span composition, it is possible to build a parser with a much smaller action space that still directly constructs non-binary trees and remains empirically competitive with traditional arity-specific systems, without sacrificing the modeling of high-arity constituents.

Abstract: Non-binary bottom-up constituency parsing is usually taken to require arity actions: reductions such as \(\textsc{Reduce-}X\#k\) specify both the mother label and the number of children to be composed. We show that this arity parameter is not a necessary transition primitive. Our parser introduces constituent labels separately and recovers reduction spans from delimiter-bounded stack configurations. In a well-formed reduction configuration, arity is uniquely determined by the active delimiter and the label marker, making it a derived property of parser state rather than an action label. This factorization removes label--arity-specific reduce actions while preserving direct construction of original non-binary trees. Experiments on PTB and CTB show that the delimiter-guided parser remains competitive with an arity-specific bottom-up baseline under the same implementation framework, with substantially smaller action inventories. Analyses further show that its predicted arity profile remains close to the gold treebanks and that high-arity constituents do not collapse when arity actions are removed.

</details>


### [31] [Eval-Pair Matrix: Answer-Paired Meta-Evaluation of LLM Judges for Grounded RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10626)
*Sriram Selvam,Anneswa Ghosh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether using the same LLM family as both generator and judge in RAG leads to systematic self-leniency, and finds little evidence for it under a tightly controlled setup.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-as-a-judge has become a standard way to evaluate RAG, but many studies let a model family both generate and judge answers. This could introduce bias (self-leniency), yet prior work usually compares aggregate diagonal vs. off-diagonal scores in evaluation matrices, which can confound model and answer differences. A more controlled, answer-paired protocol is needed to see if same-model judging is truly biased.

Method: They build a meta-evaluation benchmark for source-grounded RAG based on GaRAGe questions and passages. For each record, they inject exactly one hidden answer-relevant contradiction into the grounding passage. Multiple LLMs from three families (GPT, Grok, Gemini) generate answers from these perturbed passages. The same models, used as blind judges, evaluate each candidate answer against the original (unperturbed) passages, yielding a 3x3 generator–judge matrix with 300 core records, 897 generator outputs, and 2,683 judge verdicts; 275 records are fully validated and used for the primary analysis. Crucially, instead of comparing entire rows/columns, they compare different judges on the identical candidate answer, estimating same-model vs. cross-model effects via answer-paired F1 and recall, and further stratify by whether the answer adopts the induced contradictory claim or avoids it. They also conduct targeted human evaluation to interpret apparent false positives.

Result: Diagonal (same-family generator–judge) and off-diagonal F1 scores look similar under their answer-paired analysis. The estimated same-model recall effect is near zero (-0.5 percentage points; 95% cluster bootstrap CI [-2.7, +1.7]), suggesting no strong self-leniency at the level of detecting contradictions. The only robust effect is that when an answer avoids repeating the induced contradictory claim, judges of the same model family are somewhat less likely to flag issues (-4.3 percentage points). Human review of cases initially labeled as false positives shows they mostly reflect alternative error detections (source issues), labeling mistakes about whether the induced claim was adopted, or inherently ambiguous cases, rather than genuine judge hallucinations or over-penalization.

Conclusion: Under a carefully controlled, source-grounded meta-evaluation, reusing the same LLM model family as both generator and judge in RAG does not show strong systematic self-leniency when analyses are done on answer-paired comparisons. Apparent diagonal vs. off-diagonal differences seen in unpaired analyses can be misleading. The work argues that RAG judge evaluations should provide full generator–judge matrices, compute answer-paired effects, analyze behavior across meaningful strata (e.g., whether the induced claim is adopted or avoided), and closely examine how human labels align with the judge’s actual task, to avoid misinterpreting model-judge behavior.

Abstract: LLM-as-a-judge evaluation is widely used for retrieval-augmented generation (RAG), but reusing the same model family as both generator and judge makes self-leniency difficult to identify. We introduce Eval-Pair Matrix, a controlled meta evaluation protocol for source-grounded RAG. Starting from GaRAGe questions and grounding passages, we induce one hidden answer-causal contradiction per record, generate answers from perturbed passages with GPT, Grok, and Gemini models, and then use the same models as blind judges to evaluate each answer against the original passages. The experiment contains 300 core records, 897 labeled generator outputs, and 2,683 judge verdicts in a crossed 3 x 3 matrix; the primary analysis uses 275 fully validated records. Instead of comparing diagonal and off-diagonal cells across different answers, we estimate same-model effects by pairing judges on the exact same candidate answer. This changes the interpretation: diagonal and off diagonal F1 are similar, and the paired same-model recall effect is near zero (-0.5 pp; 95% cluster bootstrap CI [-2.7, +1.7]). The only robust paired gap is lower matching-judge flagging for answers that avoided the induced claim (-4.3 pp). A targeted human evaluation finds that reviewed apparent false positives are alternate source-error detections, mistakes in labeling whether the induced claim was adopted, or unclear cases; none were adjudicated as genuine false alarms. The lesson is methodological: RAG judge studies should report full matrices, answer-paired effects, behavior strata, and label-task alignment.

</details>

*Song-Ze Yu,Joseph Suh,Serina Chang,David M. Chan*

Main category: cs.CL

Method: The authors build Anamnesis, a web-based interactive platform that uses large language models conditioned with structured narrative backstories, following the Anthology and Alterity frameworks, to create rich, demographically grounded virtual respondents. Result: In two case studies—replicating segments of Pew Research Center's American Trends Panel (political typology and biomedical questions) and modeling preferences in the New Yorker Caption Contest—Anamnesis generates distributions of opinions/preferences that more closely resemble actual human survey results than those produced by baseline persona-prompting approaches. This indicates that the structured narrative conditioning and demographic control improve the fidelity of simulated survey populations.

Conclusion: Anamnesis demonstrates that combining narrative backstory frameworks with demographic control in an accessible web interface can provide a practical, transparent, and reproducible way to simulate survey responses. It outperforms simple persona prompting in matching real-world distributions and offers an open-source alternative to closed, proprietary simulation tools, positioning itself as a useful instrument for pretesting and stress-testing surveys and exploring virtual population responses.

Abstract: We present Anamnesis, an interactive system for demographically controllable survey simulation using large language models. Open-source, and designed for non-technical users/researchers, Anamnesis enables the prototyping and stress-testing of survey instruments on virtual populations rather than real human subjects. The platform operationalizes the recently introduced Anthology and Alterity frameworks, which use structured narrative backstories to condition model responses, within a unified web interface. It supports open-ended generation, probabilistic demographic resampling, and multimodal (image and audio) surveys. We evaluate the system through two case studies: (1) replicating segments of Pew Research Center's American Trends Panel (ATP) on political typology and biomedical issues and (2) emulating human preference in the New Yorker Caption Contest. In both cases, Anamnesis produces opinion distributions that more closely match real-world survey data than standard persona-prompting baselines, offering a transparent, reproducible, and open-source alternative to proprietary simulation services.

### [33] [MafiaScope: Non-Invasive, Time-Resolved Belief Probing for LLM Agents in Social Deduction Games](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10645)
*Ilia Karpov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MafiaScope, a testbed that repurposes the social deduction game Mafia to quantitatively measure LLMs’ Theory of Mind using hidden structured probes rather than only public gameplay.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Public behavior of LLM agents in social games or tasks can be misleading: an agent may appear to reason socially (e.g., vote correctly, lie convincingly) without actually tracking beliefs or others’ mental states. There is a need for tools that expose and evaluate the internal social reasoning and belief states of LLM agents, not just their outward performance.

Method: The authors build an engine that runs Mafia games between LLM agents. After each public utterance, every agent privately answers a configurable set of structured probe questions about the game state and others’ beliefs. These probes never influence the game. The engine automatically scores the probe answers against the known ground truth and logs them. An interactive visualizer then displays belief trajectories in several modes: (1) impersonate mode shows the evolving game as seen by a chosen agent; (2) panels chart time-aligned belief accuracy and confidence calibration; and (3) a counterfactual replay tool can fork the game at any recorded step to explore alternative continuations. They conduct a case study with DeepSeek over 32 games, collecting and parsing 13,815 probe answers, and run a 30-fork counterfactual replay experiment.

Result: In the DeepSeek case study, the authors find that stated confidence is poorly calibrated, with an expected calibration error (ECE) of 0.17. Agents systematically overestimate how much others suspect them by a factor of about 1.5. The 30-fork replay experiment demonstrates that the counterfactual replay pipeline works in practice. They also produce and release a corpus of 200+ cross-model games, along with their engine and visualizer, as open resources.

Conclusion: MafiaScope provides a practical, open, and visual framework for turning the Mafia social deduction game into a measurement instrument for LLM Theory of Mind. By decoupling hidden structured probes from public game actions and offering tools for visualization and counterfactual analysis, it exposes discrepancies between agents’ apparent social competence and their internal belief tracking and calibration. The framework and released corpus enable systematic, reproducible research on social reasoning in LLM agents.

Abstract: An LLM agent's public behaviour reveals little about its social reasoning: an agent that votes correctly may be guessing, and an agent that lies well leaves no trace of what it actually believes. We present MafiaScope, an open testbed that turns the social deduction game Mafia into a measurement instrument for machine Theory of Mind. After every public utterance, every agent privately answers a configurable set of structured probe questions; the answers never re-enter the game and are scored automatically against the ground truth the engine knows. An interactive visualizer renders the belief trajectories: impersonate mode shows the game as one agent sees it, panels chart timeline-aligned accuracy and calibration, and counterfactual replay forks any recorded step. In a 32-game DeepSeek case study with 13{,}815 parsed probe answers, stated confidence is poorly calibrated, with expected calibration error 0.17, agents over-predict being suspected 1.5 times, and a 30-fork replay experiment walks the counterfactual replay workflow end to end. Engine, viewer and a corpus of 200+ cross-model games are released under an open licence; live demo: https://karpovilia.github.io/mafiascope/; screencast: https://vimeo.com/1208920221.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The BEA 2025 Shared Task defines key evaluation tracks, but practical tools optimized for these tasks and capable of scalable, automated, and reliable assessment are missing. The authors aim to fill this gap with a dedicated evaluator model that can systematically assess AI tutors along core pedagogical dimensions.

This augmented training data is then used to fine-tune FATE for the specified tasks. These results indicate that FATE can both effectively learn specialized evaluation behavior and provide discriminative benchmarking across different tutoring models.

By leveraging knowledge distillation to overcome scarce labeled data, the model attains strong performance gains and can serve as a practical automated evaluator for educational LLMs. The benchmarking of major commercial models shows that FATE can differentiate pedagogical quality across systems, suggesting its utility for researchers and practitioners who need scalable, standardized evaluation of AI tutoring behavior.

Abstract: The rapid integration of Large Language Models (LLMs) into K-12 and higher education has outpaced the development of reliable methods for evaluating their pedagogical quality. As the research community starts to explore the space of automating evaluation of AI tutors, we introduce FATE (FLC AI Tutor Evaluator), a specialized 8B-parameter language model designed to evaluate AI tutors. Aligned with the four core evaluation tracks from the BEA 2025 Shared Task, our model assesses pedagogical ability across Mistake Identification, Mistake Location, Guidance, and Actionability. Because pedagogical evaluation is a specialized task with limited labeled data, we leverage knowledge distillation from a frontier LLM to generate additional supervision, yielding absolute performance gains up to 22.63 percentage points. Finally, we demonstrate FATE's utility as an automated evaluator by benchmarking instructional responses generated by popular commercial models, including ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini, and DeepSeek. ### [35] [Unlocking Parallelism in Autoregressive Language Models via Speculative Decoding with Progressive Tree Drafting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10661)
*Zipeng Gao,Zhi Zheng,Qingrong Xia,Junda Lin,Ziwei Zhao,Tong Xu,Zhefeng Wang,Enhong Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Progressive Tree Drafting (PTD), a training-free, model-agnostic speculative decoding method that structures parallel draft generation as a guided tree with pruning, doubling decoding speed for LLMs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding speeds up LLM inference but usually needs an extra draft model, causing extra training and communication cost. New self-drafting methods avoid auxiliary models but still underuse the target model’s inherent parallelism because they lack a coordinated structure for generating and managing multiple drafts. There is a need for a method that fully exploits model parallel capacity while remaining training-free and broadly applicable.

Method: The authors propose Progressive Tree Drafting (PTD), which organizes draft generation as a progressive tree over possible continuations. In one forward pass, the LLM explores multiple semantic paths in parallel. A stepwise pruning mechanism removes low-potential branches while retaining diverse yet coherent candidates, effectively guiding the model’s parallel exploration. PTD is designed to be plugged into existing LLMs without retraining or architectural changes, treating them as black boxes while structuring how token drafts are sampled and filtered across the tree.

Result: Across multiple benchmarks, PTD achieves up to 2× decoding speedup compared with standard decoding, without requiring additional training or model-specific customization. It maintains coherence while improving diversity of drafts and reduces the need for auxiliary draft models or extra communication overhead.

Conclusion: PTD demonstrates that a structured, guided tree-based drafting strategy can unlock more of an LLM’s latent parallel capacity for speculative decoding. By remaining training-free and model-agnostic, it provides a practical way to accelerate LLM inference with significant speedups and broad applicability, suggesting that structural coordination of drafts is as important as model design itself for efficient decoding.

Abstract: Speculative decoding has significantly accelerated Large Language Model (LLM) inference by alleviating memory-bound bottlenecks. However, traditional speculative decoding typically relies on auxiliary draft modules, incurring significant training and communication overhead. Although recent methods attempt to generate drafts within the target model itself, they often fail to fully exploit its latent parallel capacity due to a lack of structural coordination. In this paper, we propose \textbf{Progressive Tree Drafting (PTD)}, which employs a structured, guided parallel drafting strategy to harness the model's parallel potential. By coupling a progressive tree structure with a stepwise pruning mechanism, PTD actively guides the LLM to explore multiple semantic paths in a single forward pass, ensuring both draft diversity and coherence. Experiments demonstrate that PTD achieves up to $2\times$ decoding speedup across various benchmarks while remaining training-free and model-agnostic. Our code is available at: https://github.com/MINE-USTC/PTD.

*Saadeldine Eletter,Owais Aijaz,Preslav Nakov*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multimodal retrieval-augmented generation systems are usually evaluated with clean, reliable retrieved content, which does not reflect real-world conditions where retrieval is noisy and can be adversarially manipulated. The authors want to systematically understand how different kinds of multimodal “pollution” affect factual QA performance and to design methods that remain reliable when retrieved images or texts are misleading or corrupted. They also seek a principled way to decide when to trust parametric model knowledge versus retrieved multimodal evidence.

Method: The authors build QIMG-7, a controlled benchmark for multimodal retrieval pollution in multi-sentence factual QA, combining four datasets and seven families of image attacks (e.g., semantic edits, overlays, adversarial patches, style transfer), producing 16 clean vs. polluted retrieval regimes and 1,760 evaluation instances per method. They evaluate four different generator/gating stacks, including a gpt-4o-mini based stack, with naive multimodal fusion as a baseline. They then introduce Source-Aware Trust Resolution (SATR), a training-free decision layer that generates candidate answers from three sources—Parametric-only, Text-only retrieval, and Full multimodal retrieval—and then compares and selects among them (or falls back) based on estimated source reliability. A Field-Selector variant further refines this by choosing which source to trust per answer field. They conduct ablations to dissect the contributions of text reliability modeling versus other components.

Result: On QIMG-7, naive multimodal fusion shows substantial brittleness to polluted retrieval: in the main gpt-4o-mini stack, support for correct answers with Full multimodal input falls from 0.908 with clean text to 0.490 with polluted text, and relying solely on parametric knowledge can sometimes be safer than using retrieval. The proposed SATR framework, particularly the Field-Selector variant, significantly improves robustness; it attains a balanced score of 0.816, an 11.7 point gain over Full-MM and a 2.7 point gain over a strong Cascaded Router baseline. Ablation studies indicate that the main performance gains come from modeling text reliability explicitly within a text-first RAG setting, rather than from more complex multimodal reasoning alone.

Conclusion: Multimodal RAG systems are highly vulnerable when retrieved content—especially images—is polluted or adversarially manipulated, and naive unconditional fusion of parametric knowledge with multimodal retrieval can degrade factual QA performance. The QIMG-7 benchmark exposes these vulnerabilities in a controlled, systematic manner. The proposed Source-Aware Trust Resolution approach, which selectively decides when and how to trust parametric, text-only, or full multimodal evidence, substantially improves robustness without additional training. The findings argue for selective, source-aware trust mechanisms and careful text reliability modeling in text-first factual QA, instead of blindly fusing all retrieved multimodal information. The authors release QIMG-7 and related artifacts to support further research on robust multimodal RAG under retrieval pollution.

Abstract: Multimodal retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) is often evaluated with clean evidence, yet real retrieval can return topically relevant but unreliable content: false text and misleading images from corrupted metadata, entity swaps, typographic overlays, semantic edits, adversarial patches, blends, or style transfer. We introduce QIMG-7, a controlled benchmark for multimodal retrieval pollution in multi-sentence factual QA, spanning four datasets, seven image-attack families, and 16 paired clean/polluted regimes, for 1,760 evaluation rows per method. Across four generator/gate stacks, naive multimodal fusion is brittle: in the main gpt-4o-mini stack, Full-MM support drops from 0.908 with clean text to 0.490 with polluted text, often making Parametric fallback safer than retrieval. We propose source-aware trust resolution (SATR), a training-free approach that compares Parametric, Text-only, and Full-MM candidate answers and selects among candidate answers or falls back based on source reliability. The Field-Selector variant achieves the best balanced score, 0.816, improving over Full-MM by 11.7 points and over the Cascaded Router by 2.7 points. Ablations show that, in this text-first setting, explicit text-reliability modeling is the dominant driver of these gains. Overall, in text-first factual QA with multimodal retrieval conflict, our results support selective trust rather than unconditional fusion. Artifacts are available at https://github.com/SaadElDine/Trust_Before_Fusion.

</details>


### [39] [Diagnosing and Mitigating Thinking Collapse in On-Policy Self-Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10805)
*Keqin Peng,Chen Li,Yuanxin Ouyang,Yancheng Yuan,Liang Ding*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and fixes a failure mode of on-policy self-distillation in LLMs, called “thinking collapse,” where the model loses its internal reasoning, and proposes AD-OPSD, a modified self-distillation framework that preserves reasoning while still improving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: On-policy self-distillation is widely used to improve and align LLMs, but for complex reasoning tasks it can unexpectedly reduce downstream performance. The authors aim to understand why this happens, characterize the underlying optimization pathology, and design a principled method that fixes the issue without giving up the benefits of self-distillation.

Method: They first analyze token-level behavior under OPSD, introducing the notion of epistemic-token density (ET per 1k) as a measure of intermediate reasoning content. Using entropy-based gradient masking and token-level target analysis, they study how teacher gradients interact with high-entropy student decisions and where student–teacher divergences concentrate. Based on this analysis, they design Adaptive Dual-Perspective OPSD (AD-OPSD), which dynamically adjusts the self-distillation objective: tokens deemed at high risk of harmful suppression are "sandboxed" and anchored to a reference prior taken from a frozen base model, with an asymmetric pointwise divergence gate to decide when to trust the prior vs. the teacher. This forms a robust control-style framework around standard OPSD.

Result: Empirical evaluation on multiple competitive mathematical reasoning benchmarks shows that AD-OPSD achieves up to +4.1% absolute average accuracy gain over standard OPSD across different model sizes and datasets. Token-level analyses indicate that AD-OPSD maintains higher epistemic-token density and avoids the sharp drops characteristic of thinking collapse, and it transfers well across various post-training setups.

Conclusion: Standard on-policy self-distillation can induce a severe optimization trap—thinking collapse—where internal reasoning tokens are aggressively suppressed, harming complex reasoning performance. By explicitly identifying and controlling high-risk tokens via a dual-perspective objective anchored to a frozen base model, AD-OPSD preserves the model’s native reasoning while retaining the corrective benefits of OPSD. This both improves accuracy on challenging reasoning tasks and offers a more robust and general framework for self-distillation-based post-training of LLMs.

Abstract: On-Policy Self-Distillation (OPSD) has emerged as a crucial paradigm for enhancing and aligning Large Language Models (LLMs). However, in complex reasoning tasks, OPSD paradoxically degrades downstream performance. In this paper, we systematically investigate this pathology and identify a severe optimization trap we define as \textbf{Thinking Collapse} -- a sharp decline in the model's native intermediate reasoning behavior, measured by epistemic-token density (ET per 1k). Through entropy-based gradient masking and token-level target analysis, we show that this collapse is triggered by aggressive teacher gradients at high-student-entropy decision forks, where student epistemic tokens are frequently suppressed into teacher non-epistemic targets and are highly concentrated in high pointwise student-teacher divergence regions. To resolve this optimization pathology, we propose \textbf{Adaptive Dual-Perspective OPSD (AD-OPSD)}, a robust control framework that dynamically moderates the self-distillation objective. AD-OPSD selectively anchors high-suppression-risk sandboxed tokens to a reference prior derived from the frozen base model via an asymmetrical pointwise divergence gate, preserving native thinking capacity while retaining OPSD's error-correcting power. Extensive experiments across competitive mathematical benchmarks show that AD-OPSD improves over standard OPSD by up to \textbf{+4.1\%} absolute average accuracy across diverse model scales and datasets. Further analysis demonstrates that AD-OPSD mitigates thinking collapse and generalizes robustly to different post-training paradigms.

### [40] [Abstractiveness Metrics for Evaluating Text Summarization: A Refined Formulation with Empirical Validation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10806)
*Praveenkumar Katwe,Rakesh Chandra Balabantaray,Kali Prasad Vittala*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes new heuristic metrics to quantify how abstractive a generated summary is, going beyond traditional overlap-based metrics like ROUGE.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing summarization evaluation largely relies on surface-level overlap metrics (e.g., ROUGE) that cannot distinguish extractive copying from genuinely abstractive summarization. There is a need for principled, automatic measures that capture how much a system moves away from directly copying source text, while also signaling potentially risky hallucinations that may require human inspection.

Method: The authors define three related metrics: Reference Abstraction (RA), Summary Abstraction (SA), and Abstraction Ratio (AR). These are based on a formulation that uses the harmonic mean of document and summary lengths combined with a cubic non-overlap factor between source and summary text. This design ensures dimensional consistency, bounded score ranges, and heightened sensitivity near the extractive–abstractive transition. They then compute these metrics for summaries generated by four models (BART-large-cnn, Pegasus-xsum, DistilBart, MT5-small) on 100 XSUM documents.

Result: On the XSUM sample, the metrics clearly separate extractive from abstractive behavior: extractive models obtain low SA values (~0.12–0.26), whereas more abstractive models attain much higher SA values (~0.96–1.77). The Abstraction Ratio further highlights summaries that are highly abstractive and thus more likely to contain hallucinations, flagging them for potential manual evaluation.

Conclusion: The proposed RA, SA, and AR metrics offer a simple, principled way to quantify the abstractiveness of system-generated summaries beyond ROUGE-style overlap. They effectively discriminate between extractive and abstractive models and provide a practical signal (via AR) for when human validation may be needed to check hallucinations, supporting more nuanced evaluation of summarization systems.

Abstract: Quantifying abstractiveness in generated summaries is essential for evaluating summarization models beyond
  surface-level metrics like ROUGE. We introduce Reference Abstraction (RA), Summary Abstraction (SA), and Abstraction
  Ratio (AR) -- a set of principled heuristic metrics that measure how much a summary diverges from extractive copying
  of the source text. The formulation uses the harmonic mean of document lengths modulated by a cubic non-overlap
  factor, yielding dimensionally consistent, bounded output with non-linear sensitivity to the extractive-abstractive
  boundary. Evaluation on 100 XSUM documents across four summarization models (BART-large-cnn, Pegasus-xsum, DistilBart,
  MT5-small) demonstrates that the metrics successfully discriminate between extractive models (SA ~ 0.12-0.26) and
  abstractive models (SA ~ 0.96-1.77), and that the Abstraction Ratio identifies summaries requiring manual evaluation
  for potential hallucination. Code and results are available at https://github.com/katweNLP/AbstractionStudy.

*Fabrizio Marozzo,Stefano Iannicelli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a stratified, classification-guided sampling framework for LLM-based opinion summarization that preserves semantic diversity while greatly reducing token usage and cost, outperforming conventional and LLM baselines on multiple review and social media datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Opinionated text from reviews and social media has valuable information about user experiences and preferences, but corpora are large, redundant, and imbalanced (e.g., skewed sentiments or topics). Directly feeding all text to LLMs is expensive and can lead to summaries that overrepresent majority views and miss minority but important perspectives. There is a need for an efficient way to generate summaries that are both cost-effective and faithful to the diversity of opinions.

Method: The authors propose a framework that first applies multidimensional classification to opinions (e.g., categorizing by sentiment, topic, or other attributes). The focus is on preserving semantic diversity while minimizing input tokens and computational resources.

Result: On datasets of Amazon product reviews, Tripadvisor hotel reviews, and X/Twitter posts, the framework achieves substantial reductions in token usage and computational cost compared with feeding larger or full corpora to LLMs. Empirical evaluations show that the method consistently outperforms traditional AI summarization approaches and standard LLM summarization baselines in metrics related to content coverage, viewpoint balance, and preservation of semantic nuances in the source opinions.

Conclusion: Classification-guided, stratified sampling before LLM prompting is an effective strategy for opinion summarization: it reduces cost and input size while improving the faithfulness and balance of generated summaries. This indicates that carefully structured pre-processing and prompt design can significantly enhance LLM-based summarization of large, noisy, and imbalanced opinion corpora.

Abstract: Opinionated text - spanning product reviews, hotel feedback, and social posts - captures rich signals about user experiences, preferences, and concerns. However, the scale, redundancy, and imbalance of such corpora make it challenging to analyze opinions effectively, particularly when the goal is to generate summaries that remain faithful to the diversity of viewpoints expressed. This paper presents a framework that preserves semantics in LLM-based opinion summarization while minimizing token usage. We combine multidimensional classification (e.g., sentiment, topics) with a family of stratified sampling strategies to select compact yet representative subsets of opinions before prompting the LLM. Tailored prompts then produce balanced summaries that surface the salient aspects expressed in the opinions (e.g., strengths and weaknesses of products/hotels). Experiments on Amazon product reviews, Tripadvisor hotel reviews, and X/Twitter posts demonstrate that our method significantly reduces token usage and computational cost while consistently outperforming traditional AI-based and standard LLM summarization baselines in terms of content coverage, balance, and semantic preservation.

### [42] [Quantifying the Sources of Instability in LLM-Based Stance Analysis of Public Discourse](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10846)
*Bo Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how different automated preprocessing pipelines and measurement methods affect findings in computational social science, using YouTube interview data, and proposes a framework to diagnose and separate these sources of instability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Computational social science relies heavily on automated pipelines to convert raw audio/video into text, but different pipelines and measurement instruments can produce different results from the same raw data. This instability threatens the reliability and comparability of empirical findings, especially when studying relationships between psychological or linguistic constructs. The authors want to understand how much of the variance in results comes from preprocessing choices versus the choice of measurement method, and to provide a way for researchers to diagnose and manage this instability.

Method: The authors use 256 YouTube interviews from 41 public figures spanning five domains. They construct two alternative speaker-diarization pipelines and two alternative measurement methods: (a) an LLM-based annotation approach and (b) a keyword lexicon approach. All combinations are applied to the same underlying interview data to estimate the coupling between affective valence (especially negativity) and epistemic modality (e.g., emphasis). They then compare how estimates of this coupling and aggregate valence proportions vary across pipelines and measurement methods, focusing on differences by speaker and by sampling density (number of videos per speaker).

Result: (1) Sensitivity to preprocessing pipelines is mainly an issue for poorly sampled speakers (≤5 videos). For well-sampled speakers (≥16 videos), the mean absolute change in the correlation between negative affect and emphatic modality, r(neg, emph), induced by switching pipelines is modest (~0.13). (2) Disagreement across measurement methods (LLM vs keyword lexicon) is substantially larger and systematic: the two methods sometimes infer opposite directions of coupling for several well-sampled speakers, even when using the same preprocessing pipeline. (3) Despite these instabilities in cross-dimensional relationships, simple aggregate valence metrics like the overall proportion of negative language are very stable (changes in p(neg) are less than 6 percentage points across conditions), which can hide the deeper instability in inferred relationships.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that measuring relationships between dimensions (e.g., affect and modality) in interview data is sensitive to both preprocessing pipeline choices and measurement method, but that the latter is often the larger and more systematic source of variation. Because high-level aggregates can appear stable while cross-dimensional couplings vary substantially, researchers should explicitly test robustness to both pipeline and measurement choices. The authors provide a diagnostic framework to disentangle these two sources of instability, advising particular caution and robustness checks around the choice of measurement method (LLM vs lexicon) when drawing substantive conclusions.

Abstract: Computational social science increasingly relies on automated preprocessing pipelines -- speaker diarization, ASR transcript cleaning, sentence segmentation -- to convert raw media into analyzable text. When these pipelines produce different outputs from the same input, two distinct sources of instability can arise: the preprocessing pipeline itself (diarization method, segmentation rules) and the downstream measurement instrument (LLM annotation vs.\ keyword lexicon). Using 256 YouTube interviews across 41 public figures from five domains, we compare two speaker-diarization pipelines and two measurement methods, all targeting the coupling between affective valence and epistemic modality. We find that (1) preprocessing pipeline sensitivity is concentrated in speakers with limited video samples (N $\leq 5$); for the four best-sampled speakers (N $\geq 16$), the mean absolute pipeline-induced change in $r(\text{neg}, \text{emph})$ is only $0.13$; (2) cross-method disagreement is larger and more systematic -- the LLM and keyword-lexicon methods assign opposite coupling directions to several well-sampled speakers, even within the same preprocessing pipeline; and (3) aggregate valence proportions are highly stable ($|Δp(\text{neg})| < 6$pp) regardless of pipeline or method, masking both sources of instability. The contribution is a diagnostic framework that separates pipeline effects from measurement effects: researchers studying cross-dimensional relationships in interview data should verify that their conclusions are robust to both sources of variation, with particular attention to measurement method choice.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing biomedical evaluations of large language models are unreliable because many benchmarks are saturated and open-ended answers are often graded by other language models, which can bias results. There is a need for cleaner, more rigorous evaluation of cutting-edge models on biomedical tasks, explicitly accounting for model refusals.

Method: The authors test Claude Fable 5 on eight biomedical benchmarks (four text-only and four multimodal) using deterministic scoring against fixed answer keys, avoiding any LLM-as-grader setup. They compare Fable 5 with two earlier Claude models and GPT-5. In all analyses, refusals are treated as a distinct outcome and tracked in the tables, and they investigate refusal patterns by benchmark categories and disease domains.

Result: Fable 5 shows high refusal rates, ranging from 8.0% to 99.4% depending on the benchmark, unlike earlier Claude models and GPT-5. When refused questions are removed from the denominator, Fable 5 matches or outperforms all baselines on every benchmark. Two refusal patterns emerge: (1) concentration on basic-science/mechanistic questions in MedQA and MedXpertQA MM, corroborated using each benchmark’s own labels, and (2) a domain-specific pattern on RareBench, with near-universal refusals for inborn metabolic diseases versus much lower refusals for adult-onset autoimmune conditions.

Conclusion: Claude Fable 5 is highly capable on biomedical benchmarks when it chooses to answer, often surpassing strong baselines, but its practical biomedical utility is significantly constrained by conservative refusal behavior. Understanding and adjusting this refusal policy, rather than improving raw reasoning ability, appears to be the key lever for increasing its usefulness in biomedical contexts.

Abstract: Frontier language models are increasingly evaluated on biomedical benchmarks, but two problems undermine most published evaluations: legacy benchmarks are near-saturated, and open-ended responses are graded by other language models. We evaluate Claude Fable 5, Anthropic's most capable publicly available model, across eight biomedical benchmarks, four text and four multimodal, using deterministic scoring against fixed answer keys throughout. We include two Claude predecessors and GPT-5 as baselines. Refusal is tracked as a distinct outcome in every result table. That decision produces the paper's central finding. Fable 5 refuses between 8.0% and 99.4% of questions depending on the benchmark, a pattern absent in both predecessors and in GPT-5. Once refused items are excluded from the denominator, Fable 5's accuracy exceeds or meets every other model on every benchmark in this study. We identify two distinguishable refusal patterns: one concentrating in basic-science and mechanism content across MedQA and MedXpertQA MM, confirmed independently on two benchmarks using each benchmark's own category labels; and a separate disease-domain pattern on RareBench, where inborn metabolic disease presentations are refused near-universally while adult-onset autoimmune presentations are not. The primary constraint on Fable 5's biomedical usefulness is willingness to engage, not capability once it does.

Method: They build NL2SQL pipelines based on two backbone architectures, SmBoP and RASAT, and systematically add several components: (1) an intermediate representation called NatSQL, (2) a natural-language preprocessing step, (3) fine-tuning using synthetic data, and (4) a novel reranker that chooses among SQL queries in the final beam. Result: The experiments show that the effectiveness of each extension varies with the backbone model and with the presence of other components. Conclusion: Pipeline extensions for NL2SQL are not plug-and-play; their utility is highly interaction dependent. Abstract: In the age of large language models, Natural Language to SQL (NL2SQL) translation remains an open problem with many useful applications. We explore interactions between several NL2SQL pipeline extensions to inspire development of more lightweight models. Specifically, we integrate the NatSQL intermediate representation, include a preprocessing step and a fine-tuning step based on synthetic data, and develop a novel reranker model to improve SQL selection in the final beam. We perform an ablation study supplemented by a Shapley analysis of these different components integrated with two backbone architectures, SmBoP and RASAT. We find that simply combining all of them does not lead to best results, but that their impact depends on their interactions with the baseline system, as well as each other.
