### [45] [EasyOPD: An Easy-to-use On-Policy Distillation Framework for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11012)
*Jie Sun,Mao Zheng,Mingyang Song,Qiyong Zhong,Gengsheng Li,Zhepei Hong,Chang Wu,Pengfei Liu,Junfeng Fang,Xiang Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EasyOPD is a unified, extensible framework for on-policy distillation of large language models, providing modular implementations for several OPD variants on top of the verl RL backend.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing on-policy distillation (OPD) approaches for language models are powerful but fragmented: they use different types of supervision signals, assume different tokenizer setups, require varying degrees of teacher access, and operate at different granularities (sequence-level vs step-wise). This heterogeneity makes methods hard to reproduce, compare, and extend, especially at scale. The authors aim to provide a standardized, modular infrastructure that can support multiple OPD variants in a common codebase and execution environment, thereby accelerating research and practical adoption of OPD for evolving language-model policies.

Method: The authors design EasyOPD as a framework layered on top of verl, a distributed reinforcement-learning framework for LLMs. They enforce a clean separation between: (1) user-side configuration (e.g., YAML configs), (2) method-specific supervision logic (how teacher signals are computed and used), and (3) verl-based large-scale execution. Method modules plug into a shared backend via well-defined extension boundaries for: constructing losses, attaching and using rollout metadata, processing rewards, aligning tokenizers between student and teacher, and running teacher-side computation or evaluation. Using this architecture, they instantiate concrete modules for three representative OPD settings: cross-tokenizer OPD (teacher and student differ in tokenizers), on-policy self-distillation (student distills from its own stronger checkpoints or ensembles), and step-wise OPD (fine-grained supervision at the step/token level).

Result: Across benchmarks spanning reasoning, code generation, scientific knowledge, and tool use, the instantiated OPD methods all run on the same verl-based backend while preserving their distinct objective formulations. The empirical results show that: (1) the framework can express and execute diverse OPD algorithms without sacrificing their intended behavior; and (2) each OPD variant exhibits its own task-dependent performance profile, indicating that the modular implementation is faithful enough to reproduce known strengths and weaknesses of different OPD styles. The system is released with runnable configs and demos, supporting immediate practical use.

Conclusion: EasyOPD provides a unified, extensible infrastructure for on-policy distillation of LLMs, resolving prior fragmentation in implementations and enabling consistent experimentation across heterogeneous OPD methods. By cleanly separating configuration, method logic, and distributed execution, and by defining explicit extension points for losses, rewards, metadata, tokenization, and teacher computation, the framework can host diverse OPD variants—including cross-tokenizer, self-distillation, and step-wise supervision—on a shared backend. The accompanying open-source release, documentation, and demos aim to make OPD methods more accessible, reproducible, and easier to extend for future research and applications.

Abstract: Conventional language-model distillation often relies on fixed teacher-generated data, which may not cover the states encountered by an evolving student policy. On-policy distillation (OPD) instead collects teacher or evaluator supervision on student-generated rollouts. However, existing OPD methods differ substantially in supervision form, tokenizer compatibility, teacher access, and supervision granularity, leading to fragmented implementations that are difficult to reproduce and extend. We present \textsc{EasyOPD}, an on-policy distillation framework built on verl, a distributed reinforcement-learning framework for large language models. \textsc{EasyOPD} separates user-side configuration, method-specific supervision logic, and verl-based execution. Its method modules connect to the shared backend through extension boundaries for loss construction, rollout metadata, reward processing, tokenizer alignment, and teacher-side computation. We instantiate representative methods for three OPD settings -- cross-tokenizer OPD, on-policy self-distillation, and step-wise OPD. Experiments on reasoning, code-generation, scientific-knowledge, and tool-use benchmarks show that these implementations can be executed through the same verl-based backend while retaining their method-specific objectives and task-dependent performance profiles. We release \textsc{EasyOPD} with runnable YAML configurations, documentation, and an installable demonstration package and video.

</details>


### [46] [Can a Language Model Learn Facts Continually in Its Weights?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11020)
*Charles O'Neill*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper empirically studies how well large language models can accumulate and retain new factual knowledge through sequential weight updates, showing that broad, diverse training data creates more robust, usable knowledge than bare factual statements, but that later updates still interfere with access to earlier facts, making context a more reliable channel than weights for long-term factual use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Continual learning aims to let language models keep learning new facts after initial training, but it is unclear whether repeatedly writing new information into model weights can both (1) make that information usable in varied reasoning settings and (2) preserve it across many subsequent updates. Prior work has not fully characterized how the style of training data (simple statements vs diverse restatements) affects the robustness and accessibility of newly written knowledge or how sequential writes interact and interfere. The authors want to understand whether practical continual learning through weight updates is viable, what kind of data leads to truly usable knowledge, and how later fine-tuning steps degrade earlier inserted facts and other capabilities.

Method: The authors perform controlled continual-learning experiments on Qwen3 language models. They "invent" new facts and sequentially "write" them into the model via fine-tuning-like updates, running sequences of 20–100 such writes. They vary the training data for each fact: (a) bare-statement training, where the fact is given as a simple direct assertion, versus (b) broad study data with diverse restatements and contexts that imply but do not directly present the final conclusion. They evaluate using held-out questions of five types to test both rote recitation and flexible use of the knowledge. The original model, given the fact in its prompt, serves as a reference for ideal behavior. They track accuracy over time, log-probability changes, interference between facts, and damage to unrelated abilities using KL divergence from the original model. They also test recovery of "forgotten" facts by reintroducing them in the prompt context.

Result: Broad, diverse training data leads to knowledge the model can use more flexibly, shrinking the gap between simple recitation and actual application from 27.4 to 5.4 percentage points, even without training directly on the final conclusion. After 20 sequential writes, facts trained only via bare statements are almost entirely lost (about 1% accuracy), while those trained with broad study data retain about 46% accuracy. Behaviourally forgotten facts are not fully erased from the weights: they retain most of the log-probability increase the write originally gave them, and under bare-statement training, 70% of incorrect answers still incorporate the most recently written fact, indicating redirected access rather than deletion. The same sequence of writes has little effect on the model’s ability to use the facts when they are supplied in the prompt, with forgotten study facts recovering to 77–80% accuracy when provided as context. Interference from later writes occurs regardless of storage style, and degradation of unrelated abilities correlates with KL divergence from the original model. No tested intervention, including ones leveraging accurate local measurements of each write, prevents earlier facts from becoming difficult to reach via normal questioning.

Conclusion: Continual learning by directly writing new facts into model weights faces a fundamental accessibility problem: while broad, diverse training data can create more robust and usable factual knowledge than bare statements, subsequent writes still interfere with the ability to retrieve earlier facts, even though those facts are not fully erased from the weights. The knowledge becomes "question-keyed," with later updates redirecting which questions activate it. External context (e.g., prompting with the relevant fact) reliably restores performance, whereas weight-based storage alone cannot guarantee stable, composable access across many updates. A frozen reference model can help preserve overall capability, and KL divergence serves as a proxy for collateral damage, but under the studied conditions, the only dependable channel for composing facts and ensuring their survival through many later updates is contextual information rather than permanent changes to the weights.

Abstract: Continual learning promises a language model that keeps acquiring knowledge after training, with each new fact written into its weights. Whether weight writes can support accumulation remains undecided. We follow invented facts written into Qwen3 models from creation through sequences of twenty to one hundred later writes, using held-out questions of five types, with the original model given the fact in its prompt as the reference. Across these experiments, the breadth of the training data determines the kind of knowledge created. Bare-statement training produces recitation, while diverse restatements reduce the recitation-to-use gap from 27.4 to 5.4 points without showing the model a conclusion. This difference carries into later writes: after twenty sequential writes, bare-statement facts retain 1% accuracy while facts written from broad study data retain 46%. We also find that facts can be behaviourally forgotten without being erased. Forgotten facts keep most of the log-probability added by their write, and under bare-statement training 70% of wrong answers about them contain the most recently written fact. The same writes barely degrade the model's use of facts in context, and a forgotten study fact supplied in the prompt recovers to 77-80% on its questions. These results describe knowledge that is stored but question-keyed: later writes redirect the questions that reached it. Damage to unrelated abilities tracks KL divergence from the original model, and the later writes cause interference regardless of how the earlier fact was stored. Broad data can create usable knowledge, and a frozen reference can preserve capability, but no intervention we tested, including those built on accurate local measurements of each write, keeps earlier facts reachable. When facts must be composed or survive later writes, the reliable channel is context rather than the weights.

</details>


### [47] [Dimensionality in Satisfaction Ratings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11026)
*Andrew Hong,Jason Potteiger*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper uses GPT-4.1 to automatically score multiple dimensions of customer satisfaction from support chat logs, validates these scores against human survey ratings, and shows that LLM-based decomposition provides reliable, nuanced insight into customer experience at scale.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional customer satisfaction measurement relies heavily on post-interaction surveys, which are sparse, biased toward certain customers, and usually provide only one or two coarse scores. Firms lack granular, scalable insight into what specifically drives satisfaction or dissatisfaction in each contact. With the rise of large language models, there is an opportunity to use them to read and interpret every support conversation, decomposing satisfaction into distinct components and overcoming limitations of survey-based measurement—if such model-based scores can be shown to align well with customers’ own ratings.

Method: The authors apply GPT-4.1 to about 9,000 real customer support conversations from a global consumer-goods company. The model is prompted to assign scores along several satisfaction axes: overall satisfaction, satisfaction with the agent, with the outcome, with the product, and customer effort. They then compare the LLM-generated scores with customers’ own satisfaction ratings (where available) using correlations as a primary validation metric. They analyze not only the raw (unadjusted) correlations but also how correlations change when excluding a small subset of conversations where LLM and customer ratings diverge strongly. They further examine collinearity among axes and compare the LLM-based full-population estimates of satisfaction with the subset who completed surveys.

Result: LLM-generated annotations for four of the five axes (overall, agent, outcome, and effort) align closely with customers’ self-reported satisfaction, with raw correlations around 0.65 for overall, agent, and outcome, and -0.54 for effort (negative as expected). Product satisfaction performs poorly due to limitations in the available proxy measure. The misalignment between LLM scores and survey ratings is concentrated in a small tail of highly divergent sessions rather than widespread disagreement; removing the most severe divergences increases the correlation to 0.811, and removing the entire divergent tail yields a correlation of 0.914. The different satisfaction axes are highly collinear, so including them alongside the overall score does not significantly improve predictive power for the survey rating. However, when LLM scores are computed for every contact (a full census) instead of only for survey responders, average satisfaction is much lower: 2.91 vs. 3.62 on a 5-point scale, revealing that surveys overstate satisfaction. The decomposed LLM-based view shows richer, more nuanced patterns in customer experience.

Conclusion: A large language model can reliably infer multiple dimensions of customer satisfaction from raw support conversations, producing scores that closely track customers’ own ratings where those exist. The main benefit of this approach is not better prediction of survey scores but far greater coverage and richer attribution: the ability to read every contact, decompose satisfaction into interpretable components, and uncover gaps between survey-based metrics and the broader population’s experience. Decomposed satisfaction using LLMs thus offers a scalable, fine-grained methodology for understanding and managing customer experience in conversational channels.

Abstract: We used a large language model (GPT-4.1) to annotate the text of about 9,000 support conversations at a global consumer-goods firm, decomposing customer-care satisfaction into component axes (overall, agent, outcome, product, and customer effort), and validated the LLM annotations against the satisfaction ratings customers gave themselves. Four of five axes track self-reported satisfaction closely (overall, agent, and outcome near an unadjusted 0.65; effort -0.54), while product satisfaction is weak against the available proxy. The unadjusted correlation also understates the alignment: the disagreements concentrate in a small, readable tail of divergent sessions rather than in general drift, and the overall correlation rises to 0.811 when only the severe divergences are excluded and to 0.914 when the full divergent tail is excluded. The axes are also highly collinear, and adding them to the overall score does not improve prediction of the customer's rating, the decomposition's value is not incremental prediction but attribution and coverage. And, with greater coverage the picture of the data changes. Read on every contact rather than the few that return a survey, satisfaction is markedly lower than the survey reports (a full-census 2.91 against the surveyed 3.62 on a five-point scale). The promise of decomposed satisfaction as a methodology is the ability to identify more nuanced drivers of customer experience in conversational data.

### [48] [Flout at Your Own Risk: LLMs Struggle with Pragmatic Cooperativity Under Epistemic Asymmetry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11053)
*Hannah VanderHoeven,Abhijnan Nath,Nikhil Krishnaswamy*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how well large language models (LLMs) handle pragmatic, cooperative communication in multi-party collaborations when each participant has only partial information.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly used as collaborators and agents, they must interpret and produce context-sensitive, cooperative language, even when they don’t share the same information as their partners. Human conversation handles this via pragmatic reasoning and Grice’s cooperative principle, but it is unclear to what extent current LLMs can do this, especially under epistemic asymmetry (different participants knowing different things). Understanding these capabilities and limitations is crucial for deploying LLMs in real-world collaborative workflows.

Method: The authors define a formal notion of collaborative epistemic asymmetry that connects objective task success to Grice’s cooperative principle (and its maxims). They then design a multi-party collaborative task in which agents have partial, asymmetric information and must communicate to succeed. Different LLMs are evaluated in roles of both speaker and listener, under various prompting strategies and post-training methods, to see how well they follow cooperative principles and manage pragmatic reasoning under incomplete information.

Result: Empirically, the tested LLMs show some non-trivial pragmatic skills: they can sometimes infer or convey relevant contextual information, and their cooperative behavior can be improved with tailored prompting and post-training. However, the models still display systematic weaknesses in pragmatic communication when information is incomplete. Certain characteristic error patterns correlate with violations ("floutings") of Grice’s maxims—such as giving irrelevant, uninformative, or misleading responses—that the models themselves do not detect as problematic.

Conclusion: LLMs possess emerging but limited pragmatic reasoning abilities in multi-party, partial-information collaborations. While prompting and post-training can enhance cooperative behavior, current models still struggle to robustly adhere to Grice’s cooperative principle under epistemic asymmetry, leading to predictable failure modes. This indicates that more targeted architectural, training, or alignment approaches are needed to achieve human-like pragmatic competence in collaborative, agentic applications.

Abstract: Fruitful collaborations rely on cooperative communications, including of contextual cues to incorporate into reasoning. The increasing use of LLMs in collaborative and agentic pipelines raises questions about the extent to which they exhibit these pragmatic capabilities, especially in scenarios where they may not have access to the same information as their collaborators. In this paper, we perform a novel investigation into the pragmatic reasoning capabilities of LLMs in a multi-party collaborative task under partial information conditions. We formalize a notion of collaborative epistemic asymmetry that explicitly connects objective task success to Grice's cooperative principle and empirically assess various LLMs' abilities to act cooperatively as both speakers and listeners, including both prompting and post-training strategies. Our results show that while LLMs exhibit certain pragmatic capabilities in collaborative settings, and these can be elicited through prompting and post-training, they still face challenges in pragmatic communication with incomplete information, and that certain failure modes do correlate with floutings of Grice's maxims that go unrecognized.

### [49] [MJ: Multi-turn LLM Jailbreaking via Decomposed Credit Assignment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11070)
*Junyoung Park,Namgyu Park,Sechan Lee,Yoon-Chan Jhi,Jihoon Cho,Sangdon Park*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes DC-GRPO, a turn-level credit assignment framework for training multi-turn jailbreak attack policies against LLMs, achieving significantly higher attack success rates than prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-turn jailbreaking reflects realistic interactive use of LLMs, but learning effective multi-turn attack strategies is hard because existing methods assign a single coarse trajectory-level reward, which cannot distinguish which dialogue turns actually contributed to a successful jailbreak. This poor credit assignment limits the effectiveness of automated red teaming and adversarial policy learning.

Method: The authors introduce decomposed credit GRPO (DC-GRPO), which extends Group Relative Policy Optimization to multi-turn dialogues by giving each turn its own group-relative learning signal. For each turn, they combine an immediate credit term (how much that turn itself contributes) with a future credit term (how later turns and final outcome depend on it). They define both static and dynamic weighting schemes for blending these two components but keep the same turn-level decomposition. They then train jailbreak attacker policies with DC-GRPO and evaluate them across multiple victim LLMs and benchmarks.

Result: Both static- and dynamic-weight DC-GRPO variants attain very high attack success rates in a 5-query, top-3 success metric (ASR5@3), averaging 97.88% and 98.26%, respectively, on multiple benchmarks. These results substantially outperform state-of-the-art baselines such as SEMA (86.58%) and TROJail (86.23%), demonstrating that DC-GRPO enables far more effective multi-turn jailbreak strategies.

Conclusion: Turn-level group-relative credit assignment is the key factor for strong performance in learning multi-turn jailbreak attackers. By decomposing reward signals over turns and combining immediate and future credit, DC-GRPO overcomes credit misassignment from trajectory-level scoring and yields consistently superior attack success, largely independent of the specific weighting rule used. This makes DC-GRPO a powerful and general framework for automated multi-turn red teaming of LLMs.

Abstract: Modern large language models (LLMs) operate in interactive multi-turn settings, making multi-turn jailbreaking a realistic threat model and an important setting for automated red teaming. A core challenge in learning multi-turn jailbreak attackers is credit assignment: different turns contribute differently to the final outcome, yet existing learning signals are often too coarse to identify their individual contributions. We propose decomposed credit GRPO (DC-GRPO), a unified turn-level credit assignment framework for Group Relative Policy Optimization in multi-turn jailbreak learning. DC-GRPO assigns a separate group-relative learning signal to each turn by combining immediate and future credit, avoiding the credit misassignment induced by broadcasting a single trajectory-level score across the dialogue. We instantiate this framework with static and dynamic weighting rules that differ in how the two credit sources are balanced while sharing the same turn-level structure. Across multiple victim LLMs and benchmarks, the dynamic- and static-weighted variants achieve average ASR5@3 scores of 98.26% and 97.88%, respectively, substantially outperforming the state-of-the-art methods, including SEMA (86.58%) and TROJail (86.23%). Their consistently strong performance indicates that the central empirical benefit comes from turn-level group-relative credit assignment rather than a particular weighting rule. Warning: This paper contains examples of harmful content.

</details>


### [50] [ResearchQA: Benchmarking Citation-Grounded Question-Answering on Scientific Papers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11074)
*Saba Imran,Debanjum Singh Solanky*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents ResearchQA, a benchmark and evaluation framework to test whether language models’ answers about a scientific paper are correctly grounded in citations to that paper.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many language models can answer questions about scientific texts, but current evaluations rarely test whether their answers are explicitly supported by verifiable citations, allowing hallucinated or weakly grounded responses to go undetected. The authors want a way to rigorously measure citation-grounded question answering on scientific papers.

Method: They build ResearchQA, a dataset of 6,211 question–answer pairs derived from 494 open-access papers across eight scientific domains and four QA types (lookup, comprehension, multi-hop, adversarial). Each question is paired with at least one valid supporting passage, and the benchmark is constructed so that refusing to answer when the paper lacks support is rewarded. They then evaluate eight state-of-the-art open- and closed-weight models in a chat-with-paper setting using two evaluation components: (1) a deterministic citation matcher that checks whether cited passages support the answer, and (2) an LLM-based rubric evaluator to score answer quality. They compare citation-based metrics such as section coverage and citation accuracy with the LLM-based scores.

Result: Citation-grounded metrics clearly distinguish models better than rubric-based LLM evaluator scores, which tend to be compressed and less discriminative. Models differ substantially in how well they cover relevant sections of papers and how accurately they cite supporting evidence. Open-weight models achieve citation accuracy close to the best closed-weight model while also being 3–6× faster per example.

Conclusion: Citation-aware evaluation is crucial for assessing scientific QA systems, and benchmarks like ResearchQA reveal differences in grounding that standard LLM-based scoring misses. Open-weight models can be competitive with leading closed models in citation grounding while offering lower latency. The authors release the dataset, evaluation code, and prompts to facilitate further research on reliable, citation-grounded scientific question answering.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly used to assist scientific reading, but existing evaluation methods often fail to detect whether answers are supported by verifiable citations. We introduce ResearchQA, a benchmark of 6,211 single-paper question-answer pairs from 494 open-access papers spanning eight domains and four question types: lookup, comprehension, multi-hop, and adversarial. ResearchQA is designed for citation-grounded evaluation: it permits multiple valid supporting passages for a claim and rewards grounded refusal when the source paper does not support an answer. We evaluate eight leading closed- and open-weight models in a citation-grounded chat-with-paper setting using a deterministic citation matcher and an LLM-based rubric evaluator. Citation-based metrics separate systems more clearly than LLM-evaluator scores: section coverage and citation accuracy vary substantially across models, while evaluator scores remain tightly compressed. We further find that open-weight models approach the best closed-model citation accuracy while achieving 3 to 6 times lower per-example latency. We release the benchmark, evaluation harness, and evaluator prompt.

### [51] [Do LLMs Fabricate Legal Citations? A Bilingual Benchmark on Saudi Data Protection Law and the GDPR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11127)
*Noura Suliman Alrajeh*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how often large language models fabricate legal article citations when answering regulatory-compliance questions, using a bilingual benchmark on GDPR and Saudi PDPL, and finds high accuracy for GDPR but widespread, confident hallucinations for PDPL, showing that external verification is necessary.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As organizations start relying on LLMs for legal and compliance support, wrong statutory citations can propagate into policies and advice without being noticed. Existing evaluations rarely focus on citation-level reliability in specific jurisdictions or on non-Western laws. The authors want to systematically test whether LLMs hallucinate legal article references, especially in less-resourced legal regimes and across languages, to understand risks for real-world compliance use.

Method: The authors build a 120-question benchmark covering two data-protection laws: the EU GDPR and the Saudi PDPL. Each question is written in both English and Arabic and falls into three types: (1) direct citation retrieval (asking for the article number that governs a specific issue), (2) false-premise verification (testing whether models challenge incorrect assumptions), and (3) unanswerable “trap” questions, including references to a repealed article and to deadlines that exist only in subordinate regulations, not in the statutes. They define a manually verified gold reference set and evaluate three freely accessible LLMs (Gemini 2.5 Flash, GPT-OSS-120B, Nemotron-3-Super-120B). They automatically score whether returned citations are correct, fabricated, or confused, and correlate fabrication with language, jurisdiction, and model confidence scores.

Result: Across models, citation accuracy is very high for GDPR questions (94–100% on direct retrieval) but much worse for Saudi PDPL (only 23–40% correct on some tasks, with 60–77% of answers involving fabricated citations). Fabrication rates are similar whether the question is asked in English or Arabic, but depend strongly on the law being queried. The most frequent error source (67% of fabrications) is confusion between statutory provisions and implementing regulations, such as inventing deadlines that appear only in secondary rules. Moreover, 91% of fabricated citations are delivered with high model confidence (≥ 0.8), so confidence scores fail to flag unreliable answers.

Conclusion: LLMs’ reliability in legal citation is highly jurisdiction-dependent: they perform near-perfectly on well-resourced, globally salient laws like the GDPR but hallucinate heavily on less-represented regimes such as the Saudi PDPL, regardless of query language. Since fabricated citations are often delivered with high confidence, model self-assessment is not a reliable safeguard. For institutional or regulatory use, LLM outputs must be gated by external, verbatim verification mechanisms (e.g., checking citations against an authoritative legal database) rather than relying on the models’ own confidence or fluency.

Abstract: Organizations and regulators increasingly consult large language models (LLMs) for regulatory-compliance questions, yet a wrong statutory citation can silently propagate into legal advice, compliance documentation, and policy decisions. We introduce a bilingual benchmark of 120 questions probing whether freely accessible LLMs fabricate article citations for two data-protection instruments: the EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) and the Saudi Personal Data Protection Law (PDPL). The benchmark pairs direct citation retrieval questions with false premise verification probes and deliberately unanswerable "trap" questions -- including questions about a repealed article and about deadlines that exist only in implementing regulations, not in the law itself. Every question is posed in both Arabic and English, and all scoring is fully automatic against a manually verified gold reference. Evaluating three freely accessible models (Gemini 2.5 Flash, GPT-OSS-120B, Nemotron-3-Super-120B), we find a dramatic jurisdiction gap: near-ceiling citation accuracy on the GDPR (94-100% on direct retrieval) against majority fabrication on the Saudi PDPL (60-77%), invariant to query language; the highest fabrication rates (67%) arise from statute-vs-regulations confusion, and 91% of fabricated citations are asserted with confidence >= 0.8. Fabrication tracks the jurisdiction of the law, not the language of the query, and model confidence provides no protection -- indicating that verbatim-verification safeguards, rather than model self confidence, must gate any institutional reliance on LLMs for compliance screening.

### [52] [TIGER: Text-Conditioned Visual Gated Routing with Acceptance Alignment for Multimodal Speculative Decoding](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11131)
*Quynh Vo,Cong-Duy Nguyen,Ponhvoan Srey,Luu Anh Tuan,Thong Nguyen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper presents TIGER, a framework that improves speculative decoding for vision-language models by dynamically routing only text-relevant visual tokens and training the drafter to maximize verifier-accepted prefix length for better speedup without sacrificing accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Speculative decoding speeds up autoregressive generation by having a small drafter propose multiple tokens that a larger model verifies, but its benefits are limited in vision-language models (VLMs). In VLMs, drafters often diverge on visually critical content, and existing multimodal acceleration techniques fail to handle irrelevant or excessive visual evidence and do not directly optimize the length of verifier-accepted prefixes, which is the key factor governing speedup. The work aims to address these shortcomings to make speculative decoding more effective for multimodal tasks.

Method: The authors introduce TIGER, a Text-conditioned vIsual GatEd Routing framework for multimodal speculative decoding. TIGER dynamically selects a sparse subset of visual tokens that are most relevant to the drafter’s current textual state, instead of feeding the full set of visual tokens or a fixed compressed interface. For training, they first warm-start the drafter via distillation from the target model with KL anchoring, then further optimize it using acceptance-aligned, group-based policy training. The reward signal for this policy training is derived from the verifier and is based on the accepted prefix length of speculative continuations, explicitly encouraging generations that pass verification over longer spans.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that TIGER increases the verifier-accepted prefix length and thus improves speculative decoding speedup for VLMs under exact verifier-side speculative decoding. The method also achieves favorable quality-latency trade-offs and maintains comparable downstream task accuracy, supported by analyses on visual routing behavior.

Conclusion: TIGER effectively adapts speculative decoding to vision-language models by combining text-conditioned sparse visual routing with acceptance-aligned training of the drafter. This leads to longer accepted prefixes and consistent speed gains, while preserving task performance and yielding efficient, context-relevant use of visual information during generation.

Abstract: Speculative decoding accelerates autoregressive generation by letting a lightweight drafter propose multiple tokens that are verified by a larger target model. Although effective for text-only LLMs, speculative decoding yields limited gains in VLMs because drafters often diverge on vision-critical content, while existing multimodal acceleration methods do not directly address irrelevant visual evidence or optimize the verifier-accepted prefix length that governs speedup. We propose TIGER, a Text-conditioned vIsual GatEd Routing framework for multimodal speculative decoding. TIGER dynamically selects a sparse set of context-relevant visual tokens based on the drafter's current textual state, rather than expose the full visual token set or a fixed compressed interface. To better align training with inference-time efficiency, we optimize the drafter with acceptance-aligned group-based policy training using verifier-derived rewards based on accepted prefix length, built on top of distillation warm start with KL anchoring. This encourages the drafter not only to imitate the target model, but also to produce speculative continuations that survive verification for longer prefixes. Experiments show that TIGER yields consistent gains in accepted prefix length and speculative speedup under exact verifier-side speculative decoding, while achieving favorable quality-latency trade-offs with comparable downstream accuracy in visual-routing analyses.

### [53] [Unified Gradient Projection: Language-Balanced Continual Learning for Multilingual Low-Resource ASR](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11163)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors propose Unified Gradient Projection (UGP), which constrains parameter updates by projecting them into a unified space defined by reference gradients obtained via language-balanced replay; this projection equalizes per-language gradient contributions, reducing bias from dominant languages, and is combined with standard data-level replay to jointly improve stability and plasticity.

Result: UGP reduces dominant-language bias, improves cross-lingual stability, and, when combined with data-level replay, yields complementary gains in both stability and plasticity; experiments across multiple low-resource language groups and model scales show effective adaptation with substantially mitigated forgetting, reaching near-zero average forgetting on Whisper-large-v3.

Abstract: Large-scale pretrained ASR models such as Whisper exhibit strong multilingual capabilities. However, fine-tuning on low-resource languages often causes catastrophic forgetting. Although continual learning mitigates this issue, existing methods struggle to regulate cross-task interference in multilingual settings, where dominant languages bias optimization. We propose Unified Gradient Projection (UGP), which constrains parameter updates using reference gradients from language-balanced replay in a unified projection space. By equalizing per-language contributions in the projection, UGP reduces dominant-language bias and improves cross-lingual stability. We further show that combining gradient-level projection with data-level replay yields complementary gains in stability and plasticity. Across diverse low-resource language groups and model scales, UGP enables effective adaptation while substantially mitigating forgetting. Abstract: A thematic corpus is a collection of semantically coherent documents that collectively describe different aspects of a shared thematic event. ### [55] [Amplitude-Only FFN Intervention for Tool-Structured LLM Inference Method: Gated Evaluation Protocol, and Cross-Model Empirical Results](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11183)
*Sheng Xu,Boyuan Huang,Ke Jia,Jiadun Zhu,Zhen Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a way to intervene in large language model feed-forward networks at inference time to improve structured outputs (like tool calls and JSON) without retraining, using a new method called Amplitude Gating (AG).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Tool-using LLM agents often fail due to small formatting or function-call errors, even when their reasoning is correct. Retraining models or adding external scaffolding can be expensive or brittle. The authors want a lightweight, inference-time mechanism to improve structured outputs by directly modulating internal activations, without changing model weights or requiring full fine-tuning.

Method: They study interventions in SwiGLU feed-forward network (FFN) layers during inference. Starting from Orthogonal Residual Projection (ORP), which changes activation directions but proves too disruptive, they develop Amplitude Gating (AG), which keeps the learned FFN directions fixed and only scales activation magnitudes. They define a detailed taxonomy of intervention locations (P1/P2/P3 and branch-specific P1s/P2a/P2b) and design an evaluation protocol that separates oracle upper bounds from fixed and learned gate configurations, tracks performance per sample, and uses task-appropriate metrics for binary and partial-credit tasks. They test AG variants (entropy-based and Newton–Schulz-windowed) across multiple Qwen model families and task categories, especially tool and structure-heavy benchmarks (e.g., Hermes function calls and JSON mode).

Result: AG interventions are mildly beneficial overall and notably effective on tool-structured tasks. For Qwen3.5-9B, a learned, category-level AG improves tool/structured/agentic performance from 38.66% to 42.92% (+4.27 points), with Hermes function-call tasks gaining about +7.6 points. On Qwen3-8B, Hermes JSON mode improves by +11.36 points. Qwen2.5-7B shows substantial oracle headroom, but current learned gates cannot fully realize it, indicating that routing and configuration must be tuned per model and task category. Neither entropy-based nor Newton–Schulz-windowed AG is uniformly superior across settings.

Conclusion: Inference-time FFN-level interventions focused on amplitude modulation (AG) can safely and measurably improve LLM performance on tool and structured-output tasks without retraining model weights, though gains are modest and model-specific. Tool-structured inference emerges as the most promising early application of such techniques. However, robust deployment will require model- and category-specific routing strategies, better gate-learning methods to exploit available oracle headroom, and broader cross-model and online validation to ensure reliability and safety.

Abstract: Large language models increasingly operate as tool-using agents, where small format, argument, or function-call errors can invalidate otherwise plausible responses. We study inference-time feed-forward network (FFN) intervention for improving structured outputs without retraining model weights. Our project began with Orthogonal Residual Projection (ORP), a direction-changing repair attempt that revealed sensitive SwiGLU FFN intervention sites but often caused more harm than fixes. We therefore propose Amplitude Gating (AG), a non-destructive alternative that preserves pretrained FFN weight directions and modulates only activation magnitudes during generation. We define a fine-grained intervention system spanning P1/P2/P3 and branch-specific P1s/P2a/P2b sites, and introduce an evaluation protocol that separates combination-oracle headroom from fixed configurations and learned gates, enforces sample-level accounting, and uses task-aware metrics for binary and partial-credit datasets. Across Qwen3.5-9B, Qwen3-8B, and Qwen2.5-7B, AG is weakly positive in aggregate but strongest on tool-structured tasks. On Qwen3.5-9B, a category-level learned gate improves tool/structured/agentic performance from 38.66% to 42.92% (+4.27 percentage points), with Hermes function-call tasks reaching about +7.6 points. On Qwen3-8B, Hermes JSON mode improves by +11.36 points. Qwen2.5-7B retains oracle headroom but current learned gates fail to capture it, showing that deployment requires model- and category-specific routing. Comparisons of entropy AG with Newton-Schulz-windowed AG show that neither family is uniformly dominant. These results identify tool-structured inference as the most credible first target for safe FFN-level inference optimization, while prospective online validation and broader cross-model evaluation remain necessary.

### [56] [ProgramTab: Boosting Table Reasoning of LLMs via Programmatic Paradigm](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11207)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a need for a method that can both handle large tables and cope with noisy or weak structure while still enabling accurate reasoning.

Method: The authors introduce ProgramTab, a framework that uses large language models with in-context learning to generate Python programs for tabular data preprocessing. The LLM is prompted to write and execute Python code that cleans and restructures the table, extracts important rows and columns, and then produces an SQL representation or SQL queries over the processed table. This pipeline creates smaller, more relevant sub-tables and structured queries that are easier for the LLM to reason over, without depending on clean original table schemas.

Result: On multiple table reasoning datasets, ProgramTab achieves better performance than all compared LLM-based baselines. It is particularly effective on tasks involving large tables or noisy web tables, where prior methods typically show substantial performance drops. The experiments indicate improved accuracy on question answering over tables and robust handling of diverse table formats.

Conclusion: ProgramTab demonstrates that guiding LLMs to write and use Python code for table preprocessing and content extraction, followed by SQL generation, can substantially improve table-based reasoning, especially on large and unstructured tables. This program-based approach overcomes input length and structure limitations of prior LLM and text-to-SQL methods and represents a promising direction for robust, scalable table reasoning systems.

Abstract: Table-based reasoning with large language models (LLMs), which requires reasoning based on natural language questions and structured tabular data, has gained widespread attention. However, a series of issues still constrain the application of this task. The previous approaches suffered from significant performance degradation when faced with large tables due to the difficulty of long text modeling and the limitation of input length for LLMs. The text-to-SQL approach is used to efficiently extract key information from tables and generate smaller sub-tables. However, tabular data, especially web tables, often lack the necessary structure and consistency, making them unsuitable for performing mathematical logic operations using SQL queries. We propose the ProgramTab framework, which guides LLMs employing in-context learning to perform tabular data preprocessing with Python code, as well as the momentous contents extraction with row and column extraction and SQL generation. The experiment results on table reasoning datasets demonstrate that the ProgramTab framework effectively deals with table-based reasoning tasks and outperforms all LLM-based baselines.

### [57] [When the Target Domain Changes: AI-Mediated Construct Drift in High-Stakes English Language AssessmenW](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11213)
*Yi Gui*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper argues that as generative AI becomes integral to academic communication, high‑stakes English tests that assume unaided performance risk losing validity, and proposes “bounded AI mediation” (a controlled common AI assistant during tests) as a way to realign test constructs and score interpretations with AI-mediated language use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: High‑stakes English proficiency tests are used for consequential decisions based on the assumption that unaided performance reflects readiness for academic communication. However, real academic contexts are rapidly incorporating generative AI, so the traditional link between test scores and real‑world communicative ability is weakening. Existing research mostly treats AI as a practical issue (scoring, security, cheating) rather than a fundamental question about what language ability the test should measure. The paper is motivated by this gap: how to rethink validity and construct definitions for language tests when AI is routinely available in the target language use domain.

Method: The paper develops a conceptual validity argument rather than an empirical study. It synthesizes and organizes the current literature into three layers: (1) work that embeds AI into assessment infrastructure (automated scoring, feedback, security), (2) more limited work that questions how AI changes the construct of language proficiency, and (3) emerging discussions about extrapolating from test performance to AI-mediated communication. Through this literature-based argument, the author introduces two key concepts—AI-mediated construct drift and bounded AI mediation—and uses them to critique current test practices and suggest design principles.

Result: The conceptual analysis shows that most existing work positions AI as a tool that supports assessment operations, leaving underexplored how AI reshapes the communicative abilities actually needed in academic settings. The author identifies the risk of AI-mediated construct drift: as AI becomes embedded in communication, the construct targeted by unaided tests grows increasingly misaligned with real-world demands. The paper formulates the design principle of bounded AI mediation, specifying conditions under which a standardized, institutionally controlled AI assistant could be integrated into tests without undermining fairness or interpretability, by carefully limiting assistance and differentiating between support for understanding and generating responses.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that treating AI purely as an operational concern in language assessment is insufficient; it must be integrated into the core validity argument and construct definition. Unaided test performance can no longer be straightforwardly extrapolated to AI-mediated academic communication. To maintain meaningful score interpretations, high-stakes tests should (a) recognize and explicitly narrow what their scores claim to represent under unaided conditions, and (b) develop complementary designs using bounded AI mediation when inferences about AI-mediated communicative readiness are desired. This reframing positions AI as a central issue in test validity rather than a peripheral technical problem.

Abstract: High-stakes English proficiency tests treat standardized, unaided performance as evidence for score interpretations about academic English proficiency. This interpretation remains meaningful, but as target language use domains increasingly involve generative AI, the extrapolation from unaided test performance to academic communicative readiness becomes less self-evident. This conceptual validity argument reframes AI as a score-interpretation problem in high-stakes language testing, not only an operational issue of scoring, feedback, security, or misconduct. Synthesizing current literature in three uneven layers, the paper shows that most work treats AI as assessment infrastructure, while far less theorizes its implications for construct validity and extrapolation warrants. It defines AI-mediated construct drift as the misalignment that arises when communicative abilities required in the target domain change through AI mediation while test constructs remain anchored to an unaided-performance model. It proposes bounded AI mediation as a validity-oriented design principle: a standardized condition in which all test takers access the same institutionally controlled AI assistant, with predefined assistance boundaries, logged interactions, and tasks that distinguish comprehension support from answer generation. The paper argues that score interpretations should be narrowed and supplemented when used to support claims about AI-mediated academic communication.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing sign language translation models are typically trained for a single native sign–spoken language pair (e.g., ASL–English), which limits accessibility across different language communities. Multilingual SLT is desirable but current approaches struggle to learn a single model that both captures shared semantics across languages and preserves language-specific nuances, leading to cross-lingual conflicts and suboptimal performance.

Method: Q-BridgeNet introduces a two-sided approach. On the sign-language side, it learns discrete representations called Q-units using adaptive temporal segmentation and residual vector quantization: a shared base codebook captures language-agnostic semantic primitives, while per-language residual codebooks model language-specific signing variations. On the spoken-language side, a multilingual large language model is fine-tuned to consume and generate in this Q-unit space, leveraging its cross-lingual knowledge to build one unified SLT model for multiple sign–spoken language pairs.

Result: On three benchmark datasets—PHOENIX14T, How2Sign, and CSL-Daily—Q-BridgeNet outperforms previous methods on their respective native sign–spoken language pairs, establishing new state-of-the-art results. It also shows strong generalization ability when translating between non-native sign–spoken language combinations, indicating that the learned Q-unit space successfully captures reusable cross-lingual semantics while limiting interference between languages.

Conclusion: By discretizing sign-language video into shared and language-specific Q-units and aligning this representation with a multilingual LLM, Q-BridgeNet effectively mitigates cross-lingual conflicts in multilingual SLT. The framework delivers state-of-the-art performance on standard benchmarks and exhibits promising generalization to unseen language pairs, suggesting a scalable path toward more universal sign language translation systems.

Abstract: Most sign language translation (SLT) methods focus on isolated native sign-spoken pairs (e.g., American Sign Language - English). Extending language-specific SLT models to multilingual translation would improve accessibility by enabling communication across diverse sign and spoken language communities. However, existing multilingual SLT approaches still struggle to learn a unified model that minimizes cross-lingual conflicts while capturing shared cross-lingual semantics and preserving language-specific variations across different sign languages. Therefore, we propose Q-BridgeNet, a unified framework for multilingual SLT that jointly mitigates cross-lingual conflicts across both the sign language and spoken language sides. On the sign language side, Q-BridgeNet learns discrete Q-units via adaptive segmentation and residual vector quantization: a shared base codebook provides language-agnostic semantic primitives, while language-specific residual codebooks refine heterogeneous signing semantics. On the spoken language side, a multilingual LLM is fine-tuned to operate in the Q-unit space, leveraging cross-lingual priors to enable a unified SLT model. Experiments on PHOENIX14T, How2Sign, and CSL-Daily show that Q-BridgeNet effectively mitigates cross-lingual conflicts, achieving state-of-the-art performance on native sign-spoken pairs while also demonstrating strong generalization to non-native pairs. Our source code is publicly available at: https://github.com/FengLiQ/Q-BridgeNet

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to build an automated, domain-adaptable pipeline that reduces the effort of locating candidate issues while keeping humans in the loop for final judgment.

Method: They design a modular multi-agent pipeline called AI Textbook Auditor. It ingests textbook PDFs through either vision-based page rendering or PyMuPDF text extraction. Conclusion: AI Textbook Auditor can effectively serve as a triage tool for textbook quality assurance, automatically surfacing likely factual, technical, and grammatical problems while leaving final decisions to human experts. Its modular, domain-adaptable multi-agent design and dual ingestion modes make it suitable for diverse subject domains and languages, improving the efficiency of textbook auditing without replacing expert oversight.

Abstract: Ensuring the quality of educational materials requires more than standard proofreading: textbooks must be audited for factual accuracy, domain-specific technical correctness, and linguistic quality simultaneously -- a task that general-purpose grammar checkers cannot address. We present \textbf{AI Textbook Auditor}, a modular multi-agent pipeline for automated quality assurance of educational materials across subject domains. The system accepts a textbook PDF and produces a structured, human-reviewable report via two analysis tracks: a \textbf{Factual and Technical Track} in which an ensemble of specialized LLM agents detects factual inaccuracies, code errors, incorrect definitions, and conceptual inconsistencies, augmented with web search for humanities domains; and a \textbf{Grammar Track} operating PDF-natively to preserve diacritical encoding. A \textbf{Judge Agent} filters false positives using domain-specific rules before presenting findings to a human reviewer. The pipeline supports two ingestion modes -- vision-native page rendering and PyMuPDF text extraction -- and is domain-adaptable via custom prompts encoding subject-specific error taxonomies. We demonstrate the system on two Romanian upper-secondary textbooks: a CS textbook (56 technical findings across seven categories, with an expert-validated precision of 62.5\%) and a history and social sciences textbook (72 findings spanning factual errors, ideological bias, and grammar). The system is designed as a triage tool that reduces the manual effort of locating candidate issues, with human expert validation required before any editorial action.

Conclusion: Combining complementary word and subword embedding spaces with an attention-based GRU sequence model yields more robust and accurate hate speech detection for low-resource, code-mixed dialectal Arabic than standard baselines. Hate speech can incite discrimination, harassment, and violence against individuals or communities based on attributes such as ethnicity, religion, gender, nationality, or political affiliation. Consequently, automatic hate speech detection has become a major research topic in natural language processing (NLP) and an essential component of content moderation systems.
  This paper investigates automatic hate speech detection in the Algerian Arabic dialect (Darija) on social media. This task remains challenging because of the dialect's linguistic diversity, characterized by the coexistence of Arabic, French, and Arabizi (Arabic written using the Latin alphabet). We compare four categories of text classification approaches: (1) traditional machine learning models using TF-IDF features, (2) deep learning models based on recurrent neural networks, (3) Transformer-based language models, including DziriBERT and multilingual BERT, and (4) a novel hybrid architecture, FAD-SA-GRU, which combines semantic representations from DZ FastText, DZ AraVec, and DziriBERT through multi-embedding fusion, followed by a self-attention-enhanced GRU encoder.
  Experiments on an annotated dataset of Algerian Darija social media comments for binary hate speech classification show that FAD-SA-GRU outperforms all baselines, achieving 93.2% accuracy, 93.4% precision, 91.0% recall, 92.1% F1-score, and 97.0% ROC-AUC. Method: The authors propose RefineEvo, an evolutionary framework with two main components: (1) a Planner that dynamically schedules evolutionary operators and decides when to trigger refinement based on the current search state, and (2) a Reflector that extracts useful lessons from past search trajectories and stores them in a Bidirectional Experience Pool, capturing both positive strategies and negative pitfalls. This pool then conditions future generation, enabling planning-guided, experience-aware search that adapts tools and operators to the problem’s evolving complexity.

Conclusion: RefineEvo successfully transforms automatic heuristic design from a static, trial-and-error procedure into a planning-guided, experience-driven process. By dynamically scheduling operators and systematically capturing both successful and unsuccessful search patterns, it enhances autonomy, solution quality, and token efficiency in LLM-based heuristic design for combinatorial optimization.

Abstract: Automatic Heuristic Design (AHD) has emerged as a transformative approach for solving combinatorial optimization problems. While recent Large Language Model (LLM)-based methods have shown promise, they predominantly rely on fixed evolutionary operators and struggle to effectively accumulate and reuse historical search experience. This paper proposes RefineEvo, a novel evolutionary framework that transforms AHD from a static trial-and-error process into a planning-guided, experience-driven system. RefineEvo introduces a Planner to dynamically schedule evolutionary operators and trigger refinement based on the current search state, and a Reflector to distill valuable lessons into a Bidirectional Experience Pool containing both positive insights and negative pitfalls. This synergistic framework enables the system to adapt its search tools to the evolving complexity of the problem and leverage trajectory-aware, situation-conditioned insights to guide generation. Experiments on several classic combinatorial optimization benchmarks demonstrate that RefineEvo consistently outperforms strong baselines. In particular, RefineEvo delivers superior solution quality while improving token efficiency, enabling more efficient and autonomous heuristic design.

### [65] [Beyond Sally-Anne: Evaluating Theory of Mind in LLMs using Epistemic Schelling Points](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11363)
*Roberta Rocca,Sami Boukortt,Geoff Keeling,Winnie Street*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new interactive dialogue game (EAST) to more robustly test Theory of Mind in LLMs and finds that even strong models struggle with functional social reasoning and epistemic tracking.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing text-based Theory of Mind evaluations for LLMs (e.g., Sally-Anne style tasks) can be gamed through training data contamination and mostly assess static question-answer behavior rather than generalizable, functional ToM in interactive, naturalistic settings. The authors want an evaluation that better reflects real social reasoning demands, especially under different information asymmetries between agents.

Method: They design the Epistemic Asymmetry Schelling Task (EAST), a two-player dialogue coordination game. Two LLM agents must independently converge on the same semantic Schelling point (a focal choice) while operating under varying degrees of epistemic transparency—i.e., differing what-each-agent-knows-about-what-the-other-knows. They run multiple LLM-LLM dyads on EAST, compare performance across model scales and families, and analyze generated reasoning traces to identify where and why coordination fails, focusing on epistemic tracking errors.

Result: Only the strongest, frontier models manage to consistently coordinate under the more demanding epistemic conditions. Many models that do well on conventional ToM benchmarks nonetheless fail to coordinate reliably in EAST. Error analysis shows that failures largely stem from incorrect epistemic tracking—for example, mixing up private knowledge with mutual or common knowledge, or incorrectly assuming shared information—rather than from general language or reasoning deficits.

Conclusion: Conventional ToM benchmarks overestimate LLMs’ practical social reasoning abilities. Robust, interactive Theory of Mind—especially accurate tracking of who knows what and what is mutually known—remains a key weakness even for high-performing models. EAST offers a more realistic and discriminative benchmark and highlights epistemic tracking as a concrete target for future model training and evaluation strategies.

Abstract: Text-based evaluations of Theory of Mind (ToM) in Large Language Models (LLMs) often involve cognitive tests akin to the Sally-Anne task that can be gamed due to exposure to relevantly similar tasks in pre-training and do not obviously test models' functional ToM abilities in ways that generalize to naturalistic settings. To address these issues, we introduce the Epistemic Asymmetry Schelling Task (EAST), a two-player dialogue game designed to benchmark robust and generalizable ToM abilities. By requiring LLM-LLM dyads to independently converge on semantic Schelling points under varying states of epistemic transparency, we evaluate whether models can robustly apply ToM to achieve coordination. Our results reveal a significant capability gap in functional social reasoning, with only frontier models successfully navigating the varying epistemic demands of the tasks. Analysis of reasoning traces shows that coordination failures are primarily driven by epistemic tracking errors, such as conflating private knowledge with mutual knowledge. Despite high performance on traditional static benchmarks, our study shows that robust social reasoning and epistemic tracking remain a critical bottleneck, providing concrete targets for future LLM evaluation and development.

### [66] [Agentic Routing: The Harness-Native Data Flywheel](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11399)
*Xinchen Liu,Hang Zhou,Yingjie Zong,Yuchuan Tian,Liuyang Song,Shuo Zhang,Yulong Li,Wei He,Mengyu Zheng,Runke Liu,Siyang Cheng,Xiang Kuang,Hailin Hu,Kai Han,Yunhe Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes harness-native agentic routing: a step-level approach to selecting LLMs inside agents using full execution state, turning each routing decision into labeled data for better future routing and model training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLM agents rely on complex execution harnesses and different models specialize in different skills (code, tools, math, latency, etc.), choosing which model(s) to use at each agent step becomes a systems problem. Existing routing methods focus on single-turn cost-quality tradeoffs and ignore multi-step execution state, intermediate failures, and feedback loops, limiting agent performance and efficiency.

Method: The authors introduce harness-native agentic routing that operates at the step level within an agent’s harness, conditioning routing decisions on full harness state. Each decision records a structured trace: query, harness state, chosen model(s), execution trace, outcome, and cost. These records form a ‘data flywheel’ used to train better routers and harness-native models over time. They implement this in OpenSquilla with a four-layer routing stack, an open LightGBM cold-start ranker, and a staged router-model pipeline that converts logged arena data into increasingly strong routing policies. They support both singleton routing (one best-fit model) and multi-model routing (ensembles) per step.

Result: Using the OpenSquilla instantiation, they evaluate both single-model and multi-model routing on agentic benchmarks like DRACO and PinchBench. The system demonstrates improved cost-effectiveness and accuracy by exploiting specialized models and ensemble routing, and shows that exploiting harness state and execution traces yields stronger routing policies than single-turn routers.

Conclusion: Agentic routing should be viewed not just as a way to control inference cost, but as a central data engine for training agent-native routers and models. By treating each harness step as a labeled data point, harness-native routing creates a flywheel where better traces lead to better routing and models, which in turn generate better traces, improving both performance and efficiency for LLM agents.

Abstract: Large language model agents are increasingly executed not by a single model call, but by an execution harness that manages observation, context, control, action, state, and verification. At the same time, frontier and open models are becoming structurally specialized: a model that is strong at code editing, long-context recovery, tool use, mathematical reasoning, or low-latency response may not dominate on the other axes. This makes model selection inside an agent a core systems problem rather than a per-query serving trick. Existing routing methods mostly optimize single-turn cost-quality trade-offs and therefore miss the execution state, intermediate failures, and feedback loops that make agents different from chat completion. We propose Harness-Native agentic routing, a step-level routing paradigm that selects either a single best-fit model for cost-effective execution or multiple complementary models for ensemble-style accuracy improvement, conditioned on the full harness state. The key insight is that every routing decision naturally produces a structured data record -- consisting of the query, harness state, model choice or model set, execution trace, outcome, and cost -- whose labels are supplied by the environment rather than by the router itself. These records form a harness-native data flywheel: execution traces train better routers and harness-native models, which improve cost-quality trade-offs and generate more traces under the same budget. We instantiate this idea in OpenSquilla with a four-layer routing stack, an open LightGBM cold-start ranker, and a staged router-model path that turns logged arena records into progressively stronger routing policies. The report studies singleton and multi-model routing on agentic benchmarks including DRACO and PinchBench, and argues that agentic routing is not merely cost control, but a data engine for agent-native training.

### [67] [Cross-Architecture LLM Ensembles, Feature-Based Reranking and Retrieval-Augmented Prompting for Legal Information Processing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11400)
*Amal Saad Alshehri,Nelly Bencomo,Amir Atapour-Abarghouei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper reports Team DU’s systems and results across all five COLIEE 2026 legal information processing tasks, showing that task-specific architectures, prompting, and ensembling of open-weight models can outperform many proprietary and official baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Legal NLP tasks like case retrieval, entailment, statute interpretation, and judgment prediction require precise text matching, complex reasoning, and robust generalisation, often under limited supervision and competition rules restricting training data and model dates. The authors aim to explore how far open-weight models and systematic engineering (ensembles, prompting, feature-based ranking) can go under these constraints, and to understand which inductive biases work best for different legal tasks.

Method: The team builds separate but related systems for each COLIEE 2026 task, largely using open-weight language models. For statute entailment (Task 4), they construct a cross-architecture ensemble of nine models from three model families. For the Pilot Task on tort prediction and rationale extraction, they design a multi-view pipeline: five distinct claim-level models generate predictions that are then combined, and verdicts are refined using features derived from per-claim outputs. For legal case entailment (Task 2), they systematically vary only the prompting format (single- vs multi-selection) while keeping the underlying model fixed. For statute retrieval and entailment (Task 3), they swap in a larger open-weight model (Qwen3-235B) with a structured legal reasoning prompt specifically crafted for the domain. For case retrieval (Task 1), they build a learning-to-rank framework that fuses lexical and semantic retrieval scores with 34 handcrafted features capturing structure, citation authority, and temporal aspects, then rank cases accordingly. Across tasks, they compare competition submissions with post-competition ablations and upgrades to isolate the effect of each design choice.

Result: Task 4 (statute entailment): the nine-model, three-family ensemble obtains 96.3% accuracy and ranks first among 33 submissions from 11 teams. Pilot Task (tort prediction and rationale extraction): their multi-view system yields 73.1% accuracy on tort prediction and 68.2% F1 on rationale extraction as an unofficial run, outperforming all official entries on prediction and matching the top F1 for rationales. Task 2 (case entailment): simply changing the prompt to a multi-selection format increases F1 from 0.343 to 0.555 on released gold labels, surpassing the best official submission (F1 = 0.490). Task 3 (statute retrieval and entailment): switching to Qwen3-235B plus a structured reasoning prompt lifts accuracy from 79.3% to 91.5% in post-hoc analysis. Task 1 (case retrieval): the learning-to-rank system achieves F1 = 0.314, ranking 11th among 54 submissions from 22 teams. Overall, they show competitive to state-of-the-art or better performance on several tasks using only open-weight models and careful system design.

Conclusion: Different legal NLP tasks benefit from different inductive biases and system designs: cross-architecture ensembling is highly effective for statute entailment; multi-view and feature-based refinement help in complex prediction tasks like tort liability and rationale extraction; prompt design has a surprisingly large impact on case entailment; and large open-weight models with structured reasoning prompts substantially improve statute-related tasks. For retrieval, combining lexical and neural signals with rich legal features via learning-to-rank yields solid performance. The overarching conclusion is that, even under competition constraints and without proprietary models, careful use of open-weight systems, ensembling, retrieval-augmented prompting, and feature-based reranking can deliver top-tier results across diverse legal information processing tasks, and that system architecture must be tailored to each task’s specific reasoning and matching needs.

Abstract: Legal information processing spans retrieval, entailment and judgment prediction problems, requiring text matching, reasoning and robust generalisation with limited supervision. We report Team DU's participation in all five tasks of COLIEE 2026, using open-weight systems for legal case retrieval, case entailment, statute retrieval and entailment, and legal judgment prediction. For Tasks 3 and 4, all models predate the 15 July 2025 cutoff required by the rules. For Task 4 (statute entailment), a cross-architecture ensemble of nine models from three families achieves 96.3% accuracy, placing first among 33 submissions from 11 teams. For the Pilot Task (tort prediction and rationale extraction), a multi-view system combining five claim-level models and refining the verdict using features derived from the claim predictions achieves 73.1% TP accuracy and 68.2% RE F1 as an unofficial submission, scoring above all official entries on TP and matching the highest on RE. For Task 2 (legal case entailment), changing only the prompt from single- to multi-selection raises F1 from 0.343 to 0.555 in post-competition evaluation on released gold labels, exceeding the best official submission (F1 = 0.490). For Task 3 (statute retrieval and entailment), replacing the entailment model with Qwen3-235B and a structured legal reasoning prompt raises accuracy from 79.3% to 91.5% in post-competition analysis. For Task 1 (legal case retrieval), a learning-to-rank system combining lexical and semantic retrieval with structural, citation authority, and temporal features (34 in total) achieves F1 = 0.314 (rank 11 of 54 submissions from 22 teams). Overall, legal information processing benefits from different inductive biases across tasks, with cross-architecture ensembling, feature-based reranking and retrieval-augmented prompting each proving most effective in different settings.

### [68] [Confidently Wrong: Detecting Hallucinations in Financial Question Answering from LLM Internal States](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11414)
*Richard Zhe Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how to detect confident hallucinations of large language models in financial QA tasks by probing internal activations, and shows that simple linear probes outperform standard confidence baselines for identifying confidently wrong answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In high‑stakes financial applications, the most dangerous LLM failures are confidently wrong answers that can quietly corrupt downstream decisions. Existing confidence measures based on output probabilities or self-assessment are unreliable, especially when the model appears behaviorally confident. The authors aim to understand whether internal model activations contain additional, exploitable information to detect such confident hallucinations and support safer deployment of LLMs in finance.

Method: The authors use linear probes trained on the residual stream activations of several LLMs (Qwen3‑8B, Llama‑3.1‑8B, Gemma‑2‑9B) while the models answer questions from two financial QA benchmarks (FinQA and TAT‑QA) derived from real filings. They define behavioral confidence via agreement across eight resampled answers per question. Probes are trained to distinguish correct from hallucinated answers using internal activations, and their performance (AUROC) is compared against baselines such as token log‑probabilities and the model’s own True/False self-evaluation of its answer.

Result: On FinQA, even among maximally behaviorally confident answers (all eight resamples agree), 15–23% are still wrong, showing a substantial confident hallucination rate. Linear probes applied to the residual stream achieve AUROC of 0.68–0.77 in detecting these hallucinations, while standard baselines including log‑probabilities and self-assessment only reach 0.55–0.63. This performance gap holds consistently across the evaluated models. Similar evaluations are carried out on TAT‑QA, though exact numbers are not detailed in the abstract.

Conclusion: Internal activations of LLMs encode useful signals about the correctness of their answers that are not fully captured by external confidence measures. Simple linear probes can exploit these signals to more reliably detect confident hallucinations than typical baselines. Such probing approaches offer a practical, cost‑effective triage mechanism to flag high‑risk answers for human review in financial decision-making workflows, improving the safety and reliability of LLM-based systems.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) in financial applications fail most consequentially when they are confidently wrong. Hedged, uncertain answers invite scrutiny, whereas confident errors silently degrade downstream decisions without warning. We ask how reliably such confidently wrong answers, or confident hallucinations, can be detected from a model's internal activations, and whether those activations carry information beyond its observable outputs. We train linear probes on the residual stream and evaluate them on two established question-answering (QA) benchmarks built from real filings, FinQA and TAT-QA. Behavioral confidence is measured as the agreement among eight resampled answers to the same question, and probe effectiveness is compared against baselines, such as token log-probabilities and the model's own True/False self-assessment of its answer. Our findings show that among confident answers, those for which all eight resamples agree, 15-23% are wrong on FinQA. There the probes have a significant advantage over baseline methods in detecting hallucinations, holding 0.68-0.77 AUROC while the best baselines fall to 0.55-0.63, across Qwen3-8B, Llama-3.1-8B, and Gemma-2-9B. Our results suggest that probing can be a cost-effective triage mechanism for routing LLM answers to human review and quality control procedures in high-stakes financial applications.
