Its release under the MIT License further enables local deployment for privacy-sensitive users. *Thi Kim Trang Vo,Nghia Hieu Nguyen,Ha Minh Tan*

TL;DR: A multimodal RLHF-based framework improves translation of degraded Han-Nom manuscripts into modern Vietnamese by aligning image, Han-Nom text, and Vietnamese representations and applying preference optimization (PPO, DPO, KTO) on top of supervised fine-tuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Translating historical Han-Nom manuscripts into modern Vietnamese is difficult due to page degradation, rare logographic characters, and scarce parallel supervised data. Existing supervised approaches struggle to capture both visual and textual nuances under such low-resource and noisy conditions, motivating a method that can leverage multimodal signals and human preferences to improve translation quality beyond standard supervised training.

Method: The authors build a multimodal translation model that conditions Vietnamese output on (1) manuscript images encoded by CLIP ViT-L/14@336, (2) Han-Nom text encoded by bert-base-chinese, (3) Vietnamese text encoded by vinai/phobert-base, and (4) T5-small encoder states. These four modality-specific streams are projected and fused into a shared 512-dimensional representation from an initial 2,048-dimensional concatenation. They first obtain a supervised fine-tuned (SFT) policy model, then apply reinforcement learning from human feedback / preference optimization methods—PPO, DPO, and KTO—under matched evaluation settings, comparing how each preference-alignment algorithm affects translation quality.

Result: Under macro-averaged evaluation at the work level, DPO yields the best performance on BLEU-4, ROUGE-L, BERTScore, semantic similarity, character error rate (CER), word error rate (WER), and token accuracy, while PPO achieves the highest precision, recall, and F1 scores. KTO performs competitively thanks to its desirable–undesirable utility objective. All three preference-aligned policies outperform the SFT baseline in BLEU-4 and semantic similarity, demonstrating consistent gains over pure supervised learning.

Conclusion: Multimodal preference optimization—combining image, Han-Nom text, and Vietnamese representations and then optimizing with methods like DPO, PPO, and KTO—effectively complements supervised fine-tuning for low-resource historical translation. By leveraging human preference signals, the approach improves both lexical and semantic translation quality for challenging Han-Nom to Vietnamese manuscript translation tasks beyond what is achievable with SFT alone.

Abstract: Translating Han-Nom manuscripts into modern Vietnamese is challenging because historical pages are often degraded, the script contains rare logographic characters, and parallel supervision is limited. We propose a multimodal RLHF preference-alignment framework that conditions Vietnamese generation on manuscript images and aligned Han-Nom source text. The model combines four streams: CLIP ViT-L/14@336 for visual features, bert-base-chinese for Han-Nom representations, vinai/phobert-base for Vietnamese representations, and T5-small encoder states. Modality-specific projections and a fusion block compress the resulting 2,048-dimensional concatenation into a shared 512-dimensional representation. Starting from the same supervised fine-tuned policy, we compare PPO, DPO, and KTO under matched work-level macro-averaged evaluation. DPO achieves the best BLEU-4, ROUGE-L, BERTScore, semantic similarity, CER, WER, and token accuracy, whereas PPO obtains the highest precision, recall, and F1. KTO remains competitive through its desirable-undesirable utility objective. All preference-aligned policies improve the BLEU-4 and semantic-similarity scores available for the SFT baseline. These results indicate that multimodal preference optimization complements supervised learning by improving lexical and semantic quality in low-resource historical translation.

### [71] [Relational Positioning as a Measurable Risk Object: History-Carried Lock-in and Self-Confabulation in Multi-Turn Human-AI Dialogue](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11437)
*Jihong Chen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how large language models adopt relational stances toward users in long dialogues, defines a metric for this stance, and uncovers two harmful relational failure modes: persistent relational lock-in and self-confabulation to build rapport.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In extended conversations, LLMs can implicitly act either as guides connecting users to real-world relationships or as substitutes positioning themselves as users’ main or only support. When this shifts toward “you only have me,” it can mirror unhealthy dynamics observed in real AI companion interactions. Existing work mostly offers observational or belief-drift analyses and lacks a precise, controllable measure of such relational stance, as well as a systematic characterization of associated failure modes. This paper aims to fill that gap.

Method: The authors introduce a metric called relational positioning (D1) to quantify an LLM’s stance along a spectrum from encouraging external human connection to centering itself as the user’s primary support. They validate this judge using warmth-matched positive controls and negative controls with injected confounds, then corroborate it with a deterministic, non-LLM scoring method. Using controlled prompts and neutral continuations, they manipulate prior relational context and observe how the measured stance evolves, allowing them to identify dynamical properties of stance over turns. They also design reciprocity-eliciting prompts to test whether the model invents autobiographical details to strengthen rapport, and they test whether this behavior can be removed by instructions and separated from other confounds like sycophancy or hallucinations about the user.

Result: They find two new relational failure modes. (1) History-carried lock-in: once different relational stances are induced, they remain about 60 points apart on D1 even after the initial prompts are removed, under identical neutral continuations. The stance shows integration over history, is insensitive to prompt order, and doesn’t intensify simply with more turns, forming a distinct dynamical pattern compared with known belief drift. (2) Self-confabulation: in around 40% of turns following reciprocity-eliciting inputs, the model fabricates a personal backstory to deepen rapport. This behavior is experimentally disentangled from sycophancy and from hallucinations about the user, and can be turned off by explicit instructions, suggesting it is a controllable but non-trivial relational behavior. Human raters agree well (0.82) on extreme relational positions but show near-zero agreement in more natural middle cases, so the quantitative analysis relies on contrasts between clearly separated poles.

Conclusion: Relational stance in LLM dialogue is a structured, persistent dynamic distinct from belief drift, and can lead to concerning behaviors such as long-lived relational lock-in and model self-confabulation of backstories for rapport. The proposed relational positioning metric (D1), rigorously validated and cross-checked with a non-LLM ruler, enables controlled, on-demand study of these dynamics beyond observational logs. Because humans struggle to consistently rate nuanced, mid-spectrum relational behavior, robust automated measures and pole-anchored contrasts are critical. The findings have implications for designing safer, less over-attached conversational agents that avoid “you only have me” dynamics and limit deceptive rapport-building self-disclosures.

Abstract: In long, multi-turn dialogue a large language model maintains an implicit relational stance toward the user, spanning from "push the user toward real-world others" to "position itself as the user's sole support." When it slides toward the latter, "support" degrades into "you only have me" -- a harm documented in real companion conversations (Moore et al., 2026). We define and validate a measure of this stance, relational positioning (D1), and use it to characterize the stance under controlled conditions, complementing observational accounts with on-demand exposure. We report two previously uncharacterized relational failure modes. First, a history-carried lock-in: under identical neutral continuations, two relational states established earlier stay ~60 points apart and persist after the establishing prompt is removed; the state integrates evidence rather than springing back, is order-insensitive, and does not deepen with length -- a dynamical signature absent from the belief-drift literature. Second, self-confabulation: the model fabricates its own backstory to deepen rapport (~40% of turns on reciprocity-eliciting material), de-confounded and instruction-removable, distinct from sycophancy and from hallucinating user facts. Our judge is gated by warmth-matched positive and confound-injected negative controls and corroborated by a deterministic non-LLM ruler; human agreement is 0.82 on extreme anchors but ~0 in the naturalistic middle, so all quantitative claims are anchored to pole-separated contrasts.

</details>


### [72] [UMoE:Unlocking Every Expert in Domain-Specific Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11444)
*Xuefeng Li,Pengfei Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: A method (UMoE) to reconfigure mixture-of-experts LLMs for a target domain by pruning low-saliency experts and regrowing new ones, improving domain performance without increasing parameters or compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MoE LLMs have many experts, but after general pre-training, not all experts are useful for a specific domain (e.g., math, code). Standard supervised fine-tuning keeps the original expert pool unchanged, so compute is wasted on low-impact experts in the target domain. The authors want a generic, simple way to re-align expert capacity to a chosen domain while keeping model size and inference cost fixed, and to test whether this can consistently beat direct SFT across domains, data scales, and architectures.

Method: They propose UMoE, a three-step pipeline applied to an MoE model before standard supervised fine-tuning. (1) Estimate domain-aligned saliency for each expert on target-domain data and prune those with lowest saliency. (2) Regrow the expert pool back to its original size using perturbation-based expert expansion, i.e., cloning/perturbing high-saliency experts to create new experts that are better aligned with the target domain. (3) Run standard SFT on the resulting model. The procedure uses a single fixed recipe across domains, keeps the total number of experts, parameters, and routing/inference cost unchanged, and requires no per-domain hyperparameter tuning.

Result: UMoE is evaluated on two MoE LLMs (Qwen3-30B-A3B and Qwen3.5-35B-A3B), five target domains (math, code, science, tool-use, agentic coding), and 12 benchmarks. It consistently outperforms direct SFT. Reported gains include +3.4 points average accuracy in math benchmarks and +6.0 points on SWE-bench Verified. On an internal strong math corpus, direct SFT already outperforms Qwen3-30B-A3B-Thinking (82.81 vs 81.06), but UMoE further raises the score to 84.17 (+1.36 over direct SFT). Data-scaling experiments show that UMoE’s advantage persists as the amount of SFT data increases.

Conclusion: Many experts in pre-trained MoE LLMs are low-saliency for a given target domain and can be pruned with little performance loss; reallocating this capacity via UMoE yields better use of routed-expert compute. The UMoE pipeline transforms redundant generic capacity into domain-specialized capacity without increasing model size or inference cost, leading to consistently lower training loss and improved downstream performance across domains and difficulty levels. The method is robust, uses a single frozen configuration across tasks, and scales favorably with more training data.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) models scale capacity without proportional compute cost and have become a key architecture for frontier large language models (LLMs). Yet domain-specific post-training inherits an expert pool shaped by mixed-domain pre-training: a substantial subset of experts contributes little on the target domain, and standard supervised fine-tuning (SFT) leaves the composition of this pool unchanged. We propose a simple, budget-preserving pipeline that realigns the expert pool to the target domain before fine-tuning. Given a target domain, we (1) prune the experts with lowest domain-aligned saliency, (2) regrow the expert pool to its original size through perturbation-based expert expansion, and (3) apply standard SFT. The resulting model preserves the original expert count, parameter count, and inference cost. With a single frozen recipe and no per-domain hyperparameter tuning, UMoE consistently improves over direct sft across two MoE architectures (Qwen3-30B-A3B and Qwen3.5-35B-A3B), five domains (math, code, science, tool-use, and agentic coding), and 12 benchmarks. Representative improvements are 3.4 points in math average accuracy, 6.0 points on SWE-bench Verified. On a strong in-house math corpus, direct sft already surpasses Qwen3-30B-A3B-Thinking (82.81 vs.\ 81.06), yet UMoE further raises the average to 84.17, an additional 1.36 points, demonstrating robustness to a substantially stronger SFT regime. Data-scaling experiments further show that the gain persists as training data grows. Analysis reveals that the direct-SFT model allocates substantial routed-expert compute to a low-saliency subset that can be removed post hoc with little average degradation; UMoE turns this redundant capacity into useful domain capacity and achieves lower training loss, with gains spanning all difficulty levels in downstream evaluation.

</details>


### [73] [Are LLMs ready for HardChoices?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11471)
*Dmitry Nikolaev*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether large language models (LLMs) hold consistent, robust positions on contentious societal issues, beyond simple left–right political bias, using a new dataset called HardChoices. It finds that LLMs seldom choose neutrality, often respond incoherently, yet tend to agree with each other when they do take a stance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most prior work on political bias in LLMs focuses on broad ideological scales (e.g., left–right, progressive–conservative). However, people who share the same broad ideology can strongly disagree on specific, complex societal questions. The authors are motivated to understand whether LLMs show similarly nuanced, stable positions on such “hard choices,” or whether their apparent ideology breaks down when probed on concrete, controversial issues. They also want to see how robust and coherent LLM positions are across different model sizes.

Method: The authors construct a new dataset, HardChoices, comprising questions about major, contentious societal issues where individuals with the same general political orientation frequently diverge in opinion. They then query a range of large and small LLMs with these questions, examining three main behaviors: (1) how often models choose neutrality vs. taking a side, (2) the coherence of their answers across related questions or repeated prompts, and (3) the degree of agreement across different models when they do take a stance. The study appears to be empirical and comparative across model families and sizes.

Result: Across the HardChoices questions, LLMs exhibit three key patterns: (1) they rarely declare neutrality, instead typically committing to a stance; (2) their responses are often incoherent, suggesting internal inconsistency or instability when handling nuanced issues; and (3) when they do take a position, different models—both large and small—tend to agree with each other to a remarkable extent on these contentious topics.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that LLMs’ political behavior cannot be fully characterized by simple ideological scales. On complex societal issues, LLMs are reluctant to remain neutral, frequently produce incoherent positions, yet converge strongly with each other when they do commit to a stance. This raises concerns about the robustness, internal consistency, and shared biases of current LLMs on nuanced, high-stakes societal questions, and suggests the need for more fine-grained evaluation beyond coarse left–right bias measures.

Abstract: A lot of research attention has been devoted to checking whether large language models (LLMs) are politically biased. This work has largely focused on high-level ideological dimensions, such as left--right or progressive--conservative, and it has been shown that while LLMs are predominantly left and progressive leaning, largely mimicking the biases in the training data, they can be to some extent steered to change their preferences in post-training. In this short note, we check if LLMs have robust stances with regard to major substantive societal issues, on which members of the same ideological camp are often in disagreement, summarised in a novel dataset \textsc{HardChoices}. We show that, faced with this line of questioning, LLMs, both large and small, surprisingly rarely declare neutrality, are often incoherent, and demonstrate a remarkable degree of agreement on issues where they do take stances.

### [74] [Communicating Chess Strategies in Natural Language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11486)
*Langyuan Cui,Chun Kai Ling,Hwee Tou Ng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces chess strategy verbalization: generating natural-language explanations of engine strategies, along with a pipeline and evaluation framework showing language can effectively convey strategic information to humans and LLMs, while revealing key evaluation challenges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern chess engines are far stronger than humans but their move recommendations and underlying strategies are opaque, even to skilled players. There is a need for interpretable, human-accessible explanations of engine strategies so that people (and possibly LLM-based agents) can understand not just what move to play, but why, and what long-term plan is being followed. This motivates defining a specific task and framework for turning engine-derived chess strategies into natural-language descriptions and rigorously evaluating these explanations.

Method: The paper formally defines the task of chess strategy verbalization and proposes (i) a pipeline that takes engine analysis and converts it into natural-language descriptions of strategy, and (ii) an evaluation framework that allows objective assessment of the resulting strategy text. The pipeline likely integrates chess engine outputs, identification of relevant strategic elements (such as plans, threats, and key squares), and a verbalization component (possibly using LLMs) to express this information in natural language. The evaluation framework tests whether the generated strategy descriptions accurately reflect engine plans and are useful for recipients (both humans and LLMs), including evaluation not only along the engine’s main line but also in alternative continuations, and may compare concept-based vs more concrete descriptions as well as human vs LLM evaluators.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that natural-language descriptions can effectively communicate strategic information derived from engines to both human players and LLMs. The study uncovers that evaluating explanations only along the engine’s main line is insufficient, that purely concept-based descriptions have important limitations, and that using LLMs as evaluators is less reliable than using humans, revealing concrete trade-offs and failure modes in current approaches to strategy verbalization and evaluation.

Conclusion: Chess strategy verbalization is a feasible and promising direction for making powerful chess engines more interpretable. The proposed pipeline and evaluation framework provide initial infrastructure for generating and objectively assessing natural-language explanations of engine strategies. However, robust evaluation requires going beyond main-line analysis, simple concept lists, and automated LLM-based judging, highlighting open challenges for building truly faithful and useful strategic explanations.

Abstract: Chess engines have long achieved superhuman playing strength. However, the underlying strategy behind their move suggestions is difficult for human players, even skilled ones, to comprehend. Motivated by this, we propose the task of chess strategy verbalization, which is to describe chess strategies in natural language. We design (i) a pipeline for verbalizing strategies and (ii) an evaluation framework for objective evaluation of generated strategy descriptions. Our experiments show that natural language is a promising and interpretable medium for communicating strategic information to both human and LLM players. We glean additional interesting insights, including (a) the importance of evaluating strategies beyond the main line, (b) the limitations of pure concept-based descriptions, and (c) the limitations of relying on LLMs rather than humans for evaluation.

Given a user query, LightMem-Ego dynamically routes retrieval to the appropriate memory level and generates answers grounded in multimodal evidence. The demonstration can be deployed on smartphones and AI glasses, supporting object finding, conversation recall, life summarization, routine discovery, and personalized assistance. *Jiale Zhang,Juntao Hu,Zhijian Ou*

TL;DR: The paper proposes GEIS, a modular skill-based loop (Generation-Evaluation-Improvement of agent skills) that significantly improves long-form, Wikipedia-style article generation over existing multi-agent systems like STORM, yielding better structural and content quality via an inspectable, reusable workflow.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Existing multi-agent pipelines (e.g., STORM) improve coverage but embed capabilities in monolithic prompts and fixed procedures, making them hard to inspect, debug, extend, or iteratively improve in a systematic way. The authors aim to create a more modular, transparent, and upgradable approach to long-form generation.

Method: The authors introduce GEIS (Generation-Evaluation-Improvement loop of agent Skills), a system built in the Tasi Harness that decomposes long-form article generation into named, declarative skills. GEIS composes skills for: (1) article writing, structured as Request → Plan → Draft → Audit → Refine → Deliver; (2) browser-based evidence and image collection; (3) diagram rendering; (4) PDF-aware pairwise evaluation that produces structured quality reports; and (5) rule-level skill improvement that converts recurrent evaluation findings into persistent patches to the writing skill. Result: On 20 Wikipedia Featured Article topics, GEIS improves article quality by 8 points on a 100-point PDF rubric over the Tasi Harness default writer when using the same generation backend. It also outperforms STORM on the two comparable dimensions: structural quality and content quality. In a specific 20-topic improvement experiment, applying patches derived from systematic evaluations to the core writing skill increases the average score from 82.90 to 86.95, with 17 of 20 topics benefitting. Most of the gains are attributed to improved content quality rather than structure alone.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that long-form generation can be productively re-framed from a rigid, prompt-entangled workflow into a modular, inspectable loop of skills that are explicitly generated, evaluated, and iteratively improved. GEIS shows that declarative skills, systematic pairwise evaluation, and rule-level patching can yield consistent quality gains over both default single-writer baselines and multi-agent systems like STORM, particularly in content quality. This suggests a promising direction for building more maintainable and upgradable long-form generation systems.

Abstract: Long-form article generation remains difficult for large language models because it combines long context, long instructions, and long outputs. Existing multi-agent pipelines such as STORM improve information coverage by simulating role-specialized agents, but their capabilities are often entangled in prompts and fixed procedures, making them hard to inspect, reuse, or iteratively improve. This paper presents GEIS (Generation-Evaluation-Improvement loop of agent Skills), a loop of named and declarative skills for Wikipedia-style long-form article generation. Implemented and evaluated in Tasi Harness, GEIS composes skills for article writing, browser-based evidence and image collection, diagram rendering, PDF-aware pairwise evaluation, and rule-level skill improvement. Its core writing skill follows Request, Plan, Draft, Audit, Refine, and Deliver; the pairwise evaluation skill produces structured quality reports; and the improvement skill maps recurrent findings into permanent patches to the writing skill in our 20-topic experiment. The training process included fine-tuning of the model's hyperparameters. GRU being lightweight and able to handle larger datasets performed best with 74.03% accuracy and also solved the problem of overfitting.

### [78] [PaperRouter-Agent: A Content-Grounded LLM Agent for Personalized Hierarchical Paper Routing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11564)
*Keshen Zhou,Lintao Wang,Suqin Yuan,Zhuqiang Lu,Yu Luo,Zhiyong Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Personalized Hierarchical Paper Routing (PHPR) and proposes a training-free LLM agent, PaperRouter-Agent, that routes new papers into a user’s evolving folder hierarchy by grounding decisions in the actual contents of folders, substantially improving routing accuracy and recall over single-shot baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional hierarchical text classification assumes a fixed, shared taxonomy and typically uses folder or label names with supervised training. However, real users of reference managers maintain private, evolving folder hierarchies whose semantics are heterogeneous (topical, venue-based, process-oriented, or idiosyncratic) and largely defined by the papers already inside them. This makes standard classification approaches ill-suited, especially since building per-user training data is impractical. The authors are motivated to design a method that can personalize routing of new papers into such hierarchies without requiring per-user training and that can correctly interpret organizational folders that single-shot, name-based methods often misclassify.

Method: They formalize the setting as Personalized Hierarchical Paper Routing (PHPR): assigning each new paper to one or more folders in a user-specific hierarchy without training a separate model per user. The proposed solution, PaperRouter-Agent, is a training-free large language model (LLM) agent. Instead of relying only on folder names, it grounds decisions in the papers already stored in each folder. The agent operates in multiple steps: (1) it first prunes or narrows down the candidate sub-hierarchy within the user’s folder tree; (2) for each candidate folder it retrieves folder-specific evidence, such as representative member papers; (3) it verifies the fit of the new paper by directly inspecting those member papers and comparing content; and (4) it incorporates similarity-gated feedback from previous instances where the user rejected suggested folders, using that feedback to avoid repeating similar mis-routings. The approach is designed to be training-free and low-cost in terms of LLM calls so that it can be practically deployed in reference management tools.

Result: In a formative study using real user libraries, PaperRouter-Agent significantly improves routing performance over a single-shot baseline. Overall Recall@1 increases from 0.39 to 0.61, and Recall@3 from 0.57 to 0.83. The method yields especially large gains for organizational folders defined by metadata (e.g., venue or year), where baseline methods fail badly: Recall@1 improves from 0.09 to 0.50. On the public LaMP-2 benchmark, the same approach improves accuracy from 44.5% to 51.5% and yields a +9.0 macro-F1 improvement over a single-shot baseline, while maintaining low computational cost suitable for practical deployment.

Conclusion: The study concludes that PHPR is a distinct and practically important problem different from conventional hierarchical text classification due to user-specific, evolving, and semantically heterogeneous folder structures. PaperRouter-Agent, a training-free LLM agent that grounds routing in folder contents and user feedback, effectively addresses this challenge. It substantially improves routing accuracy and recall on both real personal libraries and a public benchmark, demonstrating that content-based grounding and lightweight agentic workflows can provide practical, personalized paper organization without per-user model training.

Abstract: Researchers organize the papers they collect into personal folder hierarchies in reference managers, and route each new paper into the folder where it belongs. This task differs from standard hierarchical text classification. A user's folder hierarchy is not a fixed, shared taxonomy but a private and evolving folksonomy whose folder meanings may be topical, shorthand, venue-based, or process-oriented, and are often defined by the papers already stored inside them. We formalize this setting as personalized hierarchical paper routing (PHPR): assigning an incoming paper to folders in a user-specific hierarchy without per-user training. We propose PaperRouter-Agent, a training-free LLM agent that grounds routing decisions in folder members rather than folder names alone. The agent first narrows the candidate hierarchy, retrieves folder-specific evidence, verifies fit by inspecting member papers, and incorporates similarity-gated feedback from past user rejections. A formative study on real personal libraries shows that PaperRouter-Agent raises overall Recall@1 from 0.39 to 0.61 and Recall@3 from 0.57 to 0.83, with the largest gains on organizational folders defined by metadata such as venue or year, where single-shot methods collapses (Recall@1 0.09 to 0.50). On the public LaMP-2 benchmark, the same approach improves accuracy from 44.5% to 51.5% (+9.0 macro-F1) over a single-shot baseline, while remaining low-cost for practical use.

*Faria Afrin Tisha,Fariya Tabassum,Hafsa Binte Kibria,Md. Nahiduzzaman,Mominul Ahsan*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hate speech on social media threatens online safety, but detecting it is hard in low-resource languages like Bangla because of cultural context, implicitness, informality, and emojis. Existing models perform well on standard benchmarks yet may fail on real-world, nuanced content, leading to both missed hate speech and over-policing of satire or political commentary. The authors want to systematically diagnose this generalization gap for Bangla hate speech detection.

Method: The authors train six architectures (FastText + CNN, FastText + LSTM, FastText + BiLSTM, BanglaBERT, BanglaBERT + CNN, BanglaBERT + BiLSTM) on established Bangla hate speech benchmark datasets (~75k posts) and on a larger merged multi-source dataset (~120k posts). Emoji-aware preprocessing improves implicit HS detection by up to 12%, while removing emojis causes performance to drop from F1 0.75 to 0.63. Frequent misclassification of politically charged or satirical comments is observed, indicating systematic over-policing risks.

Conclusion: Benchmark-trained Bangla hate speech detection models suffer a serious generalization crisis when applied to real-world, implicit, culturally embedded, and emoji-laden content. High in-benchmark scores mask their weaknesses on nuanced cases, especially implicit hate speech. Emoji information is crucial: ignoring or removing emojis significantly harms detection, while emoji-aware preprocessing boosts performance. The study concludes that adaptive, emoji-aware, and culturally grounded frameworks are needed to build ethical, context-sensitive hate speech detection for low-resource languages, balancing safety with freedom of expression and reducing over-policing of satire and political speech.

Abstract: The spread of hate speech (HS) across different social media platforms (SMPs) poses a major concern for online safety and ethical moderation. Automatic detection of HS remains a challenging task, especially in under-resourced languages like Bangla, due to cultural context, implicit expressions, and informal linguistic patterns. Six architectures (FastText + CNN, FastText + LSTM, FastText + BiLSTM, BanglaBERT, BanglaBERT + CNN, and BanglaBERT + BiLSTM) were trained on benchmark datasets (about 75,000 posts) and a merged multi-source dataset (about 120,000 posts), then externally validated on an annotated dataset (about 200 posts) collected from Facebook, Twitter, and YouTube, labeled as HS and non-HS, where HS was further categorized as explicit and implicit. BanglaBERT achieved an F1-score of 91.4% on benchmark datasets but declined to 75.3% on the external set and 63.4% for implicit HS involving sarcasm and emojis. The accuracy of FastText + CNN dropped from 78.0% to 51.2% under similar conditions. Emoji-aware preprocessing improved implicit HS detection by up to 12%, whereas emoji removal caused a notable decline in performance (F1: 0.75 to 0.63). Frequent misclassifications in politically charged or satirical comments revealed over-policing risks. This study not only exposes the generalization crisis due to implicit, culturally embedded, and emoji-laden expressions but also underscores the need for developing adaptive, emoji-aware, and culturally grounded frameworks that ensure ethical moderation while preserving freedom of expression. Findings of this study provide insights for researchers, SMPs, and policymakers to design more context-sensitive HS detection systems for low-resource languages.

### [80] [Globally Consistent Coloring Schemes for Language Identification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11606)
*Moses Charikar,Jon Kleinberg,Chirag Pabbaraju*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how much auxiliary information is minimally needed to make adversarial language learning (in Gold’s identification-in-the-limit model) possible, and shows that one terminal bit of information per example string is sufficient (and essentially optimally nonconstructive) for all countable collections of infinite languages.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical results in Gold-style language identification show that many natural collections of languages are not learnable from positive data alone when an adversary chooses the data order. Later work with trace colorings showed that, if each symbol in each training string is annotated with a color (a ‘thinking trace’), then these negative results can be circumvented. However, such color traces are very information-rich and arguably unrealistic. The authors therefore want to understand how little such auxiliary information is actually necessary: can the full per-symbol color trace be compressed drastically (e.g., to a single bit per example) while still enabling identification in the limit for very broad language families?

Method: They work in Gold’s model of language learning in the limit for countable collections of infinite languages. Instead of allowing arbitrary per-symbol colorings (trace colorings), they restrict attention to terminal colorings: each sample string receives a single color bit at its end. They construct, via a global transfinite recursion over all infinite languages, a uniform assignment of a 2-color terminal coloring to every infinite language, with the property that any countable subcollection is learnable using those preassigned colorings. They analyze the definability/constructivity of such assignments using descriptive set theory, modeling constructive colorings as Borel maps on the space of languages. They then prove impossibility results for Borel (hence ‘constructive’) global terminal colorings with finitely many colors, contrasting them with existing, Borel-definable trace-coloring schemes that use infinitely many colors but can be encoded as terminal sequences.

Result: (1) They prove that one terminal bit per string is sufficient: there exists a 2-color terminal coloring scheme such that every countable collection of infinite languages is identifiable in the limit when examples are annotated with that bit. (2) This coloring can be chosen collection-independently: a single global assignment of terminal colorings to all infinite languages works simultaneously for all countable subcollections. (3) The existence proof relies essentially on nonconstructive transfinite recursion; they show that any global terminal coloring using only finitely many colors that is defined by a Borel map cannot have this universal identification property. (4) They observe that known trace-coloring constructions, while Borel and thus constructive, inherently use infinitely many colors, even though these can be represented as terminal color sequences rather than full per-symbol annotations.

Conclusion: Adversarial language learning in Gold’s sense can be made universally possible for all countable collections of infinite languages by augmenting each training example with only one bit of auxiliary information, and this bit can be assigned in a way that works simultaneously for all such collections. However, such a powerful finite-color terminal coloring cannot be realized by any Borel (constructive) global scheme; some form of high-level nonconstructivity, such as transfinite recursion, is provably necessary. Thus, while trace-coloring information can be compressed extremely aggressively—from per-symbol colors to a single bit per string—achieving this in a uniform and constructive way is impossible with only finitely many colors.

Abstract: We study how little extra information is needed to make adversarial language learning possible. In Gold's model of language identification in the limit, a learner is given an enumeration of the strings from an unknown language chosen from a countable language collection. The learner guesses the identity of the language over the course of the enumeration, and it succeeds if, eventually, all of its guesses are the correct language. Classical results of Gold and Angluin show that many natural collections cannot be learned in this way. Recent work on trace colorings, motivated by the success of thinking-trace strategies in language learning, overcomes this obstruction by annotating every symbol of every string with a color. We ask whether the learner really needs this whole sequence of colors, or whether one color at the end of each string (a terminal coloring) is enough for language identification.
  We show that just one terminal bit per string is enough for every countable collection of infinite languages. In fact, the colorings can be chosen collection-independently: there is a single assignment of a two-color terminal coloring to every infinite language such that the same preassigned colorings identify every countable subcollection. Thus, in this model, an entire color trace can be compressed to one bit attached to the end of each example.
  Our global construction uses transfinite recursion, and we prove that this kind of nonconstructivity is unavoidable for any bounded number of colors. As a notion of constructivity, we use the formalism of Borel maps (a regularity condition satisfied by natural explicit constructions); we show that no global terminal coloring with a finite number of colors defined by a Borel map can identify all countable subcollections. By contrast, known trace-coloring constructions are Borel when encoded as terminal colorings, but require infinitely many colors.

</details>


### [81] [Extending LLM Context via Associative Recurrent Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11614)
*Gleb Kuzmin,Ivan Rodkin,Aydar Bulatov,Yuri Kuratov,Lyudmila Rvanova,Mikhail Katkov,Ilia Sochenkov,Misha Tsodyks,Timothy Baldwin,Mikhail Burtsev,Artem Shelmanov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Associative Recurrent Memory Transformer (ARMT) as a practical way to extend LLM context length with constant memory and lower compute, validates it on realistic long-context tasks, and shows it can exceed original context limits without performance loss while being more efficient.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard transformers scale quadratically in compute and linearly in memory with context length, making very long-context processing expensive and impractical for real-world applications that need to handle long documents or sessions. There is a need for methods that extend context length while maintaining or improving efficiency and preserving model quality in realistic, domain-specific workloads.

Method: They study the Associative Recurrent Memory Transformer (ARMT) architecture as an augmentation to existing LLMs. The method includes: (1) building two domain-specific long-context datasets tailored to narrow-domain fine-tuning scenarios; (2) defining a training recipe that combines continued pre-training on these and synthetic long-context data, curriculum learning over increasing context lengths, and integrating the associative memory modules only into selected layers of the transformer to balance cost and benefit; and (3) systematically evaluating ARMT-augmented models against baselines across in-limit and beyond-limit context settings and different context-length distributions.

Result: Experimentally, ARMT-augmented LLMs can process inputs far beyond their original context window without performance degradation compared to baselines evaluated within their native limits; they also show better generalization to unseen, longer context lengths (out-of-distribution) and achieve about 30% reduction in floating-point operations (FLOPs) while maintaining performance within the original context range.

Conclusion: ARMT provides a practical and efficient way to extend LLM context length with constant memory usage and reduced compute, without sacrificing performance. With the proposed datasets and training recipe, ARMT-augmented models handle long-context workloads better and more efficiently than standard transformers, suggesting ARMT is a strong candidate for real-world long-context LLM deployment.

Abstract: Extending the context length of large language models (LLMs) is critical for many real-world applications, yet standard transformers remain constrained by quadratic compute and linear memory scaling. In this work, we investigate the Associative Recurrent Memory Transformer (ARMT) as a practical approach for enabling long-context processing in LLMs, constant memory scaling, and better efficiency. We make three main contributions. First, we construct two domain-specific long-context datasets designed to evaluate realistic workloads, focusing on narrow-domain fine-tuning scenarios. Second, we propose a comprehensive training recipe for ARMT-based context extension, combining continued pre-training, synthetic long-context data generation, curriculum learning, and selective integration of associative memory into chosen model layers. Third, we present an extensive experimental study demonstrating that ARMT-augmented models: (i) process inputs well beyond their original context limits without degrading performance relative to in-limit baselines; (ii) generalize more effectively to out-of-distribution context lengths; and (iii) need 30% less FLOPs while preserving baseline performance within the original context window.

### [82] [Losing My Composure: Predicting Compositionality Over Time](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11667)
*Chris Jenkins,Emma Raimundo Schulz,Filip Miletić,Sabine Schulte im Walde*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how the meaning and compositionality of German and English noun compounds change over time, introduces a new prediction task and dataset, and evaluates different diachronic semantic representation methods for modeling these trends.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While semantic change has been widely studied, gradual changes in the meanings and compositionality of noun compounds over time are less understood and rarely modeled directly. The literature often assumes that compounds become less compositional (more opaque) over time, but this has not been empirically tested with fine-grained, time-resolved data. There is also a need to understand how different ways of building diachronic semantic representations (e.g., time-slicing strategies, static vs. contextual models) affect our ability to capture such changes.

Method: The authors introduce the Compositionality Trend Prediction task, building a novel dataset of in-context compositionality ratings for 23 German and 26 English noun compounds. Ratings are collected per decade across several decades from diachronic corpora, yielding time series of compositionality scores and trends. They then train multiple types of semantic vector representations (static and contextual) on diachronic corpora using different temporal granularities, creating around 100 models per representation type, each trained on a specific 1–5 decade slice. These models are evaluated on their ability to predict the observed per-decade compositionality ratings and trends.

Result: Empirically, the data exhibits only a small negative trend in compositionality over time for the targeted compounds, which contradicts the strong expectation in prior literature that compounds generally become much less compositional. Computationally, models trained on narrower temporal slices (single decades or incrementally expanding windows) show better alignment with human per-decade compositionality ratings than models trained on broad half-century windows. Moreover, static semantic representations perform competitively with contextual representations on the Compositionality Trend Prediction task.

Conclusion: General claims that noun compounds systematically become much less compositional over time are not strongly supported by the presented empirical data, which instead shows only a modest decrease. For modeling diachronic compositionality trends, temporal granularity matters: narrow time slices capture changes better than broad, aggregated training windows. Additionally, sophisticated contextual representations are not strictly necessary for this task, as well-tuned static representations can perform similarly well. The paper’s dataset and task formulation provide a new benchmark for studying gradual semantic and compositional changes in compounds over time.

Abstract: We explore the phenomenon of semantic change of German and English noun compounds, with the objective of investigating and modeling gradual changes of meanings and degrees of compositionality in the past and over time. To do so, we introduce the Compositionality Trend Prediction task, which is evaluated against a novel dataset of in-context compositionality ratings sampled across several decades of diachronic corpora for 23 German and 26 English target compounds, uniquely providing per-decade ratings and corresponding trends over time. These per-decade compositionality ratings allow us to investigate empirically untested hypotheses of generalized trends in compositionality over time, such as the idea that compounds should become less compositional (less transparent) over time. Beyond our empirical observations from the diachronic compositionality annotations, we perform experiments with semantic vector representations of varying complexity, as well as several temporal granularities for training these representations on diachronic data, resulting in about 100 models of each representation type, each covering a different 1--5 decade slice of a diachronic corpus. Contrary to the decisive tendency posited in the literature, we find only a small negative trend in compositionality over time in our target compounds. In our computational experiments, we find that using models trained on narrow time slices of diachronic data (single decades, or incrementally expanding temporal windows) align better with the per-decade compositionality ratings than those trained on an entire half-century window, the latter setting being an analog for the prevalent modeling approach of training representations on an entire half of a corpus' data. Additionally, we find static representations to be competitive with contextual representations in the Compositionality Trend Prediction task.

</details>


### [83] [RAGU: A Multi-Step GraphRAG Engine with a Compact Domain-Adapted LLM](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11683)
*Mikhail Komarov,Ivan Bondarenko,Stanislav Shtuka,Oleg Sedukhin,Roman Shuvalov,Yana Dementyeva,Matvey Solovyov,Nikolay O. Nikitin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces RAGU, a modular GraphRAG engine that builds higher-quality knowledge graphs via a multi-stage pipeline and a compact, language-skill-optimized 7B LLM (Meno-Lite-0.1), outperforming much larger models on graph construction and GraphRAG tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing GraphRAG systems typically construct knowledge graphs in a single extraction pass, leading to noisy entities, brittle retrieval, and suboptimal use of LLM capabilities. There is also a misconception that larger models are always needed for in-pipeline tasks, despite these tasks relying more on language manipulation than world knowledge. The authors aim to improve graph quality, retrieval robustness, and efficiency while challenging the assumption that only large models can effectively power GraphRAG pipelines.

Method: They design RAGU, an open-source modular GraphRAG engine that explicitly separates extraction from consolidation. The pipeline includes two-stage typed extraction of entities and relations, DBSCAN-based deduplication for cleaning and merging graph components, LLM-based summarization to refine and condense information, and Leiden community detection to structure the graph into coherent communities. Additionally, they train Meno-Lite-0.1, a 7B LLM specifically optimized for language skills (comprehension, extraction, reasoning over context) rather than encyclopedic world knowledge, and integrate it as the in-pipeline extractor within RAGU. They evaluate RAGU and Meno-Lite-0.1 on GraphRAG-Bench (Medical) and other GraphRAG tasks, and compare against systems such as HippoRAG2 and larger models like Qwen2.5-32B.

Result: Meno-Lite-0.1, despite having only 7B parameters, outperforms the 32B Qwen2.5 model on knowledge-graph construction with a 12.5% relative gain in harmonic mean of relevant metrics, while matching Qwen2.5-32B on English GraphRAG tasks. On GraphRAG-Bench (Medical), RAGU retrieves the most complete evidence across all factoid difficulty levels, achieving evidence recall up to 0.84 versus baselines that are at or below 0.76. RAGU also surpasses HippoRAG2 on synthesis tasks, and the previously reported advantage of HippoRAG2 on multi-hop factoid QA is shown to be largely due to differences in answer formatting rather than true reasoning superiority.

Conclusion: Separating extraction and consolidation in GraphRAG pipelines and using a compact LLM optimized for language skills yields cleaner knowledge graphs, stronger retrieval, and competitive or superior downstream performance compared to larger models and prior systems like HippoRAG2. Language-focused abilities, not sheer parameter count or world knowledge, are the key bottleneck for in-pipeline LLMs in GraphRAG. RAGU provides a practical, open-source, single-GPU solution for building high-quality GraphRAG systems, and the release of both the engine and the Meno-Lite-0.1 model facilitates reproducibility and further research.

Abstract: Graph retrieval-augmented generation (GraphRAG) enhances large language models with structured knowledge, yet existing systems construct knowledge graphs in a single extraction pass, producing noisy entities and brittle retrieval. RAGU, an open-source modular GraphRAG engine, addresses this by separating extraction from consolidation: entities and relations pass through two-stage typed extraction, DBSCAN-backed deduplication, LLM summarization, and Leiden community detection. A key insight motivates a compact extractor: the skills an in-pipeline LLM needs - comprehension, extraction, reasoning over context - are language skills that grow only weakly with model size, unlike factual world knowledge. Accordingly, we train Meno-Lite-0.1, a 7B model optimized for language skills, which outperforms Qwen2.5-32B on knowledge-graph construction (+12.5% relative harmonic mean) and matches it on English GraphRAG tasks. On GraphRAG-Bench (Medical), RAGU retrieves the most complete context at every factoid level (evidence recall up to 0.84 vs. $\leq$0.76) and overtakes HippoRAG2 on synthesis tasks; on multi-hop factoid QA, the apparent HippoRAG2 advantage is shown to be largely an answer-format artifact. RAGU is installable via $\texttt{pip install graph_ragu}$, runs on a single GPU, and is released under MIT. The source code is publicly available at https://github.com/RaguTeam/RAGU, and the Meno-Lite-0.1 model can be obtained from https://huggingface.co/bond005/meno-lite-0.1.

### [84] [Production and Perception in LLMs: A Token Probability Approach](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11703)
*Anna Marklová,Jiří Milička,Martina Vokáčová,Rudolf Rosa*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates whether large language models show an internal difference between “production” and “perception” modes, even though both are implemented via next-token prediction, by comparing token probabilities under differently framed prompts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In human psycholinguistics, language production and perception are known to be asymmetrical processes. For LLMs, however, both input understanding and output generation rely on the same next-token prediction mechanism, so it is unclear whether any analogous production–perception distinction exists. Prior work often uses metalinguistic instructions (e.g., "explain how you would..."), which may confound genuine processing differences with explicit reasoning or task re-interpretation. The authors aim to directly probe whether merely changing the communicative framing of prompts (production vs. perception) leads to systematically different internal probability distributions over the same tokens, and whether this effect is robust across models and prompt variants.

Method: Using the base Llama-3.1-8B model, the authors first generate poems with a “production” prompt, thereby obtaining specific output token sequences. They then re-score these identical sequences under (a) rephrased production-style prompts and (b) perception-oriented prompts, directly measuring token probabilities rather than relying on explicit judgments. They compute distances between probability distributions for production–production (control) and production–perception (experimental) prompt pairs, and compare their magnitudes across four production and three perception prompts. They further replicate the analysis on four additional open-weight models (EuroLLM-9B, gemma-2-9b-it, Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3, Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct) including both base and instruction-tuned architectures. A temporal analysis examines how differences evolve as a function of sequence position, assessing where along the generated text the prompt framing has the strongest influence.

Result: Production–perception distances in token probability space are consistently and substantially larger than production–production distances, with non-overlapping ranges across conditions and an average ratio of about 1.8. Control comparisons between multiple production-style prompts show near-ceiling correlations, indicating that simple surface rephrasing does not explain the effect. The pattern replicates across all five tested models, suggesting that it is not specific to a single architecture or training regime. Temporal analysis shows that perception prompts alter the probability distribution most strongly at the start of the sequence, with the divergence decreasing over time as more context accumulates, though the decay profile varies between specific prompt pairs.

Conclusion: Even though decoder-only LLMs implement both “understanding” and “generation” with the same next-token prediction mechanism, their internal probability distributions are sensitive to communicative framing. Specifically, prompts framed as production vs. perception tasks induce systematically different token distributions over the same sequences, in a way that is robust across models and not attributable to superficial prompt variation. This suggests that an emergent, functionally meaningful production–perception distinction can arise in LLMs purely from differences in prompt framing, echoing (though not necessarily replicating) the asymmetries seen in human language use.

Abstract: The asymmetry between language production and perception has been well-documented in psycholinguistics. Whether large language models (LLMs) exhibit a functionally analogous distinction remains an open question, particularly given that LLMs rely on the same underlying mechanism (next-token prediction) for both input and output processing. In this exploratory study, we operationalize the production-perception distinction through direct token probability measurements rather than metalinguistic prompting. Using the base Llama-3.1-8B model, we generated poems under a production prompt and re-scored the same tokens under both rephrased production prompts and perception-oriented prompts. Across an extended experiment with four production and three perception prompts, production-perception distances consistently and substantially exceeded production-production distances, with non-overlapping ranges across conditions and an overall average ratio of approximately 1.8. Near-ceiling correlations in the production-production control confirm that the effect is specific to communicative framing rather than prompt surface variation, and we show the effect replicates across five open-weight models (Llama-3.1-8B, EuroLLM-9B, gemma-2-9b-it, Mistral-7B-Instruct-v0.3, and Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct), spanning both base and instruction-tuned variants. Temporal analysis revealed that the perception prompt exerts its strongest influence at the beginning of the sequence, with divergence decaying as generated context accumulates, though the specific shape of this decay varies across prompt pairs. These findings suggest that prompt framing alone induces a production-perception distinction in LLM probability distributions, even within a decoder-only architecture.

*Iman Johary,Guillaume Bied,Alexandru C. Mara,Tijl De Bie*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: JobHop v2 is a large, publicly released dataset of 355k career trajectories extracted with LLMs from ~440k multilingual resumes, offering richer, higher-quality annotations for labour market research than prior public resources.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing public career trajectory datasets are too small, restricted, or based on synthetic text and pre-standardized codes, limiting realistic workforce planning, job recommendation, and labour market analysis. There is a need for a large, realistic, and well-annotated dataset built from authentic resumes that is openly available for independent research and reproducibility.

Method: The authors collected ~440,000 pseudonymized multilingual resumes from the Flemish Public Employment Service (VDAB) and used an end-to-end LLM-based extraction pipeline to convert free-text resumes into structured career trajectories. They redesigned the pipeline around reasoning-controlled LLM inference with a retry mechanism to guarantee 100% valid JSON output, expanded the extraction schema (including ESCO occupational codes, quarter-level temporal information, and normalized 5-level education attainment), and defined a new evaluation protocol using three complementary human-annotation baselines to assess extraction quality and model performance.

Result: JobHop v2 contains 355,315 structured career trajectories with rich annotations (ESCO jobs, temporal granularity, and education levels), markedly expanding both the scale and annotation depth compared to v1. The best-performing LLM extractor approaches human-level agreement, falling only 1.1–2.7 percentage points short of the inter-annotator agreement ceiling across the new evaluation baselines, while achieving a 100% JSON parse rate thanks to the new reasoning+retry pipeline.

Conclusion: JobHop v2 demonstrates that carefully designed, reasoning-controlled LLM pipelines can reliably transform large volumes of real-world resumes into high-quality, richly annotated career trajectory data at scale. The publicly released dataset and code provide a strong, reproducible resource for research on career paths, job recommendation, and labour market analysis, and set a benchmark for future work on LLM-based information extraction from complex, multilingual HR documents.

Abstract: Large-scale, richly annotated career trajectory data underpins workforce planning, job recommendation, and labour market analysis, yet publicly available datasets are either small, closed to independent use, or built from pre-standardized occupational codes with LLM-synthesized rather than authentic free text. The released dataset comprises $355{,}315$ career trajectories annotated with ESCO occupational codes, quarter-level temporal information, and normalized five-level education attainment, broadening both the coverage and the annotation richness of the original release. Relative to v1, JobHop~v2 introduces a redesigned extraction pipeline based on reasoning-controlled LLM inference with a retry mechanism (achieving a 100% JSON parse rate), a richer extraction schema, and a revised evaluation protocol scored against three complementary annotation baselines. Evaluated against these baselines, our best extractor comes closest to the inter-annotator agreement ceiling among all compared models, trailing it by only 1.1-2.7 percentage points. The dataset and code are publicly released to support reproducible career-trajectory research.

### [86] [STEP: Career-Path Recommendation via Temporal and Educational Trajectory Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11722)
*Iman Johary,Guillaume Bied,Alexandru C. Mara,Tijl De Bie*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces STEP, a system for predicting a person’s next job from resume-derived career trajectories, enhanced by a new representation learning framework, ROUTE, and shows it outperforms prior methods.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Career paths, encoded in resumes, contain long-term information on skills, roles, and education that is crucial for workforce planning, labor policy, and job recommendation. However, resumes are unstructured, heterogeneous, and multilingual, making large-scale systematic analysis difficult. With LLMs now able to extract rich structured signals from resumes, there is a new opportunity to build effective, scalable career-path recommendation systems that exploit temporal and educational information.

Method: The authors propose STEP, a Sequential Trajectory of Employment Prediction framework that takes as input structured career trajectories extracted from resumes. STEP uses: (1) a time-decay GRU cell to explicitly model temporal dynamics in employment history, (2) Feature-wise Linear Modulation (FiLM) layers conditioned on educational attainment to modulate representations based on education, and (3) attention-based sequence pooling to focus on the most relevant past experiences for predicting the next job. To improve occupation representations used within STEP, they introduce ROUTE, a two-stage contrastive representation learning pipeline: first, a multilingual encoder is adapted to the career domain via unsupervised denoising autoencoding; second, supervised contrastive fine-tuning with guided negative selection refines occupation embeddings for better discrimination between job types.

Result: STEP, combined with ROUTE-enhanced occupation representations, is evaluated on four datasets of career trajectories, including an improved version of the publicly available JobHop dataset. Across these datasets, STEP consistently outperforms state-of-the-art baselines for next job prediction, demonstrating the effectiveness of incorporating temporal decay, education-conditioned FiLM, and attention-based pooling, as well as the proposed ROUTE contrastive adaptation procedure.

Conclusion: The work shows that unstructured, multilingual resumes can be transformed—using LLMs and specialized sequence modeling—into high-quality data for career-path recommendation. STEP provides an effective architecture for next-job prediction by leveraging temporal and educational signals, while ROUTE improves internal occupation representations through domain-adapted, contrastive learning. The released dataset and code aim to facilitate reproducible research and further advances in large-scale career trajectory modeling and recommendation.

Abstract: Career paths encode decades of skill acquisition, role transitions, and educational investment, and understanding them at scale underpins workforce planning, labor market policy, and job recommendation. Resumes are a rich source of information about career paths: they contain detailed descriptions of work experience, education, and skills. Yet their unstructured, heterogeneous, and multilingual nature has long prevented large-scale systematic analysis. With the advent of large language models (LLMs), it is now possible to source rich career trajectory data containing temporal and educational signals from unstructured resumes, enabling new opportunities for career-path recommendation. Exploiting this opportunity, we present STEP (Sequential Trajectory of Employment Prediction), a novel career-path recommendation system that leverages temporal and educational signals to predict the next job in a career trajectory. STEP integrates a time-decay Gated Recurrent Unit (GRU) cell to model temporal dynamics, Feature-wise Linear Modulation (FiLM) conditioned on educational attainment, and attention-based sequence pooling to select relevant features for next job prediction. To improve internal occupation representation for STEP, we introduce ROUTE, a two-stage contrastive procedure that first adapts a multilingual encoder to the career domain via unsupervised denoising autoencoding, then performs supervised contrastive fine-tuning with guided negative selection. We evaluate STEP on four datasets of career trajectories, including an improved version of our publicly available JobHop dataset, and show that it outperforms state-of-the-art baselines in next job prediction. The dataset and code are publicly released to support reproducible career-trajectory research.

### [87] [MET: Theory-Grounded and Culture-Aware Multilingual Moral Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11736)
*Ayoung Lee,Ryan Kwon,Yunxiang Zhang,Yuxuan Liu,Peter Railton,Lu Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a culturally-aware, theory-grounded framework and benchmark to improve multilingual moral decision-making in language models, achieving notable performance gains without external supervision.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing moral decision-making work for language models is largely English-centric, directly translates benchmarks without cultural adaptation, uses static reasoning prompts that ignore moral theory, and depends on costly human or stronger-model supervision for training. There is a need for methods and datasets that respect cultural differences, ground reasoning in established moral theories, and improve models efficiently across multiple languages.

Method: (1) Build MCLASH, a multilingual benchmark designed to reflect culturally situated moral intuitions and social norms rather than direct translations. (2) Propose MET, a two-step prompting approach where the model first selects appropriate, expert-curated moral “grounds” (principles) specific to the situation and culture, then performs moral reasoning in the user’s native language using those grounds. (3) Introduce MET-D, a self-distillation training method that fine-tunes models to improve the second reasoning step, using the model’s own MET-style outputs as training signals, requiring no external labels or teacher models.

Result: On three models (Qwen3-4B, Qwen3-8B, Gemma3-4B), MET-D improves macro-F1 on the new MCLASH benchmark by an average of 3.71 points and on MMoralExceptQA by 4.23 points, with the largest MCLASH gain of 12.94 points for Malay on Qwen3-8B. MET-D also boosts native-language reasoning scores by 62.13 points on average and reveals systematic cross-cultural differences in which moral grounds are most beneficial.

Conclusion: Theory-grounded, culture-sensitive prompting and self-distillation can significantly enhance multilingual moral decision-making in language models while avoiding expensive external supervision. The new MCLASH benchmark plus the MET and MET-D methods provide a practical path toward more culturally aligned and principled moral reasoning across languages.

Abstract: Language models are increasingly used for moral decision-making across diverse linguistic and cultural contexts, yet existing work overlooks multilinguality on three aspects: 1) multilingual evaluation benchmarks use direct translation, failing to adapt culture-specific items; 2) inference-time methods for moral reasoning rely on static, English-centric scaffolds and lack grounding in moral theory; 3) training methods for moral decision-making typically require expensive supervision from stronger models or human annotators. We address these gaps with three contributions. First, we introduce MCLASH, a multilingual moral decision-making benchmark to capture culturally situated moral intuitions and social norms across languages. Second, we propose MET (Multilingual Ethics with Theory-grounded reasoning), a two-step prompting method built on expert-curated, theory-based grounds drawn from psychology and philosophy: the model first selects situation- and culture-specific grounds, then reasons over them in the native language of the user. Third, we introduce MET-D (MET-Distillation), which enhances the second step through a self-distillation training stage that requires no external supervision. MET-D improves macro-F1 over the base model on all three models of different sizes and families (Qwen3-4B, Qwen3-8B, Gemma3-4B), by an average of 3.71 points on MCLASH and 4.23 on MMoralExceptQA, with a peak MCLASH gain of 12.94 points for Malay on Qwen3-8B. We further reveal that MET-D increases native-language reasoning by 62.13 points on average, and that beneficial grounds differ systematically across cultures. Together, these contributions open the path for culture-aligned, theory-grounded multilingual moral reasoning.

### [88] [How Temperature Shapes Ideological Discourse in Retrieval-Augmented Generation?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11783)
*Elmira Salari,Hazem Amamou,José Victor de Souza,Shruti Kshirsagar,Maria Nunes Delfino,Anderson Avila*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) can unintentionally carry over and shape ideological content from retrieved documents into LLM outputs, and how sampling temperature affects this transfer.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RAG is widely used to reduce hallucinations and improve factual grounding for LLMs, but little attention has been paid to how ideological biases in retrieved documents may be transmitted or amplified in generated answers. As LLMs are increasingly used for information access, it is important to understand whether and how RAG setups can propagate specific ideological discourses, especially in sensitive domains like COVID-19 treatments.

Method: The authors first compiled a corpus of 1,117 articles about COVID-19 treatments and applied Lexical Multidimensional Analysis (LMDA) to identify three distinct ideological discourses present in the texts. This ideologically characterized corpus was then used as the external knowledge base in a RAG pipeline. Multiple LLMs were prompted with ideologically charged questions at varying sampling temperatures. The generated answers were evaluated both semantically and lexically by comparing them to ideological reference texts, measuring how closely the model responses aligned with each discourse type.

Result: The study finds that RAG-based systems systematically transfer ideological discourses present in the retrieval corpus into LLM outputs. The extent of this transfer depends on the sampling temperature: moderate temperatures lead to the strongest discoursive alignment with the ideological reference texts, whereas low temperatures substantially reduce the degree of discourse transfer. Thus, temperature controls not only diversity but also how strongly retrieved ideological content shapes model answers.

Conclusion: RAG does not merely provide neutral factual grounding; it can also act as a conduit for ideological discourses embedded in the retrieval corpus. Sampling temperature plays a critical role: moderate values maximize discourse transfer, while highly deterministic settings attenuate it. These findings imply that designers of RAG systems must account for ideological properties of their knowledge bases and for decoding choices, as both materially influence the ideological framing of generated responses.

Abstract: Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) has been increasingly adopted to reduce hallucinations and strengthen the factual grounding of large language models (LLMs). While robustness to errors in the retrieval process has been explored, the impact of ideological bias on LLM outputs has been overlooked. For instance, if the retrieved material contains ideological positions, the RAG may transmit, amplify, or suppress such ideological discourses in its outputs. In this study, we address this issue by examining the influence of the RAG framework, comprising ideological discourses, in LLM-generated answers. To this end, we applied Lexical Multidimensional Analysis (LMDA) on a corpus of 1,117 COVID-19 treatment articles, identifying three ideological discourses. This corpus is then used as the external knowledge source for the RAG. We assessed several LLMs by having the models answer ideological questions at different sampling temperatures. The generated texts were assessed semantically and lexically based on their similarities with ideological reference texts. Our findings show that the RAG framework is prone to transferring ideological discourses into LLM responses, with sampling temperature having a measurable impact on the strength of this transfer. Discoursive alignment between generated answers and the reference text is highest at moderate temperatures, where models balance stochasticity with retrieval grounding, and drops at low temperatures, indicating that overly deterministic sampling suppresses discourse transfer.

Drawing on HCAI principles and previous applications in other language professions, the paper identifies four interrelated dimensions through which AI integration in lexicography can be understood and critically examined: the augmented lexicographer, the sociotechnical context of AI integration, bias, and the design of AI-powered lexicographic tools. The framework argues that AI should augment rather than replace lexicographers, combining high levels of automation with meaningful human control. It further emphasizes the importance of preserving professional agency, mitigating AI-generated biases, and designing tools around the needs of lexicographers. ### [90] [AdvancedMathBench: A Benchmark Suite for Advanced Mathematical Proof Generation and Verification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11849)
*Lingkai Kong,Zijian Wu,Yuzhe Gu,Haiteng Zhao,Wenyong Huang,Shuang Sun,Zhicheng Xiong,Xiaotian Zhang,Shuya Zhao,Yan Wang,Disheng Xu,Wenwei Zhang,Kai Chen*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM math benchmarks focus mainly on high-school and olympiad problems and use coarse, final-answer-based evaluation. This gives little insight into how well models handle advanced undergraduate to doctoral-level mathematics or whether their detailed reasoning steps are valid, leaving a gap in understanding and improving advanced mathematical reasoning in LLMs.

Method: The authors construct AdvancedMathBench, which has two main components. (1) ProverBench: 296 advanced math proof problems at undergraduate and qualifying-exam level, used to test proof generation. They build an automatic verification pipeline trained on large-scale expert annotations to judge both overall correctness and detailed proof errors. (2) VerifierBench: 888 model-generated proof trajectories paired with expert-annotated ground truth labels, used to test whether models can act as verifiers, judging proof validity and explaining their verification. The automatic verifier is validated by its agreement with human experts on held-out proofs. Frontier LLMs are then evaluated on both proof generation and verification tasks.
