The automatic verification pipeline itself shows strong agreement with expert judgments on separate test data.

Conclusion: AdvancedMathBench exposes substantial remaining gaps in LLMs’ advanced mathematical reasoning, particularly in constructing correct proofs for higher-level problems and in reliably detecting errors in given proofs. The benchmark and automatic verification pipeline offer a more fine-grained, scalable way to measure and analyze progress in advanced math reasoning, highlighting critical error detection as an important area for future model improvement.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have achieved remarkable performance on high-school and olympiad-style mathematics, yet their capabilities on advanced mathematics remain poorly understood. Existing benchmarks, however, fall short in both scope and evaluation granularity: they provide limited disciplinary coverage and often rely on final-answer correctness or coarse judgments, leaving the validity of the reasoning process inadequately assessed. To bridge this gap, we introduce AdvancedMathBench, a benchmark suite designed to evaluate advanced mathematical reasoning capabilities. Its core proof-generation benchmark, ProverBench, contains 296 problems spanning undergraduate and doctoral qualifying-exam levels. To provide reliable evaluation of the proofs, we develop a dedicated automatic verification pipeline trained on large-scale expert annotations to produce both correctness verdicts and fine-grained assessments of proof errors, which exhibits strong agreement with human experts on held-out proof trajectories. We further introduce VerifierBench, consisting of 888 model-generated proof trajectories paired with expert ground truth, to evaluate whether models can correctly judge proof validity and provide sound verification rationales. Experiments show that AdvancedMathBench remains challenging for frontier models. On proof generation, the best-performing model, GPT-5.5-xhigh, achieves only 75.8 and 66.1 on the UGD and QE splits, respectively, indicating substantial room for improvement on advanced mathematical proof construction. On proof verification, the best model attains a Balanced F1 of only 65.1, and models generally exhibit low true negative rates, suggesting that critical error detection remains a major bottleneck.

### [91] [A Durability and Cross-Language Transfer Benchmark for a Validated Teaching-Feedback Classification Protocol](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11873)
*Esteban U. Vega Barajas*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether a previously validated protocol for classifying open-ended teaching evaluation comments remains effective with newer text representation methods and in another language, finding that the protocol is robust and that newer, more expensive models mainly affect deployment choices rather than the protocol’s validity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Institutions gather large amounts of open-ended feedback in teaching evaluations but read and use only a small portion because manual analysis is costly. A prior study created a rigorous protocol for automatically classifying these comments by theme and sentiment using 2019-era frozen embeddings in Spanish, but its long-term relevance is uncertain. Specifically, there are open questions about whether the protocol remains competitive as representation technologies evolve (e.g., transformers, large language models) and whether it can transfer effectively to another language such as English.

Method: The authors replicate the earlier protocol on the original Spanish corpus using three generations of text representations: (1) sparse lexical features, (2) frozen transformer embeddings, and (3) prompted large language models. They treat the original classifications by thematic category and sentiment as the prediction tasks. They then transfer the sentiment component of the protocol to English using a new, balanced corpus of 45,000 comments. This English dataset is cross-checked against an existing aspect-labeled education dataset to validate coverage and label consistency. Model comparisons across representation types are analyzed descriptively, rather than as strict statistical hypothesis tests, focusing on F1 performance for thematic classification and sentiment.

Result: On the Spanish data, a frontier 2026 large model achieves the best F1 scores on the most difficult thematic classification task, confirming that newer representations can yield incremental gains in some areas. However, for sentiment classification, the frontier model does not outperform a cheaper model, despite its higher capacity. On the English transfer task, the more advanced models similarly fail to show clear descriptive separation from the cheaper model on sentiment. Overall, performance differences across model generations are modest and task-dependent rather than uniformly favoring the newest models.

Conclusion: The study concludes that the original protocol for annotating and classifying open-ended teaching-evaluation comments is durable over time and across languages. Advances in representation technologies do not invalidate the protocol; instead, they offer optional performance improvements in certain thematic tasks. Since frontier models do not consistently outperform cheaper models on sentiment, the choice of model is primarily a practical deployment decision (e.g., cost, latency, accessibility) rather than a methodological requirement. This supports viewing the protocol as a stable, transferable framework for institutional analysis of open-ended student feedback.

Abstract: Institutions collect far more open-ended teaching-evaluation feedback than they read. A prior study introduced a validated protocol for classifying such comments by thematic category and sentiment, built from a documented annotation guide, an intra-annotator reliability measurement, stratified cross-validation, and a held-out evaluation on a Spanish institutional corpus with a frozen-encoder design. Two questions limit its reuse: whether a protocol fixed to 2019-era frozen embeddings stays competitive as representation methods advance, and whether it transfers to a second language. We re-run it on the original Spanish data across three representation generations, sparse lexical features, frozen transformer embeddings, and prompted large language models, and transfer its sentiment task to English with a balanced 45,000-comment corpus checked against an aspect-labeled education dataset. Treating paired comparisons as descriptive, we find the protocol durable: a 2026 frontier model posts the highest thematic F1 on the hardest Spanish task, yet shows no sentiment advantage over a cheap model and no descriptive separation from it on English, so model choice is a deployment decision, not a property of the method.

</details>


### [92] [Metacognition in LLMs: Foundations, Progress, and Opportunities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11881)
*Gabrielle Kaili-May Liu,Areeb Gani,Jacqueline Lu,Jordan Thomas,Mark Steyvers,Arman Cohan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: This paper is a survey of metacognition in large language models (LLMs), organizing existing work, methods, benchmarks, and applications, and outlining open challenges and future directions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Metacognition—thinking about one’s own thinking—is central to human-like intelligence and is increasingly seen as essential for AI systems that are capable, reliable, and transparent. Despite rapid progress of LLMs, there is no clear, unified understanding of when and how they exhibit metacognitive abilities, how to measure these abilities, or how to systematically enhance and exploit them. The field is fragmented and lacks a comprehensive overview, making it difficult for researchers and practitioners to navigate existing methods, benchmarks, and findings.

Method: The authors conduct a structured literature review of recent work on metacognition in LLMs. They analyze and categorize prior research into a taxonomy that covers (1) benchmarks and evaluation methods for metacognitive abilities (such as self-assessment, calibration, and uncertainty estimation), (2) techniques to elicit, train, or improve metacognition in LLMs (e.g., prompting strategies, training objectives, auxiliary modules), and (3) applications and empirical findings. They synthesize this body of work into an organized framework and maintain a curated list of relevant papers in an online repository.

Result: The paper delivers a comprehensive taxonomy and synthesis of the emerging area of metacognition for LLMs. It summarizes existing benchmarks and evaluation protocols, catalogs methods for inducing and leveraging metacognition in LLMs, and compiles reported empirical effects on capabilities, reliability, and transparency. It also aggregates and systematizes insights scattered across individual studies into a coherent landscape view of the field.

Conclusion: Metacognition is both important and still poorly understood in the context of LLMs. Current research shows promising techniques and preliminary evidence that metacognitive abilities can improve AI systems’ reliability, interpretability, and performance, but there remain significant gaps in theory, evaluation, and practical integration. The authors conclude that a more systematic, principled exploration of metacognition in LLMs is needed and hope their survey, taxonomy, and curated paper list will serve as a foundation to guide future research and discussion.

Abstract: Metacognition is a foundational component of intelligence critical to effective learning, problem solving, decision-making, communication, and more. In recent years, it has become increasingly recognized as a cornerstone of capable, transparent AI systems. Yet while LLMs have made significant progress across diverse real-world tasks, it is not yet clear when, how, or to what extent they can exhibit or be endowed with effective metacognitive abilities, nor how such abilities can be adapted to advance the fundamental capabilities, reliability, and intelligence of AI systems. This paper bridges this gap by presenting the first comprehensive overview of the current state of knowledge on metacognition for LLMs. We analyze and taxonomize the landscape of this emerging field and summarize recent technical advancements, including methods and benchmarks to measure and evaluate LLMs' metacognitive abilities, techniques to elicit, improve, and apply metacognition in LLMs, and findings and implications of ongoing research. We also discuss applications, open questions and challenges, and promising directions for future work. Our aim is to provide a detailed and up-to-date review of this topic and stimulate meaningful research and discussion. An organized list of papers can be found at https://github.com/yale-nlp/LLM-Metacognition.

### [93] [From ML Predictions to Informed Diagnostic Assistance Using the Toulmin Model of Argumentation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09664)
*Anca Marginean,Adrian Groza*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes an argumentation-based framework for interpretable image-based medical diagnosis, decomposing ML outputs into Toulmin-style argument components to support expert decision-making.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Image-based medical diagnoses from ML models are often opaque and hard for clinicians to trust. Traditional explainable AI (XAI) provides some insights but may not organize evidence and reasoning in a structured way that aligns with how experts reason about clinical arguments. The authors want a more interpretable, argumentative structure that clinicians can scrutinize, challenging or accepting model outputs with clear justifications.

Method: They model each ML diagnosis as a structured argument following Toulmin’s model: claim, grounds, warrant, qualifier, rebuttal, and backing. A biomarker-extraction model processes the image and produces quantitative features that serve as the grounds. A MedGemma agent with medical knowledge analyzes how these grounds support the diagnostic claim, acting as the warrant. The overall reliability of both the biomarker model and the warranting agent is quantified to produce a qualifier that expresses diagnostic confidence. A rebuttal is generated via image similarity analysis using MedSigLip, surfacing potentially conflicting cases. All components are then presented to the clinician as an integrated, interpretable argument.

Result: The framework yields a decomposed, argument-style representation of ML diagnoses where each element—evidence from the image, medical reasoning linking evidence to diagnosis, confidence levels, and counter-evidence via similar images—is explicitly surfaced. This gives clinicians richer information than a bare prediction or standard XAI alone, though specific quantitative performance metrics are not provided in the abstract.

Conclusion: Organizing ML-based diagnoses as structured Toulmin arguments can improve interpretability and critical evaluation in medical imaging. By separating evidence, reasoning, confidence, and rebuttals, the approach aims to make ML outputs more transparent and clinically usable, complementing or extending traditional XAI methods.

Abstract: To provide a structured and interpretable assessment, we decompose the image-based diagnosis into components following the Toulmin model of argumentation. This model consists of a claim, grounds, warrant, qualifier, rebuttal, and backing. Consider a claim generated by a machine learning (ML) model for retinal diagnosis. Rather than accepting this claim at face value, one could either apply explainable AI (XAI) methods or adopt an argumentation-based approach. In our framework, a model specialized in biomarker extraction from images provides the grounds. The warrant-linking the grounds to the claim - is analyzed by an agent equipped with medical knowledge; in our architecture, this role is fulfilled by a MedGemma agent. The qualifier is determined based on the overall quantitative evaluation of both the warrant and grounds models. Finally, a rebuttal is constructed using image similarity measures computed with MedSigLip. All these components are presented to the human expert, enabling a more informed and critical assessment of the ML-generated diagnosis.

</details>


### [94] [Format Sensitivity Index: Token-Controlled Prompt Wrapper Robustness and Schema Compliance in LLM Benchmarking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09665)
*Deep Pankajbhai Mehta*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that small changes in prompt formatting (“prompt wrappers”) can significantly change model accuracy and output parseability, making current leaderboard-style benchmarking statistically fragile.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluation leaderboards and benchmarks for LLMs often assume that task accuracy is a stable property of a model-task pair, but in practice, scores can vary dramatically depending on how the prompt is wrapped or formatted. This undermines fair comparison between models and obscures important failure modes like non-compliance or unparseable answers. The authors want to systematically quantify how sensitive model performance is to prompt formatting and to highlight the role of parseability/compliance in this variance.

Method: The authors design a token-controlled evaluation protocol where only the prompt wrapper/formatting is varied while holding task content constant. They introduce two metrics: (1) Format Sensitivity Index (FSI), which measures the range of accuracy induced solely by changing the prompt wrapper, and (2) Parseability Sensitivity Index (PSI), which measures the corresponding range in the parseability of answers. They run large-scale experiments with about 140,000 generations using multiple OpenRouter-accessible instruct models (7B–72B parameters), five families of prompt wrappers, and seven question-answering tasks. They also fit a fixed-effects regression model to test whether parseability predicts accuracy after controlling for task, model, and wrapper effects.

Result: They find that average FSI differs by more than 30x across models, indicating that some models are much more sensitive to prompt formatting than others. Much of this sensitivity is attributable to compliance failures—cases where the model does not follow the requested output format or instructions. The regression analysis shows that parseability remains a strong and significant predictor of task accuracy even when accounting for fixed effects of task, model, and wrapper choice.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that reporting a single accuracy number without accounting for variance due to prompt wrappers and compliance is statistically fragile and potentially misleading. For robust benchmarking and fair model comparison, researchers and practitioners should report sensitivity to prompt formatting and track parseability/compliance explicitly. The authors offer practical recommendations for benchmark design and for deploying structured-output systems so that they are less vulnerable to prompt-formatting artifacts and compliance failures.

Abstract: Prompt wrappers often differ only in formatting, yet they can change model scores enough to flip leaderboard conclusions. We study this variance under a token-controlled protocol and introduce two complementary metrics: the Format Sensitivity Index (FSI), the accuracy range induced by wrapper choice, and the Parseability Sensitivity Index (PSI), the corresponding range in answer parseability. Across 140,000 OpenRouter generations spanning 7 QA tasks, 5 wrapper families, and 4 instruct models from 7B to 72B parameters, we find that mean FSI varies by over 30x across models and is largely explained by compliance failures. A fixed-effects regression shows that parseability remains a strong predictor of accuracy even after controlling for task, model, and wrapper. We argue that reporting accuracy without wrapper variance and compliance is statistically fragile, and we give practical recommendations for both benchmarking and structured-output deployments.

</details>


### [95] [Faithful, Not Corrective: Message-Format Effects in Multi-Hop Agent Relays Are Tier-Dependent](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09678)
*Zayx Shawn*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how different message formats (natural language vs structured formats like JSON) affect information fidelity and cost when LLM agents pass information across multiple hops.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing work on LLM message formats is split: some show that structured formats reduce cost without hurting accuracy, others show that adding structure harms generation quality. Moreover, prior work rarely examines multi-hop relays, where copy fidelity over several steps matters more than one-shot generation. The authors want a controlled, quantitative understanding of how message format affects multi-hop transmission, especially under different model strengths and cognitive loads.

Method: They build a controlled relay testbed where briefs containing twelve atomic facts are passed through a chain of LLM agents for six hops. At each hop, the message is re-encoded in one of five formats: free natural language, precision-instructed natural language, JSON, triples, or key-value. Outputs are graded by a fixed strong model against programmatically generated ground truth. They vary (1) the relay model tier (strong vs 1.5B ‘weak’), (2) whether extra cognitive load is added per hop, and (3) whether an explicit wrong value is injected in a paired-fork setting to track how errors propagate.

Result: For strong models with faithful-relay instructions, multi-hop transmission is nearly lossless across formats, contradicting the usual “telephone game” failure narrative; imposing extra cognitive load increases generation cost by 24–53% but does not significantly change fidelity across formats (within about ±1.8 points). For a weaker 1.5B model, format choice matters much more: the spread in six-hop recall between formats grows from 2.3 to 20.5 points. Rigid structured formats pay an “encoding toll” (harder to serialize accurately), but JSON with a fixed-key schema gains drift resistance over hops, which changes which format is best mid-trajectory. In error-injection tests, once a wrong value appears, it almost always persists to the end (83–100% of chains) at the same rate as correct values, and without harming neighboring facts, across all formats.

Conclusion: Message-format effects depend strongly on relay strength. Strong relays can faithfully pass information over many hops regardless of format, even under added cognitive load, though at increased cost. For weaker relays, formats trade off initial encoding difficulty against resistance to drift, and a fixed-key JSON schema can outperform others over multiple hops. Structured formats act as faithful, error-localizing channels but do not correct errors: once a wrong value is encoded, it is preserved just like a correct one. Therefore, format choice should be based on the weakest model in the pipeline rather than on one-shot generation results.

Abstract: When LLM agents hand off information to one another, does the message format matter? Two literatures disagree: format-optimization work reports that structured messages cut cost without hurting accuracy, while format-restriction work finds that imposing structure degrades generation -- and neither measures what happens when a message traverses multiple hops, where copy fidelity, not one-shot generation, dominates. We introduce a controlled relay testbed: briefs of twelve programmatically generated atomic facts are re-encoded hop-by-hop in five formats (free NL, precision-instructed NL, JSON, triples, key-value) over six hops, scored by a fixed strong grader against programmatic ground truth, across two relay-capability tiers, a cognitive-load condition, and a paired-fork error injection. We find that message-format effects are tier-dependent. (i) Under faithful-relay instructions a strong relay is nearly lossless -- the documented "telephone-game" collapse does not occur -- and adding per-hop cognitive load leaves format-level fidelity unchanged (within +/-1.8 points) while raising generation cost by 24-53%. (ii) Under a weak (1.5B) relay the across-format spread of six-hop recall grows by a factor of 8.7 (from 2.3 to 20.5 points), driven by two opposing mechanisms -- an encoding toll paid by the rigid formats and drift resistance specific to the fixed-key JSON schema -- that flip the format ranking in transit. (iii) In a paired-fork injection, an injected wrong value, once present, persists to the final hop in 83-100% of chains in every format, closely matching each format's retention of the true value, with no detectable collateral damage to neighboring facts. Structure buys a faithful, error-localizing channel -- not an error-correcting code -- and format choice should follow the weakest relay in the pipeline.

</details>


### [96] [Boltzmann MapReduce: A Partition-Function Reduce for Forkable Sandboxes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09689)
*Yossi Eliaz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Under local asymptotic normality, each data chunk’s confidence density behaves like a Gibbs–Boltzmann distribution with inverse temperature equal to its sample size, making MapReduce aggregation equivalent to combining independent Boltzmann factors, whose mode corresponds to precision-weighted pooling; frequentist consistency appears as the zero-temperature limit.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper aims to reinterpret statistical confidence densities, especially in distributed or chunked data settings (like MapReduce), using concepts from statistical physics. By doing so, it provides an intuitive and mathematically structured way to understand how information from different data chunks combines and how large-sample properties (like consistency) emerge.

Method: Assume local asymptotic normality so that, for a chunk of size n, the likelihood or confidence density over the parameter θ can be approximated by an exponential of a quadratic form, which is then written as a Gibbs–Boltzmann measure exp(-βE(θ)) with β=n. Show that, in the Gaussian/linear case, the Boltzmann factors from disjoint chunks multiply, giving a global partition function Z=∫∏_k h_k(θ)dθ. Analyze the mode of this product to demonstrate it coincides with precision-weighted (inverse-variance) pooling and study the behavior as n→∞ (T→0).

Result: Under LAN, each worker’s confidence density is asymptotically a Gibbs–Boltzmann distribution with inverse temperature equal to the local sample size. For Gaussian/linear models, this representation is exact. The product of independent chunk-wise Boltzmann factors corresponds to the MapReduce “reduce” step and yields a partition function whose mode is exactly the classical precision-weighted estimator. As sample sizes grow, the effective temperature goes to zero, and the distribution concentrates on the true parameter (consistency).

Conclusion: Statistical inference in a distributed setting can be naturally framed in thermodynamic terms: each data subset defines a Gibbs–Boltzmann factor, aggregation is a partition function, and classical estimators emerge as modes of the resulting energy landscape. Frequentist consistency corresponds to a zero-temperature limit where the distribution collapses around the true parameter. This unifies concepts from statistical physics and asymptotic statistics, especially for Gaussian/linear models and approximately under LAN.

Abstract: To leading order under local asymptotic normality (LAN), the confidence density a worker emits over a chunk of size $n$ is a Gibbs--Boltzmann measure $\exp\{-βE(θ)\}$ whose inverse temperature is the sample size, $β=n$. Three consequences are exact in the Gaussian/linear case and first-order otherwise: disjoint chunks carry independent Boltzmann factors, so the MapReduce \emph{reduce}, read literally, is a partition function $Z=\int\prod_k h_k\,dθ$ whose mode is precision-weighted (inverse-variance) pooling; frequentist consistency is the zero-temperature limit $T=1/n\to0$

</details>


### [97] [Interpreting Latent CoT Reasoning as Dynamical Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09698)
*Sabari Iyyappan Duraipandian,Shreya Sanjay Boyane,Manju Nagesh,Jerome Francis,Archana Vaidheeswaran,Kevin Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how latent chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning evolves over time in language models by treating hidden states as trajectories in representation space and analyzing their dynamical properties.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing latent reasoning methods like CODI and COCONUT keep multiple candidate reasoning traces superimposed in hidden space, making them harder to interpret than explicit chain-of-thought which follows a single visible reasoning path. Prior mechanistic interpretability work identifies phenomena like compression and superposition but does not explain how reasoning unfolds step by step in these latent methods. The authors want to understand and characterize the temporal evolution and stability of latent reasoning processes.

Method: They model the sequence of latent tokens as a trajectory in representation space and apply tools from dynamical systems analysis. Quantitative metrics include step-to-step change, direction consistency of the trajectory, and Lyapunov sensitivity to perturbations. They complement these with qualitative visualizations using dimensionality reduction and dynamical embedding methods such as UMAP, DMD, and PHATE. They apply this framework to compare different latent CoT methods: CODI, COCONUT, and SIM-CoT supervision.

Result: They find that latent CoT dynamics are structured and non-random, and can be divided into two distinct stability classes. CODI behaves like a stable attractor where trajectories converge, whereas COCONUT behaves like an unstable expanding system with diverging trajectories. Adding SIM-CoT supervision makes both methods’ behaviors more tightly focused (reduced variability) but does not change the fundamental type of dynamics each exhibits.

Conclusion: The proposed dynamical-systems-based framework improves interpretability of latent CoT reasoning by revealing its stability properties and trajectory structure in representation space. It shows that different latent reasoning methods have qualitatively different dynamical behaviors and that supervision can refine, but not fundamentally alter, these patterns. These insights can be used to guide the design and tuning of latent reasoning approaches to achieve better and more reliable performance.

Abstract: Recent latent reasoning methods, such as CODI and COCONUT, face a fundamental interpretability problem: they maintain multiple superimposed candidate traces in the hidden space at each step, unlike explicit- CoT, which follows a single transparent reasoning trace. Existing mechanistic methods show compression, shortcuts, and superposition without explaining how reasoning evolves across latent steps. To address this gap, we model latent token sequences as trajectories in representation space and apply dynamical systems analysis to characterize the evolution of reasoning. Using quantitative measures, such as step-to-step change, direction consistency, and Lyapunov sensitivity, alongside qualitative projections, such as UMAP and DMD/PHATE, we show that latent CoT exhibits structured, non-random dynamics with two distinct stability classes. CODI behaves as a stable attractor, while COCONUT behaves as an unstable expanding system, and SIM-CoT supervision tightens both behaviors without changing the underlying dynamics. This framework advances the interpretability of latent CoT reasoning dynamics and provides actionable insights for improving latent reasoning performance. Code1 and Project page2 available online.

### [98] [YUKTI: From Natural-Language Situations to Robust, Verifiable Decisions An Uncertainty-Typed Proposition IR, Assumption-Robust Pareto Frontiers, and a Regret Certificate](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09706)
*Suyash Mishra*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: YUKTI is a robust decision-optimization framework where LLMs build uncertainty-aware problem representations instead of single-shot point-estimate optimization models, yielding decisions with much lower regret under misspecification.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing NL-to-optimization pipelines (NL4Opt, OptiMUS, ORLM, OR-LLM-Agent) translate language descriptions into single-objective optimization problems with point estimates for all coefficients and then solve them once. This is dangerous in high-stakes settings (budget, clinical, regulated decisions) because every numeric coefficient encodes uncertain assumptions. Plans that are optimal only under exactly correct assumptions are fragile and can incur high regret when reality deviates from these guesses. There is a need for an autoformulation approach that explicitly models structural and parametric uncertainty, preserves provenance and traceability, and yields decisions that remain good across a range of plausible assumptions, not just a single guessed model.

Method: The authors propose YUKTI, a framework that changes the representation and solution pipeline. Instead of a single deterministic optimization model, YUKTI builds a typed-proposition graph where nodes and relationships carry (1) shape priors, (2) uncertainty over coefficients, and (3) provenance information. It routes each subproblem to an appropriate solver type (exact, nonlinear, or evolutionary). Stages are coupled via a distributional Pareto hand-off rather than a single optimal point. They introduce Assumption-Robust Pareto Frontiers (ARPF), in which they repeatedly resample the underlying assumptions—including structural perturbations modeled as epsilon-contamination—and evaluate how frequently each candidate action remains Pareto-efficient; this survival frequency defines a robustness score rho. They prove a bound linking rho directly to decision regret. They also provide auditable traces of the formulation and generate a benchmark-faithful synthetic data foundation (SRJANA) when no real benchmark data exist.

Result: Through three validation studies, YUKTI shows substantial robustness gains. (1) In controlled misspecification experiments, the robust compromise solution reduces both mean and tail regret by over 90% relative to a naive single-point plan. (2) In a real regulated commercial decision, YUKTI optimizes choices that respect a legally constrained action space and expresses downside risk in monetary terms (euros). (3) On a public dataset with 41,188 logged decisions, an out-of-sample backtest shows YUKTI’s policies outperform the historical status quo by 34% and a naive point-estimate rule by 4%, while also mitigating the optimizer’s curse. A head-to-head comparison shows that both an LLM given correct numerical parameters and a traditional single-objective optimizer incur around 47 times the held-out regret of YUKTI, indicating that LLMs are better used as formulators than as solvers.

Conclusion: YUKTI demonstrates that shifting LLM-based optimization from single-shot, point-estimate formulations to an uncertainty-aware, graph-based representation with Assumption-Robust Pareto Frontiers drastically improves decision robustness and reduces regret. The framework does not rely on novel solvers or state-of-the-art benchmark wins; its contribution is architectural and conceptual—combining standard solvers with explicit modeling of assumption uncertainty, causal coupling limits, and traceable provenance. The results indicate that for complex, long-range-coupled decisions, forward-only optimization pipelines become unsound and should be replaced or augmented by backward-induction causal policies, and that LLMs should primarily serve as formulation engines rather than optimization engines.

Abstract: Language models turn a worded situation into a numeric plan, and the dominant pipelines (NL4Opt, OptiMUS, ORLM, OR-LLM-Agent) commit to a single objective and point-valued coefficients, then solve once. For decisions that allocate real budget, effort, or clinical attention, that confidence is the failure mode: every objectified number is an assumption, and a plan optimal only if the guesses are exactly right is fragile -- mimicry of computation. YUKTI changes the target of autoformulation. Its representation is a typed-proposition graph whose relationships carry shape priors, coefficient uncertainty, and provenance. YUKTI routes each stage to an exact, nonlinear, or evolutionary solver; couples stages by a distributional Pareto hand-off; and introduces Assumption-Robust Pareto Frontiers (ARPF), resampling assumptions (including structural epsilon-contamination) to score how often each action survives (rho). We prove a bound making rho an exact factor of decision regret, add auditable traceability, and synthesize a benchmark-faithful data foundation when none exists (SRJANA). We validate three ways: under controlled misspecification the robust compromise cuts mean and tail regret by over 90% versus a naive point plan; on a regulated commercial decision we optimize inside a lawful action space and price the downside in euros; and on a real public dataset of 41,188 decisions an out-of-sample backtest beats the logged status quo by 34% and a naive point rule by 4% while reducing the optimizer's curse. The solvers are standard; we claim no benchmark-SOTA win. A head-to-head shows an LLM given the correct numbers, and single-objective optimization, both incur about 47x the held-out regret of YUKTI -- an LLM is a formulator, not a solver. Under long-range causal coupling, the forward hand-off becomes unsound, locating where it must become a backward-induction causal policy.

</details>


### [99] [GES-TSP: Graph Edge Sparsification for TSP](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09708)
*Tianfeng Chen,Xianyue Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Learning-based graph edge sparsification method (GES) for Euclidean TSP that removes most edges while preserving near-optimal tours and greatly speeding up exact solvers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Exact solution of large-scale TSP instances is extremely expensive due to the dense complete graph (O(n^2) edges). Classical sparsification relies on hand‑crafted, instance‑agnostic heuristics, which may remove useful edges or keep too many unnecessary ones. There is a need for an adaptive method that exploits problem geometry and instance‑specific structure to safely prune edges while preserving near‑optimal solutions, thereby reducing computational burden.

Method: Introduce Graph Edge Sparsification (GES), a learning‑based sparsification framework tailored to Euclidean TSP. GES uses geometric structural information and ideas from combinatorial optimization to learn, for each new instance, which edges are likely to participate in good tours. The method outputs a reduced graph (sparsification graph) with only a small subset of edges retained. This sparsified graph is then passed to an exact or high‑quality TSP solver. The approach is trained to balance aggressive pruning with a small optimality gap, and to generalize across instances (e.g., MATILDA, TSPLIB).

Result: On the MATILDA dataset, GES can safely prune up to 95% of the edges while maintaining a solution gap within 1% of the optimal tour length. On some large‑scale instances, it achieves pruning rates above 99% with an optimality gap still below 1%. The reduced graphs lead to significant acceleration of the solving process. The approach also generalizes well to standard benchmarks such as TSPLIB.

Conclusion: Learning‑based, instance‑adaptive sparsification of Euclidean TSP graphs is highly effective: it can drastically reduce the number of edges while keeping tour quality within 1% of optimal and speeding up exact solvers. This shows that combining geometric information with machine learning and combinatorial optimization offers a powerful alternative to fixed heuristic sparsification, with strong pruning performance and robust generalization across datasets.

Abstract: Solving large-scale instances of the Traveling Salesman Problem (TSP) exactly is computationally expensive. Researchers often employ graph sparsification methods to improve computational efficiency. Traditional sparsification methods typically rely on fixed heuristics and fail to fully exploit instance-specific structural information. In this paper, we propose Graph Edge Sparsification (GES), a learning-based sparsification approach for Euclidean TSP. By incorporating geometric structural information and combinatorial optimization technology, our proposed method adaptively generates a sparsification graph for different instances, significantly reducing the graph size and accelerating the solving process. Experimental results demonstrate that our sparsification method can prune up to 95% of edges on the MATILDA dataset, while keeping the solution gap within 1% of the optimal value. Moreover, our approach exhibits strong generalization capability on the TSPLIB benchmark.In some large-scale instances, the pruning rate exceeds 99%, while the optimality gap remains below 1%.

### [100] [The Verifier is the Curriculum: Execution-Gated Self-Distillation for Cross-Family Game Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09709)
*Chenyu Zhou,Qiliang Jiang,Shuning Wu,Xu Zhou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that filtering code-generation outputs using a strict, deterministic runtime launch check (rather than a learned judge) and then self-distilling from the successful runs markedly improves game project generation, demonstrating that verifier design effectively defines the learning curriculum.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Learned judges used to train code or project generators can be gamed: models learn to optimize proxy signals that please the judge without truly improving executable, functional artifacts. The authors want a training signal that is deterministic, ungameable, and directly tied to real functionality. They explore whether such a signal, based on whether a generated game project cleanly launches in a headless engine, can drive meaningful improvements via self-distillation, especially in generalizing to unseen game families.

Method: They introduce a strict-launch filter that only accepts generations whose full Godot game project launches cleanly in a headless engine. Using this as a gate, they run rejection-sampling self-distillation over multiple rounds on the GameCraft-Bench task, where the model must map a natural-language specification to a complete Godot project. Starting from a 14B base model (Qwen3-14B+LoRA), they iteratively generate candidates, keep only strict-launch successes, and fine-tune on them. They construct several controls: (1) gold-duplication, where they add exact copies of gold data instead of strict-launch data, (2) count-matched decomposition to separate the effects of data quantity vs. quality, and (3) a variant that swaps strict-launch for a lenient BUILD check that nearly all generations pass. They also track a secondary ungameable metric: headless execution grounding, measuring how functionally grounded generations are during execution.

Result: After three rounds of strict-launch-gated self-distillation, the 14B model’s clean-generation rate on four unseen game families improves from 8.8% to 42.2% per candidate, and best-of-K coverage of tasks increases from 18/25 to 25/25, matching the gold upper bound. Each round’s gain is statistically significant. The gold-duplication control underperforms the base model (5.6% vs. 8.8%), indicating benefits are not from simply adding more data. The count-matched analysis attributes the first big improvement roughly equally to data quality and quantity. Replacing strict-launch with a nearly-always-passing BUILD check eliminates the improvement, showing that verifier precision, not just optimization, is crucial. The headless execution grounding metric rises steadily across rounds, and at equal data budgets strict-launch produces many more grounded candidates than gold-duplication (16 vs. 5), confirming the improvements are functional, not trivial or empty projects.

Conclusion: A deterministic, precise runtime verifier (whether a generated project launches cleanly and executes in headless mode) can serve as a powerful, ungameable training signal for code/game generation. When used as a gate in rejection-sampling self-distillation, it yields substantial improvements in both success rate and coverage, especially for generalization to novel game families. The negative results from gold-duplication and lenient BUILD checks show that what truly matters is the precision and functional relevance of the verifier, not just more data or any pass/fail signal. The core takeaway is encapsulated as “the verifier is the curriculum”: the specific properties that the verifier certifies are exactly what the model learns to satisfy.

Abstract: Post-training a code generator against a learned judge can optimize proxy features that raise the score without improving the artifact. We study the opposite signal: a deterministic, judge-free, ungameable filter -- whether a generated project launches cleanly under a headless engine (strict-launch). Under this gate, rejection-sampling self-distillation compounds out-of-family generalization. On GameCraft-Bench (mapping a natural-language brief to a complete Godot project), a 14B model (Qwen3-14B+LoRA) distilled under strict-launch raises clean generation on four unseen game families from 8.8% to 42.2% per-candidate and best-of-K coverage from 18/25 to 25/25 (the gold ceiling) over three rounds, each a significant gain (p=0.0019, p<1e-4, p<1e-4). The gain is not from merely adding data: an exactly-matched gold-duplication control regresses below the base model (5.6% vs. 8.8%, p=0.019), while a count-matched decomposition splits the round-1-to-2 jump into comparable quality (+8.8pp) and quantity (+8.5pp) channels. Most directly, rerunning the loop with only the filter swapped -- the lenient BUILD check, which passes 99.9% of generations, in place of the launch gate -- erases the gain entirely (back to base, p=1e-3 vs. the launch-gated round), isolating verifier precision rather than the optimizer. A second ungameable signal, headless execution grounding, rises monotonically across rounds and yields far more grounded candidates than gold-duplication at a matched budget (16 vs. 5), confirming the gains are functional, not launch-but-empty. Game generation is a verifiable testbed for one lesson: the verifier is the curriculum -- what it certifies is what the model learns.

</details>


### [101] [Closed-Loop Control with Rule-Aligned Small Language Models and Multi-Agent Self-Correction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09713)
*Yuchen Wang,Javal Vyas,Tong Liu,Mehmet Mercangoz*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper explores whether a compact small language model, coupled with a validator and reprompting loop, can reliably generate control actions from natural-language requirements for industrial systems, achieving high action alignment and robust control with low latency suitable for edge deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autonomous industrial operation requires the ability to automatically synthesize and reconfigure control policies from natural-language requirements without extensive manual redesign. Large cloud-based models are often too slow, opaque, and data-sensitive for real-time, edge-level closed-loop control. There is a need to determine if smaller, more efficient language models can handle control reasoning when combined with plant-aware validation, enabling practical, reconfigurable autonomous control at the edge.

Method: The authors retrain a compact Qwen2.5-1.5B small language model for control reasoning using Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO). They embed this SLM within a validator-guided correction loop that includes: (i) an action agent that proposes control actions based on requirements and state, (ii) a symbolic/digital-twin-style validation layer that checks candidate actions against plant dynamics and safety constraints, and (iii) a reprompting agent that iteratively refines the SLM outputs when validation fails. They evaluate the framework on randomized thermal-control simulations consisting of 30 experiments with 500 steps each, measuring action-alignment accuracy, inference latency, and robustness under symbolic re-mapping.

Result: In randomized thermal-control simulations, the SLM+validator framework attains an average action-alignment accuracy of 91.5%, with per-case accuracies ranging from 86.3% to 100%. The mean inference latency is 3.84 seconds, indicating feasibility for certain edge applications. When symbolic re-mapping is applied—changing the mapping between tokens and physical actions—the system still preserves a 95% in-range rate for controlled variables, demonstrating robust physical regulation even when token-level action agreement declines.

Conclusion: The study concludes that a small language model, when retrained for control reasoning and coupled with a digital-twin-style validator and reprompting loop, can reliably generate valid control actions from natural-language specifications with relatively low latency. The strong performance in thermal-control simulations and robustness to symbolic re-mapping suggest that SLM+validator architectures are a practical and promising approach for reconfigurable autonomous control in edge industrial settings, mitigating some of the drawbacks of large cloud-based models.

Abstract: A key step toward autonomous industrial operation is the ability to create and reconfigure control policies from natural-language requirement specifications, with minimal or no manual redesign. In this setting, policy generation by AI agents can be a credible path when paired with a plant-aware validator (e.g., a digital twin) that can check generated candidate actions before execution. However, practical deployment is constrained by inference latency and compute footprint: large cloud-based models are often too slow, opaque, or data-sensitive for edge closed-loop use. This work investigates whether a compact Small Language Model (SLM) can be retrained for control reasoning and embedded in a validator-guided correction loop. We use a Qwen2.5-1.5B model aligned via Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO), combined with (i) an action agent, (ii) a symbolic/digital-twin-style validation layer, and (iii) a reprompting agent that iteratively steers outputs toward valid actions. In randomized thermal-control simulations (30 experiments with 500 steps each), the framework achieves 91.5% average action-alignment accuracy (86.3%--100% across cases) at 3.84\,s mean inference latency. Under symbolic re-mapping, it maintains a 95% in-range rate, indicating robust physical regulation despite reduced token-level agreement. These results support SLM+validator architectures as a practical path toward reconfigurable autonomous control at the edge.

### [102] [Feedback-Coupled Memory Systems in Continuous Time](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09714)
*Stefano Grassi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper instantiates the Feedback-Coupled Memory Systems (FCMS) architecture by giving concrete definitions to the agent and environment update operators, derives a global stability (dissipativity) threshold for the resulting closed-loop system, and validates it via small-scale simulation and mean-field analysis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The original FCMS framework formalizes closed-loop coordination with abstract operators, but leaves the core dynamics of agent updating (f_i) and environmental evolution (Ψ) unspecified. Without concrete, well-posed definitions of these operators and corresponding stability conditions, FCMS remains largely conceptual and cannot be tested, simulated, or applied. The paper aims to fill this gap by grounding FCMS in specific economic and physical principles and deriving rigorous stability thresholds that clarify how memory and feedback must be balanced for stable coordination.

Method: 1) Define the agent update operator f_i using a Mechanism-Based Intelligence (MBI) framework, where each agent updates locally via a decentralized price mechanism consistent with economic principles. 2) Define the environmental update operator Ψ via a Coupled Memory Graph Process (CMGP), which treats the environment as a non-Markovian physical substrate encoding trajectory history and responding coherently without external forcing. 3) Embed these definitions in a continuous-time FCMS instantiation. 4) Analyze the resulting dynamical system to derive Lyapunov-based global dissipativity conditions, yielding an explicit, computable stability inequality relating memory dissipation and feedback gain. 5) Validate the theory through numerical simulations with N=2 agents and a mean-field limit with N=10^6 agents to test both the stability threshold and behavior beyond it.

Result: The continuous-time FCMS instantiated with MBI agents and CMGP environment yields a Lyapunov global dissipativity condition given by the computable threshold 4β^2 < 2ηγγ^2 (as stated: 4β^2 < 2ηγ^2), which extends: (a) the discrete-time FCMS stability condition 4ηβ^2 < γ, and (b) CMGP's physical bifurcation threshold α_c = 1/K. This shows that the new model is consistent with and generalizes previous results. Numerical experiments with a small system (N=2) and a large-scale mean-field regime (N=10^6) empirically verify the predicted stability boundary and demonstrate a self-reinforcing coordination cascade when the inequality is violated.

Conclusion: By specifying the previously abstract FCMS operators with Mechanism-Based Intelligence for agents and a Coupled Memory Graph Process for the environment, the paper turns FCMS into a concrete continuous-time dynamical system with an explicit Lyapunov stability threshold. The unified condition connects and generalizes earlier FCMS and CMGP results and leads to the conceptual insight that stable coordination requires memory dissipation to dominate feedback gain as a universal organizing principle. Simulations across scales corroborate both the stability threshold and the emergence of coordination cascades when it is breached.

Abstract: The Feedback-Coupled Memory Systems (FCMS) architecture formalizes closed-loop coordination through four abstract operators, two of which - the agent update operator $f_i$ and the environmental update operator $Ψ$ - are left axiomatically undefined in the original framework. To address this, $f_i$ is defined by Mechanism-Based Intelligence (MBI), where agents update locally through a decentralized price mechanism and economic principles, and $Ψ$ is defined by the Coupled Memory Graph Process (CMGP), a non-Markovian framework where the environment is treated as a physical substrate that records and responds to trajectory history coherently without external forcing. The resulting continuous-time FCMS instantiation achieves Lyapunov global dissipativity governed by the computable threshold $4β^2 < 2ημγ^2$. This generalizes both the discrete FCMS stability condition $4ηβ^2 < γ$ and CMGP's physical bifurcation threshold $α_c = 1/K$, confirming that memory dissipation must outpace feedback gain as a universal organizing principle. Numerical simulation with $N=2$ agents and mean-field validation at $N=10^6$ confirm the stability threshold and the self-reinforcing coordination cascade that emerges when it is violated.

### [103] [AGM-like Paraconsistent Partial Meet Abductive Expansion Operation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09729)
*Ulisses Franceschi Eliano*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces a new AGM-style operation for expanding beliefs by abduction within a paraconsistent logic, allowing contradictory explanatory hypotheses without trivializing the belief set.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical AGM belief revision and Pagnucco’s abductive expansion assume consistency; when contradictions arise, the system trivializes (everything becomes derivable). Many real-world reasoning tasks require handling inconsistent but non-trivial information and abductive hypotheses. The author aims to extend AGM-like abductive expansion into a paraconsistent setting so that conflicting explanations can be incorporated rationally.

Method: Building on Pagnucco’s 1996 AGM-like abductive expansion and a taxonomy of abductive reasoning inspired by Atocha Aliseda, the author defines a new expansion operation in the framework of a specific paraconsistent logic, RCbr, an LFI (Logic of Formal Inconsistencies). They formulate postulates for this operation and give a transitively relational partial-meet construction, adapting AGM-style representation theorems to the paraconsistent case while exploiting properties of RCbr, especially self-extensionality (replacement).

Result: The paper defines a concrete paraconsistent AGM-like abductive expansion operator (within a system named AGMpabd), proves that it satisfies a set of rationality postulates, and shows that it can incorporate mutually inconsistent abductive hypotheses without causing trivialization of the belief set. A partial-meet style representation (using relational structures) is established for this operator in RCbr.

Conclusion: The author concludes that this is, as far as they know, the first paraconsistent AGM-like abductive expansion operation in the literature. It demonstrates that AGM-style abductive belief change can be successfully generalized to LFIs such as RCbr, preserving key structural properties while tolerating inconsistencies. However, in this first system (AGMpabd) the specifically paraconsistent operators of negation and consistency are not yet given a substantive epistemic role; this will be addressed by an enhanced operation in a subsequent paper (AGMcircabd).

Abstract: In his 1996 doctoral thesis, Maurice Pagnucco created the first AGM-like abductive expansion operation. Taking his operation as a basis, as well as a taxonomy -- inspired by Atocha Aliseda -- responsible for highlighting and formalizing the main components of abductive reasoning, the main aim of this paper is to present a new paraconsistent AGM-like abductive expansion operation -- capable of assimilating contradictory explanatory hypotheses without trivialization and the consequent absurd epistemic state -- with its postulates and its transitively relational partial meet construction. To a large extent, the formal development presented in this paper was only made possible by the recent creation of the paraconsistent logic RCbr, an LFI (Logics of Formal Inconsistencies) that establishes properties especially relevant to belief revision contexts, in particular, the ability to be self-extensional -- i.e., to satisfy the replacement property. This is the first of two papers: the paraconsistent abductive expansion operation announced here -- which is part of a new system called AGMpabd -- despite bringing many interesting features, does not assign any relevant epistemic role to the paraconsistent operators of negation and consistency. Only in a second paper will an analogous paraconsistent abductive expansion operation -- which is part of another new system, AGMcircabd -- be enhanced in this direction. Nevertheless, to the best of my knowledge, the operation developed in this paper is the first of its kind in the AGM literature.

### [104] [Coresets Before Score Sets: Evaluation-Unsupervised Prompt Subset Selection for LLM Benchmarks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09739)
*Jihan Yao,Gantavya Bhatt,Arnav Das,Peter Jin,Ke Bao,Qiaolin Yu,Khushi Bhardwaj,Chang Su,Jialei Wang,Yikai Zhu,Sugam Devare,Damon Mosk-Aoyama,Zhen Dong,Venkat Krishna Srinivasan,Yineng Zhang,Oleksii Kuchaiev,Jiantao Jiao,Banghua Zhu,Jeff Bilmes*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes an evaluation-unsupervised method to select a small, representative subset of prompts (a benchmark coreset) across many LLM benchmarks using submodular optimization, showing that a facility-location objective over prompt embeddings preserves model rankings and scores better than prior methods and works well even when score data is available.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Evaluating large language models on many benchmarks and tens of thousands of prompts is computationally expensive and time-consuming. Existing coreset or benchmark selection methods often rely on model evaluation scores, operate at the level of whole benchmarks rather than individual prompts, or do not scale well to large heterogeneous benchmark suites. There is a need for a principled, efficient way to compress benchmark suites while preserving induced model rankings and scores, ideally without requiring expensive evaluations to drive the selection.

Method: The authors frame benchmark compression as a coreset selection problem over prompts across multiple benchmarks and adopt submodular subset selection to exploit its theoretical and algorithmic benefits. They design and compare several submodular objective functions, including determinantal point process (DPP)-based diversity objectives, submodular mutual information, and facility location (FL) functions. Their main approach is an evaluation-unsupervised method where selection uses only inexpensive semantic embeddings of prompts, not model scores. They conduct experiments on a new large-scale suite of 35 heterogeneous benchmarks across five capability categories, involving 18 frontier LLMs and over 61K prompts, and also test a variant where limited evaluation scores are available and only a few whole benchmarks must be chosen.

Result: On the large benchmark suite, the facility location objective applied to prompt embeddings consistently preserves LLM scores and rankings more accurately than twelve diverse baselines, including both score-based and diversity-based methods, across multiple coreset sizes. In the setting where the task is to select a small number of entire benchmarks and substantial score data is available (e.g., MMLU and MTEB leaderboards), the same FL-based submodular objective matches or surpasses state-of-the-art benchmark selection approaches while requiring far less computation. These results hold across different budget regimes and model sets, showing robustness of the proposed approach.

Conclusion: Submodular optimization, and specifically facility-location-style objectives over semantic representations of prompts, provides an effective and computationally efficient framework for compressing LLM benchmarks. Even without access to model evaluation outcomes, the method can select small prompt coresets that largely preserve the rankings and scores of a wide range of LLMs across heterogeneous benchmarks. When evaluation data is available, the same objective remains competitive or superior to existing methods for selecting entire benchmarks. Overall, submodularity emerges as a strong, general tool for benchmark compression in LLM evaluation.

Abstract: We study LLM benchmark coreset selection: selecting a small subset of prompts over multiple benchmarks whose induced model scores and rankings approximate those obtained from the full benchmark suite. In evaluation-unsupervised benchmark coreset selection (our approach), the selection algorithm uses no model evaluation outcomes, and operates on a fine granularity by producing subsets of prompts over multiple benchmarks rather than producing a sub-collection of entire benchmarks. We use submodular subset selection, and we develop and evaluate many different submodular functions for this purpose, including determinantal point process (DPP) based approaches, submodular mutual information functions, and facility location-based functions. On a new large-scale suite of 35 heterogeneous benchmarks spanning five different capability categories, 18 frontier LLMs, and over 61K prompts, we find that the facility location (FL) function operating exclusively on inexpensive semantic prompt embeddings preserves LLM scores better than twelve separate score-based and diversity-based baselines, across a range of coreset budgets. Moreover, we show our proposed objective is not limited to the evaluation-unsupervised regime: in the setting where only a handful of whole benchmarks must be selected and a large amount of model scores are available, the same objective matches or outperforms state-of-the-art baselines on the MMLU and MTEB leaderboards, while being substantially cheaper to compute. Together, our results suggest that submodularity, in general, is a strong and reliable tool for benchmark compression.

### [105] [A Dynamic Scene Interaction Reasoning Framework for Scene-level Lane-Change Intention and Trajectory Prediction of Multiple Interacting Vehicles](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09740)
*Joshua Kofi Asamoah,Blessing Agyei Kyem,Eugene Denteh,Armstrong Aboah*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safe motion planning for ADAS and autonomous vehicles needs reliable predictions of how all nearby vehicles will move. Existing methods either focus on one target vehicle’s lane change or give multi-agent trajectory forecasts without clearly indicating each vehicle’s maneuver. There is a gap for a model that jointly reasons about multi-vehicle interactions, explicit maneuver intentions, and their effect on future motion at the scene level.

Method: Model the traffic scene as a time-varying interaction graph with vehicles as nodes and explicit edge features capturing spatial and kinematic relations. Apply temporal graph-attention message passing to encode evolving inter-vehicle dependencies and early pre-maneuver cues. Use an intention-guided decoder that conditions trajectory generation on predicted lane-change maneuvers for each vehicle. Add a scene-level consistency loss to encourage multi-vehicle futures that are mutually compatible. Train and evaluate on NGSIM I-80, NGSIM US-101, and highD datasets, including ablation, sensitivity, robustness, and qualitative analyses.

Result: On NGSIM I-80 and US-101, DSiGAT achieves lane-change intention prediction accuracies of 90.12% and 90.97%, respectively. It reduces trajectory RMSE by up to 52.94% compared to the strongest baseline, and yields lower inter-agent collision rates and joint displacement errors, indicating more coherent multi-agent predictions. Ablation and other analyses show each component contributes to performance gains.

Conclusion: Jointly modeling lane-change intentions and trajectories for all vehicles via a dynamic scene graph with temporal graph attention improves both accuracy and consistency of multi-agent traffic prediction. The proposed DSiGAT framework delivers state-of-the-art intention and trajectory forecasting, generates more coherent scene-level futures, and its components are empirically validated through extensive experiments and analyses.

Abstract: Safe motion planning in advanced driver-assistance systems and autonomous vehicles requires an accurate understanding of how the surrounding traffic scene is likely to evolve. However, many existing lane-change prediction methods remain centered on a single target vehicle, while multi-agent forecasting approaches often describe scene evolution only through future positions and provide limited explicit information about the maneuver associated with each vehicle. This study proposes a dynamic scene graph attention framework that predicts the lane-change intention and future trajectory of every relevant vehicle within a local traffic scene. The scene is represented as a time-varying interaction graph in which vehicles are modeled as nodes and their spatial and kinematic relationships are encoded through explicit edge features. Temporal graph-attention message passing captures evolving inter-vehicle dependencies and pre-maneuver cues, while an intention-guided decoder links each predicted maneuver to its corresponding future motion. A scene-level consistency objective further encourages compatible multi-vehicle futures. Experiments on the NGSIM I-80, NGSIM US-101, and highD datasets demonstrate consistent improvements over competing baselines. DSiGAT achieves intention prediction accuracies of 90.12% and 90.97% on NGSIM I-80 and US-101, respectively, and reduces trajectory RMSE by up to 52.94% relative to the strongest baseline. It also produces lower inter-agent collision rates and joint displacement errors, indicating more coherent scene-level predictions. Ablation, sensitivity, robustness, and qualitative analyses further validate the contribution of the proposed components and the effectiveness of the scene-focused formulation.

### [106] [Scaffolding the Strategist: Architecture-Dependent Reasoning Interventions in Hotelling Spatial Markets](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09743)
*Pratyush Singh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper tests whether different structured reasoning prompts help or hurt strategic economic reasoning in different LLM architectures, finding strong model-by-scaffold interactions and a gap between knowing the right strategy and executing it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand not only if structured reasoning prompts improve LLM reasoning in strategic economic settings, but also when they might backfire and how these effects differ across standard vs. reasoning-optimized model architectures. The authors also want a principled, quantitative way to benchmark such effects using a well-understood economic model.

Method: They use Hotelling's linear city model as a controlled testbed for strategic reasoning. Two models are evaluated: GPT-4.1-mini (instruction-following) and GPT-5-mini (reasoning-optimized). Each model is tested under five prompting conditions: an unscaffolded baseline plus four structured reasoning interventions. Performance is measured on eight carefully designed questions requiring deductive and abductive reasoning, and under three prompt framings with three repetitions each, giving 720 human-judged responses. They use statistical analysis (t-tests, effect sizes, correlations) to examine interaction effects between scaffolding type and model architecture, sensitivity to adversarial stress-testing, and the gap between declarative (identifying correct strategies) and procedural (executing them) competence.

Result: They find a significant crossover interaction between scaffolding and model type: commitment scaffolding helps the standard model but harms the reasoning model, while principled separation harms the standard model but helps the reasoning model. Both effects are statistically robust and consistent across most questions. Adversarial stress-testing degrades performance for both models, but the reasoning model is much more affected, especially on easier baseline questions. Both models exhibit a notable gap between being able to state correct strategies and being able to carry them out; principled separation closes this gap for the reasoning-optimized model but no intervention closes it for the standard model.

Conclusion: Structured reasoning prompts do not uniformly improve LLM strategic reasoning; their effects depend critically on model architecture and can even be harmful. Reasoning-optimized models interact differently with scaffolding than standard instruction-following models, and they are more vulnerable under adversarial stress. There is a structural declarative-procedural gap in current models, which can be mitigated—but only for some architectures and with specific scaffolds, such as principled separation. Designers of prompts and systems must therefore tailor reasoning interventions to the underlying model rather than assuming general benefits.

Abstract: We investigate whether structured reasoning interventions improve the strategic economic reasoning of large language models, and whether their effects depend on model architecture. Using Hotelling's linear city model as a diagnostic vehicle, we evaluate GPT-4.1-mini (a standard instruction-following model) and GPT-5-mini (a reasoning-optimized model) under five conditions - an unscaffolded baseline and four reasoning interventions - across eight questions spanning deductive and abductive reasoning, three prompt framings, and three repetitions per condition, yielding 720 individually judged responses. We find a statistically significant crossover interaction between scaffolding type and model architecture ($t(7) = 4.79$, $p = 0.002$, $d = 1.69$): commitment scaffolding improves the standard model ($+0.21$) while degrading the reasoning model ($-0.63$), and principled separation shows the opposite pattern ($-0.40$ vs. $+0.31$). Both crossovers are individually significant (commitment: $p = 0.040$; separation: $p = 0.002$) and hold across all eight questions with 7/8 directional consistency. Adversarial stress-testing harms both models, with $2.6\times$ greater degradation for the reasoning model ($-1.47$ vs. $-0.57$; $p = 0.038$), and the damage correlates negatively with baseline difficulty ($R^2 = 0.36$, $p = 0.014$). We further document a persistent declarative-procedural gap in which both models identify correct strategies at rates far exceeding their ability to execute them; separation fully closes this gap for the reasoning model while no intervention helps the standard model.

### [107] [A Theory of Least Autonomy in AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09744)
*Christophe Parisel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces and formalizes the notion of "least autonomy" for agentic AI systems as a generalization of least privilege, including metrics and graph-based methods to measure and constrain AI agents' combined influence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional least privilege focuses on limiting individual identities' permissions, which is inadequate for agentic AI systems that can coordinate, chain actions, and combine permissions across workflows and domains. The paper is motivated by the need for a more appropriate control principle that captures how AI agents can amplify their impact through composition, communication, and cross-domain interactions.

Method: The authors (1) define a compositional blast radius d(a,b) that quantifies structural separation between actions using an ultrametric tree combined with lattice-valued labels for confidentiality, integrity, and control context; (2) construct a directed agent influence graph G(theta) where edges represent potential influence via shared resources or conservative agent-to-agent communication, conditioned on a policy threshold theta; (3) develop a catalogue-radius profile to calibrate and audit the chosen threshold; and (4) define a collusion predicate based on graph reachability to detect problematic compositions such as authorization combination, decision manipulation, and cross-domain capability aggregation.

Result: The paper delivers a formal framework that (a) generalizes least privilege into least autonomy for agentic AI, (b) provides a quantitative measure of separation between actions, (c) specifies how to construct an influence graph that captures multi-agent and cross-domain influence channels, and (d) introduces a formal collusion predicate for identifying risky compositions of authorizations and capabilities. These constructs together offer a structured way to reason about and constrain AI agent autonomy at scale.

Conclusion: Least autonomy is proposed as a more suitable governing principle than least privilege for agentic AI systems. Using blast radius metrics, influence graphs parameterized by a policy threshold, and a collusion predicate over reachability, the framework aims to ensure that AI agents cannot inappropriately combine or amplify permissions and decisions across system boundaries, thereby offering enterprises a principled way to design, calibrate, and audit safer AI workflows.

Abstract: Least privilege, the principle that an identity should hold only the permissions strictly required for its task, has been a foundational primitive of access control for decades. We argue that this principle is insufficient for agentic AI systems, which do not merely hold permissions but can combine, approve, and amplify them across workflows and system boundaries.
  We propose least autonomy as an appropriate generalization and develop a formal theory. First, we define a compositional blast radius d(a,b) that measures structural separation between actions in an enterprise hierarchy, combining an ultrametric tree with lattice-valued confidentiality, integrity, and control-context labels. Second, we define a directed agent influence graph G(theta). An arc from U to V requires a directed shared-resource write-to-read meeting or a conservative undirected agent-to-agent (A2A) communication meeting, and a meeting-conditioned influence potential at or above an externally selected policy threshold theta. A catalogue-radius profile supports calibration and audit of theta. Finally, we define a collusion predicate over graph reachability that detects authorization composition, decision manipulation, and cross-domain capability composition.
