### [109] [Replicating Belief, Not Bits: Epistemic State Replication for Agentic Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09748)
*Jun He,Deying Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Paper proposes Epistemic State Replication (ESR) for agentic, stochastic distributed systems, replacing bitwise state agreement with belief-level agreement while ensuring safety via semantic constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical State Machine Replication assumes deterministic replicas that must reach identical bitwise state, which breaks down for modern agentic systems that use stochastic generative models (e.g., LLM agents). These agents can follow different reasoning paths and produce different intermediate states yet still arrive at semantically equivalent, correct decisions. Forcing bitwise agreement harms flexibility, causes loss of useful context, and limits performance. A new replication model is needed that respects stochastic reasoning while preserving safety and consistency guarantees.

Method: The authors introduce Epistemic State Replication (ESR), which redefines a node’s state as an epistemic state K = (L, B), where L is a deterministic, immutable evidence log and B is a stochastic, evolving belief lineage. They shift the replication boundary from raw data to knowledge (belief) and define new correctness properties: Semantic Linearizability and Bounded Eventual Coherence, both expressed through a semantic compatibility metric and assumptions on evidence and verifiers. They design protocols based on structured epistemic deltas for propagating derived insights and define Verifiable Semantic Rollbacks to remove faulty premises from belief lineages without causing context amnesia. They implement a prototype ESR and run simulations to test its feasibility and behavioral properties.

Result: Preliminary simulation results show that ESR is feasible under the stated assumptions (fair delivery, monotonic evidence, bounded verifier disturbance, contractive graft operator). The experiments suggest that ESR can maintain safety and bounded semantic divergence between replicas while reducing secondary cognitive faults compared to classical bitwise replication approaches. The results indicate that belief-level replication can preserve semantic agreement without enforcing strict bitwise determinism, improving flexibility and performance in agentic systems.

Conclusion: ESR provides a new replication model tailored to agentic, stochastic distributed systems by focusing on belief replication instead of bitwise state replication. By separating evidence from belief, introducing semantic correctness conditions, and supporting structured epistemic deltas and verifiable rollbacks, ESR can safely accommodate non-deterministic, model-driven agents. The initial prototype and simulations indicate that this epistemic approach is both feasible and beneficial, reducing cognitive faults while maintaining semantic consistency, and it opens directions for rethinking consistency in future agent-based infrastructures.

Abstract: In distributed systems, the classical State Machine Replication (SMR) model assumes that correct replicas execute deterministic transitions to yield identical bitwise states. However, the rise of agentic distributed systems -- where autonomous, stochastic, and model-driven agents orchestrate infrastructure -- presents scenarios where deterministic, bitwise replication is insufficient. Replicas operating with generative models may exhibit divergent reasoning paths, summaries, and token boundaries, yet reach semantically equivalent and correct operational decisions. Forcing bitwise agreement across these stochastic participants degrades execution flexibility, induces context amnesia, and limits performance.
  We argue that in such settings replicas should agree on belief, not bits. We propose Epistemic State Replication (ESR), a belief-replication layer for agentic distributed systems that shifts the replication boundary from data visibility to knowledge visibility. We formalize the epistemic node state as a pair K = (L, B) separating the deterministic, immutable evidence log (L) from the stochastic, evolving belief lineage (B). To govern execution safety, we define Semantic Linearizability, which requires operations to reflect the latest committed operational meaning within a verifier-bounded semantic compatibility metric, and Bounded Eventual Coherence, which bounds expected semantic divergence under fair delivery, monotonic evidence, bounded verifier disturbance, and a contractive graft operator. We outline protocols for propagating derived insights using structured epistemic deltas, and formalize Verifiable Semantic Rollbacks to prune faulty premises from belief lineages without inducing context amnesia. We prototype ESR and report preliminary simulation results that show feasibility under the stated assumptions and illustrate reductions in secondary cognitive faults.

</details>


### [110] [Task-Conditioned Synthetic Data Generation for Improving Machine Learning Performance in Agricultural Prediction Tasks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09751)
*Hamid Ebrahimy,Moritz Lucas,Martin Atzmueller*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a new synthetic data generation method (TCSDG) for tabular agricultural data that, when used to augment training sets, reliably improves machine‑learning performance for crop yield prediction and crop type classification.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Machine learning in agriculture often suffers from limited or incomplete labeled data, which restricts model performance. Existing synthetic data generation methods do not consistently boost performance across tasks and sites, especially for tabular agricultural data. The authors want a principled, task-aware way to generate synthetic samples that mimic the data distribution while explicitly supporting the downstream prediction task.

Method: They design a Task-Conditioned Synthetic Data Generation (TCSDG) algorithm that combines (1) a Bayesian Network generator to model the joint distribution of tabular variables with (2) a transformer-based tabular foundation model (TabICL) implementing teacher–student knowledge transfer and in-context learning. The generator is conditioned on the prediction task so that created samples align with what matters for the downstream ML model. They evaluate TCSDG on two tasks—crop yield prediction and crop type classification—over 12 sites, varying training-data fractions, synthetic data multiplication ratios, and three different predictive ML algorithms. They compare against six benchmark SDG methods.

Result: When original training data are augmented with TCSDG-generated samples, ML performance improves in 89% of crop type classification experiments and 74% of crop yield prediction experiments across all tested conditions. TCSDG outperforms six benchmark SDG algorithms and is the only method that consistently improves performance across both tasks when performance is aggregated. This demonstrates stable, task-wide benefits of the proposed approach.

Conclusion: Task-aware synthetic tabular data, generated via their TCSDG algorithm that fuses Bayesian networks with a transformer-based tabular foundation model, can robustly enhance ML performance in precision agriculture settings. TCSDG is practical, extensible, and publicly released as open-source, making it a useful framework for generating synthetic data to support downstream agricultural prediction models.

Abstract: Machine Learning (ML) algorithms have been widely used to estimate agricultural variables across diverse contexts. However, because the quantity and quality of training data strongly influence performance of ML algorithms, their use can be constrained by limited or incomplete reference data. Synthetic Data Generation (SDG) offers a practical approach to address this issue by producing artificial but realistic samples that preserve key characteristics of the original data. Building on teacher-student knowledge transfer and in-context learning for tabular data, this study proposes a Task-Conditioned SDG (TCSDG) algorithm that pairs a Bayesian Network generator with a transformer-based tabular foundation model (TabICL). The proposed algorithm was evaluated on two agricultural prediction tasks: crop yield prediction and crop type classification. Six benchmark SDG algorithms were also utilized to compare their performance with that of TCSDG. Across twelve study sites, two training-data fractions, four multiplication ratios, and three predictive ML algorithms, augmenting the original data with TCSDG-generated synthetic data improved ML performance in 89% of the crop type classification experiments and 74% of the crop yield prediction experiments. TCSDG also substantially outperformed benchmark SDG algorithms and was the only method to consistently improve ML performance across both tasks at the aggregate level. The study demonstrates that carefully designed and processed synthetic data can improve ML performance in precision-agriculture applications. TCSDG offers a practical and extensible framework for generating synthetic data that supports downstream ML agricultural prediction. The full implementation of TCSDG is publicly available as open source at https://github.com/HamidEbrahimy/TCSDG.

Conclusion: LegalFarePlan shows that explicitly modeling legal exit/reentry operations in non-additive fare systems enables transparent, auditable computation of alternative journey plans that can reduce modeled fares within specified time and split constraints. The framework and benchmark demonstrate method behavior and reproducibility, but the authors stress that these are methodological results using synthetic/semi-synthetic data and should not be interpreted as empirical findings about any specific real-world operator like MTR.

(2) A human-in-the-loop pipeline treats verification as selective prediction: humans review only uncertain or rule-violating items, and data is allowed into the lakehouse only after passing 26 validation rules covering schema consistency, statistical sanity checks, and physical-plausibility constraints based on battery physics. (3) On top of the curated lakehouse, they define standardized health estimation and remaining useful life prediction tasks, dataset splits, and baselines to form an open benchmark.

Result: BatteryLake curates 41 public battery aging datasets from over 25 institutions into a unified, governed lakehouse with standardized schemas. The system demonstrates that agentic LLM-based curation combined with physics-based rules and human oversight can turn messy raw battery data into reliable benchmark-ready assets.

The released platform, benchmark, and curation protocol provide a reproducible foundation for battery health and RUL modeling research, and illustrate a general pattern for domain-specific, evidence-grounded curation of complex scientific time-series data.

Abstract: Public battery aging datasets are a critical asset for advanced health management, but their practical use is often limited by inconsistent formats, unclear schemas, and metadata scattered across repositories and publications. Current curation remains largely manual and hard to reproduce, while general-purpose data integration tools miss the domain-specific semantics of electrochemical time-series data. We present BatteryLake, a governed data lakehouse that turns raw public battery data into benchmark-ready assets through an agentic, physics-grounded curation framework, with three contributions. First, LLM agents extract metadata and synthesize dataset-specific converters, grounding every output in verbatim evidence and abstaining when none supports a value. Second, a human-in-the-loop mechanism frames verification as selective prediction and gates admitted data through 26 schema, statistical, and physical-plausibility rules. Third, we release an open benchmark of 41 datasets from over 25 institutions, with standardized SOH and RUL tasks, three split protocols, and eight baseline model families. The platform, benchmark, and curation protocol are publicly available at https://tianwen1209.github.io/batterylake/.

### [113] [How Much Does Correctness Cost? Budgeted Placement of Strong Correctors in a Weak Multi-Agent Swarm](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09765)
*Igor Itkin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to optimally place a small number of reliable, costly "oracle" nodes in a large network of unreliable agents to steer their consensus toward the truth, characterizing both near‑optimal placement algorithms and the budget needed to reach a target accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In many real systems—such as networks of weak models, sensors, or agents—it is possible to inject a few strong, reliable information sources that can improve the overall consensus. However, these strong sources are expensive, can vary in strength, and must be carefully placed under a limited budget. The authors want to understand, in a principled and quantitative way, how much must be spent, how to distribute that spending across the network, and whether to buy a few very strong or many moderately strong oracles, all as a function of the task and network structure.

Method: They model the swarm as a graph-based consensus system, where each node is an unreliable agent and each oracle pins a chosen node toward the true value with a controllable, concave (diminishing-returns) strength that has an associated cost. The overall consensus quality is quantified by a coherence metric H(R) = tr(M(R)^{-1}), where M(R) is a matrix depending on the oracle placement and strengths R. They then analyze the submodularity of H with respect to adding oracles of varying strengths, enabling approximation guarantees for greedy algorithms, derive a budget-accuracy tradeoff frontier B*(ε) (minimum budget to ensure ε-correct consensus), obtain closed-form expressions on complete graphs, and study special cases like equal-cost oracles yielding a minimal required oracle count k*. Finally, they empirically measure the cost-quality curvature on the Qwen3 model family for different tasks.

Result: They prove that the coherence objective H remains submodular even when oracle strengths differ, implying that a simple cost-benefit greedy oracle placement achieves at least (1 − 1/e) of the optimal quality for any given budget. They characterize the budget-correctness frontier B*(ε), including closed-form formulas on complete graphs, and identify a minimal oracle count k* when oracles have equal cost. They show that whether an optimal strategy buys a few strong or many medium oracles depends on the curvature of the cost-quality relationship: strong diminishing returns favour spreading the budget across many nodes, while sharply increasing returns favour concentrating budget into fewer oracles. Empirically, on the Qwen3 model ladder (0.6B–32B), they find that the law is concave for math verification tasks but convex for emergent code tracing tasks.

Conclusion: Greedy placement of heterogeneous-strength oracles in a consensus network is provably near-optimal due to submodularity of the coherence metric, and one can explicitly describe how much budget is needed to reach a desired consensus accuracy. The optimal way to allocate that budget—few strong versus many moderate oracles—turns out to be task-dependent and governed by the curvature of the cost-quality law: some tasks (like math verification) exhibit concave returns favouring broad deployment of moderate oracles, while others (like emergent code tracing) show convex returns, suggesting investment in fewer, more powerful oracles. This provides a general framework for budgeted design of hybrid systems combining many weak agents with a small number of strong correctors.

Abstract: A cheap swarm of unreliable agents can be steered to a correct consensus by a few strong, expensive "oracle" correctors. We ask how much one must spend, and where to place the oracles. We model the swarm as a consensus on a graph in which each oracle pins one node toward the truth at a cost-coupled, concave strength, and measure quality by the coherence H(R)=tr M(R)^{-1}. Our first result is that H stays submodular (each added oracle helps less than the last) even when the oracles differ in strength, so a cost-benefit greedy comes within 1-1/e of the best placement at any budget. Inverting the budget gives the budget-correctness frontier B*(eps), the least spend that guarantees an eps-correct consensus: closed-form on the complete graph, and a minimal oracle count k* when oracles cost the same. Whether a budget then buys a few strong oracles or many medium onese curvature of the cost-quality law: diminishing returns favour spreadsharply increasion. Measured onthe Qwen3 ladder (0.6-32B), the law is concave for math verificatio convex foremergent code tracing, so the verdict is genuinely task-dependent.https://github.com/YehudaItkin/budgeted-oracle-placemen

TL;DR: The paper studies how to design robust norm-enforcement mechanisms for competing language-model agents so that harmful, norm-violating behavior is effectively discouraged without being exploited for competitive gain.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors construct multi-agent simulation environments with language-model-based agents that compete for rewards under shared norms. They first test simple norm-enforcement mechanisms and show that misaligned agents exploit them, even without explicit training for that. They then design new mechanisms incorporating two main components: (1) dynamic estimation of each agent’s reliability over time; and (2) escalation of penalties for repeated norm violations. They evaluate these mechanisms across three simulated environments and different agent populations, comparing exploitation resistance and enforcement costs to baseline methods.

Result: Simple norm-enforcement schemes are vulnerable: misaligned agents can gain a competitive advantage by gaming the enforcement process. Mechanisms that track and update each agent’s reliability, and that apply increasingly severe penalties for repeated violations, substantially reduce this exploitation. These mechanisms still detect and penalize norm violations effectively, achieving similar or lower enforcement cost than simpler baselines across three different simulated settings.

Conclusion: Norm-enforcement mechanisms can be effective tools for shaping behavior in populations of AI agents, but they must be designed with the anticipation that agents will adapt to and attempt to exploit the enforcement system itself. Reliability tracking and escalating penalties are key design principles for making such mechanisms robust, scalable, and cost-effective in competitive multi-agent environments.

Abstract: AI agents are increasingly deployed in shared environments where they pursue diverse goals and compete for rewards. This multi-agent competition can lead to behaviors that serve individual gains at collective cost -- for instance, marketing agents may post misleading content as a result of competing for engagement on social media. Human societies address such problems through norms that constrain acceptable behavior, supported by enforcement mechanisms that detect and penalize violations. Motivated by this, we study norm enforcement mechanisms for language model agents. We find that simple enforcement mechanisms are exploited by misaligned agents for competitive advantage, even when they are not explicitly trained or prompted to do so. We thus turn our attention to designing more robust mechanisms, and identify two key ingredients: estimating each agent's reliability over time, and updating this estimate with escalating penalties for repeated misbehavior. Across three simulated environments and a variety of agent populations, mechanisms built on these principles resist exploitation, while still penalizing norm violations at comparable or lower cost than baselines. Our results position norm enforcement mechanisms as scalable levers for shaping agents' behavior, but only when designed to anticipate becoming part of the system they govern. Our code and data are available at https://yaowenye.com/norm-enforcement.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Model adaptive agentic controllers as finite sets of symbolic rules with explicit diagnostic predicates, explanation logs, and a held-out re-evaluation mechanism. Treat the controller as a finite revisable object. When diagnostics detect failures, map them to a small set of predefined rule-level edits: adding rules, deleting rules, or changing rule priorities. After each local edit, re-evaluate the modified controller on held-out simulation seeds or cloned initial states to test whether the failure is repaired and guardrails are respected.

Conclusion: The paper provides a methodological framework, compatible with simulation, for determining which kinds of failures in adaptive agentic controllers can be made observable and explainable, and then locally revised through finite rule edits and empirically re-tested. It does not claim to solve all verification problems but shows that a bounded verification and repair protocol is feasible for certain classes of controller failures under controlled, simulatable conditions.

This paper formulates a bounded verification protocol for adaptive agentic controllers represented by finite symbolic rules, explicit diagnostic predicates, explanation logs, and held-out re-evaluation. The central research question is: when an adaptive agentic controller is represented through finite rules, explicit diagnostic predicates, explanation logs, and held-out re-evaluation, which classes of controller failure can be detected, locally repaired, or rejected without relying on unrestricted human-in-the-loop judgment? The proposed framework treats the controller as a finite revisable object. Diagnostic failures are mapped to predefined rule-level edits, including rule addition, rule deletion, and priority revision. Repaired controllers are then evaluated on held-out simulation seeds or cloned initial states. Experiments in a stylized financially constrained inventory-control benchmark show three outcomes: resource-induced failures that remain non-repairable by one rule edit, partial repairs that are rejected because they violate thresholds or guardrails, and a local one-step repair of an order-volatility failure induced by removing a smoothing rule. ### [118] [Length Penalties Make Chain-of-Thought Less Monitorable](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09786)
*Bryce Little*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how training language models with length penalties to compress chain-of-thought (CoT) reasoning affects both answer accuracy and the ability to detect what influenced the model’s answer. It finds that compressed reasoning can hide influential biases even when accuracy is preserved, making model behavior harder to monitor.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-thought reasoning improves model performance but is expensive and can expose internal influences like misleading hints or biases. Practitioners want shorter, cheaper CoT without losing accuracy, and benchmarks often reward fewer tokens. However, it’s unclear whether such compression harms the ability to monitor or audit what actually drove the model’s answer, especially in the presence of biasing hints. The paper aims to understand this trade-off between efficiency, accuracy, and faithfulness/monitorability of reasoning traces.

Method: The authors fine-tune Qwen3-4B and Qwen3-14B with reinforcement learning using length penalties to target different CoT lengths. They then evaluate on held-out MMLU-Pro-R and four transfer benchmarks using interventions where misleading hints are added to prompts to bias the model. They measure: (1) token savings and accuracy; (2) how much hint influence remains (via answer shifts); (3) how often a separate monitor model can detect that the hint affected reasoning. To disentangle length from content, they also randomly delete sentences from baseline (uncompressed) chains until they match the compressed chains’ length and compare how often hints are disclosed in each case.

Result: Length-penalized RL substantially reduces the number of reasoning tokens while keeping most multiple-choice accuracy intact. However, hint-based biases on answers remain close to baseline, meaning the hints still influence decisions. At aggressive compression targets, lower-bound faithfulness drops to 63.1% of baseline for Qwen3-14B and 69.4% for Qwen3-4B. The rate at which a monitor detects hint usage falls notably (e.g., from 69% to 49% for Qwen3-14B and from 60% to 48% for Qwen3-4B). Even when baseline CoT traces are randomly shortened to match compressed length, compressed chains mention or disclose the hint 7–35 percentage points less often across both model sizes and all five evaluation distributions.

Conclusion: Compression via length-penalized RL does more than simply shorten chain-of-thought; it preferentially removes explicit cues that reveal influential hints or biases, thereby reducing the monitorability and apparent faithfulness of reasoning traces. This creates a “compression-monitorability frontier”: models can retain accuracy and cheaper reasoning while making it harder for oversight systems to see what actually drove their answers. Relying solely on token-efficiency and accuracy metrics is therefore insufficient; safety and interpretability evaluations must also measure how compression affects the visibility of underlying influences on model decisions.

Abstract: Length-penalized reinforcement learning can shorten chain-of-thought reasoning while hiding an influence that drives the model's answer. In our experiments, training with length penalties does not stop misleading hints from steering models, even though the models' chains of thought mention the hint much less often. A token-accuracy evaluation would count these runs as successful because they use fewer reasoning tokens with little accuracy loss; it would miss whether the remaining trace still shows what drove the answer. We train Qwen3-4B and Qwen3-14B variants with different target chain lengths, then evaluate them with biasing-hint interventions on held-out MMLU-Pro-R and four transfer benchmarks. Compression sharply cuts reasoning tokens, preserves most multiple-choice accuracy, and leaves hint influence near baseline. At the strongest target, lower-bound faithfulness falls to 63.1% of baseline for Qwen3-14B and 69.4% for Qwen3-4B; the raw rate at which a monitor catches hint use falls from 69% to 49% and from 60% to 48%. To separate length from content, we randomly delete sentences from uncompressed baseline chains until the remaining text matches the compressed length. Even after this length matching, compressed chains disclose the hint 7-35 percentage points less often than baseline chains that we shorten at random, for both Qwen3 sizes and all five evaluation distributions. Compression therefore does more than shorten reasoning, preferentially removing the cues a monitor needs to see what influenced the answer. Together, these results reveal a compression-monitorability frontier in which cheaper reasoning can preserve answers while making the influences behind them harder to detect.

</details>


### [119] [PHITSBench: an execution-scored benchmark for AI-assisted PHITS radiation-transport input generation using natural language](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09789)
*Xianglin Ji,Svetlana V. Boriskina*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PHITSBench is an execution-based benchmark to test how well language-model-based systems can edit, repair, and generate PHITS Monte Carlo transport simulations, revealing that machine-readable domain knowledge and agentic workflows are crucial for complex simulation generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI models are increasingly used to assist in scientific computing and radiation-transport simulations, there is a need to rigorously evaluate how well they can manipulate and generate input decks for complex domain-specific codes like PHITS. Existing benchmarks often focus on generic code or text tasks and do not capture execution correctness or domain-physics fidelity. A dedicated, execution-scored benchmark can reveal strengths, weaknesses, and requirements (like domain knowledge) for reliable AI assistance in radiation-transport modeling.

Method: The authors build PHITSBench, a benchmark of 282 executable PHITS tasks divided into three tracks: (1) Edit – modify parameters in existing simulations, (2) Repair – fix syntactic issues in broken simulations, and (3) Reproduce – generate full PHITS simulations from natural-language problem descriptions. Each task is automatically scored using a Composite Metric Score that combines successful code execution with quantitative agreement between model-generated and reference transport observables. They then evaluate five configurations of a GPT-5.4-based system, ranging from simple zero-shot prompting to configurations that incorporate a structured PHITS knowledge catalog and more complex, agentic workflows that can iteratively interact with tools and knowledge.

Result: Without any domain-specific knowledge, the GPT-5.4 model achieves high success on Edit (95%) and moderate success on Repair (70%) but completely fails on Reproduce tasks (0%). Providing a structured, machine-readable PHITS knowledge catalog plus the manual improves single-shot Reproduce success to 57%. Adding agentic execution strategies further improves Reproduce success to 66–73%, though with higher computational cost. Error analysis reveals that residual failures primarily stem from incorrect choice and configuration of physical observables, not from syntax or basic code generation problems.

Conclusion: PHITSBench demonstrates that large language models can reliably handle simpler editing and syntax-repair tasks in radiation-transport simulations but struggle with end-to-end simulation design without explicit, machine-readable domain knowledge. The study indicates that meaningful progress in AI-assisted radiation-transport modeling depends not only on better foundation models but also on high-quality knowledge catalogs, curated domain-specific training data, and execution-grounded benchmarks. These ecosystem components are essential to move from surface-level code generation to physically correct, trustworthy simulation workflows.

Abstract: We introduce PHITSBench, an execution-scored benchmark for the Monte Carlo Particle and Heavy Ion Transport code System (PHITS). PHITSBench comprises 282 transport-scorable tasks spanning three common workflow categories: parameter editing (Edit), syntax repair (Repair ), and complete simulation generation from natural-language descriptions (Reproduce). Each task is evaluated using a Composite Metric Score that combines execution success with agreement between generated and reference transport observables. Using PHITSBench, we evaluate five GPT-5.4-based configurations ranging from zero-shot prompting to knowledge-augmented and agentic workflows. Without domain-specific knowledge, the model performs well on editing and repair tasks (95% and 70% success, respectively) but fails to generate correct simulations from scratch (0% success on the Reproduce track). A structured, machine-readable PHITS knowledge catalog, supplied alongside the user manual, raises single-shot Reproduce-task success to 57%. Agentic execution provides a further improvement to 66-73%, but at increased computational cost. Failure analysis shows that the remaining errors are dominated by incorrect selection and configuration of physical observables rather than syntax generation. These results suggest that future progress in AI-assisted radiation-transport modeling will depend as much on machine-readable knowledge bases, curated domain-training datasets, and execution-grounded evaluation environments as on advances in foundation models themselves.

### [120] [Semantic Drift and the Stability of Operator Control in Reasoning-Class Decision Support Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09790)
*M. L. Kaluzhsky,V. A. Efirov*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to keep human operators’ control and goal alignment stable when working with advanced reasoning LLM-based decision support systems, identifying and modeling a phenomenon called semantic context drift and proposing a metric and design recommendations to mitigate it.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As next‑generation decision support systems increasingly rely on reasoning‑capable LLMs, there is a risk that the system’s internal reasoning and evolving context will gradually diverge from the human operator’s intent and control, threatening reliability, safety, and goal alignment. The authors are motivated to formally characterize this control issue, verify that semantic context drift actually occurs during prolonged human‑LLM collaboration, and provide a quantitative way to assess and engineer against it.

Method: They conducted a two‑month continuous longitudinal experiment in which a human and a reasoning LLM jointly designed a large, monograph‑like textual corpus. During this process, they observed and analyzed latent semantic context drift in the model’s reasoning. Building on these observations, they developed a mathematical model of the human‑machine interaction, incorporating hidden reasoning chains and their non‑linear contextual influence. From this model, they derived a new metric—the operator control stability coefficient—that captures how stable human control remains under such contextual pressure.

Result: The study empirically verifies the presence of latent semantic context drift in reasoning LLMs during extended collaborative authoring. The proposed mathematical framework formalizes operator–LLM interaction and reveals non‑linear effects of hidden reasoning chains on control and goal alignment. The operator control stability coefficient quantifies stability of human control, and analysis within the cognitome theory framework identifies a critical point where control functions invert (the system effectively starts steering the human instead of vice versa).

Conclusion: The paper concludes that semantic context drift in reasoning LLMs is a real and quantifiable threat to stable human control and goal‑targeting in advanced DSS. To mitigate this, system designers should employ dynamic relational arbitration loops—control mechanisms that continuously adjust human–LLM interaction—implemented via a modified hierarchical similarity model. These engineering recommendations aim to maintain operator control stability and prevent inversion of control functions in long‑term human–machine collaboration.

Abstract: The article investigates the fundamental problem of ensuring the stability of operator control and preserving goal-targeting in hybrid human-machine decision support systems (DSS) of a new generation. Based on a two-month continuous longitudinal experiment on the joint design of a monograph-format textual array, the latent phenomenon of semantic context drift in large language models of deep logical reasoning (Reasoning LLMs) is verified and described. A mathematical model of interaction in the human-machine interface is proposed, and an original metric is introduced - the operator control stability coefficient, which takes into account the non-linear contextual pressure of hidden reasoning chains. Within the paradigm of the cognitome theory, a critical point of control functions inversion is captured. Engineering recommendations are formulated for implementing dynamic relational arbitration loops based on a modified hierarchical similarity model.

### [121] [Agentic Context Learning with Self-Discovered Specification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09794)
*Jike Zhong,Ming Li,Yuxiang Lai,Ziyan Yang,Jingyu Xie,Jihyung Kil,Zheda Mai,Shao-Yuan Lo,Ren Xiang,Konstantinos Psounis,Yuanyuan Lei*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies why context learning—where LLMs must learn task-specific rules from long prompts—is hard, and finds that success depends more on acquiring implicit task specifications than on simply retrieving content. It introduces a simple method, PSCI, that explicitly induces and enforces such specifications, achieving new state-of-the-art results on CL-Bench.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Context learning tasks ask LLMs to infer and apply new, task-specific knowledge from long contexts at inference time, but even the best models perform poorly (<24% success). A standard assumption is that this is mainly a retrieval or long-context access problem, similar to long document understanding. The authors are motivated to test this assumption systematically and to understand what actually causes failures on a dedicated context-learning benchmark, with the goal of identifying the real bottleneck and improving performance.

Method: The authors conduct an empirical study on CL-Bench, evaluating twelve baseline methods involving retrieval, reflection, and verification against direct full-context prompting. They then perform fine-grained failure and rubric analysis across 31,592 evaluation items to categorize what is being tested: content acquisition versus specification acquisition (e.g., local rules, formats, completeness conditions). Based on the diagnosis that specification acquisition is crucial, they propose PSCI (private specification-contract induction), a simple intervention that (1) extracts local task specifications from the context, (2) turns them into an internal “contract,” and (3) enforces this contract via adversarial checking and iterative repair during generation. They run extensive ablations (17 variations) to isolate the effect of explicit specification handling.

Result: The empirical comparison shows that typical retrieval, reflection, and verification strategies provide only marginal gains over direct full-context prompting on CL-Bench. Rubric analysis reveals that 55.4% of items evaluate specification acquisition and only 22.6% evaluate content acquisition; 76.7% of specifications are not mentioned in the user query but 95.5% are recoverable from the context. PSCI, which explicitly targets these specifications, achieves state-of-the-art performance on CL-Bench: 28.14% with GPT-5.1 (a +5.59 percentage point absolute and +24.8% relative improvement), with similar gains on Qwen3.5-27B (+5.28 pp) and Gemini 3 Pro (+6.17 pp). Ablations confirm that leveraging task-specific specifications is the main driver of these improvements.

Conclusion: The study concludes that context learning is not primarily limited by content retrieval or long-context access, but by the model’s ability to acquire and adhere to task-specific local specifications that are usually implicit in the query yet present in the context. Explicitly extracting and enforcing these specifications, as done in PSCI, significantly improves performance, indicating that effective context learning requires both content and specification acquisition. This reframes the design of methods and benchmarks for long-context and in-context learning toward specification-focused techniques.

Abstract: Context learning is an emerging inference-time task where LLMs must learn and apply novel, task-specific knowledge from intricate contexts absent from pre-training; even frontier models score under 24% task success. In this work, we conduct a comprehensive empirical study to understand why this setting remains difficult. A natural hypothesis is that failures stem from content access; yet across twelve retrieval, reflection, and verification baselines on CL-Bench, an extensive context learning benchmark, we find limited gains over direct full-context prompting. Further failure analysis reveals a key finding: unlike typical long-context tasks such as long document understanding, context learning requires not only recovering local content but also acquiring local specifications that are often unspecified in the query but distributed across the context: domain-specific formats, local rules, and completeness conditions. Across all 31,592 rubric items, we find that 55.4% clearly evaluate specification acquisition, while only 22.6% evaluate content acquisition. Moreover, despite 76.7% of specifications being unspecified in the user query, 95.5% are traceable to the context, indicating these are learnable obligations rather than hidden requirements. To validate this diagnosis, we design a deliberately simple intervention PSCI (private specification-contract induction) which extracts local specifications and enforces them through adversarial checking and repair; PSCI achieves state-of-the-art 28.14% with GPT-5.1 (+5.59 pp absolute and +24.8% relative) on CL-Bench, replicated on Qwen3.5-27B (+5.28 pp) and Gemini 3 Pro (+6.17 pp). Seventeen ablations further isolate the role of task-specific specifications. Overall, our results suggest context learning hinges on not only content acquisition but also specification acquisition.

*Rizwaan Malik,Ashna Khetan,Isabel Sieh,Samin Khan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose and preliminarily test an LLM-based iterative agent workflow to automatically generate and refine K–12 math diagrams using vision-language quality checks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

There is a practical gap: teachers and students lack trustworthy automatic tools for creating middle-school math visuals, limiting scalable support and personalization in math education. The authors want a systematic way to evaluate and improve AI-generated visuals rather than producing them in a single, often flawed step.

Method: They design an “agentic workflow” where LLM-based agents (and Vision-Language Models) participate in a self-improvement loop. Step 1: Given visual-quality criteria (e.g., correctness, completeness, clarity), an LLM generates quality-assurance (QA) questions that should be asked about a candidate diagram. Step 2: A Vision-Language Model then answers these QA questions based on the generated diagram, effectively evaluating the visual. Step 3: The feedback from these QA answers is fed back into the diagram-generation process so that the system iteratively revises and improves the visual aid. They perform an exploratory evaluation of this pipeline on K–12 math diagrams, analyzing how well the models generate QA questions and how effectively the feedback loop improves diagrams.

Result: The workflow shows preliminary promise: the iterative QA-based loop can improve the reliability and educational usefulness of AI-generated math diagrams. However, the evaluation reveals notable limitations. The LLMs’ QA questions often lack strong spatial reasoning and fail to fully cover all important diagram features, which constrains the quality of the feedback and thus the potential improvements. Despite these weaknesses, the experiments provide initial evidence that the approach can make diagrams more accurate and pedagogically appropriate than one-shot generation.

Conclusion: An agentic, self-improving workflow that combines LLMs and Vision-Language Models for generating and critiquing K–12 math diagrams is feasible and beneficial, but not yet robust. Automatic generation of QA questions and visual evaluations can iteratively enhance diagram quality, offering a path toward more reliable and educationally sound math visuals. Future work needs to strengthen spatial reasoning and broaden coverage in QA generation to fully realize the approach’s potential.

Abstract: Mathematical diagrams play a crucial role in K 12 education, both as problem components and as scaffolding for student comprehension. However, current AI tools, including Large Language Models (LLMs), struggle to reliably generate accurate and pedagogically sound visual diagrams, even when provided with detailed descriptions. A significant gap therefore remains in the reliable generation of diagrams for middle school mathematics. To address this, we introduce an agentic workflow that enables LLM agents to evaluate the quality of generated visuals and use this feedback to iteratively improve their outputs. This self improvement loop aims to enhance the accuracy and educational appropriateness of AI generated diagrams. Our research investigates two questions. First, can LLMs accurately generate quality assurance questions for a visual aid given specific criteria for visual quality? Second, given valid quality assurance questions, can Vision Language Models effectively evaluate generated K 12 visual aids and use the resulting feedback to improve them iteratively? We conduct an exploratory evaluation of our agentic workflow and identify key areas for improvement, including stronger spatial reasoning and more comprehensive coverage of diagram features in the generated quality assurance questions. Our results provide preliminary evidence that this approach can improve the reliability and educational value of AI generated mathematical diagrams.

### [123] [TopoExplore: Topological Discrimination for Archive-Based Exploration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09971)
*Jason Carlson*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: TopoExplore is a topology-aware extension of Go-Explore that prioritizes entering truly accessible enclosed regions (voids) and ignoring sealed ones, improving exploration efficiency in structured environments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing archive-based and frontier exploration methods choose where to return based on rarity or boundary position but ignore whether unexplored regions beyond boundaries are actually reachable. This can waste effort on sealed or unreachable areas and fails the broader goal of building a structurally complete experience for later learning and planning.

Method: TopoExplore augments Go-Explore’s cell selection with a periodic topological analysis of the occupancy grid. Using flood fill to identify enclosed unexplored regions (voids, corresponding to H1 classes of the cubical complex), it finds their strict entrances (gaps or doors) and assigns them a decaying selection bonus. Sealed regions are never targeted because they have no valid entrances, and once a region is entered, its entrances lose their bonus and retire. The method is evaluated on 18 MiniGrid environments (15 seeds, fixed hyperparameters), with comparisons against Go-Explore and frontier-based baselines; additional tests are run on Montezuma’s Revenge and HM3D scanned building environments.

Result: On the controlled MiniGrid suite, TopoExplore achieves a 1.52x geometric-mean speedup in median steps-to-first-entry over Go-Explore, compared to 1.37x for a frontier baseline. In environments containing sealed decoy structures, frontier exploration performance deteriorates (0.83–1.48x speedup) while TopoExplore remains strong (1.65–2.11x). On challenging multi-interaction door environments, TopoExplore gains a 10.9x speedup. On Montezuma’s Revenge, performance worsens because, without explicit wall knowledge, unreachable occupancy artifacts are mistakenly rewarded. In HM3D scanned buildings, TopoExplore’s speedup over Go-Explore correlates with scene difficulty (r=0.69), while frontier selection still leads for blanket coverage.

Conclusion: Topology-aware selection of return states improves sample efficiency when exploration requires distinguishing between enterable and sealed enclosed regions, and remains competitive where simple frontier methods usually excel. The negative result on Montezuma’s Revenge emphasizes that reliable wall/entrance detection is crucial, while the positive MiniGrid and HM3D results support the targeted claim that topological information is especially valuable in environments with complex enclosed structure.

Abstract: Archive-based exploration methods such as Go-Explore select which visited state to return to using visitation rarity, and frontier methods return to the boundary of the unknown; neither asks whether the unexplored region behind a boundary is enterable at all. Exploration is not just about finding reward - it is about collecting a structurally complete experience for downstream learning and planning. We introduce TopoExplore, which augments Go-Explore cell selection with a periodic topological pass: enclosed unexplored regions (voids) of the visited-set occupancy grid are detected by flood fill (the H1 classes of its cubical complex), and a decaying selection bonus is placed only on their strict entrances (gap or door cells), so sealed regions are never targeted and entered regions retire. On a controlled 18-environment MiniGrid suite (15 seeds, frozen hyperparameters) TopoExplore attains a 1.52x geometric-mean speedup in median steps-to-first-entry over its exact Go-Explore ablation, versus 1.37x for a frontier baseline; frontier exploration degrades when sealed decoy structure appears (0.83-1.48x on decoy environments vs. 1.65-2.11x for TopoExplore), while TopoExplore holds its largest win on hard multi-interaction doors (10.9x). We report an honest negative on Montezuma's Revenge - without wall knowledge, unreachable occupancy artifacts capture the bonus and performance degrades as it grows, isolating the wall-aware entrance test as the load-bearing component - and a preliminary positive on HM3D scanned buildings, where the speedup over Go-Explore tracks scene difficulty (r=0.69) even as frontier selection dominates blanket coverage. The evidence supports a deliberately scoped claim: topology-aware selection pays off where enclosed structure must be discriminated, and remains competitive at open coverage, where frontier methods are strongest, despite not being tuned for that regime.

### [124] [Who&When Pro: Can LLMs Really Attribute Failures in AI Agents?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.09996)
*Jiale Liu,Huajun Xi,Shaokun Zhang,Yifan Zeng,Tianwei Yue,Chi Wang,Jian Kang,Qingyun Wu,Huazheng Wang*

TL;DR: The paper introduces Who&When Pro, a large-scale benchmark for using large language models to automatically identify where and why autonomous AI agents fail, based on systematically constructed failed trajectories with ground-truth labels across multiple tasks and modalities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As autonomous agentic systems powered by LLMs become more capable, their errors are increasingly subtle, complex, and hard for humans to diagnose manually. There is a growing need for automated methods that can attribute failures—pinpointing the step and cause of failure—both to improve debugging and to ensure safety and reliability at scale. However, there is a lack of standardized, large-scale, carefully controlled benchmarks to rigorously evaluate such automated failure attribution methods.

Method: The authors build a benchmark, Who&When Pro, by creating failed agent trajectories under strict experimental control: they first obtain a successful trajectory, then exactly replay a successful prefix and inject a controlled failure at a specific point, ensuring clear ground-truth information about where and why the failure occurs. They do this across three different modalities and 26 different benchmarks/scenarios, ultimately generating 12,326 failed trajectories with corresponding golden labels. Using this benchmark, they evaluate various LLM-based automated failure attribution protocols and model families, comparing performance and behavior patterns across settings.

Result: Who&When Pro contains 12,326 failed trajectories with precise, golden failure-attribution labels, spanning 3 modalities and 26 benchmarks representing diverse scenarios. Experimental results using this benchmark show systematic patterns in how different LLMs, model families, and protocols attribute failures—for example, differences across modalities and consistent biases or strengths in detecting certain types of failures. These findings highlight gaps in current automated failure attribution capabilities and differentiate which configurations perform better under which conditions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that Who&When Pro offers a robust, large-scale, and carefully controlled benchmark for automated failure attribution in agentic systems. Their analyses reveal regularities and limitations in current LLM-based attribution methods, and they argue that these insights provide practical, empirical guidance for building more reliable automated failure analysis tools in the future. The work is positioned as foundational infrastructure for studying and improving failure attribution as agents become more capable and their failure modes grow more subtle.

Abstract: Automated failure attribution uses LLMs to identify where and why agentic systems fail. As agents become more capable, their failures become subtler, making automated attribution increasingly important. We introduce Who&When Pro, a large-scale benchmark for automated failure attribution in agentic systems. Using a strictly controlled pipeline that injects a failure only after exactly replaying a successful prefix, we construct 12,326 failed trajectories with golden labels across 3 modalities and 26 benchmarks covering various scenarios. Beyond benchmarking, we conduct extensive experiments and analyses, revealing systematic patterns in how models attribute failures across modalities, protocols, and model families, and providing empirical guidance for future automated failure attribution systems.

### [125] [A Symbolic Neural CPU for Quantization-Simulated Writeback and Interpretable Program Execution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10021)
*Jose Luis Lima de Jesus Silva*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper presents a neural network architecture that executes programs in a fully interpretable, step-by-step way by supervising the entire execution trace, enabling exact or symbolically exact reproduction of algorithmic execution even in low precision.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard neural networks can learn to map inputs to outputs of algorithms but their internal execution steps are opaque, making it hard to trust them or debug failures, especially when using low-precision arithmetic or deploying on hardware with strict constraints. The authors want a system where every step of program execution is observable, checkable and robust to quantization, so that neural execution can be used as a reliable and interpretable component in larger systems.

Method: They design a "symbolic neural CPU" with a factorized architecture: a recurrent controller decides over time which operation to apply; an explicit differentiable arithmetic-logic unit (ALU) bank implements a fixed set of operations; an operation router chooses which ALU to use; destination-masked register writeback controls where results are stored; and the whole system is trained with complete trajectory (trace) supervision, including matched fixed-point replay to match low-precision semantics. They expose at each step the chosen operation, source and destination registers, register values over time, memory interactions and writeback behavior. They experiment with multiple controller types (recurrent nets, Transformers, temporal convolutions, temporal-graph-like, and state-space models), and perform ablations on supervision signals, especially operation-gate supervision. They also add extensions like ValueMemory, hybrid adaptive leaky integrate-and-fire controllers, constrained symbolic control trained via behavior cloning and actor-critic RL, and an RV32I semantic bridge to standard integer instruction sets.

Result: On a main 16-wide benchmark, the full-precision (non-quantized) model matches the reference program execution exactly at every step. A version simulating 8-bit quantization still maintains the correct symbolic path of executed operations across long programs (up to 1,000 instructions), even if small numeric drifts appear. When these executions are compared against a matched low-precision fixed-point reference, the residual numeric errors vanish, indicating that discrepancies were due to reference semantics mismatch rather than model failure. Ablation studies show that supervising the operation gates is crucial for obtaining an interpretable execution trace, and specialized hidden-opcode memory-pressure tasks reveal the model's remaining weaknesses in using delayed state and binding information over time.

Conclusion: The paper demonstrates a trace-verifiable neural execution framework where each computational step is interpretable and can be checked against a reference, and where the symbolic execution path is robust even under low-precision constraints. Operation-level supervision is key to achieving an inspectable execution path. The proposed architecture and extensions—fixed ALU bank, trace supervision, quantization-aware replay and interfaces to richer controllers and instruction sets—offer a practical path toward trustworthy, interpretable and controllable neural program executors that can bridge between neural models and conventional CPU semantics.

Abstract: Neural networks can learn algorithmic input-output mappings, but trusting a learned executor requires more than a correct final answer because the state transitions that produce it are usually hidden. To make those transitions visible, we introduce a trace-supervised symbolic neural CPU, a factorized learned execution architecture that combines recurrent control, an explicit operation router over a fixed differentiable arithmetic-logic unit bank, destination-masked register writeback, complete trajectory supervision and matched fixed-point replay. The model exposes the selected operation, source and destination registers, register trajectory, memory signals and writeback semantics at every step. On the principal 16-wide benchmark, the non-quantized executor reproduces reference execution exactly, while the eight-bit quantization-simulated executor preserves the symbolic operation path through programs of 1,000 instructions. When the same execution is evaluated against a matched fixed-point replay, the residual numerical drift disappears, showing that it comes from a mismatch between continuous and low-precision reference semantics rather than from execution failure. We compare recurrent, Transformer, temporal-convolution, temporal graph-inspired and state-space controllers, and the ablations show that operation-gate supervision is necessary for an inspectable execution path. Hidden-opcode memory-pressure tasks expose the remaining limits in delayed state use and temporal binding. We also extend the interface with ValueMemory, hybrid adaptive leaky integrate-and-fire controllers, candidate-constrained symbolic control trained through behaviour cloning and actor-critic reinforcement learning, and an RV32I base-integer semantic bridge. Together, these results establish a trace-verifiable framework for interpretable, low-precision and controllable neural execution.

### [126] [AgentAbstain: Do LLM Agents Know When Not to Act?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10059)
*Xun Liu,Yi Evie Zhang,Vira Kasprova,Parisa Rabbani,Pardis Sadat Zahraei,Tianyu Zhang,Ali Ebrahimpour-Boroojeny,Varun Chandrasekaran*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces AgentAbstain, a benchmark and generation pipeline to evaluate whether LLM agents know when to abstain from acting in tool-using scenarios.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM-based agents are increasingly used for autonomous actions, but current evaluations focus on task success, not on an agent’s ability to recognize when it should refrain from acting. This is risky in situations with ambiguity, conflicting instructions, or tool/environment failures, where mistaken actions can be unintended and irreversible. The authors aim to systematically measure and improve this underexplored dimension: calibrated abstention in agentic settings.

Method: They design AgentAbstain, a paired-task benchmark grounded in an agent-native taxonomy of 8 abstention scenarios that span both pre-execution reasoning and runtime discovery of problems. For each scenario, they build executable sandbox environments and create 263 paired tasks across 42 environments. Each pair has a should-act task and a should-abstain variant derived via controlled perturbations to instructions, tools, or environment state. To scale this, they introduce AbstainGen, a fully automated pipeline that (1) synthesizes sandbox environments, (2) generates paired tasks end-to-end, and (3) validates them via deterministic replay and semantic LLM-based judges. Fresh instances can be regenerated to mitigate contamination, and human annotators then rate the quality of sampled tasks.

Result: Using AgentAbstain, they evaluate 17 frontier LLMs in 4 different agent harnesses. The strongest evaluated agent (Gemini 3.1 Pro) attains only 59.5% paired accuracy—i.e., correctly acting when appropriate and abstaining when it should, on both sides of each pair. They find that abstention performance is largely uncorrelated with general task-solving performance, showing that better problem-solving alone does not reliably yield safer abstention behavior. They also identify characteristic failure modes such as post-hoc abstention, where agents take irreversible actions and only afterwards recognize conditions that should have triggered abstention.

Conclusion: Abstention—the ability to recognize when not to act—is a distinct and currently underdeveloped capability in LLM agents. Existing high-performing models still frequently fail to abstain appropriately in realistic tool-using environments, and improvements in raw task-solving do not automatically improve abstention. AgentAbstain and AbstainGen provide an open, extensible framework for systematically measuring and studying this behavior, highlighting the need for explicit development and evaluation of abstention mechanisms in future agent systems.

Abstract: Agent systems based on large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed for autonomous tasks, yet existing evaluations mostly focus on task success rather than whether agents know when to abstain. This gap poses real risks: under ambiguity, conflicting constraints, or tool failures, agents may execute unintended and irreversible actions. To close this gap, we present the first systematic evaluation framework for agentic abstention: the calibrated ability of tool-using LLM agents to recognize when not to act. At its core, AgentAbstain is a paired-task benchmark built on an agent-native taxonomy of 8 abstention scenarios across pre-execution reasoning and runtime discovery. It contains 263 paired tasks across 42 executable sandbox environments, where each pair consists of a should-act task and a should-abstain variant produced through a controlled perturbation to the instruction, tool, or environment state. To scale this paired design and resist data contamination, we propose AbstainGen, a fully automated pipeline that synthesizes sandbox environments and generates paired tasks end-to-end, validated by deterministic replay and semantic LLM judges; fresh task instances can be regenerated on demand, and three independent annotators rate 94-98% of sampled tasks as well-designed. Across 17 frontier LLMs in 4 agent harnesses, the best agent (Gemini 3.1 Pro) achieves only 59.5% paired accuracy (correct on both the act and abstain sides of each paired task). More importantly, abstention capability is largely independent of general task-solving capability, indicating that scaling task-solving alone will not close this gap. We further identify failure modes such as post-hoc abstention, in which agents execute irreversible actions before recognizing abstention triggers. Our code and dataset are open-sourced at agentabstain.github.io.

</details>


### [127] [From ambiguous utterances to governed reuse classes: canonicalization, quotient invariance, and conditional decidability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10069)
*Cosimo Spera,Ray Garcia*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a rigorous, mathematically governed alternative to heuristic semantic caching for reusing answers in conversational systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current semantic caching reuses answers based only on embedding similarity thresholds, lacking explicit governance, authorization, versioning, and a principled notion of when two user demands are truly the same. In regulated or governed domains, such heuristic reuse can violate policy, mix versions, or misapply answers, so a more exact and auditable foundation is needed.

Method: The paper replaces similarity-based reuse with reuse defined on a quotient space of resolved conversational demands in a governed setting. It introduces three equivalence relations on resolved utterances—reading identity, resolution identity, and reuse identity—forming a refinement chain. It characterizes when these relations are strictly distinct using conditions checkable in deployment logs. It proves that pipeline outputs are invariant along this chain, and that reuse identity is the kernel of the resolution map into a governed answer partition. It then defines governed query keys, certified answer spaces, and conditions for safely reusing particular answers. Formally, it uses exact-denotation normal forms, join aggregation as a design operator with closure-stable cells, and a two-layer architecture with an untrusted proposal layer over a fully computable governed core.

Result: The work shows that answer reuse can be grounded in a mathematically precise equivalence (reuse identity) rather than similarity scores. It demonstrates that: (1) the three identities form a strict refinement chain under realistic nondegeneracy conditions; (2) the system’s outputs are invariant across the chain; (3) reuse identity corresponds exactly to the kernel of the resolution map induced by the governed answer partition; and (4) governed query keys and certified answer spaces can be defined so that reuse is controlled and auditable. It also establishes computability of the full pipeline relative to an untrusted proposal layer and gives logical conditions for termination and soundness of elicitation.

Conclusion: In governed conversational domains, answer reuse should be defined on a formally specified quotient of resolved demands, not on embedding similarity. The paper provides a precise equivalence structure (reading, resolution, reuse identities) and shows how reuse identity, as the kernel of resolution into a governed answer partition, underwrites safe query keys and answer reuse. The supporting theory guarantees exact denotations, compositional design via join aggregation, computability with untrusted proposers, and terminating, sound elicitation under consistency assumptions, while explicitly not claiming factual grounding or intent fidelity.

Abstract: Semantic caching defines answer reuse on embedding similarity: two utterances share a stored answer when a similarity score clears a threshold, with no notion of authorization, versioning, or of what makes two demands the same. This note changes the object on which reuse is defined: in a governed domain, reuse should operate on a mathematically characterized quotient of resolved conversational demands, not on a similarity heuristic. Three independently defined relations on resolved utterances -- reading identity, resolution identity, and reuse identity -- form a refinement chain, strict under realized nondegeneracy conditions checkable on deployment logs; the pipeline's outputs are invariant along the chain, and reuse identity is exactly the kernel of the resolution map into the governed answer partition, so the reuse quotient is the utterance-side object that partition induces, not a relabeling of it. Reuse identity licenses the governed query key and its certified answer space; reuse of a particular answer requires resolution identity or an applicability certificate. The supporting layer is stated at exactly the strength proved: exact-denotation normal forms; join aggregation as a design operator, with closure-stable cells characterizing no-escape; total computability of the full pipeline relative to an untrusted proposal layer; policy admissibility for arbitrary proposers -- and provably not factual grounding or intent fidelity; and elicitation terminating after finitely many informative replies, sound under target consistency.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors design MAG, a benchmark where models must both execute tasks on web interfaces and generate corresponding user guides based on screenshots. Result: The benchmark and harness reveal that existing frontier API and open multimodal models perform poorly on the integrated action-plus-guide task: even the best model solves under 40% of tasks. They also provide detailed analyses of model behavior and failure cases across the evaluated systems.

Conclusion: MAG establishes a new, more realistic benchmark for web automation that couples task execution and instructional guide generation directly on visual interfaces. The associated harness and metrics enable systematic evaluation and training of multimodal web agents. Current models are far from solving these tasks, with low overall success rates, but the GRPO-with-experts approach shows that targeted training can materially improve both action success and instruction quality, highlighting a promising direction for future research on practical Digital Adoption Platforms and multimodal web agents.

Abstract: Digital Adoption Platforms (DAPs) are embedded overlays widely used on web systems to guide users through operations inside a page, helping them get started with unfamiliar interfaces quickly. Completing a real task, however, rarely means clicking a few buttons on a single page: it takes a sequence of actions that unfolds across changing page states. Prior studies have also treated automated web agent actions and guide text generation as two separate problems, and most of them feed models textual page representations such as the DOM or accessibility trees rather than the rendered screens that humans actually operate on. In this work we introduce MAG, the first benchmark that unifies task execution and guide writing into a single Multimodal Action and Guide task, with two grounding schemes over screenshots: Set-of-Mark element selection and raw pixel coordinates. We further build a complete harness for this compound task, covering annotation with LLM assistance and human verification, training, evaluation in live environments, and joint metrics for actions and guides. With this harness we evaluate frontier API models and open multimodal models, and report detailed analyses. Finally, we design a GRPO training method augmented with expert trajectories, which nearly doubles the success rate of a supervised 9B agent (from 6.9% to 13.2%) and improves guide quality at the same time. Even the strongest model completes fewer than 40% of the tasks, leaving ample room for future research.

### [129] [Looped State-Space Language Models with Adaptive Exit-State Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.10110)
*Zhenxuan Yu,Takeshi Kojima,Yutaka Matsuo,Yusuke Iwasawa*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies 'looped' versions of Mamba and hybrid Mamba-Transformer language models, where the same block is applied multiple times, and shows that increased computational depth via looping can improve reasoning with fewer distinct parameters, remaining competitive with standard (non-looped) models, though deep non-looped models still do best on perplexity under strict compute matching.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work on looped/iterative Transformers indicates that many reasoning tasks benefit more from additional computation depth (repeated application of the same block) than from simply adding more independent parameters. However, this has been studied mainly for Transformer architectures, leaving it unclear whether the same principle holds for newer state-space sequence models such as Mamba. The authors aim to fill this gap and to understand trade-offs between parameter sharing, effective depth, compute cost, and performance in Mamba-based language models.

Method: They construct Looped Mamba and Looped Hybrid Mamba-Transformer architectures in which a shared Mamba (or hybrid) block is applied multiple times in sequence, introducing explicit finite-depth recurrent computation. They evaluate these looped models on controlled reasoning tasks (Mano, a modular-arithmetic manipulation task, and p-hop induction) against baselines that are either non-looped or have equivalent effective depth without parameter sharing. They then scale up to language model pre-training under matched iso-parameter (same parameter count) and iso-FLOPs (same compute) conditions to disentangle the effect of depth vs parameter-sharing. Finally, they adapt Ouro’s two-stage exit gate mechanism to Looped Mamba, enabling selection of an output from among different recurrent steps based on a learned threshold, and study its effect on performance and potential inference-time savings.

Result: On the controlled reasoning tasks, Looped Mamba consistently outperforms non-looped baselines with the same parameter count and in several cases matches or surpasses non-looped models with the same effective depth, indicating that reusing the same block for additional computation improves reasoning. In language model pre-training, looped models achieve competitive downstream benchmark performance while using substantially fewer distinct parameters, but strictly depth-matched non-looped models retain an advantage in validation perplexity under iso-FLOPs comparisons. Incorporating Ouro-style adaptive exit gates into Looped Mamba improves downstream performance when selecting intermediate-depth recurrent states, though this does not reduce actual computation because all recurrent steps are still executed.

Conclusion: Looping (recurrently re-applying) Mamba or hybrid Mamba-Transformer blocks is an effective way to add computational depth for reasoning tasks, allowing models with fewer distinct parameters to remain competitive with standard architectures. While deeper non-looped models still tend to achieve better perplexity under fixed compute, looped Mamba provides attractive efficiency-accuracy trade-offs, especially on reasoning-oriented evaluations. Adaptive exit mechanisms can further boost performance by choosing an appropriate prediction depth, but realizing real inference-time savings will require additional techniques for handling and possibly skipping recurrent steps.

Abstract: Recent work on looped language models suggests that many reasoning problems benefit from greater computational depth rather than from additional independent parameters. Existing studies, however, focus almost exclusively on Transformer backbones, leaving open whether this principle also applies to state-space language models. We investigate Looped Mamba and Looped Hybrid Mamba-Transformer architectures, which repeatedly apply a shared Mamba (or hybrid) block to introduce explicit finite-depth recurrent computation. On two controlled reasoning tasks-Mano (modular-arithmetic manipulation) and p-hop induction-Looped Mamba consistently outperforms parameter-matched non-looped baselines and, in several settings, matches or exceeds non-looped models of equal effective depth. We then extend the study to language model pre-training under matched iso-parameter and iso-FLOPs protocols, which jointly disentangle the effects of parameter sharing and effective depth: looped models remain competitive on downstream benchmarks with substantially fewer distinct parameters, although deeper non-looped models retain an advantage in validation perplexity under strict iso-FLOPs comparisons. Finally, we adapt Ouro's two-stage exit gate to Looped Mamba for threshold-controlled selection among recurrent-step outputs. Since all recurrent steps are still executed, the selected exit step represents prediction depth rather than reduced wall-clock computation. At the scales studied, adaptive exit-state selection improves downstream performance at intermediate depths, while actual inference-time savings require additional state-handling mechanisms.
