### [177] [A Formal Hierarchical Architecture for Agentic Orchestration with Stack-Based Execution and Lazy Discovery](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11138)
*Prashant Devadiga,Abhishek,Adithya Mishra,Alok Singh,Amisha Sinha,Asit Desai,Gaurang Dahad,Harshit Bhushan,Mandati Pramod Reddy,Prakhar Gupta,Rupesh Patil,Siddhi Behere*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a hierarchical, skill-based architecture for LLM agents to overcome the limitations of a flat tool registry, improving routing accuracy, scalability, and isolation for enterprise deployment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents often rely on a flat, monolithic registry of tools, forcing the model to choose among hundreds or thousands of options at once. This causes decision-space explosion, overuse of the context window, and worse routing performance, especially as tool catalogs grow and workflows become multi-step. These issues are particularly problematic in production, enterprise settings where reliability, efficiency, and isolation between tasks are required.

Method: The authors design a hierarchical orchestration architecture in which capabilities are arranged as a rooted tree: internal nodes specialize in routing decisions, while leaf nodes are deterministic executors of specific tasks. The runtime uses a single-step execution loop controlled by a Last-In-First-Out (LIFO) stack, giving the system pushdown-automaton-like memory for tracking nested execution contexts. Capability discovery is manifest-driven and lazy: only children of the currently active node are loaded into the prompt at any time, which keeps memory and prompt size proportional to the explored path rather than the global tool registry. The architecture replaces global memory with localized stack frames to isolate execution branches. The paper also includes a formal mathematical description of the orchestration state, an algorithmic analysis of the execution loop, and benchmarking experiments contrasting flat vs. hierarchical routing under different stressors (tool catalog size, workflow length, and schema-token exposure).

Result: The hierarchical, skill-based orchestration approach scales more gracefully than flat registries as tool catalogs and workflow complexity grow. By loading only local capabilities per step and using stack-based execution, the system reduces context window pressure, improves routing accuracy, and prevents information leakage across execution branches. Benchmarks show that hierarchical routing maintains better performance than flat routing under large tool catalogs, multi-step workflows, and high schema-token exposure scenarios, making it more suitable for production deployments like the UPI Help support product.

Conclusion: Organizing LLM-agent capabilities as a hierarchical skill tree with stack-based execution provides a principled and scalable alternative to flat tool registries. This architecture mitigates decision-space explosion, controls prompt and memory usage via lazy loading, and offers isolation guarantees needed for regulated enterprise use cases. The formalization and empirical comparisons support the claim that hierarchical orchestration yields more reliable and efficient agent behavior, especially as tool and workflow complexity increase.

Abstract: The rapid expansion of capabilities in Large Language Model (LLM) agents has exposed a critical architectural bottleneck: when agents are given access to a flat, monolithic registry of tools, the model must evaluate hundreds or thousands of options simultaneously. This leads to decision-space explosion, context window saturation, and degraded routing accuracy. To address these limitations, this paper presents a hierarchical, skill-based architecture for agentic orchestration. Capabilities are organized as a rooted tree where internal nodes make routing decisions and leaf nodes execute deterministic tasks. The runtime enforces a single-step execution loop governed by a Last-In-First-Out (LIFO) stack, giving the agent a form of memory akin to a Pushdown Automaton, therefore enabling it to track nested execution contexts and resume deterministically from any depth. Capability discovery follows a manifest-driven, lazy-loading protocol: only the immediate children of the active node are loaded, so memory and prompt costs scale with the explored path rather than the global registry. By replacing global memory with localized stack frames, the architecture prevents outputs from one execution branch from leaking into another, establishing the isolation guarantees required for deployment in regulated enterprise environments. We also discuss UPI Help, an AI-powered digital payments support product, as a motivating production deployment context. We provide a mathematical formalization of the orchestration state, detailed algorithmic analysis of the execution loop, and controlled benchmarks comparing flat and hierarchical routing under increasing tool catalogs, multi-step workflow pressure, and visible schema-token exposure per LLM call.

### [179] [The Hidden Footprint: Making Storage a First-Class Metric for LLM Agent Evaluation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11149)
*Chenglin Yu,Hongquan Gui,Ying Yu,Hongxia Yang,Ming Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes AgentFootprint, a benchmark to quantify and compare how much persistent storage LLM agent systems use after runs, revealing large variation and inefficiencies beyond accuracy or cost metrics.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agent benchmarks focus on task success, reliability, and inference cost, but ignore the persistent data artifacts left on disk (logs, context, checkpoints, etc.), which impact storage cost, privacy risk, and system scalability. Naive byte counting also mismeasures true duplication and overhead due to how frameworks serialize and store data. There is a need for a principled, comparable way to evaluate and improve agents’ storage footprints.

Method: Define AgentFootprint, a cross-framework benchmark and metric suite that is aware of serialization details. It measures several dimensions of storage: total bytes retained, composition across storage channels, duplication, growth behavior over time, compressibility, and how well full conversation histories can be reconstructed. They use a fixed-trace control to isolate agent logic from persistence-layer amplification by replaying identical trajectories across seven different persisting frameworks. They also analyze exported trajectories from 108 SWE-bench Verified submissions and experiment with a content-addressed storage design to quantify potential reductions while preserving reconstructability.

Result: Replaying the same trace through seven agent frameworks yields a 6.7x difference in storage usage purely from persistence-layer effects. Configurations using the same model, tools, and tasks but all achieving 100% accuracy still differ by 15.7x in retained bytes because their defaults store different amounts and types of data. Some configurations that keep full history grow superlinearly in a stress test with repeated observations. In real SWE-bench submissions, normalized per-instance storage spans three orders of magnitude with no correlation to task resolution rate. A content-addressed storage scheme cuts retained data by 4.8x–32.7x while keeping all reconstructability scores unchanged.

Conclusion: Persistent storage footprint is a critical but previously neglected resource dimension for LLM agents. AgentFootprint shows that current frameworks vary widely and often inefficiently in what they retain, independent of accuracy. Naive byte-level metrics hide substantial duplication and overhead. Careful, serialization-aware measurement and designs like content-addressed storage can dramatically reduce storage without sacrificing the ability to reconstruct conversations or audits. The paper argues that storage metrics should be reported alongside accuracy and reconstructability when evaluating and comparing agent systems.

Abstract: LLM agent benchmarks measure task completion, reliability, and inference cost, but not the persistent data an agent run leaves on disk, including logs, context snapshots, checkpoints, and debug traces. We introduce AgentFootprint, a cross-framework benchmark of post-run agent storage footprint. Its serialization-aware metric suite measures total retention, channel composition, duplication, growth, compressibility, and conversation-history reconstructability. It addresses a measurement trap: naive byte-level measurement understates duplication by an order of magnitude because database paging and JSON escaping obscure repeated content. A fixed-trace control separates agent-generated logical volume from persistence-layer amplification: replaying the same trajectory through seven persisting frameworks yields a 6.7x spread. Under identical models, tools, and tasks, configurations with 100% accuracy differ by 15.7x in retained bytes, although their defaults support different recovery and audit capabilities. Three full-history configurations grow superlinearly on a repeated-observation stress task. Exported trajectories from 108 instance-normalized SWE-bench Verified submissions span three orders of magnitude per instance, with no detectable correlation with resolve rate. A content-addressed store reduces retention by 4.8x-32.7x while preserving every reconstructability score. These results establish persistent storage as a resource metric to report jointly with accuracy and reconstructability.

### [180] [STAMP: Provenance-Guided Credit Assignment for Deep Search Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11172)
*Ke Xu,Han Xu,Xinran Chen,Yuqian Wang,Zhixuan Li,Xiaojian Liu,Changwo Wu,Jianqiang Xia,Yuchen Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces STAMP, a method to give step-level credit to document-search actions in deep-search reinforcement learning agents, improving performance across multiple benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing deep-search RL agents typically reward only the final trajectory-level outcome (e.g., correctness, citation quality, evidence coverage), ignoring which intermediate actions actually discovered the useful supporting documents. This reward-credit mismatch means the agent isn’t explicitly encouraged to learn good search and retrieval behaviors, limiting performance and efficiency.

Method: STAMP builds a training-time evidence graph and uses a reference-based verifier to check whether each cited document truly supports a queried entity or relation. Using first-exposure attribution, it traces each correctly supporting citation back to the action that first surfaced that document. It then injects a step-level credit signal via sign-preserving advantage modulation, which redistributes advantage across steps while keeping the overall trajectory-level reward and relative ranking of trajectories within each comparison group unchanged. STAMP is applied on top of a GRPO baseline and can be combined with both outcome-only and citation-aware base rewards.

Result: On three benchmarks—BrowseComp, BrowseComp-ZH, and xbench-DS—STAMP outperforms a GRPO baseline by +2.0, +5.5, and +3.0 points respectively, under controlled conditions (same SFT initialization, training data, and tools). Ablation studies show that both the provenance-based credit assignment and the sign-preserving advantage modulation are necessary, each contributing meaningfully to the observed gains.

Conclusion: Assigning explicit step-level credit to the actions that surface useful evidence, while preserving the original trajectory-level reward structure, leads to better training of deep-search RL agents. STAMP effectively resolves the reward-credit mismatch, produces consistent improvements across several datasets, and integrates cleanly with different base reward designs.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning for deep-search agents has largely focused on trajectory-level scoring -- outcome correctness, citation-aware rewards, and evidence coverage. Yet the actions that expose supporting documents receive no targeted credit, a gap we call the reward-credit mismatch. We propose STAMP, in which a reference-based verifier judges whether each cited document supports an entity or relation in a training-time evidence graph, and first-exposure attribution traces each supported citation back to the action that first surfaced it. This step credit is injected through sign-preserving advantage modulation, which redistributes advantage across steps without changing the trajectory-level reward or the relative ranking of trajectories within each group. On BrowseComp, BrowseComp-ZH, and xbench-DS, STAMP improves the GRPO baseline by +2.0/+5.5/+3.0 points under matched SFT initialization, training data, and search tools, and composes with both outcome-only and citation-rubric base rewards. Component ablations confirm that the provenance-based credit signal and the sign-preserving advantage modulation each contribute to the gains.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

Second, new training techniques: (a) Frontier Sampling, which monitors performance at the task level and focuses rollouts on tasks at the current competence frontier to increase sample efficiency; and (b) Visual Context Segmentation, a sliding-window scheme over recent visual observations that replaces step-wise decomposition to better balance rollout cost and training throughput, accelerating learning.

Abstract: Computer use agents (CUAs) are emerging as a powerful interface for automating complex digital workflows through visual perception and GUI execution. To break these barriers, we introduce ScaleCUA, a unified framework that scales online RL for CUAs via verifiable task synthesis and efficient training. At the data level, we design VeriGen, an end-to-end framework for generating verifiable RL tasks through iterative docker interactions and a multi-agent feedback loop. Scaled to 100+ concurrent agent workers via a shared docker interaction probe, this pipeline produces 24K+ verifiable tasks and nearly 3K high-quality RL tasks. To maximize sample efficiency, we propose Frontier Sampling, which tracks per-task capability and allocates rollouts to the current learning frontier. On the training side, we further design Visual Context Segmentation, a sliding window over recent visual context that balances rollout and training-engine pressure, yielding a 2.83x training speedup over step-wise decomposition. Together, ScaleCUA achieves 68.7% on OSWorld and 54.0% on ScienceBoard, establishing new state-of-the-art performance among open-source computer use agents. ### [183] [What We Talk About When We Talk About LLM Planning: Evidence for Two Distinct Planning Abilities](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11197)
*Sukai Huang,Chenyuan Zhang,Fucai Ke,Zhixi Cai,Naim Rastgoo,Gholamreza Haffari,Hamid Rezatofighi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes why large language models (LLMs) show uneven performance on planning tasks and finds that performance is driven by at least two distinct planning competencies, not a single difficulty scale.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often perform inconsistently across different planning benchmarks and tasks. This is usually explained away as some tasks just being harder than others, implicitly assuming a single, one-dimensional planning ability. The authors believe this is inadequate and want to know whether different tasks actually probe different underlying planning skills (competencies). Identifying such competencies would lead to more precise evaluation and understanding of LLM planning behavior and improvement trends.

Method: They evaluate multiple LLM families on ACPBench-Hard, a challenging planning benchmark, under different test-time reasoning budgets (e.g., allowing longer or shorter reasoning traces). They then apply a multidimensional item response theory (IRT) model to the resulting performance data. This statistical model infers latent dimensions (competencies) that best explain the pattern of successes and failures across tasks and settings.

Result: The IRT analysis consistently recovers two main latent dimensions that explain LLM performance on planning tasks. The first, termed operational reasoning, corresponds to the model’s ability to check local action applicability and predict immediate state transitions. The second, termed structural enumeration, corresponds to the ability to reason about global structure, such as goal reachability and identifying necessary subgoals or landmarks. They further find that operational reasoning improves with model scaling and with more generous reasoning budgets, whereas structural enumeration shows little improvement under the same conditions.

Conclusion: LLM planning performance is not governed by a single general difficulty axis but by at least two distinct planning competencies: operational reasoning and structural enumeration. Improvements in model size and longer reasoning traces primarily enhance operational reasoning, leaving structural enumeration comparatively stagnant. Therefore, evaluations and method design should move from aggregate scores to competency-level analysis: asking which specific planning skills improve, under what conditions, and through which mechanisms, instead of only whether overall planning performance increases.

Abstract: When LLMs exhibit uneven performance across planning tasks, these gaps are often attributed to task difficulty. We argue that this explanation is incomplete, as task-level variation may reflect distinct latent planning competencies rather than differences along a single ability spectrum. We study this question on ACPBench-Hard by evaluating multiple LLM families under varying test-time reasoning budgets and applying a multidimensional item response theory model to uncover the latent competency structure underlying LLM planning. The analysis reveals two principal dimensions that shape planning performance: operational reasoning, the ability to evaluate local action applicability and immediate state transitions, and structural enumeration, the ability to reason about goal reachability and landmark structure. Operational reasoning improving under model scaling and longer reasoning traces, while structural enumeration remains comparatively insensitive. Our findings motivate competency-level evaluation of LLM planning, shifting the focus from whether models improve overall to which planning competencies improve, under what conditions, and why.

### [184] [PREF-Gate: Provenance-Constrained Relational Evidence Fusion with Validation-Gated Selection for Graph Fraud Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11212)
*Liming Liu,Chao Hu,Mingfei Lu,Yiwei Ge,Xingle Li,Heyuan Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces PREF-Gate, a fraud detection framework that cleanly separates label-free graph context from label-derived neighborhood evidence, using a validation-time gate to decide when relational evidence is safe and beneficial to use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In graph-based fraud detection, models can use both structural/feature context and neighborhood labels (e.g., nearby fraud labels). However, using neighbors’ labels is tricky: if the label of the queried node or any validation/test label leaks into the neighborhood risk computation, the evidence becomes invalid, leading to information leakage and over-optimistic evaluation. Existing methods often blur this distinction, lack explicit provenance guarantees for label usage, and may silently violate proper train/validation/test separation. The authors want a principled, auditable way to use label-derived relational evidence only when it is both valid and empirically supported, while still achieving competitive detection performance.

Method: They formalize the problem as “provenance-constrained relational evidence use,” distinguishing between context (label-free) and label-derived evidence under strict validity conditions. They design PREF-Gate, which has: (1) a **context expert** that uses only attributes, one-hop feature means, feature residuals, and degree descriptors without any labels; (2) an **evidence expert** that augments the context features with neighborhood risk built only from training labels, excluding the target node’s label, plus empirical-Bayes summaries that quantify support, uncertainty, availability, and shrinkage of this relational evidence; and (3) a **finite validation gate** that, before test inference, chooses between the context expert, the evidence expert, or one of three fixed probability mixtures of their outputs, and also fixes the decision threshold. This gate is selected solely using held-out validation data, under a finite policy set, ensuring auditability and preventing adaptive overfitting. They evaluate PREF-Gate across multiple datasets and compare it under a common splitting and protocol with many baseline methods.

Result: On three real-world fraud detection benchmarks—Amazon, YelpChi, and TFinance—using five identical stratified data splits and 14 baseline methods under the same protocol, PREF-Gate achieves mean AUPRCs of 0.9085 (Amazon), 0.8104 (YelpChi), and 0.8913 (TFinance), indicating strong ranking performance. The learned gate behavior is dataset-dependent: it consistently chooses the **label-free context expert** for all Amazon and YelpChi splits, while for all TFinance splits it prefers a **mixture** that includes relational evidence. This empirically demonstrates that label-derived neighborhood information is not uniformly beneficial; its utility varies by dataset and is recognized by the validation-driven gate. The framework also tracks failure modes and supports evaluation under different review budgets.

Conclusion: PREF-Gate shows that high-quality, auditable fraud detection on graphs is possible while strictly respecting label provenance constraints. The central conclusion is conditional: label-derived relational evidence should be used only when held-out validation clearly supports its benefit; in some datasets, label-free context alone is optimal. By providing two fixed experts, a finite gate policy, and explicit accounting of evidence provenance and failures, PREF-Gate yields a transparent, knowledge-based decision pipeline that can be audited and trusted in deployment, avoiding subtle information leakage and overfitting that can plague relational fraud models.

Abstract: Relational fraud detection can exploit both label-free graph context and label-derived neighborhood evidence, but these two information sources obey different validity conditions. In particular, neighborhood risk becomes invalid when a queried node's own label, or any validation or test label, enters its construction. We formulate this issue as provenance-constrained relational evidence use and present PREF-Gate, an auditable decision framework with two fixed experts and a finite validation gate. The context expert uses attributes, one-hop means, feature residuals, and degree descriptors without labels. The evidence expert adds self-excluded, training-label-only neighborhood risk and empirical-Bayes summaries that expose support, uncertainty, availability, and shrinkage. Before test inference, the gate selects either expert or one of three pre-specified probability mixtures and fixes the decision threshold. On Amazon, YelpChi, and TFinance, using five identical stratified splits and 14 same-protocol methods, PREF-Gate obtains mean AUPRC values of 0.9085, 0.8104, and 0.8913. It selects the label-free expert on all Amazon and YelpChi splits and an evidence mixture on all TFinance splits. Thus, the main result is conditional rather than universal: label-derived relational evidence is useful only where held-out validation supports it. The framework couples competitive ranking performance with an explicit label-provenance contract, finite selection policy, failure accounting, and review-budget evaluation, providing an auditable knowledge-based decision pipeline for graph fraud detection.

</details>

The Validator role prevents all safety breaches from being executed. Scars reduce overall token usage by 15.1% by eliminating redundant validator checks. CAS further cuts token costs by 55.9% under constrained resources by restricting unnecessary communication.

Conclusion: Separating creativity, safety, and analogy-making into different LLM roles, combined with learning from failures via Scars and managing communication via CAS, enables safer and more efficient exploration than debate-style approaches. The framework preserves strong runtime safety, improves reachability of challenging goals, and significantly reduces token costs through learned constraints and bandwidth control.

Abstract: LLM agents today are caught in an awkward bind. Lock them down with static safety instructions and they rarely venture beyond the obvious; give them free reign with tools and multi-agent debate, and safety violations quickly follow. Rather than forcing a single model to juggle both creativity and caution, we separate the concerns across specialized roles. A Disrupter generates unconventional proposals, a Validator enforces hard runtime checks at the tool gateway, and a Broker pulls in distant but relevant analogies. Failures are not discarded -- they are compiled, via MCTS, into compact, signed constraint patches we call Scars. These patches are cached locally and inherited by future cohorts, turning repeated failures into reusable, low-cost runtime constraints. In a spatial-semantic sandbox (N=20 runs, p<0.01), our cohort reaches remote targets where debate fails, the Validator prevents all executed breaches, and Scars reduce token consumption by 15.1% by avoiding redundant validator checks. Furthermore, credit-based Communication Allocation Scores (CAS) restrict outbound bandwidth, reducing overall token costs by 55.9% under resource constraints.

The ARC-AGI benchmark predominantly requires rule induction and was proposed as a measure of gf for both humans and artificial systems. However, its psychometric properties have not yet been examined in human samples. We therefore investigated the psychometric characteristics and nomological network of ARC-AGI in a first study with 100 participants. Associations with figural originality were weak. These findings provide initial support for the validity of ARC-AGI as a measure of human fluid intelligence. Future research should include more rule induction tasks as well as additional multivariate covariates. This study is unusual by studying a task in humans that was initially designed for machines. We suggest systematically embedding AI benchmarks into the nomological network of human cognitive abilities to enable more systematic evaluation and interdisciplinary cooperation.

### [187] [Valid $\ne$ Necessary: Diagnosing Latent Inefficiency in Chain-of-Thought](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11266)
*Daeyeop Lee,Hwanjo Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies and mitigates inefficient over-long reasoning in chain-of-thought outputs from LLMs using a new inefficiency-aware metric and a compression method that saves tokens without hurting accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Chain-of-Thought prompting boosts LLM reasoning but creates long, often redundant reasoning traces that are expensive in tokens. Existing step evaluators focus on correctness, not efficiency, and thus cannot detect valid but wasteful reasoning steps. There is a need for tools and benchmarks that explicitly target information inefficiency in reasoning chains so we can reduce computational cost without sacrificing accuracy.

Method: 1) Construct RIV-GSM8K, a diagnostic benchmark where CoT traces are injected with five types of reasoning inefficiencies such as circular reasoning and excessive decomposition. 2) Evaluate state-of-the-art reasoning step evaluators on this benchmark to show they cannot reliably distinguish necessary steps from inefficient ones. 3) Propose CAID (Context-Aware Information Density), a training-free, information-theoretic metric designed to score each step’s utility within its context by measuring how much new information it adds. 4) Integrate CAID into PACE, a post-hoc compression strategy that prunes low-utility steps from existing CoT traces. 5) Compare PACE against random pruning and PRM-based compression baselines across several reasoning benchmarks.

Result: Diagnostic experiments on RIV-GSM8K show existing evaluators fail to penalize inefficient but logically valid steps, confirming the blind spot. CAID successfully identifies low-utility reasoning steps, and when used within PACE, it compresses CoT traces more effectively than random or PRM-based pruning. On GSM8K, StrategyQA, and ARC-Challenge, PACE achieves 31–53% reduction in token usage while preserving task accuracy significantly better than baselines at comparable compression levels.

Conclusion: Information inefficiency is a distinct dimension of CoT quality that current evaluators largely ignore. The proposed CAID metric can detect low-utility reasoning steps without additional training, and the PACE compression framework uses it to remove informationally redundant content while keeping the logical backbone intact. This enables substantial token savings without loss of performance, demonstrating a practical path toward more cost-efficient LLM reasoning systems and providing a benchmark (RIV-GSM8K) to drive further research on efficient reasoning evaluation.

Abstract: Chain-of-Thought (CoT) prompting has significantly advanced the reasoning capabilities of Large Language Models (LLMs), yet it often incurs substantial computational costs due to over-reasoning: the generation of redundant, verbose, or irrelevant steps. While existing reasoning step evaluators effectively detect logical fallacies and factual errors, our analysis reveals a critical blind spot: they fail to penalize valid but inefficient reasoning steps that inflate token usage without contributing to the solution. To systematically diagnose this limitation, we introduce RIV-GSM8K, a diagnostic benchmark injected with five distinct types of inefficiencies, including circular reasoning and excessive decomposition. Diagnostic experiments reveal that state-of-the-art evaluators struggle to distinguish these inefficiencies from necessary reasoning. To address this gap, we propose CAID (Context-Aware Information Density), a training-free metric grounded in information theory that identifies low-utility steps. To validate the metric's practical utility, we apply it within PACE, a post-hoc compression strategy. Additional control experiments show that the gains of PACE are not explained by trivial pruning: compared with random step removal and PRM-based compression baselines, it preserves accuracy at substantially higher compression rates. Empirical results on GSM8K, StrategyQA, and ARC-Challenge demonstrate that PACE reduces token consumption by 31-53% while maintaining accuracy, confirming that CAID successfully distills informational froth from reasoning chains without compromising deductive validity.

### [188] [Efficient Test-Time Optimization for Multi-Agent Proof Autoformalization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11307)
*Tian-Shuo Liu,Shiyuan Zhang,Zijie Geng,Haoyu Liu,Runjie Xu,Pengyuan Wang,Lei Yuan,Yang Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes ToMap, a multi-agent system that autoformalizes long mathematical proofs by focusing test-time computation on improving proof decomposition, achieving better correctness and faithfulness at lower cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Autoformalizing entire mathematical proofs (not just statements) into formal proof assistants is crucial to scale verifiable mathematical reasoning. Existing methods are either too training-intensive or inefficient at inference time, and they struggle with long-horizon coordination across many proof steps. There is a need for a framework that can handle complex proof structures efficiently while ensuring both syntactic correctness and semantic faithfulness to the original natural-language proofs.

Method: The authors design ToMap, a multi-agent framework organized as a Decomposer-Formalizer-Prover pipeline. The Decomposer breaks long natural-language proofs into atomic, self-contained proof units. The Formalizer converts these units into formal language, and the Prover attempts to verify them in a proof assistant. Through bottleneck analysis, the Decomposer is identified as the key limiting component. Test-time compute is therefore concentrated on iteratively refining the Decomposer via a GEPA-inspired loop that evolves prompts and candidate decompositions. Progress in formal verification and semantic proof-quality rubrics are combined to build a Pareto frontier over candidate decompositions, which then guides the next refinement step.

Result: On the ProofFlowBench benchmark, ToMap outperforms the previous best method by 19.0% on a combined metric of syntactic correctness and semantic faithfulness, while also reducing test-time computational cost. A scaling analysis indicates that most performance improvements are realized within a small number of decomposition-evolution iterations, informing practical choices for test-time budget allocation.

Conclusion: Focusing test-time optimization on the proof-decomposition stage within a structured multi-agent pipeline significantly improves full-proof autoformalization. By coupling formal verification feedback with semantic quality rubrics and iteratively refining decompositions, ToMap achieves higher correctness and faithfulness at lower inference cost, offering a more scalable path toward verifiable, large-scale mathematical reasoning.

Abstract: Full-proof autoformalization bridges extensive mathematical proofs in natural language with formally validated reasoning, offering a pathway to elevate the ceiling of verifiable mathematical reasoning. Unlike statement-level formalization, proof autoformalization is a long-horizon challenge requiring coordination of claims, contexts, and dependencies across many proof steps, yet has only recently come under focused study. Current approaches either rely on costly model training or apply excessive, unguided repair at inference time. To this end, we introduce ToMap, a multi-agent framework that structures proof autoformalization as a Decomposer-Formalizer-Prover pipeline with efficient test-time optimization guided by formal verification and semantic rubrics for proof quality. Rather than distributing test-time compute across all agents, we perform bottleneck analysis and identify the Decomposer as the critical bottleneck: the quality of its atomic, self-contained proof units directly determines whether downstream agents can successfully formalize and prove each step. ToMap therefore treats the Formalizer and Prover as downstream executors and efficiently focuses test-time compute on Decomposer refinement. This refinement follows a loop inspired by GEPA, evolving prompts over candidate decompositions and using formal verification progress together with semantic proof rubrics to define a Pareto frontier that guides the next decomposition update. Experiments on ProofFlowBench show that ToMap improves over the best previous method by 19.0% when evaluated by both syntactic correctness and semantic faithfulness, while requiring lower test-time cost. Scaling analysis shows that most gains emerge within a few iterations of decomposition evolution, guiding test-time budget selection.

### [189] [Calibrated e-CUSUM Decoding for Quantized Reasoning Models: Why Token Log-Probability Is the Wrong Observable for Decoding Monitors](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11317)
*El Hassane Ettifouri,Ayoub Belfatmi,Mahaman Sanoussi Yahaya Alassan,Walid Dahhane*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that a popular log-probability-based signal is theoretically unsuitable for monitoring low-bit LLM decoding, and proposes a better, calibration-based controller that more selectively detects failing reasoning traces.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Low-bit quantization makes small reasoning models cheap to deploy but harms their chain-of-thought quality. Practitioners need decoder-side monitors that can detect when generation is going wrong and then intervene. A seemingly natural signal is the centered token log-probability increment, but its theoretical behavior as a martingale suggests it may not actually indicate trajectory health. The paper is motivated by understanding why this intuitive metric fails and by designing a more reliable, training-free monitor for quantized reasoning models.

Method: The authors first provide a probabilistic analysis showing that the centered token log-probability increment is a mean-zero martingale under the model’s own sampling law, so it mainly measures self-consistency rather than degeneration and is insensitive during confident repetition. They then design a new decoding controller with two components: (i) a degeneration-aware alarm score that blends token uncertainty with explicit verbatim repetition signals; and (ii) a sequential detector inspired by e-process theory. The core product process is Ville-valid under a conditional-mean null, while the practical detector uses a CUSUM-floored variant treated empirically due to history-dependence and autocorrelation. They run a pilot study on GSM8K using DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-1.5B in FP16 and INT4, comparing raw versus calibrated monitors and evaluating detection performance and downstream accuracy.

Result: The analysis proves that the centered token log-probability increment is structurally ill-suited for monitoring, as it forms a mean-zero martingale under the model’s sampling process and becomes nearly uninformative during confident repetition (both log-prob and entropy near zero). The proposed controller, once calibrated, changes behavior from oversensitive (triggering on 93–95% of generations) to a more selective detector that focuses on failing traces, achieving a failure-detection coverage φ≈0.3 and precision ≈0.6 against a 0.38 base rate on GSM8K. In experiments with DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-1.5B in INT4, the controller reduces verbatim degeneration signals and yields a modest but statistically inconclusive accuracy increase from 63% to 69% (McNemar p=0.18, n=100), at the cost of about 28% more tokens. They also empirically find that non-termination is a more common failure mode than looping on GSM8K.

Conclusion: Centered token log-probability increments are theoretically and empirically inadequate as decoder-side health signals because they are martingales under the model’s own sampling and are largely insensitive to repetitive degeneration. A better approach combines explicit degeneration-aware scoring with a calibrated sequential detector grounded in e-process ideas. While the pilot study is small and effects on accuracy are not statistically conclusive, the method substantially improves the selectivity and interpretability of failure detection. The authors position their work mainly as a methodological contribution that clarifies why a widely used observable fails and offers a more principled replacement for monitoring low-bit reasoning models during decoding.

Abstract: Low-bit quantization makes small reasoning models inexpensive to deploy but can degrade their chains of thought. This motivates decoder-side monitors that intervene when generation becomes unreliable. We show that a natural candidate, the centered token log-probability increment $\log p(w_t)+H_t$, is the wrong observable for this purpose. Under the model's own sampling law it is a mean-zero martingale by construction, so it measures sampling self-consistency rather than trajectory health and is nearly silent during confident repetition, where both $\log p(w_t)$ and entropy are close to zero. We introduce a training-free decoding controller that combines (i) a degeneration-aware alarm score fusing token uncertainty with explicit verbatim repetition and (ii) a calibrated e-process-inspired sequential detector. The raw product process is Ville-valid under a conditional-mean null, while the deployed CUSUM-floored statistic is treated as an empirical change detector because the score is history-dependent and autocorrelated. On GSM8K with DeepSeek-R1-Distill-Qwen-1.5B in FP16 and INT4, calibration turns a monitor that fires on 93--95% of generations into a selective detector of failing traces ($φ\approx 0.3$, precision $\approx 0.6$ against a 0.38 base rate). In this pilot, the controller reduces measured verbatim-degeneration signals and yields a positive but statistically inconclusive INT4 accuracy change from 63% to 69% (paired McNemar $p=0.18$, $n=100$), at a 28% token-budget cost. We also find that non-termination, rather than looping, is the dominant failure mode on GSM8K. The main contribution is methodological: an explanation of why centered token log-probability is inadequate for decoder monitoring and a calibrated, cautiously evaluated replacement.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: They design a neuro-symbolic harness that treats the language model as a proposer inside a generate-verify-repair-trace loop. Symbolic verification modules check for specific constraints such as event-local consistency, collision avoidance, and serialization legality. When verification fails, the harness either repairs the proposal or abstains, and it traces the process. They test this pipeline on 40 controlled tasks across four language-model pairs, including adversarial prompts, and compare raw model output to harnessed output using both automatic checks and blinded expert evaluation.

Result: With the harness, audited delivery yield (the proportion of outputs that meet the specified constraints) improves from 13.3% for raw generation to 48.1%. In expert blind tests, harness-generated scores are preferred over raw model outputs in adherence to rules, perceived legality of the twelve-tone technique, coherence, and overall musical quality.

Conclusion: Wrapping a large language model with a symbolic verification and repair loop substantially improves the structural validity and perceived quality of twelve-tone music generation. The approach demonstrates that neuro-symbolic harnesses can reliably enforce local constraints and filter out degenerate outputs without needing end-to-end retraining of the base model, though it stops short of guaranteeing global legality of entire pieces.

Abstract: Large language models can produce superficially legal twelve-tone scores that collapse into degenerate textures. We introduce a neuro-symbolic harness that wraps a language-model proposer in a generate-verify-repair-trace loop with symbolic verification. The complete pipeline improves event-local consistency without claiming whole-piece legality. Across 40 controlled tasks and four paired models, audited delivery yield rises from 13.3% under raw generation to 48.1% with the harness, which explicitly abstains otherwise. The pass rate of a narrower collision and serialisation-consistency check rises from 33.5% to 58.3%, while degeneracy remains near 0.05, including under exploratory adversarial prompting. A blinded evaluation by five experts also shows a descriptive aggregate preference for harness candidates over raw generation in adherence, perceived legality, coherence, and overall quality.

### [192] [Compile, Then Page: Executable SOP Programs and a Capability-Gated Runtime for Procedural LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11346)
*Chenglin Yu,Li Yin,Ying Yu,Hongxia Yang,Ming Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a way to compile enterprise standard operating procedures (SOPs) into machine-readable pseudo-code and execute them with a program-guided stack machine and an LLM, showing this improves reliability and safety for strong models while sometimes hurting weaker ones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprise use of LLM agents requires them to follow long, conditional, safety-critical standard operating procedures. Natural language SOPs are ambiguous and hard for models to follow reliably, so the authors want a systematic, machine-executable representation and runtime that improve adherence and safety without reducing capability.

Method: They compile textual SOP constraints into executable pseudo-code that a program-guided (PG) stack machine can run. The stack machine manages an active frame that is exposed to the LLM, which performs semantic execution of the steps. They evaluate this on SOPBench, a three-arm benchmark, across six models, separating the effects of the SOP representation from the runtime guidance. They perform ablations such as full-program cursor (only active frame first but retaining the whole program) and selective visibility, and run paired probe and audit measurements to understand why models differ.

Result: Compiled SOP text never significantly harms performance and can improve it by up to 16 points where the original prose SOPs underperform. Runtime PG guidance helps only for strong models: two strong models show consistent gains across seven domains (58:19 and 75:31 discordant pairs in favor of PG), while weaker models are negatively affected. The full-program cursor ablation recovers most of the refusal-rate improvement seen with PG, and selective visibility gives smaller extra gains. Probe and audit experiments indicate the strong/weak performance gap arises from differences in spontaneous state discipline rather than raw reconstruction ability. On a financial-domain benchmark (Bank), the three primary experimental arms achieve 70.4, 86.4, and 92.8 scores respectively, with 100% correctness on refusals.

Conclusion: Encoding SOPs as compiled pseudo-code and running them with a PG stack machine generally improves adherence and safety for capable LLMs, especially in safety-critical settings, while not hurting and sometimes helping even when PG is disabled. However, runtime guidance should be deployed selectively: strong models benefit, weak ones degrade. The authors recommend first compiling SOPs into structured form, then enabling active-frame paging only for models that pass a discipline check, using their probes and audits to decide when PG will help.

Abstract: Enterprise agents must follow long-horizon, conditional, safety-critical standard operating procedures (SOPs). We compile machine-readable SOP constraints into executable pseudo-code and run them with a program-guided (PG) stack machine that pages the active frame while an LLM performs semantic execution. A three-arm SOPBench study across six models separates representation from runtime: compiled text never significantly hurts and gains up to 16.0 points where official prose underperforms. Runtime guidance is capability-gated. Two strong models independently show positive seven-domain PG contrasts (58:19 and 75:31 discordant pairs), whereas weak models are harmed. A full-program cursor ablation (active frame first, complete program retained) recovers much of the strong-model refusal gain; selective visibility adds a smaller improvement. Paired probe and audit measurements track this divide to spontaneous state discipline rather than reconstruction ability. On Bank the three primary arms rise from 70.4 to 86.4 to 92.8, with 100% refusal correctness. Practical guidance: compile first; enable active-frame paging only after a model-level discipline check.

### [193] [From Neural Network Decisions to Training Cases: An Exact Account via Case-Based Decision Theory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11347)
*Manli Yan,Yuebin Lin,Yaowen Yu,Yong Zhao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: They propose a way to decompose a neural network’s linear readout into contributions from individual training cases, to audit decisions at the case level.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In high-stakes applications, stakeholders need to know which specific training examples support a model’s decision, not just feature-level explanations. Existing attribution methods don’t align well with case-based decision theory used in human reasoning and auditing. The authors aim to bridge neural network predictions with CBDT-style reasoning to enable transparent, case-level audits without retraining models.

Method: Fix a neural representation and fit an ordinary least squares (OLS) linear readout (probe) for actions/outputs. Using properties of linear regression and the empirical Gram matrix of the representation, they derive an exact decomposition of each action score as a weighted sum of the training samples’ observed returns or labels. They analyze when these weights admit a similarity-based CBDT interpretation versus when they should be viewed as signed influence coefficients from Gram geometry. They then define audit signals—such as tracing scores back to cases, measuring coherence of actions, and detecting weakly supported decisions—and evaluate the method on synthetic CBDT data and several real datasets.

Result: For multiple tasks (synthetic CBDT, PJM energy bidding, Adult Income, and Default Credit), the proposed decomposition recovers the underlying case-based preference structure and provides meaningful case-level attributions. It achieves the highest mean Top-30 consistency compared with other attribution baselines, indicating that the training cases it highlights as most influential are stable and relevant. It is also competitive at reconstructing the true support of decisions. The probe’s fidelity to the original model is validated through how well it reconstructs the original action scores.

Conclusion: A simple OLS probe on top of a fixed neural representation is sufficient to obtain an exact, interpretable case-based decomposition of model decisions. This enables practical case-level auditing—identifying which training examples support each decision, assessing coherence, and flagging weakly supported actions—without retraining the underlying network or needing access to its training trajectory. The approach both aligns with CBDT where possible and provides a general influence-based view elsewhere.

Abstract: Neural networks increasingly guide decisions in high-stakes domains such as medical diagnosis, credit approval, and energy bidding. Audit in these settings requires case-level evidence: which training cases support an action and what outcomes they carried. Case-based decision theory (CBDT) formalizes this reasoning by aggregating outcome support from remembered cases. We show that an OLS action readout fitted on a fixed neural representation admits an exact case-based decomposition. Each action score is a weighted sum of training-case returns, with coefficients determined by empirical Gram geometry. We identify a sufficient regime for CBDT similarity semantics; outside it, the coefficients should generally be treated as signed Gram-geometric influence. The decomposition yields audit signals that trace scores to training cases, measure action coherence, and identify weak support. Across synthetic CBDT, PJM, Adult Income, and Default Credit tasks, the method recovers case-level preference structure and achieves the highest mean Top-30 consistency among compared attribution baselines, while remaining competitive on support reconstruction. The audit requires only fitting an OLS top-layer probe, without retraining the representation or accessing the original optimization trajectory; probe fidelity is measured by score reconstruction.

This leads to suboptimal use of historical knowledge during multi-step diagnosis. Method: The paper introduces OpsMem, a dual-memory framework for LLM-driven failure diagnosis. Multi-agent LLM-based diagnosis is then conditioned jointly on the short-term state and the activated long-term memories. After an incident is solved, OpsMem distills and consolidates newly gained diagnostic experience back into the long-term memory so it can benefit future cases.

Result: On a real-world Huawei microservice failure diagnosis dataset, OpsMem is compared against representative baselines that use agentic reasoning and knowledge augmentation. The experiments show that OpsMem significantly improves performance, with Match and Relevant metrics increasing by up to 46.88% and 18.39%, respectively, over the strongest baseline, indicating better diagnostic accuracy and relevance of suggested evidence or hypotheses.

Conclusion: OpsMem demonstrates that explicitly modeling both short-term diagnostic state and long-term operational experience, and enabling them to interact via cross-memory resonance, can substantially enhance LLM-based failure diagnosis in complex software systems. The framework not only improves accuracy on the evaluated dataset but also provides a way to continually accumulate and operationalize diagnostic knowledge over time.

Abstract: Failure diagnosis in modern software systems requires iterative evidence acquisition and hypothesis reasoning guided by operational experience. Existing LLM-based methods improve diagnosis through agentic reasoning or knowledge augmentation, but they often lack a mechanism to coordinate the evolving diagnostic state with operational experience during iterative diagnosis. We propose OpsMem, a dual-memory framework that maintains a short-term memory for the current diagnostic state and a long-term memory for reusable operational experience. OpsMem uses cross-memory resonance to activate state-relevant long-term memory, conditions multi-agent diagnosis on the short-term and activated long-term memories, and consolidates reusable experience from solved incidents back into long-term memory. Experiments on a real-world Huawei microservice failure diagnosis dataset show that OpsMem outperforms representative agentic-reasoning and knowledge-augmented baselines, improving Match and Relevant by up to 46.88% and 18.39% over the strongest baseline, respectively.

The authors want a principled way to track and control the evolving evidence state in omni-modal QA, enabling more targeted information acquisition and better reliability.

Method: The authors propose Omni-Decision, a training-free system that formulates omni-modal QA as a query-scoped evidence-closure process. For each question, the system maintains an explicit, structured evidence state, which includes: (1) confirmed evidence; (2) unresolved conflicts; (3) explicit fact and computation dependencies; and (4) open evidence needs. All planning, tool calls, and decisions are conditioned on this shared state. Heterogeneous observations from media analysis, web search, computational tools, and verification modules are normalized into a common format and then deterministically judged and committed to the evidence state through state-update rules. This shared state guides targeted evidence acquisition, validation, repair of inconsistencies, and the decision of when to stop and answer. The authors also conduct no-state ablations and trajectory audits to isolate the effect of explicit state control.

Ablation studies without the structured state and audits of system trajectories show significant performance drops and reveal that the explicit evidence-state mechanism is central to the gains in multi-step omni-modal evidence seeking.

Conclusion: Explicit, structured evidence-state control is highly beneficial for omni-modal QA agents. By turning question answering into an evidence-closure process over a shared, inspectable state, Omni-Decision enables more targeted and reliable evidence gathering, better handling of sparse and cross-modal signals, and clearer stopping and repair decisions. The strong performance improvements on OmniGAIA and WorldSense, together with ablation and trajectory analyses, support the claim that modeling and updating an explicit evidence state is a key design principle for future multi-step, multi-modal reasoning systems.

For each query, Omni-Decision maintains a structured evidence state containing confirmed evidence, unresolved conflicts, fact and computation dependencies, and open evidence needs. A shared state view conditions planning, evidence acquisition, validation, repair, and finalization. Heterogeneous observations from media, web, computation, and verification modules are normalized, judged, and committed through deterministic state updates. ### [197] [The Ebb and Flow of Multimodal Focus: Scheduling Visual Relay Windows for Grounded VLM Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11436)
*Wencheng Ye,Yi Bin,Yujuan Ding,Hongye Fang,Zheng Wang,Xing Xu,Jingkuan Song,Yun Zhang,Sirui Da,Heng Tao Shen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies and manipulates a specific mid-layer "visual relay" phase in vision-language models to improve evidence-grounded multimodal reasoning, proposing TRACE, an inference-time method that re-allocates and preserves visual attention, yielding consistent performance gains on grounding-sensitive benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although modern vision-language models perform well on multimodal reasoning benchmarks, their use of visual evidence is fragile: visual information often gets distorted or lost once processed by the language components, leading to answers that are weakly grounded in the image. There is a lack of mechanistic understanding of how visual and textual signals are processed across model depth, and how this affects grounded reasoning quality. The authors aim to uncover the internal dynamics of multimodal attention and leverage that understanding to stably ground model reasoning in visual evidence.

Method: The authors conduct a mechanistic analysis of the attention patterns and internal activations of vision-language models across layers. They identify a three-stage progression of multimodal focus: (1) early layers organize attention based on the input question, (2) a mid-layer phase where visual tokens dominate and act as a relay of evidence (the Visual Relay Window, VRW), and (3) later layers focus on answer formation. They characterize how the VRW’s geometry changes with different tasks and show via causal interventions that this phase is critical for grounded generation and differentiates unsupported answers from well-grounded reasoning. Building on this, they introduce TRACE, a task-adaptive inference-time control framework that adds lightweight trained modules to reshape how attention and representation mass is allocated during the prefill phase (to better use the VRW) and to preserve the constructed visual support during decoding after the modality handoff from vision to language.

Result: TRACE is applied to four open-weight vision-language model backbones and evaluated on seven benchmarks, especially those where grounding in visual evidence is crucial. The method yields substantial performance improvements in grounding-sensitive settings, with an average gain of 4.33 points and maximum gain of 6.6 points over baselines. It also improves performance on more reasoning-heavy multimodal tasks, demonstrating that enforcing better visual grounding does not trade off with higher-level reasoning but instead can enhance it.

Conclusion: The internal attention dynamics of vision-language models follow a consistent three-phase pattern, with a mid-layer Visual Relay Window playing a pivotal role in transmitting visual evidence for grounded reasoning. By explicitly identifying and controlling multimodal focus across model depth, especially in this relay phase, it is possible to significantly improve evidence-grounded multimodal reasoning without changing model weights extensively. TRACE exemplifies this approach as a unified, lightweight, and effective inference-time control mechanism that enhances both grounding-sensitive and reasoning-heavy tasks across diverse VLM backbones.

Abstract: Vision-language models increasingly succeed on multimodal reasoning benchmarks, yet their visual evidence often becomes unstable once it enters the language stack, weakening evidence-grounded reasoning. To understand this fragility, we examine the internal dynamics of VLMs through a mechanistic lens and uncover a stable three-stage redistribution of multimodal attention focus across depth: an early question-conditioned organization, a critical middle visual-dominant relay, and a late return to answer formation. We operationalize the middle phase as the Visual Relay Window (VRW), and show that its geometry varies with task demand, is causally tied to grounded generation, and distinguishes unsupported answers from stronger reasoning trajectories. Guided by this internal rhythm, we propose TRACE, a task-adaptive inference-time control framework with lightweight trained modules. It reshapes relay allocation during prefill and preserves assembled visual support after handoff during decoding. Across four open-weight VLM backbones and seven benchmarks, TRACE delivers large gains on grounding-sensitive settings, improving them by 4.33 points on average and by up to 6.6 points, while also improving reasoning-heavy tasks. These results show that explicitly controlling multimodal focus across depth offers a unified and effective mechanism for strengthening evidence-grounded multimodal reasoning.

### [198] [Enhancing Query Efficiency for d-DNNF Representations Through Preprocessing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11492)
*Jean Marie Lagniez,Emmanuel Lonca*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how different CNF preprocessing techniques affect the efficiency of accessing satisfying assignments (models) for SAT formulas, especially for sampling, direct access, and enumeration, and shows that model-count–preserving preprocessing combined with d-DNNF compilation yields strong performance gains.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Accessing models of CNF formulas—whether to sample them uniformly, retrieve specific models, or enumerate all of them—is central in many applications (e.g., probabilistic reasoning, verification, configuration). However, CNF instances are often large and complex, so preprocessing is routinely used to simplify them. Most existing preprocessors are designed for SAT solving and may not preserve the exact solution space (e.g., they break equivalence or change model counts), which can invalidate tasks that need faithful access to all models. The paper is motivated by the need to understand which preprocessing techniques remain sound and useful for model access tasks and how to exploit them effectively.

Method: The authors theoretically analyze a range of existing CNF preprocessing techniques from the perspective of three tasks: uniform model sampling, direct model access, and model enumeration. They classify preprocessors based on properties such as formula equivalence preservation and model-count preservation, showing that many state-of-the-art techniques that break equivalence are unsuitable for faithful model access. They then focus on preprocessors that preserve the number of models and describe how to maintain auxiliary preprocessing information so that model access queries remain sound after preprocessing. Finally, they compile the preprocessed CNF formulas into d-DNNF representations and experimentally evaluate the impact of different preprocessing strategies on efficiency and robustness across a broad benchmark set.

Result: The paper finds that typical high-performance preprocessors that do not preserve equivalence are generally not appropriate for uniform sampling, direct model access, or complete enumeration because they distort the model space. In contrast, carefully chosen, model-count–preserving preprocessing techniques can be safely combined with model access tasks if the right side-information from preprocessing is retained. The experiments on diverse benchmarks show that these model-count–preserving preprocessing pipelines are both efficient and stable in practice. When formulas are compiled into d-DNNF after such preprocessing, model access queries benefit from substantial performance improvements compared to using unpreprocessed CNFs or unsuitable preprocessing configurations.

Conclusion: The study concludes that for applications requiring faithful access to models of CNF formulas, one must use preprocessing techniques that preserve model counts rather than aggressive equivalence-breaking simplifications common in SAT solving. By preserving and exploiting preprocessing metadata, these techniques can be effectively integrated with d-DNNF compilation pipelines, leading to robust and significant runtime gains for uniform sampling, direct model access, and enumeration. The work thus provides both guidance on safe preprocessing choices for model access tasks and empirical evidence that such choices yield practical performance benefits.

Abstract: In this paper, we investigate preprocessing techniques aimed at improving the efficiency of accessing models of propositional formulas represented in conjunctive normal form (CNF). We focus on three fundamental tasks: uniform sampling, direct model access, and model enumeration. Our analysis reveals that most state-of-the-art preprocessors, when they do not preserve formula equivalence, are generally unsuitable for these tasks. In contrast, we demonstrate that preprocessors which preserve model counts can be effectively leveraged, provided relevant preprocessing information is maintained. To validate our approach, we perform extensive experiments on a diverse suite of benchmarks from multiple domains. The experimental results show that our preprocessing methods are both efficient and robust, yielding significant performance improvements for model access queries when CNF formulas are compiled into d-DNNF representations.

### [199] [Lesioned Multimodal Language Models Reproduce Aphasic Picture-Naming Patterns](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11621)
*Yong Yang,Xiang Guan,Sophie Arheix-Parras,Saeed Ahmadi,Roger Newman-Norlund,Leonardo Bonilha,Christopher Rorden,Julius Fridriksson,Rutvik H. Desai,Srihari Nelakuditi*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: People with post-stroke aphasia show structured patterns of naming errors that inform diagnosis and theory, but current models either are not individualized or use specialized architectures. It is unknown whether modern, general-purpose multimodal language models can be perturbed to act as subject-specific ‘digital twins’ that recapitulate a person’s characteristic error distribution in picture naming tasks.

Method: The authors use LLaVA 1.6, a multimodal language model, and apply controlled perturbations (‘lesions’) by injecting noise into different layers and proportions of units at varying magnitudes. They then have the perturbed model perform picture naming analogous to the Philadelphia Naming Test. Human responses from 278 persons with aphasia on that test are labeled into seven error types via a neural classifier. The model’s outputs under many perturbation settings are similarly categorized and compared to each patient’s empirical error profile. They systematically search the perturbation parameter space and benchmark against Monte Carlo baselines to verify that any matches are due to the joint pattern across error types, not chance overlap in individual categories.

Result: Six of the seven clinical response categories—correct, semantic, mixed, unrelated, neologism, and no-response—were reproduced by the perturbed model at proportions comparable to those observed clinically, while formal paraphasias were not well captured. For 97.8% of persons with aphasia, the authors found at least one perturbation configuration that matched their individual error distribution in at least six of seven categories; for 79.5% of patients, a configuration matched all seven categories. Monte Carlo simulations showed that these matches exceed what would be expected from random category-wise similarity, indicating genuine reproduction of the joint error structure.

Conclusion: General-purpose multimodal language models, when systematically perturbed, can serve as quantitative models of individual aphasic naming behavior, capturing detailed error distributions for most patients. This supports the feasibility of using such models as ‘digital twins’ of people with post-stroke aphasia in picture naming, opening avenues for individualized simulation, hypothesis testing about lesion effects, and potentially future clinical applications, while also highlighting limitations in capturing certain error types like formal paraphasias.

Abstract: Aphasia following stroke commonly produces systematic naming errors with characteristic profiles, but whether general-purpose language models not designed for clinical simulation can reproduce these patterns remains untested. We investigated (1) whether lesions or controlled perturbations to a multimodal language model can reproduce different types of errors in picture naming, and (2) whether the framework can reproduce the complete error profile of individual persons with aphasia (PWAs). Using LLaVA 1.6, we evaluated perturbation configurations that varied the layer, proportion, and amount of noise applied to model units. We examined 278 PWAs on the Philadelphia Naming Test, classifying responses into seven categories using a validated neural classifier. Six of seven response categories (correct, semantic, mixed, unrelated, neologism, no response errors) emerged at clinically-comparable proportions across distinct parameter space regions, with formal paraphasia being the exception. Searching the perturbation space revealed configurations that reproduced the individual error profile in at least six of seven categories for 97.8% of PWAs and in all seven categories for 79.5% of PWAs. Monte Carlo baselines confirmed that this matching reflects joint inter-category structure rather than marginal overlap. These results establish a quantitative framework for reproducing individual aphasic error patterns in picture naming. They suggest the potential for language models to serve as digital twins of individuals with post-stroke aphasia.

The paper is motivated by the question of whether LLMs, trained on vast human-generated text, implicitly learn human decision biases and could be used as a flexible, scalable proxy for human behavior in uncertain choice situations such as route selection.

Method: The authors use route choice as a testbed for human decision-making under uncertainty. The LLM-generated choices are systematically compared against the behavioral patterns predicted by CPT and known from empirical human studies, focusing on whether the models’ decisions show similar deviations from full rationality.

Result: The experiments show that LLMs systematically deviate from fully rational, expected-utility-maximizing choices and instead display decision patterns aligned with core cumulative prospect theory effects, such as overweighting small probabilities, underweighting large probabilities, and exhibiting non-symmetric treatment of gains and losses. Across a variety of route-choice scenarios, LLM decisions are consistent with human-like behavioral biases under uncertainty, suggesting that the models have internalized forms of prospect-theoretic behavior without explicit parameter specification.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that large language models can effectively reproduce key human behavioral biases in choice-making—specifically those characterized by cumulative prospect theory—purely through prompt-based querying. This indicates that LLMs can act as scalable, parameter-free surrogates for human decision models in domains like route choice, potentially enabling more realistic large-scale agent-based simulations and new methodologies for AI-driven behavioral research. The findings open a path toward using generative AI as a foundational component in modeling and simulating complex human decision processes without traditional, labor-intensive behavioral calibration.

Abstract: Human choice behavior, including route choice, exhibits systematic behavioral biases that deviate from the assumptions of full rationality. Cumulative prospect theory (CPT) has been widely recognized as an effective framework for characterizing such behavioral patterns. However, its large-scale application, particularly in simulation and agent-based modeling, critically depends on specifying individual-level CPT parameters, which remain a major bottleneck. Conventional approaches typically rely on surveys and controlled experiments to calibrate CPT parameters, yet these methods are difficult to generalize and often fail to capture the full diversity of human decision-making. To address this challenge, this paper investigates whether large language models (LLMs) can reproduce human behavioral biases in choice-making without explicit specification of prospect-theoretic parameters. Experimental results demonstrate that LLMs are capable of reproducing non-rational human choice biases and can exhibit decision behaviors consistent with prospect-theoretic effects under uncertainty. These findings suggest that generative AI models may provide a scalable alternative for modeling human decision processes and offer a promising foundation for next-generation large-scale agent-based simulation and AI-driven behavioral research.

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to reduce residual decoding errors and boost trajectory fidelity without requiring more or better neural recordings.

Method: The authors design a two-stage decoding framework called CNN–LSTM–RL. First, a CNN–LSTM deep learning model decodes continuous 3D kinematic trajectories from EEG during motor imagery, capturing spatial–temporal EEG patterns. Second, a reinforcement learning agent is trained offline to operate directly on the CNN–LSTM’s predicted trajectories, without EEG input, to correct systematic residual kinematic errors. The RL agent learns a policy that transforms predicted trajectories to better match target trajectories, optimizing accuracy metrics. Performance is evaluated in 2D and VR 3D settings using Pearson correlation coefficients and RMSE along x, y, and z axes, comparing the two-stage approach with the baseline CNN–LSTM.

Result: Compared to the baseline CNN–LSTM decoder, the CNN–LSTM–RL framework yields substantially improved decoding performance. In VR 3D tasks, correlation improves from 0.6420 to 0.7780 (p = 0.0059), a 21.2% relative gain, while RMSE drops from 0.0714 to 0.0441 (p < 0.0001), representing a 38.2% reduction. These statistically significant results show that post hoc RL-based residual correction can markedly refine predicted trajectories.

Conclusion: The study concludes that a scalable two-stage decoding framework combining CNN–LSTM-based EEG decoding with offline reinforcement learning–based residual correction can significantly enhance continuous 3D motor imagery kinematic decoding. By operating only on the decoder’s output trajectories and not requiring additional neural data, the RL stage effectively reduces systematic kinematic errors, increasing correlation and lowering RMSE in both 2D and VR environments. This approach improves the robustness and precision of non-invasive BCIs, and the authors highlight its potential to advance applications in neurorehabilitation, prosthetic control, and virtual interaction systems.

Abstract: Decoding continuous three-dimensional (3D) motor imagery (MI) using non-invasive electroencephalography (EEG)-based brain--computer interfaces (BCIs) remains challenging due to signal variability and residual decoding errors. We propose a two-stage decoding framework that applies reinforcement learning (RL) to perform residual kinematic correction on the outputs of a CNN--LSTM decoder (CNN--LSTM--RL). The RL agent is trained offline without direct EEG input and instead operates on predicted kinematic trajectories to optimize movement accuracy relative to target trajectories. Decoding performance was quantified using Pearson correlation coefficients ($r$) and Root Mean Square Errors (RMSE) along the $x, y$, and $z$ axes. Compared to CNN--LSTM applied alone, CNN--LSTM--RL improved the mean correlation from $0.5076$ to $0.7181$ ($p = 0.0005$) in 2D and from $0.6420$ to $0.7780$ ($p = 0.0059$) in VR, with relative gains of $41.5\%$ and $21.2\%$, respectively. Correspondingly, RMSE was reduced from $0.0890$ to $0.0532$ (2D, $p < 0.0001$) and from $0.0714$ to $0.0441$ (VR, $p < 0.0001$), representing relative reductions of $40.2\%$ and $38.2\%$. These findings demonstrate that this scalable framework enhances 3D BCI MI decoding by correcting kinematic errors via offline residual RL without extra neural data, advancing neurorehabilitation, prosthetics, and virtual interaction.
