### [203] [HCRMap: Pressure-Aware Hot-Expert Residency Mapping for 3.5D MoE Chiplet Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11586)
*Yongqin Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces HCRMap, a runtime framework that dynamically replicates and places hot experts of MoE LLMs across 3.5D multi-chiplet memory tiers to reduce latency and resource bottlenecks, significantly outperforming prior systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mixture-of-Experts LLMs only activate a few experts per token, but real workloads show strong and persistent expert hotness skew, where some experts are frequently used while others are rarely used. On emerging 3.5D multi-chiplet systems, this skew leads not just to compute imbalance but also to severe pressure on communication links, memory bandwidth, I/O, and execution queues because hot experts become bottlenecks. Existing work mostly focuses on reducing token movement or load balancing computation, which is insufficient when the fundamental issue is how and where hot expert weights are placed and reused across heterogeneous memory tiers.

Method: The authors design HCRMap, a hot expert residency mapping framework for MoE inference on 3.5D multi-chiplet systems. At runtime, HCRMap monitors expert hotness and system resource pressures (communication, memory, queues) as well as weight loading and migration costs. Using this information, it dynamically decides which experts to promote to faster or closer memory tiers, retain in place, demote to slower tiers, or evict entirely. It also maps incoming token groups to appropriate resident expert replicas, coordinating expert replication and token routing so as to relieve bottlenecks across compute and memory hierarchies.

Result: In experiments on 3.5D multi-chiplet hardware setups, HCRMap achieves large end-to-end latency reductions compared with prior MoE systems. Specifically, relative to Hydra, it lowers latency by 43.6% for the prefill stage and 43.0% for the decode stage; compared with MoEntwine, by 34.5% and 33.1% in prefill and decode; and compared with PIMoE, by 46.7% and 46.0%, respectively.

Conclusion: Dynamically managing the residency and replication of hot experts across memory tiers is more effective than solely minimizing token movement for MoE LLMs on 3.5D multi-chiplet systems. By being pressure-aware and jointly considering communication, memory bandwidth, and queue utilization, HCRMap substantially improves MoE inference latency over state-of-the-art baselines, demonstrating that hot expert–centric mapping is a key design principle for future MoE deployments on advanced hardware architectures.

Abstract: Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) large language models (LLM) activate only a small number of experts during inference, but token routing introduces persistent expert hotness skew: a small set of hot experts continuously receives most tokens, while the remaining experts are lightly loaded. On 3.5D multi-chiplet systems, this skew not only causes compute imbalance but also amplifies pressure on communication, memory bandwidth, I/O, and execution queues. Therefore, the core problem is not simply to reduce token movement, but to dynamically place and reuse hot expert replicas across different memory tiers.
  This paper proposes HCRMap, a hot expert residency mapping framework for pressure-aware expert replica management in 3.5D MoE inference. Based on expert hotness, weight loading cost, migration overhead, and runtime resource pressure, HCRMap dynamically determines which experts should be promoted, retained, demoted, or evicted. It then maps routed token groups to suitable resident replicas, thereby jointly mitigating communication, memory, and queue bottlenecks.
  Experimental results show that HCRMap reduces end-to-end latency by 43.6% and 43.0% over Hydra in the prefill and decode stages, respectively; by 34.5% and 33.1% over MoEntwine; and by 46.7% and 46.0% over PIMoE.

### [204] [MAGIC: Transition-Aware Generation of Navigable Multi-Scene Game Worlds with Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11594)
*Tsz Hei Fan,Choi Wing Fung,Yuxuan Wan,Shuqing Li,Michael R. Lyu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MAGIC, a system that automatically generates fully playable multi-scene 3D game projects with consistent portals and navigable layouts from a single text prompt.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Creating multi-scene 3D game worlds is tedious and error-prone because each portal between scenes must align on both sides, interiors must remain navigable after furnishing, and the connectivity must stay consistent across multiple files. Existing LLM and multimodal scene generators focus on single scenes, so they cannot easily produce a coherent multi-scene world with reliable transitions. The authors aim to reduce authoring effort while ensuring cross-scene consistency, in-scene navigability, and correct, executable transitions between scenes.

Method: The authors propose MAGIC, a four-stage prompt-to-project pipeline. First, MAGIC plans a shared, transition-aware intermediate representation of the whole multi-scene world based on a single natural-language prompt. Second, it specifies each scene while enforcing portal reachability using a flood-fill validator to ensure navigable layouts. Third, it jointly generates the 3D scenes and their transition scripts grounded in this plan. Fourth, it composes all scenes and scripts into a single runnable game project. They also introduce a transition-focused evaluation agent that actually plays through the project, running each transition to test whether it functions as intended, going beyond static fidelity metrics used in prior work.

Result: On a benchmark of 100 multi-scene cases, MAGIC can always produce an executable multi-scene game project. For end-to-end transition identification, it achieves 0.99 precision, 0.95 recall, and 0.96 F1, indicating accurate detection and implementation of transitions. Stepwise analysis shows that MAGIC recovers more ground-truth portals and creates more navigable layouts than both a generic LLM baseline and the Holodeck system.

Conclusion: MAGIC demonstrates that a structured, multi-stage pipeline built around a transition-aware intermediate representation and explicit navigability checks can transform a single natural-language prompt into a coherent, runnable multi-scene game. By focusing on cross-scene consistency, in-scene navigability, and executable transitions—and by introducing an agent that evaluates transitions through play—the system substantially improves over existing single-scene LLM-based generators for constructing connected 3D game worlds.

Abstract: Multi-scene navigation (clearing an objective in one bounded space and then crossing a portal into the next) is a defining feature of contemporary 3D games, but authoring it is laborious: every portal must have consistent endpoints on both sides, each interior must remain navigable once it is furnished, and the resulting connectivity must be kept consistent across many files. Recent large language model (LLM) and multimodal LLM (MLLM) scene generators have made single-interior synthesis dramatically cheaper, yet they produce one scene at a time and cannot, by naive repetition, yield a connected multi-scene world. We identify three obstacles that single-scene methods leave unsolved: cross-scene consistency, in-scene navigability, and the evaluation of whether a transition actually works. We present MAGIC, a prompt-to-project system that addresses all three. MAGIC is a four-stage pipeline that turns a single natural-language prompt into a runnable multi-scene game project: it plans a shared transition-aware intermediate representation, specifies each scene while enforcing portal reachability with a flood-fill validator, generates the scenes together with their transition scripts, and combines them into one project. Because existing single-scene fidelity metrics never execute a transition, we further introduce a transition-focused evaluation agent that runs each transition in play. On a new benchmark of 100 multi-scene cases, MAGIC produces an executable project for every case and reaches 0.99 precision, 0.95 recall, and 0.96 F1 on end-to-end transition identification; stage by stage, it recovers more ground-truth portals and yields markedly more navigable layouts than an LLM baseline and Holodeck. Our code is available at https://github.com/sereneee1201/MAGIC/.

### [205] [Interaction Scaling: Grounding the Third Axis of Test-Time Compute](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11598)
*Bojie Li,Noah Shi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that using interaction with external tools and real-world feedback is a distinct and more powerful way to scale test-time performance than longer reasoning or more sampling, as long as the feedback and evaluation metrics are properly grounded in actual observations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing test-time scaling methods—longer chain-of-thought reasoning and best-of-N sampling—are limited because they rely solely on the model’s fixed weights and a static prompt. They cannot introduce genuinely new information, so their improvements plateau, especially on harder tasks. The authors aim to investigate whether involving external tools and real-world feedback can overcome this limitation and yield continuous gains.

Method: The authors formalize a third axis of test-time scaling: interaction. In this setup, the model proposes an artifact (e.g., code, slides, visual layouts), an external instrument observes its real behavior, and the model revises its output based on grounded feedback. They define grounding as the requirement that both the feedback signal used for revision and the metric used for evaluation must be connected to actual observations. They run experiments on challenging coding tasks under a fixed token budget, comparing (1) extended reasoning, (2) best-of-N sampling (including an oracle selector), and (3) various interaction loops such as proposer-reviewer architectures. They further evaluate on rendered visual artifacts where flaws might be hidden in screenshots and compare VLM-based judging to a tool that directly measures real layouts and properties of the figures/slides.

Result: On hard coding tasks, both extended reasoning and best-of-N sampling hit performance plateaus, even when an oracle chooses the best candidate among samples. In contrast, all tested interaction strategies continue to improve with more interaction cycles. Their proposer-reviewer framework achieves 100% pass rate with no variance across runs, and this effect generalizes across three different model families. For visual artifacts, a standard VLM judge mislabels 14 out of 15 clearly flawed figures as “perfect” because it only sees screenshots that hide critical defects. A grounded measurement tool that inspects the true layout reveals that the interaction loop eliminates 40–74% of defects across four different modalities. When the same VLM is used as the reviewer in the loop, slide layouts degrade, whereas the measurement tool-based loop improves them.

Conclusion: Interaction with grounded external feedback constitutes a third, fundamentally distinct axis of test-time scaling, separate from longer reasoning and more sampling. Its benefits only become apparent when both the feedback used for revision and the evaluation metrics are grounded in actual observations of artifact behavior. Under these conditions, interaction scaling can surpass the performance ceilings of reasoning-only and sampling-based approaches, achieving substantial and robust gains across coding and visual artifact tasks.

Abstract: There are two standard ways to spend more compute at test time: let a model reason longer, or sample more attempts and keep one. Both share a hidden limit: they are internal. Every extra token comes from the same frozen weights and the same prompt, so neither can tell the model anything it does not already know. We study a third way, interaction: the model proposes an artifact, an external instrument observes how it actually behaves, and the model revises. Each cycle imports a real observation, so interaction breaks through the ceiling the other two hit.
  We argue that a single variable governs this third axis, grounding, and that it must hold on both sides of the loop. The feedback that drives revision must come from an instrument that actually observes the flaw, and so must the metric that scores the result. On hard coding tasks at a fixed token budget, reasoning-only and best-of-N sampling both plateau (the latter even when an oracle picks the best sample), while every interaction strategy keeps improving; our proposer-reviewer harness reaches a perfect 100% pass rate with no run-to-run variance, and the gain holds across three model families. On rendered visual artifacts, the usual judge (a vision-language model, or VLM, reading a screenshot) rates 14 of 15 visibly broken figures "perfect," because the screenshot hides the flaws before the judge can see them. A tool that measures the real layout instead shows the loop removing 40-74% of defects across four modalities; and that same VLM, used as the reviewer, makes slide layouts worse where the measuring tool repairs them. Interaction scaling is real and distinct from reasoning and sampling, but only visible when both the feedback and the metric are grounded.

</details>


### [206] [Auditing the Risk Claims of Distributional Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11607)
*Hari Prasad*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper audits distributional reinforcement learning (RL) agents and finds that the “risk” encoded in their learned return distributions is largely an artifact of training rather than a faithful representation of environment stochasticity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Distributional RL agents predict full return distributions, and these predictions are increasingly used directly for interpretability, risk-sensitive decision-making, and safety monitoring. Theory suggests these risk-sensitive claims should be scrutinized, but there has been no direct empirical test of whether the learned risk trade-offs are actually true in the underlying environment. The authors aim to rigorously evaluate whether distributional RL agents’ risk claims are trustworthy or just artifacts of the training process.

Method: The authors design an auditing framework that (1) defines a decision-relevant screening metric based on the excess Wasserstein gap between the top two actions (equivalently, the amount by which first-order stochastic dominance is violated), (2) obtains ground-truth risk information using snapshot-restart Monte Carlo rollouts from specific states, and (3) wraps the whole procedure in a robust statistical harness employing permutation-based null distributions, bootstrap-based refutation, and false discovery rate (FDR) control to avoid spurious conclusions. They apply this framework to QR-DQN, C51, and IQN agents trained on MinAtar (33 runs), and to a pretrained near-SOTA QR-DQN on full Atari Breakout. They also run positive-control experiments where true risk differences are known, and test interventions such as training specifically for risk, ensembling, and recalibration.

Result: On MinAtar, 40–95% of the strongest risk trade-offs claimed by the agents are statistically refuted at 95% confidence, and virtually no risk claim is confirmable. The locations of the strongest alleged risk trade-offs are statistically indistinguishable from what a risk-blind (truth-ignorant) process would produce. The observed risk patterns are structural artifacts: they appear early in training, show no correlation with final performance, and are specific to random seeds. Similar behavior is observed at full Atari scale: for a pretrained near-state-of-the-art QR-DQN on Breakout, all top risk claims are refuted. Positive-control tests show that when true risk differences are present, the audit recovers 96–100% of them with high correlation (0.89–0.92), indicating the audit is sensitive and not at fault. Acting on the agents’ CVaR-based risk advice in highly flagged states can be beneficial in some cases but is often as bad as or worse than random choice. Attempts to fix the issue via risk-focused training, ensembling, or recalibration do not remove the artifact; recalibration only passes by effectively erasing informative risk structure.

Conclusion: The “risk” structure learned by current distributional RL agents such as QR-DQN, C51, and IQN is largely not a faithful reflection of environment stochasticity, but instead a persistent structural artifact of training. As a result, using these learned distributions directly for risk-sensitive control, interpretability, or safety monitoring is unreliable. The authors’ auditing framework can detect these failures and distinguish genuine risk information from artifacts. However, current methods fail this audit, and common fixes like risk-aware training, ensembling, or recalibration are insufficient—recalibration can pass only by making the risk estimates uninformative. The paper underscores the need for new approaches if distributional RL is to provide trustworthy risk assessments, and it provides a reusable toolkit while warning about methodological pitfalls that can yield misleadingly positive audits.

Abstract: Distributional reinforcement learning agents learn full return distributions that are increasingly read at face value: for interpretability, risk-sensitive control, and safety monitoring. We ask a question theory anticipates but that has not been measured directly: are the risk claims of a trained distributional agent true? Our audit combines a decision-relevant screening metric (the excess Wasserstein gap between the top two actions, which equals the mass by which first-order stochastic dominance is violated), ground truth from snapshot-restart Monte Carlo, and a statistical harness (permutation nulls, bootstrap refutation, FDR control) without which the audit itself manufactures false conclusions. Across QR-DQN, C51, and IQN on MinAtar (33 runs), 40-95% of the strongest claimed risk trade-offs are refuted at 95% confidence, the placement of the strongest claims is statistically indistinguishable from truth-blind, and essentially no claim is confirmable: for these agents, the learned "risk" reflects a training artifact rather than environment stochasticity. The artifact is structural (fully formed early in training, uncorrelated with final score, idiosyncratic to each seed) and appears unchanged at full-Atari scale, with every top Breakout claim of a pretrained near-state-of-the-art QR-DQN refuted. Positive controls of known magnitude confirm 96-100% of real claims (correlation 0.89-0.92): the reading measures the agents, not the audit. Acting on the heads' CVaR advice at their most-flagged states ranges from beneficial to significantly worse than chance. Neither training for risk nor ensembling removes the artifact, and recalibration passes the audit only by nullifying the claims: the head is uninformative, not merely miscalibrated. We release the toolkit and document two silent pitfalls that produced convincing but wrong audits of our own.

</details>


### [207] [Think Through a Bottleneck: Hourglass Reasoning for Rigorous Induction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11696)
*Huan Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Hourglass reasoning, a method that improves few-shot inductive reasoning in frozen large language models by enforcing strict separation between reasoning stages, allowing only a compressed symbolic rule representation to pass between them.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing self-refinement and chain-of-thought style prompting often fail to reliably improve few-shot inductive reasoning in LLMs, and explicitly verbalizing inferred rules can even hurt performance in some domains. The authors aim to understand what actually drives better inductive reasoning in frozen LLMs and to design a framework that systematically enhances it across heterogeneous tasks.

Method: They propose Hourglass reasoning, where a frozen LLM is orchestrated into four structurally isolated stages: (1) an Induction module encodes support examples into a schema φ and a transient scaffold z; (2) a Deduction module derives a task rule T from (φ, z) and then discards z; (3) an Implementer compiles (φ, T) into concrete task-specific artifacts; and (4) an error-driven Refiner updates (φ, T) and regenerates artifacts from scratch. Only the compact symbolic pair (φ, T) is allowed to cross stage boundaries, enforcing context isolation and anchoring refinement to the inferred rule.

Result: Across three benchmarks—ARC-AGI-2 for visual abstraction, ChipBench for hardware (Verilog) synthesis, and BBEH-Linguini for textual rule induction—using GPT-5.5 and Gemini 3.1 Pro, Hourglass significantly outperforms iterative-refinement baselines: up to +14 best-of-5 accuracy points on ARC-AGI-2, nearly doubling Verilog synthesis accuracy on ChipBench (31%→58% with GPT-5.5), and mitigating or reversing the negative impact of explicit verbalization on IOL-style linguistic puzzles for Gemini 3.1 Pro.

Conclusion: The improvements stem from enforced isolation between reasoning stages and the quality of the initial induction, rather than from surface prompt wording or the specific symbolic representation. Thus, for frozen LLMs, the structure of information flow—compressing and passing only a symbolic rule state between stages—is more critical for inductive reasoning performance than how that rule is verbally expressed.

Abstract: Self-refinement often fails to strengthen few-shot inductive reasoning in large language models. Prompting a model to explicitly state its inferred rule does little on its own. What actually matters is a structurally enforced isolation between reasoning stages, so that information can only pass between them as a compressed symbolic state.
  We introduce \textbf{Hourglass reasoning}, which enforces strict context isolation between reasoning stages. The frozen LLM acts as a meta-constructor, building for each task a symbolic encoder--decoder: an Induction module compresses the support examples into a schema $φ$ (encoder) and a transient scaffold $z$; a Deduction module derives rule $T$ (decoder) from these and discards $z$; an Implementer compiles $(φ, T)$ into artifacts; an error-driven Refiner revises $(φ, T)$ and regenerates artifacts from scratch. Only $(φ, T)$ crosses stage boundaries, so all refinement stays anchored to the rule.
  We evaluate Hourglass across three benchmarks spanning visual abstraction, hardware synthesis, and textual rule induction, using GPT-5.5 and Gemini 3.1 Pro. On ARC-AGI-2, it raises best-of-5 accuracy by up to 14 points over an iterative-refinement baseline. On ChipBench, it nearly doubles Verilog synthesis accuracy with GPT-5.5, from 31\% to 58\%. BBEH-Linguini draws on puzzles from the International Linguistics Olympiad, a setting where prior work has shown that explicit verbalization can hurt performance. Hourglass mitigates this tendency, and on Gemini 3.1 Pro, it reverses the effect entirely.
  Ablations confirm that these gains come from the isolation between stages and the quality of the initial induction, not from prompt wording or the particular symbolic form used. It is how information flows through the reasoning process, rather than the language used to express it, that drives inductive reasoning in frozen LLMs.

</details>


### [208] [Playful AI in Professional Email: A Field Experiment on Tone and Recipient Engagement](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.11749)
*Ziv Ben-Zion,Teddy Lazebnik*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Study tests how GPT-5-assisted email rewriting in playful vs professional tones affects workplace email outcomes, finding tone changes positivity but only indirectly affects recipient behavior via emotional positivity, not direct AI use.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand whether and how AI-assisted writing tools like LLMs actually influence recipients’ behavior (opens, replies, timing) in real workplace communications, and to unpack whether any effect comes from AI use itself or from the emotional tone it generates.

Method: Randomized crossover field experiment with 121 employees from six companies over three weeks. Each participant sent real work emails under three conditions: unaided writing, GPT-5 playful-tone rewriting, and GPT-5 professional-tone rewriting. The study analyzed 16,880 emails, measuring linguistic positivity and behavioral outcomes (open rates, reply rates, response times) and using statistical models to test direct and indirect (mediation via positivity) effects.

Result: Playful GPT-5 editing significantly increased emotional positivity in emails (B=+0.068, p<0.001), while professional GPT-5 editing significantly decreased emotional positivity (B=-0.041, p<0.001). Neither AI-assisted condition directly changed open rates, reply rates, or response times. However, higher within-sender emotional positivity strongly predicted higher open odds (OR=2.05) and reply odds (OR=3.32, p<0.001), revealing an indirect pathway from AI-assisted tone changes to recipient behavior via positivity.

Conclusion: AI-assisted workplace communication does not alter recipient behavior simply through the fact that an LLM is used; instead, its impact operates indirectly through the emotional tone of the language it produces. Choosing and managing AI-generated tone—particularly increasing positive affect—may be key to leveraging LLMs to enhance workplace engagement.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are rapidly reshaping workplace communication, yet whether AI-assisted writing changes how recipients actually behave, and through what channel, remains unknown. Here, in a randomized crossover field experiment, 121 employees across six companies sent work emails under three conditions over three weeks: unaided writing, GPT-5 rewriting in a playful tone, and GPT-5 rewriting in a professional tone. Across 16,880 emails, playful editing increased emotional positivity (B=+0.068, p<0.001), and professional editing decreased it (B=-0.041, p<0.001), yet neither condition directly altered open rates, reply rates, or response times. Instead, within-sender positivity strongly predicted both opening (OR=2.05) and replying (OR=3.32, p<0.001), a significant indirect pathway through which AI editing shaped behavior, in the absence of any direct effect. These findings suggest that AI-assisted communication shapes workplace engagement not through its use, but through the emotional tone of the language it produces.

</details>
